HEADLINE: Though Racial Politics Lurks, It Is Muted in Virginia Contest

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: RICHMOND, Va., Sept. 24

 BODY:
Because of an editing error, an article on Monday about the campaign for governor in Virginia misstated a political policy of Senator Harry F. Byrd. He followed a strict ''pay as you go'' policy on public spending, declining to issue bonds for schools and hospitals. Gov. Mills E. Godwin Jr., a Byrd protege, abandoned the policy in 1965.
More than three decades ago, at a secret meeting called by Harry F. Byrd, then Virginia's uncontested political boss, Representative Watkins M. Abbitt helped to plan the state's defiant program of massive resistance to court-ordered school desegregation.
Although eventually declared unconstitutional, the program for a time helped galvanize the rest of the South in the fight against the Supreme Court's landmark decision in 1954, Brown v. Board of Education.
Few in those years would have believed Mr. Abbitt could ever support the candidacy of a black man for governor. Fewer still would have believed a black man could make a serious run for governor in Virginia before it had been done in Massachusetts, New York or Michigan. But Mr. Abbitt, long retired from Congress, is supporting L. Douglas Wilder, the Democratic nominee, in his neck-and-neck race this fall against J. Marshall Coleman, a race that could make Mr. Wilder the nation's first elected black governor. 
 
'Represents Tremendous Change'
''This isn't the millennium,'' said A. E. Dick Howard, a professor at the University of Virginia law school, ''but it represents a tremendous change. There may be sputters and gasps along the road, and obviously race is still an element in politics here, but we aren't going back to the old days. Each one this time knows the first candidate to introduce race overtly into the contest will be hurt.''
Indeed, a Washingon Post poll today showed Mr. Wilder holding a fragile 45 percent to 42 percent lead over his Republican opponent, Mr. Coleman, a former Attorney General. The poll, conducted from Sept. 14 to 21, had a margin of sampling error of three percentage points.
The poll, which surveyed 1,008 likely voters, also indicated that 6 respond-ents in 10 believe racial attitudes are playing a significant, if muted, role in the hard-fought election that will be decided Nov. 7.
But changes in the views of people like Mr. Abbitt, together with many other factors, have made it possible for Mr. Wilder to win nomination and election as lieutenant governor, and now to make a convincing run at the state's top job. One change is the simple ability of blacks to vote.
Before the abolition of the poll tax and the Voting Rights Act of 1965, blacks were effectively disenfranchised. Now they constitute roughly 20 percent of the state's electorate. Moreover, they represent an indispensable electoral asset not only for Mr. Wilder but also for white Democrats like Mr. Abbitt's son, Watkins Jr., a member of the Virginia Legislature.
In one sense, the Virginia of 1989 is an utterly different state from that of 1959, when black children were first admitted to all-white schools in Norfolk and Arlington. At that time the population was predominantly rural and overwhelmingly native born; now it is largely urban and suburban, concentrated in Richmond, the Hampton Roads area and the Washington suburbs, and the Census Bureau says the 1990 count will almost certainly show that more than a third of the state's white population was born outside the South.
 
Seen as More of Northern State
Among the states of the old Confederacy, only Florida has a larger percentage of residents born outside the state.
State Senator Hunter B. Andrews of Hampton, whose family settled there in the 17th century, said, ''Economically and even politically, modern Virginia has more in common with the Middle Atlantic Sstates than with the South, which is where they put us when they broke up the old phone system.''
Former Representative M. Caldwell Butler of Roanoke commented: ''We've been exposed to the world. The in-migration of all these people from the Northeast and the Midwest has helped to make us a more sophisticated state than most in the South, and bigotry has gradually gone out of style here.''
Virginia has always been slightly different from the rest of the South, even though Richmond was the capital of the Confederacy and Robert E. Lee, its foremost military commander, was a Virginian. Its black population - and the fear and resentment of blacks among poor whites - has never been as large as that of South Carolina, Alabama or Georgia.
It passed the first anti-lynching law in the 1920's, and the historian Virginius Dabney says it was the only Southern state that never had to call out the National Guard to quell racial violence in the civil rights struggles of the 1960's.
''Economics are what has always mattered,'' said William Gaynes Ellison, a Richmond lawyer. ''We were always less agrarian and more modern than, say, Mississippi, and modern Virginia's mainstay has been the Federal Government, especially the military, where blacks play a major role.''
Professor Howard commented: ''Virginians have a legalistic cast of mind. Massive resistance was fought out in the courthouses, not on dark roads. We didn't have night riders, and integrationists here, as much as they may have been shunned at work, didn't live in fear of the shotgun.''
 
Then Virginia Beach Erupted
Recent events have not been as kind.
The National Guard was sent to Virginia Beach this year to help restore order after looting by black college students over the Labor Day weekend, and the episode might hurt Mr. Wilder. A service station attendant near Front Royal, who declined to give his name, said he was ''a little bit for Coleman before Virginia Beach, and a little more for Coleman after it.''
''Every other day on the front pages and on the newscasts, they're talking about 100,000 black youths rioting and looting, as the investigation goes on, and that can't help Doug,'' argued James H. Latimer, the retired chief political writer of The Richmond Times-Dispatch, ''especially since there's not much difference between the two of them on most of the key issues.''
On the other hand, Mr. Latimer said, ''Wilder condemned the violence at Virginia Beach, and he's always had a sense of values straight out of Horatio Alger's book, so white people have never found him threatening.''
Just as the Byrd organization for a time delayed desegregation in Virginia, 's surrender to desegregation, so more recent political leaders have sped the state's development of tolerance to black candidates like Mr. Wilder.
Interestingly, the first of these was Mills E. Godwin Jr., a Democrat whose election as governor in 1965 was the last gasp of the machine. He pushed through a new state constitution guaranteeing civil rights, sponsored the first sales tax, which helped Virginia catch up with other states in financing public services, and broke with Senator Byrd's policy of issuing bonds to pay for new schools and mental hospitals.
Then, in 1970, at a time of great stress over enforced busing, Gov. Linwood Holton, a Republican, chose to put his daughter into a school that was 85 percent black. He took her to class the first day, and the news photos were seen all over the state.
''That image has stuck with Virginians for almost 20 years,'' said Prof. Larry J. Sabato of the University of Virginia, a specialist in his state's politics. ''Holton set a tone of racial moderation that the state desperately needed to witness.''
Mr. Holton, one of whose sons is working for Mr. Wilder, said in an interview, 'It is a real credit to this state that no one can predict how the Wilder-Coleman race will come out.'' He acknowledged, as did others in with politics here, ''There will be some who vote for the Democrat because he's black, and some who vote against him for that reason.''
''But this is a complete reversal of those terrible days of massive resistance,'' Mr. Holton added. ''Thirty years ago, you would have said 'nigger' and the race would have been over. That has disappeared now.''
In the transitional years and since, black political leadership also played a significant part in changing old ways, especially in Richmond, which had had a viable middle-class black community since Reconstruction.
Oliver Hill, a black lawyer, was elected to the City Council in 1948. And in 1977, Henry L. Marsh 3d, a civil rights specialist, was chosen as the first black mayor of the city, whose ceremonial Monument Avenue is still dominated by statues of Generals Lee, Stonewall Jackson and J. E. B, Stuart.
''I think the astuteness and the stability of the black community made a big difference,'' Mr. Marsh said. ''The thing is to apply steady pressure, as we did, keep your head, and people will come around. It happened first with the white politicians and then with the business community here.''
While racism still exists in the state, he said, people have had ample evidence that they need not be frightened of black political leadership, ''because blacks have served as mayor not only in Richmond but also in Charlottesville, Roanoke, Newport News, Portsmouth, Petersburg and Fredericksburg, and that may have helped pave the way for a Wilder victory.''

HEADLINE: Gorbachev's Anti-Magic;
Lionized Abroad Yet Tormented at Home, He Is Falling Short of Soviet Expectations

BYLINE: By BILL KELLER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Sept. 24

 BODY:
One of the great contradictions of Mikhail S. Gorbachev is that while the outside world grows more and more captivated by his skill and charm, much of his own country seems increasingly immune to the magic.
This weekend, Mr. Gorbachev has been embraced anew as a partner in peacemaking - by the Bush Administration, after unusually warm sessions between Foreign Minister Eduard A. Shevardnadze and Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d in Wyoming.
He has been hailed as a bold visionary who will bring prosperity to his people, and who deserves all the help he can get, by Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher of Britain, who spent four hours on Saturday with the Soviet leader.
Yet at home, peace, stability and plenty seem beyond the reach of Mr. Gorbachev's personal talent, even beyond his political power. 
The Baltic republics are defiant, Armenia and Azerbaijan are reported close to civil warfare, consumers look ahead to winter with something near panic, and Mr. Gorbachev is openly blamed for not waving a wand and making the hard times go away.
While Mr. Baker and Mr. Shevardnadze were savoring the warm relations in the Tetons, Mr. Gorbachev was subjected to sarcastic gibes by some members of a militant parliamentary group who met here to prepare for Monday's opening of a new legislative session.
''What is Gorbachev up to?'' a Ukrainian legislator asked. Democratization? No. Merely ''liberalization of a totalitarian regime.''
And another legislator from the Russian city of Sverdlovsk, gesturing at the beaming figure of Mr. Gorbachev's popular opponent Boris N. Yeltsin, proclaimed, ''I consider that there is an alternative to Gorbachev.''
After listening to this for a day and a half, the chairman of one of the two chambers of Parliament, Rafik N. Nishanov, a former diplomat and a Gorbachev loyalist, was sputtering with indignation.
''For 15 years I worked abroad, took part in many parliamentary events, had contacts with legislators, kings, presidents, prime ministers,'' he said reprovingly to the dissident crowd today. ''I've never seen any place where people publicly expressed such doubts and distrust toward a legally and democratically elected head of state.''
Mr. Nishanov's anger drew derisive hoots from the crowd. This is a group that considers itself among the most ardent supporters of Mr. Gorbachev's promised reforms, but they do not share the view that he was ''democratically elected,'' let alone that he deserves their uncritical loyalty.
How can Mr. Gorbachev be perceived abroad as a master of affairs and yet seem so ineffectual to many of his own citizens?
One reason is that much of Mr. Gorbachev's international success consists of unilateral concessions - on arms control, on human rights, on the war in Afghanistan.
 
Limits on Power
Those concessions are often shrewdly timed, and are sometimes less costly than they seem. Before the Soviet Union conditionally dropped its demand that Washington abandon its plans for a space-based missile defense, for instance, it waited until it was clear that Congress was unlikely to pay for the program, popularly known as ''Star Wars.''
But this concession, which clears a major obstacle to an agreement reducing long-range nuclear weapons, was something that Mr. Gorbachev could deliver on his own initiative.
There is no unilateral concession that will bring economic recovery or ethnic peace at home. Those issues require the cooperation of many rival groups who regard their interests as matters of principle and social justice.
A second reason that many Westerners consider Mr. Gorbachev a marvel is that his endeavors abroad have brought quick gratification. Treaties have been signed and weapons demolished. Families have been reunited. Troops have come home. Surveys suggest that people feel safer.
 
Expectations Rise and Fall
What Mr. Gorbachev is selling at home is a less appealing commodity -greater liberty and the long-range hope of a better life in exchange for years of hard work, patience and sacrifice.
Mr. Gorbachev compounded his domestic problems when he implied quick results, raising what may be impossible expectations and producing today's wide disillusionment.
Third, the Soviet Union is by tradition a conservative society, one where change has been associated with upheaval.
Many Soviet citizens have been elated by the chance to speak freely, but liberty has also been unsettling and in some ways demoralizing. Comforting myths have been demolished, political rivalries are being played out, and nothing seems familiar.
 
A Terrifying Uncertainty
Even those who yearn for something different dread the process of getting there, doubting their countrymen's capacity for polite debate and parliamentary compromise.
Finally, there is Mr. Gorbachev himself, who is viewed in the West as the instrument of change, but seen at home as a more ambiguous figure - both Luther and the Pope, as one critic wrote recently.
The Soviet people might follow a tyrant out of fear, or an angry challenger - a man like Mr. Yeltsin - out of devotion, but they often seem uncertain what to make of Mr. Gorbachev, a man of cool logic who straddles the roles of reformer and pontiff.
Of course, Mr. Gorbachev's success abroad helps him at home, and not only by lowering world tension so that he can redirect attention from defense to domestic modernization.
His summitry has won him genuine respect in the Soviet Union, a feeling being encouraged these days by fulsome press accounts of the Jackson Hole love fest and of Mrs. Thatcher's near-euphoria over perestroika, his program of ecomomic and political restructuring. Even Soviet citizens unhappy with other aspects of his performance might pause before replacing a man who has established the Soviet image as benign, even chic.
 
There Is a Payoff
Moreover, Western Europeans and even some Americans - transformed into something of a perestroika cheering section - have begun to repay Mr. Gorbachev with financial and moral support.
A little-noticed example is the tone of Western radio broadcasts into the Baltic republics, where independence movements have been causing Mr. Gorbachev grief.
The United States formally regards the Baltic republics as captive nations, yet American radio stations beamed into the region have been careful not to stir separatist feelings and have urged caution, lest Baltic unrest unseat Mr. Gorbachev.
Good will abroad does pay off at home. But it does not necessarily buy hope, patience or loyalty from the Soviet public. That will take a harder, scarcer currency.

HEADLINE: Chinese Exiles Form Worldwide Opposition Group

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PARIS, Sept. 24

 BODY:
Chinese exiles and opposition figures from around the world ended an inaugural meeting today establishing the Federation for Democracy in China, an umbrella group that aims to coordinate worldwide opposition to the Beijing Government.
The 150 Chinese, in one of the most important meetings of the Chinese opposition since the crackdown in Tiananmen Square in June, elected Yan Jiaqi, an intense 47-year-old political scientist, as president of the federation. Late in July, 1,100 Chinese students from across the United States met in Chicago to press their cause. 
In an interview, Mr. Jiaqi said he was confident that economic problems in China and a power struggle among the leadership would fuel a public clamor for democracy that would lead to a rehabilitation of the democratic movement.
''Tiananmen Square was a turning point in Chinese history,'' said Mr. Yan, who was a senior adviser to the ousted Communist Party General Secretary, Zhao Ziyang.
Wuer Kaixi, an outspoken leader of the student protesters in Beijing, was elected vice president of the federation. Other founding members were Wan Runnan, one of China's most prominent entrepreneurs; Su Xioakan, a writer and intellectual, and Chen Yizi, a former adviser to Mr. Zhao and the highest-ranking Chinese official known to have gone into exile.
 
Influence Asserted
The federation's leaders asserted that they could exert effective pressure on China's Government even though they are outside the country. They say they expect to obtain funds from Chinese living in Hong Kong, Singapore, the United States and elsewhere.
''There are 56 million Chinese living abroad,'' Mr. Chen said. ''We hope to unite them.''
Mr. Wan, who had headed a computer company, said: ''We can have a great impact even though we're all in exile now. We made a lot of friends in the country, and as soon as we raise any issues, there will be a lot of people ready to follow us.''
In its founding statement, the federation, which will be based in Paris, said it opposed a monopoly of power in China and supported human rights, democracy and an economy that responds to market forces.
Mr. Yan, who had been director of Beijing's Institute of Political Science, was not optimistic about Jiang Zemin, the new party leader and the man chosen by Deng Xiaoping to succeed him as China's paramount leader.
''Jiang is not a reformer, and he is not a democrat,'' he said. ''He is a bureaucrat. What he is gifted at is navigating his way politically. He is a puppet of Deng. I don't think he can remain in power a long time.''
 
Battle to Come
Federation leaders said they foresee a power struggle between Mr. Jiang and Li Peng, the hard-line Prime Minister who was instrumental in the crackdown in June.
The federation's leaders said they would urge governments to continue sanctions against China.''If repression grows worse, then the sanctions should be increased,'' Mr. Yan said. ''But tourism should continue. We don't want China to be closed off from the rest of the world.''

GRAPHIC: Photo of Chinese exiles and opposition figures ending a meeting in France establishing the Federation for Democracy in China. (Agence France-Presse)

SECTION: Section A; Page 19, Column 5; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 766 words

HEADLINE: ESSAY;
Wide Open Spaces

BYLINE: By William Safire

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
The tectonic plates that underlie the American way of living are making loud, grinding noises. Something big is going on in the real America that most of us who live inside urban beltways are missing: proximity is losing its power.
Last time the plates shifted was about four decades ago, when a scarcity of space in big cities and a yearning for home ownership led the postwar generation out to the suburbs. Levittown, L.I., led the way to the new life, followed by a boom in road building, autos, shopping centers. 
This middle-class migration culminated in (a) the abandonment of much of the big city to the fearful rich and the crime-ridden poor, and (b)today's all-hours regional mall, which turns the spending of money into a family entertainment and transforms each suburb into a retailing Las Vegas. (We are what we buy: ''When the going gets tough, the tough go shopping.'') But the relentless expansion of ''greater metropolitan areas'' and the dreary widening of the franchise fringe need not be the wave of the future. Economic reality is now working against that sprawl: we are making more people and God is not making more land near cities. The cost of land determines the cost of housing; suburban land has become too expensive to permit the building of the kind of housing most people can afford.
Mike Kaufman wrote a perceptive and sympathetic piece in yesterday's New York Times Magazine about William J. Levitt, the man who introduced mass production to housing and started the suburban revolution. Now 82 and fighting financial setbacks, Bill Levitt (for whose company I flacked in my youth) knows that the era of turning cheap potato fields into affordable suburban homes is long gone.
As our population grows by three million a year, where will the new families go? Back to the cities, or into suburban high-rises? Bill Levitt says no; he envisions new cities, of fifty thousand to a quarter-million population, not bedroom communities or satellites or one-company towns but ''primary employment towns'' - balanced centers of several industries and varied housing, with schools and cultural amenities - located in what are now rural areas.
New towns? The very phrase draws a horselaugh from embittered bureaucrats and planners. That was the floperoo notion of the 60's, a utopian pipedream that became a developers' drain down which millions of taxpayer dollars were poured. We tried that future and it didn't work.
But one reason the idea flopped was in its execution: the Feds worked directly with developers rather than through localities, placing little emphasis on job creation. A city is first and foremost a place of commerce.
The bigger reason was that new cities in the relative boondocks were an idea whose time had not yet come. It did not make good business sense for a corporation to put its people and plant far from the center of executive action. Proximity, closeness, concentration were all.
Along comes the computer, with the accounting brain centralized no matter where employees and inventories are; along comes teleconferencing, with the ability to communicate and manage face to face while thousands of miles apart; along comes the fax machine, and no need for messengers to fight city traffic. Proximity is now superfluous; you can now be close without being near.
Suddenly it makes sense to put plants and executive offices where the living is easier and the cost of land much lower. If ''out of town'' is the aim, why not all the way?
Retailers who don't need stores don't need urban headquarters; that's why L.L. Bean and Land's End do their mail-order business from places you never heard of. Manufacturers who are end-running middlemen don't want to be near big department stores; that's why, in Martinsburg, W. Va., scores of ''factory outlet'' stores sell Ralph Lauren, Jonathan Logan and Adolfo goods at half-price to crowds of exurban shoppers. (Eighty miles from Washington and the place is packed; those customers are not all hicks.) Republicans in Washington are still guy-shy about new cities, and Democrats worry about seeming to give up on old cities, which is why ''planning'' is a dirty word. One day Congress will wake up and provide incentives to help rural county governments attract groups of core businesses. No billions needed; just a sense of the dispersed, mid-sized urban future and the enthusiasm to facilitate it.
Look out your airplane window sometime; there's plenty of room down there. With the new management technology and the old human need to breathe free, there's no longer a need to crowd together.

HEADLINE: Education and Work: The Missing Link

BYLINE: By Ann McLaughlin; Ann McLaughlin, Secretary of Labor in the Reagan Administration, is a visiting fellow at the Urban Institute, a public policy organization.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
The applause President Bush deserves for taking the dramatic step of convening an education summit meeting with all 50 governors risks being the sound of one-hand clapping unless a prominent connection is made between education and work and competitiveness.
Summit planners may be missing a crucial, and obvious, link between education reform and work. Lifelong learning and retraining of workers are reported to be only a small part of the agenda, despite the fact that our ability to compete in an increasingly global economy will depend on the quality of our workforce.
Relative to students in other countries, United States students lag behind at every grade level, in nearly every subject and at every stratum of ability. Unless we do something about it - and soon - we risk becoming a nation in decline. Our goods and services will not be competitive, our standard of living will stagnate and eventually, if both come to pass, our influence as a world power will wane. 
A few weeks ago, a bipartisan Labor Department commission, composed of business and labor leaders and academics, issued a report called ''Investing in People: A Strategy to Address America's Workforce Crisis.''
The commission's report cites many strategies that have been suggested in recent years to reverse academic failure and offers one more idea. It may be the most important. It involves linking work with school.
What is the purpose of education if not, at a minimum, to prepare people for work? After all, at the most basic level, the skills for successful work and successful living are the same.
Students must be made to see the relationship between scholastic achievement and employment prospects. Nothing, the report says, can make this relationship clearer than the business community's involvement in creating incentives for achievement and the community's assurance of employment for those with adequate academic records.
Thus, one of the commission's recommendations is that school transcripts be made an integral part of the employment process. Employers should require transcripts in making hiring decisions. It is not inconceivable to imagine students being issued portable transcripts at graduation that they could carry to job interviews.
Closely related to this proposal would be the development of national achievement tests that students could voluntarily take to demonstrate proficiency in a variety of academic and vocational subjects. The results could be included in their transcripts.
Another recommendation would have state employment agencies operate placement services in the schools. While many schools diligently try to place students in higher education, very few offer similar support to students seeking jobs.
I would go even further than the commission's report in some respects. The Job Training Partnership Act has been a success because it has directly involved local and regional businesses, through private industry councils, in decisions to allocate Federal funds for training.
Federal education money, particularly vocational dollars, could be channeled through private industry councils or similar bodies to make training more ''market-driven'' and thus more relevant. With more direct involvement by business leaders at local levels, money would almost certainly be better spent on educating students for what businesses need.
Linking work with school, of course, does not mean simply linking work with formal schooling. It means making education - and a culture of learning - relevant to a lifetime of work.
The most important recommendation the commission makes in this regard is to elliminate illiteracy by the year 2000.
Between 20 million and 40 million adults today have literacy problems. As new technologies are developed, it will be difficult for them to work or be trained for new jobs. So, in addition to getting education right the first time, the U.S. must find a way to provide second chance education to adults.
At the very least, anyone who wants to learn how to read and write should have that opportunity at any point in life. The most important objective the nation's leaders can set is the provision of basic skills education, not just to the schoolchildren but to all adults who seek it.
Obviously a single summit meeting cannot produce all the answers. But a good start can be made. What we must earnestly strive for is the will to make the education of our people, and thereby the quality of our workforce, second to none. And unless the connection between education and the workforce is made a prominent part of the summit meeting's agenda, 50 state governors and The Education President will miss the opportunity of our lifetime.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

LENGTH: 2008 words

HEADLINE: New Schools Chief: An Innovator and Taker of Risks

BYLINE: By FELICIA R. LEE

DATELINE: MIAMI, Sept. 24

 BODY:
In two years as Superintendent of Dade County Public Schools, Joseph A. Fernandez earned a national reputation as an innovator and risk taker. He forged links between the business community and the schools, invested schools with more decision-making power and spearheaded a successful drive for a $980 million bond issue to build 49 schools for a woefully overcrowded system.
Mr. Fernandez's ability to lift the expectations of urban schools with large minority enrollments won him respect in a part of the country better known for beaches and cocaine than for academic prowess. When he announced last week that he would leave the nation's fourth largest school system to become chancellor of the largest, in New York City, he gathered mostly praise from school officials, parents and city and civic leaders.
''The most important contribution Joe Fernandez made to public schools in Dade County is he encouraged us to experiment,'' said Daniel Tosado, the principal of Miami Beach Senior High School. Dr. Michael Krop, the chairman of the Dade County School Board, said: ''The important thing I see here is you've got a community that's gotten involved in education. Dr. Fernandez did that.'' 
But Mr. Fernandez also has critics in South Florida. Even some of his admirers say he can be egotistical, overly sensitive to criticism and quick-tempered. Within the school system he has been accused of having too close a relationship with the teachers union, making him too ready to accommodate it.
One school board member, Holmes Braddock, said Mr. Fernandez was ''a great C.E.O.'' but had a huge ego.
''My criticism of Joe is that there is too much 'I' in him,'' Mr. Braddock said. ''Joe is a supreme egotist. I got upset when he said 'my board,' as if he handpicked the school board. Joe was overly sensitive to criticism. If Joe got criticized, he fired off a letter or he called a press conference to straighten it out.''
Though the president of the Miami-Dade branch of the N.A.A.C.P. praised Mr. Fernandez for being ''sensitive'' and ''open to new ideas,'' some blacks have accused him of not doing enough to desegregate the schools. They also say he has reneged on promises to help award contracts and jobs to blacks in the building program financed by the bonds.
 
Support From Black Voters
''I feel he was successful in selling the black community a pig in a poke,'' H. T. Smith, a Miami lawyer, said of the bond issue, which was narrowly approved in a referendum in which most black voters supported it although it was opposed by the local branch of the National Association for the Advancement of Colored People.
''He went out personally and guaranteed people that boundary lines would change to facilitate desegregation, but it hasn't happened. What really disappoints me is that blacks are not getting a fair share of the jobs associated with the building program.''
One school board member, Robert Renick, said Mr. Smith's comments were inaccurate. ''Minorities are getting the lion's share of contracts,'' he said. ''In fact, blacks have somewhere around 20 percent of the contracts, and minorities will get over 50 percent.''
The school board draws attendance boundary lines. The superintendent can make recommendations, but the board has the final say.
Brian Peterson, an associate professor of history at Florida International University in Miami, clashed with Mr. Fernandez two years ago when Mr. Peterson wrote a report criticizing elementary schools on the quality of their teachers and low reading and mathematics scores. The Superintendent replied to the first draft of the report by writing Mr. Peterson a letter disputing the findings and then sent copies of the letter to the university president and the head of the teachers union.
 
'Very Tight' With Union
''I think of him as being a constructive demagogue,'' said Mr. Peterson, who has written five policy papers on the Dade County school system. ''He's done an outstanding job. He's done things I didn't think possible. There are all kinds of good reading and math programs going on in the schools, and he's responsible.''
''I still have concerns,'' he said. ''He's been very tight with the teachers union, so he hasn't really addresed the issue of teacher competency. In certain inner-city schools, as many as 25 percent of the teachers are functionally illiterate. He's done nothing, really, that the teachers union didn't want done.''
Mr. Fernandez, 53 years old, is the son of Puerto Rican parents who worked as a cabdriver and a maid. He was born in East Harlem, where he dropped out of school as a teen-ager to join the Air Force. He later went to college, and after graduation became a math teacher in Miami and then worked his way up.
When Mr. Fernandez took over the Dade County school system in 1987, the dropout rate was 30 percent. Teacher morale was low. Some schools were filled to twice their capacity, and some students were crowded into storefront and portable classrooms. Reading scores were below the national average. Crime in the schools, already bad, was aggravated by gun-wielding students working for drug dealers.
Despite a magnet program, in which students attend specialized schools out of their neighborhoods, schools remained as segregated as county housing patterns. At present, 72 percent of the system's students attend schools that are not racially balanced despite a 1970 court desegregation order.
 
Addressing the Problems
Dade County officials and educators say Mr. Fernandez had begun to address these problems through the school-construction bond issue, a new contract for teachers and innovative programs flowing from his school-based management program, with policy decisions made at each school by a committee made up of the principal, teachers and sometimes parents. In New York City, the central Board of Education runs the high schools while local elected boards administer the middle and elementary schools.
Some say it has been through energy and sheer force of personality that Mr. Fernandez has got things done. He showed up at meetings of parent-teacher groups. He walked the halls of inner-city schools. And he talked to people, often using humor to persuade or reach them, or to deflect criticism.
Pat Tornillo, the head of the United Teachers of Dade, the union, tells about when he and Mr. Fernandez made a joint appearance before a Tiger Bay political club. They were addressing complaints that Mr. Fernandez had been too compliant with union demands and too cozy with Mr. Tornillo.
''He got up and said, 'I want to reassure you that because we wear the same color suit, the same color socks and because I roll over in the morning and say, 'Pat, what are you wearing today?' doesn't mean we are in bed together,' '' Mr. Tornillo recalled.
At a Dade school board meeting, Dr. Krop, the chairman, mentioned that he had been reading in some education journals that students knew little about history. Mr. Fernandez said he had several task forces looking into the problem. Then he paused and said he had a better idea: ''I'm canceling all your journal subscriptions.''
Mr. Fernandez has led a school system in an area that has become accustomed to racial and ethnic tensions. The Miami area has had three major race riots in the last decade, and a bitter fight was waged to make English the official language. The school district is about 45 percent Hispanic and 33 percent black, with the rest non-Hispanic whites. Most of the Hispanic population have Cuban origins, but a refugee influx has arrived in recent years from Central America.
In his first year as Superintendent, Mr. Fernandez put black principals in predominantly Hispanic schools and transferred white principals to black schools. By the end of that year, he had reshuffled the assignments of 48 principals in a system with roughly 285 principals. On a schools tour, he decided on the spot to replace a principal whose elementary school he said was, for the third year in a row, too dirty.
''A lot of people criticized him for making snap judgments,'' said Lucille Baldino, a reading specialist at Touissaint L'Ouverture Elementary School. ''Despite that, it kept a lot of people on their toes. He was the one making the decisions, not someone else. And he'd tell you why he made the decision. He did what he did because he really cared about the schools.''
 
Surprise Visits to Schools
Mr. Fernandez and his wife, Lily, have four children, and he has sometimes scolded those people whom he felt put their interests before children's. He publicly questioned how much influence the N.A.A.C.P. had when it came out against the bond issue, which passed. He also told business leaders to get more involved with the schools if they wanted qualified potential employees.
In his first year he was also known to pay surprise visits to schools to address some finer points of education, admonishing teachers, for example, not to eat or drink in front of students.
Through the bond issue, schools have been built or renovated, even though an unexpected 14,000 additional students, most of them Nicaraguan refugees, created a projected $171 million shortfall this year in the building program.
The dropout rate has shrunk 5.5 percent, to 24.5 percent, in two years. The school-based management system has raised morale, teachers and the teachers union say, and won Dade County schools a national reputation for excellence and innovation.
Innovative programs have been instituted at 116 of Dade's 263 schools. In one, teachers evaluate other teachers. Another involves voluntary Saturday classes in schools in poor sections.
''Without Dr. Fernandez it never would have happened,'' said Dr. Gerald O. Dreyfuss, assistant superintendent for school-based management.
 
'He's Open-Minded'
Charlotte Greenbarg, the president of the Dade County Council P.T.A./ P.T.S.A., said many parents respected Mr. Fernandez. He got black parents to talk to Hispanic parents, she said, and encouraged non-Hispanic whites to feel they had a stake in a district where they were a minority. ''He's open-minded,'' she said.
Working with the teachers union, he persuaded the American Bankers Insurance Group, Miami International Airport and Miami Dade Community College to build schools at their workplaces that are run by teachers.
William O. Cullom, president of the Greater Miami Chamber of Commerce, accompanied the Superintendent to Washington, where Mr. Fernandez sought more money to help the school system absorb immigrant children. ''Our relationship with Joe has been extremely strong,'' he said.
The chamber has 66 committees on education that, with Mr. Fernandez's help, oversee a host of programs. One is a competition for teacher of the year. Another is an ''adopt a senior'' program, in which high school seniors who attend 95 percent of their classes are guaranteed job interviews.
''The bottom line is the man is good,'' said Johnnie R. McMillian, head of the Miami-Dade branch of the N.A.A.C.P.
The new Toussaint L'Ouverture Elementary School is one example of what the school district has done well. It is in the heart of Miami's Little Haiti, so named for its concentration of Haitian refugees, many of whom fled political persecution and economic misery only to find animosity from blacks and whites in Miami.
The Toussaint L'Ouverture school, named for the former slave who led Haiti's revolt against France, has a media center, a computer room and an outdoor stage. Of the 1,020 students in kindergarten through fifth grade in the school, about 700 are Haitian. Because the primary language of many Haitian children is Creole, there are special classes at the school to ease them into speaking English. The underachievers are given tutors, and slow readers can attend reading clinics.
It is a testament to both the district's overcrowding and the school's popularity that it is already 35 students over its capacity. ''The parents want to send their children here,'' said the principal, Marietta Mischia.

HEADLINE: On Prison Barge, All Hands Battle Drugs

BYLINE: By MICHEL MARRIOTT

 BODY:
In a room so humid that its painted aluminum walls sweated, Eric Lewis bolted to his feet and strode to the center of a circle of men, most of whom are like himself: young, street-hewn and black. They are also drug addicts on the mend behind bars.
''Good morning family,'' he greeted the group of 43 men in an eardrum rattling shout. Mr. Lewis then stared blankly ahead. After a dramatic pause, he spoke what he said was his ''heart.'' 
 
'I Thought This Was a Waste'
''I'm a man striving to break my addiction, this disease, which it is,'' said Mr. Lewis, a 27-year-old crack addict from the Bronx. ''At one time I thought this was a waste of time, but now I realize I want - no! - need help.''
Throughout the day in this five-story building, a jail barge moored on the lip of the East River's Pier 36, similar men with similar addictions wage similar inner wars. Since July, the barge, a one-time British troop barracks named the Bibby Resolution, has been used primarily as a special drug-treatment center for inmates with a history of substance abuse, said New York City Correction officials.
With the city's jail capacity at 110 percent, the $21 million Resolution was first sought as a means to rapidly and economically expand the prison system. But with more than 110,000 inmates, of which at least 60 percent are substance abusers, the Resolution was turned into a full-time drug-rehabilitation center.
''It is a sophisticated approach,'' said Richard J. Koehler, the city's Correction Commissioner who is a major proponent of offering more drug treatment to the ''captive audience'' in jails.
Mr. Koehler's approach is becoming more popular. With jail and prison populations expected to increase across the country, fueled largely by drug-related crimes, correction departments from Miami to Los Angeles have been seeking better ways to treat substance-abusing inmates.
And with the Bush Administration promising to press harder for the arrest and imprisonment of more drug users and sellers, the promise of a new wave of drug-abusing inmates entering the nation's correction system looms large.
But only in New York City are inmates being treated on the scale that they are at the Resolution, said Raymond Diaz, who coordinates all of the jail's anti-drug programs, includng the eight that now operate on the barge. Almost 400 specially selected inmates live on the cramped barge that - with its oval windows and low metal ceilings - more resembles a battle cruiser than a jail. It is a difference most inmates appreciate.
Because of the success of the program on the Resolution - more than 70 percent of the program's participants seek outside drug help once released - there are plans this year to create a 700-bed ''drug prison,'' said Dennis P. Whalen, an official with the State Division for Substance Abuse Services.
''To not create systems for those who are drug abusing would be a terribe waste,'' said Mr. Whalen, the deputy director for program services for the state. ''It makes plain common horse sense that if you have a population that is placed in a controlled environment you should take the opportunity to work to recognize their drug problem and do something about it,'' he said.
But correction officials said they are gravely concerned that the state is not providing more treatment slots for inmates once they are released. Like most other addicts seeking treatment, many recently released inmates find themselves on long waiting lists for overcrowded programs.
 
Treatment Outside Jail
That situation, correction officials say, is particularly tragic because much of the help the inmates get on the barge is intended to prepare them for drug treatment outside jail. One official expressed concern that if inmates were forced to languish on waiting lists, then they would soon give up on themselves.
Mr. Whalen said he is working on the problem by trying to bring the criminal-justice and drug-treatment systems closer together.
''We are certainly aware of what's happening on the barge and we want to help broker those two systems in any way we can,'' he said.
Based largely on 18-month residential drug-treatment programs like Phoenix House and Daytop Village, the Resolution drug counselors say they are first trying to help inmates identify their addictions, begin to face the consequences of them and then move to abstain from drugs.
 
Motivating the Men
''Any full recovery process might take a year and half for a full blown addict,'' Mr. Diaz said, adding that the inmate's average length of stay at the jail is 46 days. ''We looked at what the first objectives were in a recovery phase.''
He said much of his work is to motivate inmates to seek treatment in community-based organizations once they're discharged.''That, we feel we can do well,'' he said.
All day, every day, the thin metal walls of the Bibby Resolution vibrate with the sounds of 384 inmates who shout, scream and sob their way through the battle with their addictions. The inmates' ages range from 19 to the 40's.
 
Camaraderie Is Important
Like Dante's Inferno in reverse, the barge is organized so the addict-inmates who progress well move up to advanced programs on the upper floors. On the fifth floor is Project Exodus, where inmates take an active role in helping themselves and their fellow addicts. There, camaraderie becomes important, with inmates joining hands and singing Temptations' hits like ''My Girl.'' Only they change the lyrics to ''drug free.''
Many of the inmates are addicted to a range of substances like crack, cocaine, heroin and alcohol. But most, like John Lowe, are crack addicts. Mr. Lowe, who is 22, stands six feet three inches and weighs 326 pounds. He is hulking and imposing. He carries scars from a stabbing and two shootings on his left calf. ''Cross fire,'' he said.
Yet, his features turn as soft as his surprisingly boyish voice when he talks about his weeks on the barge.
''We are all part of a single family,'' he said, his eyes scanning the busy room, which is papered with hand-drawn anti-drug posters and slogans of inspiration. ''I've seen these brothers open up their hearts, admit things that made them cry.
 
The Process of Recovery
''I've cried myself,'' he said without shame.
It is all part of the process of recovery, said Mr. Diaz, who explained that inmates have to learn to place a value on sharing their pain and not hiding it.
''We call it personality reconstruction,'' he said, stressing too that the inmates relearn what most of their parents once taught them about responsibility, self-pride and self-worth.
''For me it's working,'' said Mr. Lowe, who added that if he can't get a treatment slot outside he would rather stay behind bars. ''I want much more out of life than that drug.''

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

THE ECONOMY
 
State banking regulators around the country have found that thousands of small inner-city businesses through which immigrants send money to relatives are also being used to launder drug money. The regulators say these storefront money-transmitting and check-cashing operations are sending billions of dollars to drug dealers in South America and Asia. [Page A1.] 
 
Orders for American-made machine tools rebounded in August, advancing by 18.8 percent over the July level, as orders from the automotive industry increased significantly. [D1.]
 
The Tax Reform Act of 1986 seems likely to be torn apart by lawmakers in votes this week, almost three years to the day after Congress approved the monumental legislation. [D1.]
 
COMPANIES
 
Lomas Financial sought court protection under Chapter 11 of the Federal Bankruptcy Code, frustrated by creditor talks that had dragged on since June. The company said it had halted payments indefinitely on $2.1 billion in debt. [D1.]
 
RJR Nabisco was in final talks last night to sell Del Monte's canned food and processing unit for $1.47 billion to a partnership led by Merrill Lynch, Wall Street sources said. [D1.]
 
Two top executives and founders resigned at American Healthcare Management, in keeping with a reorganization plan. [D4.]

Mexico's chief debt negotiator said it could become harder for some nations to get new loans because three large American banks have increased their cushion against third world loans. The cure for the greenhouse effect is nuclear-generated power, numerous speakers said at the World Energy Conference in Montreal this week. But others questioned that approach. [D2.]
 
OPEC is struggling to save its quota production system, as more of its members sent clear signals that they planned to increase production to meet the rising demand for oil. [D2.]
 
Negotiators took the first steps last week in multilateral trade talks in Geneva toward writing rules to liberalize trade in banking, tourism and other services. [D3.]
 
For executives in China, political posturing is a way of life, and the chairman of the state-owned China International Trust and Investment has a keen sense for when and how to do it. [D8.]

HEADLINE: Tool Orders Rose 18.8% Last Month

BYLINE: By RICHARD D. HYLTON

 BODY:
Orders for American-made machine tools rebounded in August, advancing by 18.8 percent over the July level as orders from the automotive industry increased significantly, a trade group said in a monthly report to be released today.
The group, the Association for Manufacturing Technology, said that machine-tool orders totaled $226.6 million in August, compared with $190.8 million in July.
Analysts said the August increase reflected greater capital spending by companies that were more comfortable with the immediate economic picture. 
''In August, there were some pretty good-sized automotive orders in exports, and automotive orders tend to be very large deals,'' said Robert Petrie, an analyst with McDonald & Company, a financial services company based in Cleveland.
 
Playing Carefully
''In terms of capital spending, the numbers will go up because we simply haven't spent much, as companies have waited until the last minute to place orders,'' Mr. Petrie added. ''They're playing this economic cycle very carefully. Now it seems that they've waited just about as long as they can.''
Machine tools are power-driven devices that cut or shape metal parts used in everything from appliances to vehicles. Economists consider their sales and orders important indicators of capital-spending patterns by makers of large products like automobiles and aircraft.
Compared with August of last year, machine-tool orders were down by 33.3 percent last month. And for the year to date, orders were $1.9 billion, down by 20.6 percent from $2.4 billion in the first eight months of 1988, the report said.
''While orders are down compared to last year, you have to remember that 1988 was the best year for machine-tool orders since 1980,'' said Albert W. Moore, the president of the association, based in McLean, Va.
Machine-tool shipments were $226.35 million in August, down by 4.8 percent from $237.7 million in July but 47 percent higher than shipments in August 1988. For the year to date, shipments rose to $1.97 billion, up by 39.8 percent from $1.41 billion in the comparable 1988 period, largely because of the boom in orders placed last year by domestic manufacturers and foreign companies.
''The increased shipment is because of last year's order surge,'' said Glenn Finley, the association's statistical director. ''It takes six to nine months to deliver machine tools. Automotive orders were big last year, and there was a little capital-spending boomlet.''
 
Export Shipments Up 41%
The report also said that export shipments were up by about 41 percent for the year, while export orders were down by about 30 percent as foreign demand for American tools has eased.
Richard O. Priebe, the communications director for the Cross & Trecker Corporation, the nation's second-largest manufacturer of machine tools, said, ''The slowdown that we're seeing in orders now won't start showing up until about 1990 because there are very few off-the-shelf products in the machine-tools industry.''
Orders for metal-cutting tools, which include boring mills and lathes, were up by 17.1 percent in August, to $156 million, compared with $133.2 million in July. Metal-cutting tools typically account for about two-thirds of the industry's orders.
The orders for metal-forming tools rose by 22.7 percent, to $70.60 million for the month, from $57.55 million in July.
The August backlog for machine-tool orders was $2.13 billion, little changed from the July level.

HEADLINE: Mexico Says Banks' Move Could Reduce New Lending

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 24

 BODY:
Mexico's chief debt negotiator said during the weekend that the move by three large American banks to increase their cushion against bad third world loans could make it more difficult for some nations to get new loans.
The negotiator, Jose Angel Gurria, a deputy finance minister, suggested that the increase in reserves would mean more flexibility for the banks to pursue their own policies.
The result, he said, could be that new loans to countries like Mexico, Brazil, Argentina and Venezuela will depend much more on how well their economies are growing and being managed, which means some countries could have trouble getting new loans.
''After this, they can choose to do new-money deals on the basis of the merits of each deal,'' Mr. Gurria said of the banks in an interview late Saturday night. He is one of the first senior officials of a large debtor nation to comment on the effect of the reserve move by Manufacturers Hanover, Chase Manhattan and J. P. Morgan. 
But Mr. Gurria said the move, which other banks are expected to follow, was a positive step that should help debt-reduction efforts in other countries. He added that he did not expect the reserve decisions to decrease the new money that would be lent to Mexico under a $54 billion debt agreement reached in July.
Mr. Gurria contended that setting aside the larger reserves, although it forced the banks to take losses, would make them healthier financially in the long run. They would also be better able to help developing countries that were performing well, he added.
The decisions by the three banks to increase their reserves dramatically has raised some doubts about the effectiveness of the Bush Administration's debt strategy, which puts new emphasis on reducing debt but also depends on new lending.
Some bankers and analysts have said that with the larger reserves, more banks were likely to accept debt reduction and, at the same time, decide not to make new loans. The banks' stronger financial position would also make it easier for them to resist pressure from the Bush Administration to provide new money.
The finance ministers and bankers here who are participating in the annual meeting of the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank differed over the effects of the move.
Among the bankers, those who have already decided to take debt reduction in the Mexican deal tended to think the move would hurt new lending, while those considering new loans did not. But they all emphasized the importance of the economic health of the developing nations as a factor in deciding on new loans.
 
Brady Sees New Lending
Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady, for whom the debt strategy is named, said the move by the banks should not hurt new lending. He made his comments in a briefing on Saturday night, after a meeting of the Group of Seven countries - the United States, Japan, West Germany, Britain, France, Italy and Canada.
But he seemed to agree with Mr. Gurria when he indicated that the banks' lending decisions would depend upon the economic outlook for a particular nation.
If a country has a bright future, he said, ''banks will want to be a participant in that.''
The increased reserves, Mr. Brady added, ''will put those banks in a position to do whatever they want.''
A senior Treasury official pointed out on Friday that until the Brady plan began this year, commercial banks had made almost no new net loans to developing countries over the last several years. Some banks, he said, made loans to countries so they could pay interest on existing loans.
Thomas C. Theobald, the chairman of the Continental Illinois Corporation in Chicago, the parent of the Continental Bank, said: ''I don't think it means anything for new lending. I don't think the levels of reserves determine your choice.''
Mr. Theobald said his bank, which has a 50 percent reserve against losses on third world loans, would probably choose some debt reduction and participate in new loans. He said he thought J. P. Morgan's move to a 100 percent reserve was excessive. Chase increased its reserve to 46 percent, and Manufacturers Hanover to 36 percent.
But as to lending in the future, Mr. Theobald said, ''The critical fact has been, and will always be, the performance of the country itself.''
 
Deutsche Bank's View
Asked about the future of new lending, Hilmar Kopper, the managing director of Deutsche Bank A.G. of Frankfurt, said: ''That remains to be seen. That will depend on the economic development and the economic restructuring. No one should expect private bankers to throw good money after bad.''
Mr. Kopper said Deutsche Bank, which is on the 15-member advisory committee to Mexico, would not make new loans in the debt-reduction deal with Mexico.
Under the debt plan, the banks must choose between accepting a 35 percent cut in the value of existing loans or making new loans. Mr. Gurria said he thought most of the banks had made up their minds on new lending to Mexico before the reserve increases were announced.
''Banks do new money because they have a longer-term view of things, not because of their reserves,'' he said.

HEADLINE: Toppling a Tower of Compromise

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 24

 BODY:
Three years almost to the day after Congress approved the Tax Reform Act of 1986, that monumental legislation, the product of dozens of carefully drawn political and economic trade-offs, seems likely to be torn apart by lawmakers in votes this week.
Politicians and economists say several loose threads led to the unraveling of the fabric of the legislation once heralded as the tax law to end all tax laws:
* President Bush was never committed to the central philosophy of the law.
* Lawmakers, Republicans and Democrats alike, abandoned the delicate compromises of 1986 and reverted to the normal tax policy themes of their parties and constituencies.
* Investors and a variety of interest groups maintained relentless pressure for lower taxes.
* Most Americans, rightly or wrongly, believe they pay more taxes under the law than they did under the old system. 
 
Overriding Principle
The law was based on one overriding principle: tax rates were reduced steeply for taxpayers at all income levels; in return, deductions and other tax breaks were sharply curtailed.
The new system raises about the same revenue as the old one, but some individuals end up owing more taxes and others less. On balance, the new system is, in economics lingo, slightly more progressive despite the lower rates, meaning that people tend to be taxed more as their income increases.
Now, as plans stand, the House will vote on Thursday on a proposal -backed by President Bush, most Republicans and some Democrats, mostly from the South - to reduce the maximum tax rate on capital gains to 19.6 percent from 33 percent. Capital gains are the profits realized from the sale of investments like stocks, bonds or real estate.
The proposal, said Senator Bill Bradley, the New Jersey Democrat who was an architect of the 1986 law, ''goes to the heart of tax reform in terms of principle,'' an assessment few would disagree with.
Democrats, hoping to block the proposal, will offer one of their own. It would restore tax-deductible individual retirement accounts to upper- and upper-middle-income taxpayers, one tax break that was abolished in 1986. Money put into such accounts is not taxed until it is withdrawn at retirement. The Democratic plan would also raise the tax rate of the 600,000 wealthiest taxpayers.
 
Breaking a Bargain
This proposal would also break the bargain at the core of the law: lower rates and fewer deductions. ''The best-case scenario would be that everybody would come to his senses and drop it all, but that's not likely,'' said Representative Robert T. Matsui, Democrat of California, a strong advocate of the 1986 law.
The main reason this is happening now, politicians of both parties say, is that President Bush believes in using tax breaks to accomplish social and political goals. That philosophy is antithetical to the one embraced by Ronald Reagan and embedded in the tax reform law: that market forces and not the tax code should be the determining factor in how the resources of the economy are allocated.
In his campaign, Mr. Bush not only promised to cut the capital gains rate to stimulate investment but also advocated, among other tax breaks, tax credits for child care and renewed deductions for oil and gas producers. Now his Treasury Secretary, Nicholas F. Brady, is suggesting a lower tax on corporate dividends.
''Bush never bought into tax reform when he was Vice President,'' said an Administration official who also served under President Reagan. ''He thinks people ought to be given tax incentives to do what's best for the country. I think he understood perfectly well that once he began pushing for capital gains cuts, it would open the floodgates.''
In Congress, Republicans never abandoned the view, almost as old as the party itself, that lower taxes for wealthy Americans would stimulate the economy and benefit people at all income levels. Moreover, important Southern Democrats like Ed Jenkins of Georgia and Beryl Anthony Jr. of Arkansas, whose districts have industries like timber companies that particularly benefit from reduced capital gains rates, had always campaigned for lower rates.
 
Political Motivations
Once the President and his supporters got the upper hand with their capital gains proposal, Democratic leaders had to respond with a popular tax cut of their own for political reasons, they say.
By the same token, most Democrats adhered to one of their party's fundamental positions: that wealthy people should pay higher taxes. They were distressed that during the Reagan years, the maximum tax rate was reduced to 28 percent, from 70 percent. So when the opportunity arose for them to press for higher rates on the wealthy, they jumped at the chance at the expense of the bargain they made in 1986.
In the meantime, countless interest groups with paid lobbyists continued to hound Congress and Administration officials to give them tax breaks. Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, who was primarily responsible for the portion of the 1986 law that abolished most tax shelters, said he was ''under huge pressure'' to reverse himself.
''I feel as if I'm being nibbled to death by ducks,'' said the New York Democrat, who says the 1986 law should not be changed. ''They always portray what they want as a proposition to increase investment.''
 
Public Never Bought It
Finally, although statistics show that the number of people who saw their taxes drop exceeds the number who saw a rise, the public seems never to have accepted the 1986 changes. A poll this year by the Gallup Organization for the Times Mirror Company showed that only 13 percent of adults believe the new system to be ''fairer'' than the old one. Three times as many, 39 percent, said it was ''less fair.'' The rest said they found no difference or did not know.
The poll involved 2,048 adults interviewed in person nationwide between Jan. 27 and Feb. 5. The margin of sampling error was plus or minus 3 percentage points.
Support for changes in the law has been bolstered by claims of proponents that at worst are extravagant and at best unprovable.
Politicians and lobbyists who favor the capital gains reduction argue that it would greatly improve the amount of investment in the country. Those who advocate deductible I.R.A.'s for the upper middle class and the wealthy say they would greatly increase the amount people save.
But hardly any economists believe that either change would affect the nation's economy substantially. Emil M. Sunley, director of tax analysis at the accounting firm of Deloitte, Haskins & Sells, said: ''In a $5 trillion economy, you're talking about changes worth $2 billion or $3 billion a year. You've got to think the effects will be minuscule.''
 
Hunches More Than Data
Economists have debated inconclusively for years whether lower capital gains rates or widely available I.R.A.'s would improve investment and saving even at the margins. The evidence is so scant that their views are based more on hunches than on solid data.
Asked why such disagreement existed among respected economists, Jane G. Gravelle, a senior specialist in economic and tax policy at the Library of Congress, said: ''These are just very tough problems to tackle empirically. The techniques we have to study these issues can't give you the answers.''
What is beyond dispute, however, is that both proposals would primarily benefit the well-to-do, despite claims that the middle class would reap advantages.
The Congressional Joint Committee on Taxation has calculated that under the capital gains proposal scheduled to go before the House this week, the two-tenths of 1 percent of taxpayers with incomes above $200,000 would get 60 percent of the benefits, and the 1.1 percent of taxpayers with incomes over $100,000 would get 80 percent.
As for deductible I.R.A.'s, they are already available to couples with incomes below $50,000. So the change being proposed would basically affect only those with higher incomes.
 
Who Put Money in I.R.A.'s
A 1983 study compiled when I.R.A.'s were deductible for all showed that more than 60 percent of taxpayers with incomes above $50,000 contributed, while only about 30 percent of those with incomes between $30,000 and $50,000 did so. In 1985, the last year for which the Internal Revenue Service has full data, only 7.8 percent of tax returns showed gross incomes above $50,000.
Many Democrats have also misstated the effect of their proposal to increase to 33 percent from 28 percent the marginal tax rate for the wealthiest taxpayers. (Taxable income is the amount of money earned after deductions are taken, so taxpayers with a taxable income of $200,000 could be expected to have a total income of $300,000 or more. The marginal rate is the rate paid on the last dollar earned.) For example, the House Speaker, Thomas S. Foley, maintained this week that these wealthy people now pay a lower overall tax rate than those with taxable incomes of $75,000 to $150,000. ''In correcting that,'' Mr. Foley said, ''we are not creating a new tax.''
 
Confusion on Marginal Rates
Mr. Foley confused marginal rates with actual taxes paid. Because of an anomaly in the law, families with incomes below about $200,000 now pay a marginal rate of 33 percent, while those with higher incomes pay a marginal rate of 28 percent. The anomaly occurs because the benefits of the 15 percent rate and personal exemptions are gradually reduced.
But people who pay a marginal rate of 33 percent are actually paying less than 28 percent of their taxable income, either because they are paying at a 15 percent rate on some of their income or because they are entitled to personal exemptions.
Thus, those with higher incomes who pay a marginal rate of 28 percent are paying exactly 28 percent of all their taxable income in taxes. Under the Democratic proposal, they would owe up to 33 percent.

LENGTH: 1480 words

HEADLINE: A New Effort to Protect Ozone

BYLINE: By MATTHEW WALD

 BODY:
The battle to save the atmosphere's protective layer of ozone is moving to a new front: the corner auto-repair shop, where an ounce-by-ounce struggle is under way to limit the escape of the vast amounts of air-conditioning fluid handled daily.
All types of air-conditioners and refrigerators use the ozone-depleting chemicals called chlorofluorocarbons, or CFC-12. About 41 percent of the CFC-12 used for refrigeration in the United States is used by vehicle air-conditioners. But because vehicle air-conditioners are particularly prone to leaks and need frequent replacements of refrigerant, they use 75 percent of the country's replacement CFC-12. Large amounts of CFC-12 escape into the air during the replacement and also during regular operation of auto air-conditioners.
''They're guaranteed to leak,'' said Arthur T. Anderson, a specialist in CFC's at the Energy Department. 
But this month, several new machines that will recycle the refrigerant were endorsed by Underwriters Laboratories, a consumer-products testing concern, for use on car air-conditioners under warranty. The result could be widespread changes in the way vehicle air-conditioners are serviced.
 
Payback on Equipment
The change would be expensive -costing an estimated 225,000 repair shops perhaps $750 million - and would be passed on to the owners of about 100 million cars that have air-conditioning. But the manufacturers of the machines, which cost from $2,500 to $7,000, argue that, with the cost of CFC-12 rising as production is slowed, the equipment will quickly pay for itself, in addition to helping the environment.
The United States is the only large country in which auto air-conditioners are in wide use. ''Americans who want to sound like good guys sound like jerks, because so much of our use is auto air-conditioners,'' said David D. Doniger, the director of the ozone protection project at the Natural Resources Defense Council.
Mr. Doniger's group, a nonprofit organization based in Washington that has been particularly active on the ozone issue, favors a faster phase-out, and recycling, of the refrigerant in all the places it is used.
One reason car air-conditioners leak more than window air-conditioners or home refrigerators is the harsh environment under the hood. Another is the fact that the mechanical power to run car units comes from outside the system, requiring a spinning shaft attached to the car's engine that is hard to seal and that sooner or later springs leaks. In a building's window air-conditioner, the power comes from an electric motor that is located inside the sealed system.
 
Supply Squeeze Ahead
With CFC-12 production already cut to 1986 levels under an international environmental agreement signed in January, a shortage of the refrigerant is dveloping. Prices have already doubled in the last year and are widely expected to double again in coming months. Freon, the Du Pont brand, is the best-known CFC-12.
What is more, production is scheduled to be cut by half by 1998, and many environmental officials would like to accelerate the cut. President Bush has endorsed a complete ban on production by 2000, if safe replacements can be found.
One alternative for cars is a non-CFC-12 refrigerant, but the only chemical combinations discovered so far would require $1,000 or more in modifications to existing air-conditioners.
The layer of ozone in the upper atmosphere helps screen the earth from ultraviolet rays and other harmful radiation from the sun. An increase in air pollutants produced by industrialization over the last century has caused a chemical reaction with ozone that scientists say has thinned or depleted the ozone layer in some spots. An increase in the amount of harmful rays reaching earth has been linked to increases in skin cancer and cataracts in humans, and a variety of problems in other animals and plants.
Vermont, Oregon and Hawaii have already passed laws barring the sale of small cans of refrigerant, which are used by weekend mechanics and poorly equipped gas stations, and other states are expected to follow. The cans allow car air-conditioners to be refilled cheaply, but without repairing the leak, meaning that another refill will be required.
From an environmental point of view, said Mr. Doniger, ''you're putting CFC's in your air-conditioner for a day, a month or even a season, and then into the atmosphere for 150 years.'' The cans, he said, are ''grenades.''
 
Leakage During Repair
When an auto air-conditioner has stopped working because of a refrigerant leak, it may still have as much as 70 percent of its coolant. It is standard practice to vent the system before fixing. Even when a unit is fixed, mechanics often use a full load of refrigerant just to flush out a system or check for leaks.
The alternative is a machine that will recycle the refrigerant by sucking it out of the air-conditioner and purifying it for re-use. One obstacle had been that auto manufacturers insisted on virgin coolant or they would not honor the warranty. Contaminated coolant could damage a system.
Earlier this month, however, Underwriters Laboratories certified several recycling units as able to filter coolant to a standard sufficient for use in units under warranty.
Now that recovery machines are available, manufacturers expect brisk sales. ''You can't penalize a shop, saying they must have a machine, if they can't get one,'' said Jeffrey L. Sachs, president of Draf Industries Inc., a privately held manufacturing company in Bedford Hills, N.Y. The company makes a $2,995 recycling machine that was recently approved by Underwriters.
Mr. Sachs said that, as certified machines become available, ''within 18 months, almost every state's going to have a regulation'' requiring mechanics to buy one.
Some manufacturers think adoption of the new machines will not even require state laws. ''Just the economics of using the equipment will drive the market,'' said John P. Hancock, vice president of White Industries of Indianapolis, which markets its $2,900 machine under the brand name K-Whit Tools. With refrigerant at its present price, he said, his machine would save enough to pay for itself after 300 cars.
 
Commitment by General Motors
''If the price of this stuff gets up high enough, even a junkyard will want to buy one,'' said John Tyson, vice president of the SPX Corporation, Muskegon, Mich. His company sells recycling equipment under the brand names of Robinair, Kent-Moore and Bear Automotive. The General Motors Corporation announced in August that it would make the Kent-Moore model available to its 10,000 dealers and that the dealers would have to have the equipment for 1991 cars.
Others are not certain that the economics will induce thousands of small businesses to invest. But some service stations may do so and publicize the fact to capitalize on public concern about the ozone, experts predict.
''I never hear people talk about toxic chemicals, old-growth forests or things like that,'' said Mr. Doniger, whose organization concerns itself with a varity of environmental problems. ''But people know about the hole in the ozone layer. They care about this stuff.''
The idea appeals to some service mechanics.
''I'm an environmental goof,'' said Richard A. De Silva, owner of De Silva's Auto Air Conditioning in East Detroit, Mich. ''I like to get things cleaned up. And we've got to protect the kids coming up.''
Mr. De Silva, who has 11 service bays, has been using a recycler for eight months, cutting his refrigerant use drastically. It has already paid for itself, he said.
 
Two Machines at Once
Efforts to protect the ozone may give the manufacturers of recycling equipment a double boon: one now, when service stations rush to buy so they can continue servicing air- conditioners, and one when the next generation of air-conditioners comes along. Those air-conditioners will use a refrigerant that does not damage the ozone, but it is likely to be so expensive that recycling will be the norm. And the two refrigerants cannot be mixed, so two machines will be needed as long as both kinds of air-conditioners are on the road.
Industry experts think a side-effect of widespread recycling will be a reduction in the number of service locations for air-conditioning work. The business may become similar to transmission work, with car owners either going to a specialist or to a local gas station that farms out the work to the specialist.
If the price of environmental regulations drives some companies out of the air-conditioning repair business, say the manufacturers, so be it.
''A man who cannot afford a $2,500 recovery system,'' said Thorne Gould, president of the Murray Corporation, a manufacturer of recycling equipment and other air-conditioning service parts, ''is really not in the air-conditioning business anyway.''
