HEADLINE: Experts See Nuclear Energy as Cure for Global Warming

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MONTREAL, Sept. 21-

 BODY:
The cure for the greenhouse effect, the warming of the atmosphere that is believed to be caused by the burning of fossil fuels, is a huge shift to nuclear-generated power, speaker after speaker told delegates at a World Energy Conference meeting this week.
But a few speakers predicted that more efficient electrical and electronic consumer products would make a big shift to nuclear power unnecessary, while others criticized the cost of reactor-generated electricity.
For the most part, however, the consensus at the weeklong 14th congress of the London-based organization, which has members in 80 countries, was that greater reliance on nuclear power is needed.
 
Essential for Third World
Bong-Suh Lee, South Korea's Minister of Energy and Resources, said nuclear power was the solution to environmental and supply problems and that ''this is especially so for resource-poor developing countries.'' 
John Wakeham, Secretary of State for Energy in Britain, said his country was requiring that its Government-owned electricity industry, which is now being sold to the public, generate electricity without burning fossil fuels. ''The developed world may well have to turn increasingly to nuclear energy to protect our environment in the years ahead and make room for more use of fossil energy in countries with less-suitable infrastructure,'' he said.
W. Henson Moore, the United States Deputy Energy Secretary, said it was essential to resume reactor construction, which has nearly halted in both the United States and Canada and has slowed markedly elsewhere. ''Given some time and concerted effort, we can again make nuclear energy a viable choice,'' Mr. Moore said.
Lord Marshall of Goring, the chairman of the Central Electricity Generating Board in Britain, said: ''The design of nuclear reactors has now reached an advanced, sophisticated and mature level. For the public at large, the risk is to all intents and purposes zero.''
But those who opposed nuclear power generally said that rather than replace generators fired by coal, oil and natural-gas with reactors, it would be less expensive to retire the old non-nuclear generators by decreasing the demand for electricity through use of more efficient motors, lights and other electrical and electronic products.
Some opponents of nuclear power also took issue with its cost.
''Nuclear power throughout the world's market economies, and even in Communist countries, is dying of an incurable attack of market forces,'' said Amory Lovins, a consultant who specializes in efficiency technologies.
Wall Street discounts the stock and debt issues of companies with nuclear investments, he said, and investors would remain skeptical ''until promoters bring money in themselves and stop importuning the Treasury to put our money where their mouth is.'' Nuclear plants would not be built in the United States, he said, without insurance concessions and other advantages given by Congress.
Mr. Lovins and other speakers said third world electricity producers and users were markedly less efficient than those in industrialized countries.
 
Nuclear Slowdown
A World Energy Conference study estimated that reactors would provide 7 or 8 percent of energy supplies in 2020; six years ago, the group said it would be 12 percent. Currently, about 400 reactors are operating, providing about 4 percent of the world's energy supplies. About 100 more reactors are under construction.
The construction rate is far short of what would be required to use reactors to halt global warming. Alvin Weinberg, an energy expert at Oak Ridge National Laboratories, calculated that in 100 years, if the world were to institute stringent efficiency measures and cut fuel burning to the level required to stabilize the atmosphere, 5,000 reactors would be needed.
But he concluded that current designs would not be adequate. He said the chance of a meltdown at a given plant is great enough that if multiplied by 5,000, it would produce an unacceptably high risk.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Details Expected on Rescue Bonds

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
The Resolution Funding Corporation, established by the Government earlier this year to help rescue the savings and loan industry, is likely to provide preliminary details this week about how it intends to sell $30 billion worth of securities over the next two fiscal years, credit market analysts said.
Rumors about an impending sale of the corporation's bonds circulated through the credit markets late last week. With $17.5 billion worth of Treasury notes scheduled to be auctioned this week, the rumors accentuated concerns about how easily the new long-term bonds might be absorbed.
''With the economy in an apparent soft-landing mode and the Fed not likely to ease policy in the near term, a load of supply means that there is no rush to come in and buy'' as long as yields are close to current levels, said Leonard J. Santow, a managing director at Griggs & Santow Inc., a financial consulting firm. 
At the end of last week, the Treasury's 8 1/8 percent bonds that mature in 2019 were offered at a price to yield 8.19 percent, up from a yield of 8.07 percent on Sept. 15.
Mr. Santow pointed out that the Resolution Funding Corporation represents a good deal of financing, and ''it has the market on edge right now.''
Richard M. Kelly, president of Aubrey G. Lanston & Company, a primary dealer of Government securities, said: ''An actual offering may not actually be announced, but I think the Treasury would like to remove some of the uncertainty in the market. To that end, they may reveal some of the details about the process.''
A corporaation spokesman would not comment on whether an announcement is imminent. But Mr. Santow and others said that the issues are likely to be sold in an auction-like process that is virtually identical to the way the Treasury sells its notes and bonds.
As part of a compromise that cleared the way for the passage of the savings and loan rescue bill, the Bush Administration agreed that in the current fiscal year, which ends Saturday, the first $20 billion for the corporation would be included in the 1989 Federal budget. Of that total, the Treasury raised $18.8 billion through the sale of short-term bills. The remaining $1.2 billion came from the Federal Home Loan Bank.
Mr. Santow said that an initial sale of about $5 billion worth of 30-year bonds by the corporation seems highly plausible some time in October. Although yields on the securities will almost certainly be above those offered on Treasury bonds, the difference is not likely to be terribly wide, dealers said.
''I would say that they would probably come in at 20 to 30 basis points over Treasuries initially,'' one bond trader said. ''After the first issue, the spread would probably narrow.''
 
Interest in Currency Markets
Aside from the Resolution Funding situation, credit market participants this week will be keeping a close eye on developments in world currency markets after the weekend meeting of the Group of Seven finance ministers in Washington.
There were rumors late last week that a coordinated round of interest rate increases in West Germany and Japan, in conjunction with a cut in rates by the Federal Reserve Board, might be authorized as a way of pushing down the value of the dollar, which is trading around 145 yen and 1.95 marks.
But private economists said such a move was unlikely. Little, if anything, is likely to come out of the meeting, they added.
''The Group of Seven will basically decide to sit on their hands, because despite the nervousness in the markets, everybody is quite happy,'' said Charles Taylor, an international economist at Prudential-Bache Securities in Washington.
 
August Durable Goods Data
Highlighting a very sparse economic data calendar this week is a report on durable goods orders for August. A highly volatile series, the orders figures are nevertheless closely scrutinized by analysts because, among other reasons, they are known to be a favorite of Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve Board chairman.
Alan Levenson, an economist at Salomon Brothers Inc., said, ''Durable goods bookings may have risen in August, but even the anticipated bounce-back in this erratic activity is unlikely to reverse the slowdown in capital spending growth.''
Donald J. Fine, chief market analyst at the Chase Manhattan Bank, said that the ''meat and potatoes'' of the report will be how well durables orders performed when transportation is excluded.
''Automobile orders were probably up in August, but aircraft probably fell,'' he said. ''The variety of industries showing weakness is getting larger and larger. The fourth quarter is looking like it could be kind of weak.''

HEADLINE: 4 Issues in This Week's Treasury Auctions

 BODY:
The Treasury's schedule of new issues this week includes today's auction of new three-month and six-month Treasury bills, followed by one for two-year notes tomorrow and a four-year note auction on Wednesday.
By late Friday, the three-month bill was bid at 7.77 percent, and the six-month bill was at 7.75 percent. On a when-issued basis, the yield for a two-year bill was 8.27 percent, while the yield on a four-year note was 8.23 percent. 
On Wednesday, the Federal Farm Credit Administration banks are scheduled to announce the interest rates on a four-part consolidated bond issue, totaling about $2.87 billion. The issue will be made through the Federal Farm Credit Banks and a nationwide group of securities dealers and banks.
If market conditions are favorable this week, New York City might price a negotiated bond issue totaling about $750 million in general obligations through underwriters led by Goldman, Sachs & Company.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL REPORT;
Executive in China Adjusts to Party Line

BYLINE: By SHERYL WuDUNN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BEIJING, Sept. 24

 BODY:
Employees at China's biggest investment company like to recall that their chairman stood at the window during the big pro-democracy demonstrations in May, gazing at the protesters in a daring sign of support.
Rong Yiren, the formidable chairman of the state-owned China International Trust and Investment Corporation, or Citic, appeared to be moved deeply by the fervor of the democracy movement. He allowed employees to join the demonstrations and to contribute to the student movement.
Now as Chinese business is struggling to reorient itself to the Communist Party's new hard-line ideology, Mr. Rong is solidly in line, still at the helm of China's largest and most-respected financial company. 
In June, Mr. Rong came out in full support of the Government's military crackdown on the demonstrations. ''I sympathized with the students' patriotic feelings,'' Mr. Rong said, his face saddening visibly at the mention of the crackdown. ''But they were manipulated by some people, and I feel sad about that. I resent and oppose the practice of manipulating students in this way,'' he added, folding the Party line into his comments.
 
Sense for Posturing
For business executives in China, this kind of political posturing is a way of life, and Mr. Rong has a keen sense for when and how to do it.
The son of a wealthy industrialist in Shanghai, Mr. Rong decided to stay 40 years ago to help build a new China, while other family members fled abroad. He was forced to do menial jobs during the Cultural Revolution that began in 1966 and then returned to favor with the rise to power of Deng Xiaoping, now China's top leader, who gave Mr. Rong the mandate to set up Citic 10 years ago.
Under Mr. Rong's leadership, Citic has become a fast-growing conglomerate of efficiently run businesses from Australia to Canada and the United States. Citic owns a steel plant in Delaware, a timber operation in Washington State and real estate in Arizona.
Last year Citic earned the equivalent of $55 million, of which 61 percent was accounted for by its Hong Kong subsidiary. The company will not disclose the earnings of its United States operations.
 
At the Vanguard
This is a crucial time for Citic, which has positioned itself at the vanguard of the nation's economic reorganization. On Oct. 2, the company will celebrate its 10th anniversary, with Chinese dignitaries and foreign guests, including Alexander Haig, the former Secretary of State.
Some foreign bankers worry that Citic could be hurt if China's leaders retreat from their commitment to the country's policy of economic openness. In addition, the company has already said this year's profits and investment would probably fall below last year's levels.
Mr. Rong himself initially seemed vulnerable because he had been an obvious supporter of the democracy movement and was associated with the ousted Communist Party leader, Zhao Ziyang.
Instead, Mr. Rong's stature has risen. Now there is talk that he might be appointed vice president or deputy prime minister, as a way for China's leadership to bolster confidence in the economic reorganization.
 
Barometer of Change
In many ways, Citic is a barometer of China's economic change, and it is sometimes regarded as the most capitalist of the country's state-owned enterprises. In addition to running a bank that competes directly with China's Government-owned banks, Citic issues multimillion-dollar bonds, leases airplanes and participates in loan syndications - although such activity has dropped virtually to a standstill since the military crackdown.
Like China, Citic expanded without a clear-cut strategy, growing wherever there was opportunity, and so its businesses range from technology, investments, textiles, cars, energy, steel, banking, timber and weapons. Earnings expanded 50 percent in 1987 and 27 percent last year. Now, as the Government redoubles its efforts to slow the economy, so too, will Citic temper its pace.
''Perhaps they have grown too fast, and they need a period of consolidation,'' said Henry T. H. Fei, vice president for China business at the Bankers Trust Company in Hong Kong.
Now, the challenge for Mr. Rong is to bend with the shift in Communist Party policies, while maintaining credibility with foreign executives and buoying morale among his young staff members, many of whom resent the Government's new emphasis on Communist values.
 
The New Orthodoxy
Compliance with the new orthodoxy was evident in August, as Citic paid $5.1 million in fines after an audit discovered unauthorized foreign exchange trades and tax evasion. Though acknowledging that the fine, which was the first for the company, was relatively lenient for such an offense, Mr. Rong said he welcomed it as a means to improve management in China.
The Government has also stipulated that sons and daughters of top leaders may no longer engage in business. That could cause difficulties for Citic, which is known for hiring such people.
Last week Mr. Rong said that two such employees had left the company because of the new regulation, but in an interview last month, he said that some sons and daughters of top officials would be transferred to noncommercial departments, like those engaged in manufacturing.
Mr. Rong said Wang Jun, vice president at Citic and a son of Chinese Vice President Wang Zhen, has no plans for leaving, because he technically does not belong to the category under the regulation. Some Chinese said the younger Mr. Wang was preparing for a post in the military.
 
Cagey About Rumors
If Mr. Rong is selected for a top Government position, he presumably will leave Citic. Mr. Rong is cagey about responding to rumors about such a job.
Some staff workers say he could not be appointed because he is not a Communist Party member. Yet, Mr. Rong has the political stature and breadth that outrank even most senior officials. He is one of 19 vice chairmen in China's legislature; a leader for industry in one of the nine non-Communist parties; a board member for a provincial university, and an honorary member of a softball association.
Citic staff members prefer that Mr. Rong stay and are grateful that he allowed them to march and donate money and food to the hunger-striking students in May.
''My way of handling it was not to organize anything, but not to stop it either,'' Mr. Rong said in an interview in Beijing. ''Each person acted according to his own wishes.''
If Mr. Rong is successful in sustaining good will within the company and the Communist Party leadership, another test will come in his relationship with foreign business executives.
''The challenge for Citic in the year to come is to maintain and even improve their image,'' said a Beijing-based foreign banker who asked not to be named. ''We will watch how they live up to their contracts and loan agreements.''

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL REPORT;
Financial Officials Fear Global Trade Imbalance

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 24

 BODY:
In stretch limos and blue pinstripes, the global finance community gathers in Washington this week to try to create a favorable global economic environment in the 1990's.
In meetings, and in small and large dinners and even larger cocktail parties, finance officials are trying to agree on a way to increase communal resources and prepare for the economic crises of the future.
The finance officials, joined by thousands of commercial bankers, are here for the annual meeting of the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank, the Washington-based institutions that help steer the global economy while offering money and technical assistance to poorer nations that pledge freer-market policies.
Officials began assembling this weekend. Those from the seven most powerful industrial countries issued a communique about the dollar and Poland on Saturday, signaling problems and opportunities ahead. 
 
Trade Imbalances
One of the biggest threats - that trade imbalances will worsen next year partly because of an uncontrollable rise in the dollar's value - was also addressed in a report on the World Economic Outlook published today by the I.M.F. staff.
The dollar has risen up to 20 percent this year against the Japanese yen and the West German mark -undermining efforts to stem the American trade deficit because it means higher prices for American exports while making foreign goods cheaper in the United States.
Warning that the rise of the dollar ''could adversely affect prospects for the world economy,'' the seven industrial countries - the United States, Japan, West Germany, Britain, France, Italy and Canada - pledged in somewhat stronger terms than at their last meeting here in April to take coordinated action, if necessary, to drive the currency lower.
Bankers here said that to bring the dollar down, the West Germans and the Japanese would have to raise interest rates or Americans would have to lower them. The likelihood of the Federal Reserve acting in the near future is not considered great.
 
Meaning to the Markets
The Group of Seven ''did not say very much and so it won't mean a lot to the market,'' said S. Waite Rawls 3d, vice chairman of the Continental Bank of Chicago, adding that the market would look for intervention on Monday.
According to the I.M.F. report, the ''prospect of sizable and persistent current account imbalances among the three largest industrial countries continues to cloud the medium-term outlook.'' The three are the United States, Japan and West Germany.
The I.M.F. projected that the American trade and services deficit, otherwise known as the current account, would widen by $13.6 billion next year, to $138.7 billion, based on present policies and exchange rates.
In contrast, Japan's surplus would rise $17.9 billion, to $89.7 billion, and the West German surplus by $3.4 billion, to $56.8 billion.
 
Effect of the Trade Gap
Any worsening of the American trade deficit would significantly set back the economic policies of the Bush Administration and could stir protectionist reactions in Congress.
If the financial markets are not reassured that policy changes will redress the situation, said Jacob Frenkel, the I.M.F.'s director of research, steeper deficits could be ''extremely destabilizing.''
The warning about a possible trade reversal comes as many American officials and analysts fear a similar reversal in efforts to cut the budget deficit. It is now generally held to be unlikely that Congress will meet an Oct. 16 deadline for cutting the projected 1990 deficit.
Along with a high dollar, the budget deficit - which encourages the consumption of imports over savings and investment - is considered a large contributor to the trade deficit.
 
More Than Intervention
The Finance Minister of Canada, Michael H. Wilson, said in an interview today that the Group of Seven ministers discussed policies on Saturday that would address the trade imbalance and that ''those policies are broader than market intervention.''
The I.M.F.'s World Economic Outlook also said that while the annual rate of economic growth is expected to slow in the United States next year, it saw no recession. The I.M.F. sees growth down to 2.1 percent next year, from a projected 2.9 percent this year and 4.4 percent in 1988.
The Group of Seven ministers also focused on the ''historic events'' in Eastern Europe and the Soviet Union and expressed their ''strong support for plans to create more open and market-based economies'' in Poland and Hungary.
 
Becoming Electric
At a news briefing Saturday night, Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady said, ''The whole meeting became electric when the talk turned to what is going on in the Soviet Union and Eastern Europe.''
The seven urged Poland to reach an early agreement with the I.M.F. on a lending program to make the Polish economy more efficient, including rescheduling debts.
About two-thirds of Poland's $40 billion foreign debt is held by governments and the rest by banks.
Britain's Chancellor of the Exchequer Nigel Lawson noted that the problems in Poland today are similar to those in West Germany in the immediate postwar period, where market-oriented reforms engineered by the Finance Minister, Ludwig Erhardt, who later became Chancellor, helped create what has come to be called the German miracle.
The ministers also took up a request from Japan for a greater voice in the I.M.F., but Japan's Finance Minister, Ryutaro Hashimoto, said no decisions were made.
Japan now has the fifth-largest vote in the I.M.F., after the United States, Britain, West Germany and France. It wants to jump to second place under proposals that are being debated this week to increase the I.M.F.'s $120 billion of resources.
The I.M.F. wants more capital so that the institution can keep pace with the global economy and prepare for emergency lending.
Yet not all members are in agreement. The Bush Administration said it is not yet convinced of the need for new capital. Britain and Saudi Arabia are also unenthusiastic. But 70 percent of the weighted vote of the membership backs at least a two-thirds increase in capital.
Strains became apparent also between Mr. Brady and commercial bankers. Reports were confirmed today of a meeting Friday between Mr. Brady and heads of top money center banks to try to ease doubts about his plan for cutting third world debts.
The plan, which offers inducements to banks to sell their debt at discounted prices, has stirred some misgivings among bankers, who say it swells expectations over the size of debt reductions. One participant reported some sharp exchanges but said the meeting had ''helped clear the air.''

PUB-DATE: September 26, 1989

SECTION: Section A; Page 1, Column 6; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 1153 words

HEADLINE: END THE 'SCOURGE' OF CHEMICAL ARMS, BUSH SAYS AT U.N.

BYLINE: By MAUREEN DOWD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, Sept. 25

 BODY:
President Bush, saying it was time ''to rid the earth of this scourge,'' told the United Nations today that the United States would destroy 80 percent of its chemical weapons if the Soviet Union agreed to cut its stockpile to the American level.
Mr. Bush also promised to eliminate all American chemical arms within a decade if a treaty banning such weapons all over the world was signed.
''The world has lived too long in the shadow of chemical warfare,'' the President told the delegates of the General Assembly, who responded with polite applause. ''Let us act together, beginning today, to rid the earth of this scourge.'' [Excerpts from speech, page A16.] A 'Homecoming' for the Bushes Mr. Bush, who was a delegate to the United Nations under President Richard M. Nixon, said his first speech to the General Assembly as President was ''a homecoming'' for him and for Barbara Bush. The First Lady sat in the spectators' gallery, wearing a bright red outfit and leading the applause. 
The President said he was trying to speed progress at the 40-nation negotiations in Geneva on a global ban on chemical weapons because ''time is running out.''
Mr. Bush's advisers had hoped that his speech, with its chemical weapons initiative and its paean to the flowering of democracy around the world, would match and perhaps outshine the appearance of President Mikhail S. Gorbachev of the Soviet Union at the United Nations last year.
 
Doesn't Outshine Gorbachev
But while individual delegates praised Mr. Bush's speech as impressive, the President did not receive the enthusiastic response or long round of applause that the Soviet leader garnered last December when he announced his deep, one-sided cuts in conventional forces.
Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d and Foreign Minister Eduard A. Shevardnadze of the Soviet Union traded friendly waves across the room before they listened to Mr. Bush, who hailed ''freedom's march'' in Poland, the Soviet Union and Latin America. He also called on the world leaders to join forces to fight terrorism.
''Terrorism of any kind is repugnant to all values that a civilized world holds in common,'' the President said. ''And make no mistake. Terrorism is a means that no end - no matter how just that end - can sanctify.''
 
Pleased With Wyoming Talks
Mr. Bush said he was pleased with the progress made at the American-Soviet talks last weekend in Wyoming and the agreement for a superpower summit meeting in spring or early summer of 1990.
''Each of these achievements is important in its own right, but they are more important still as as signs of a new attitude that prevails between the U.S. and the U.S.S.R.,'' he said. ''Seri-ous differences remain. But the willingness to deal constructively and candidly with those differences is news that we - and indeed the world - must welcome.''
Trying to build momentum on a subject that his advisers say is ''a fixation'' with him, Mr. Bush challenged the Soviets to set a specific time frame for mutual deep cuts in chemical weapons without waiting for a convention to be signed.
''Many have said recently that the U.S. has been dragging its feet in the chemical weapons negotiations, but this ought to put that to rest once and for all,'' Secretary Baker said after the speech.
Soviet officials, who got the chemical arms proposal during Soviet-American talks in Wyoming over the weekend, said they had taken it under advisement.
The Soviet Union said in January that it would destroy its 50,000 tons of chemical weapons but did not set deadlines or amounts.
 
Praise From Shevardnadze
After Mr. Bush's speech, Mr. Shevardnadze looked dour and offered only tepid applause. But he praised the speech as ''a constructive statement in the spirit of the times.''
On the chemical weapons initiatives, the Soviet Foreign Minister said: ''They will have to be studied later. We have a positive view of these proposals.''
Specifically, Mr. Bush outlined these steps:
* The United States will destroy more than 98 percent of its current chemical weapons stockpile within eight years after the completion of a multilateral chemical weapons convention that includes the Soviet Union.
* The remaining 2 percent would then be destroyed in the next two years after all nations capable of producing chemical arms signed the convention. The President said 20 nations had chemical arms capabilities.
* While working to complete a global chemical weapons convention, the United States will propose to the Soviet Union that the two countries agree to the destruction of a major portion of their respective stockpiles to equal levels, about 20 percent of the current American level. Since the Soviets have greater stockpiles, they would have to destroy many more weapons.
* The United States will accelerate and expand its efforts to improve verification procedures.
There were those who felt that Mr. Bush's initiative was more symbolic than substantive, since Congress has already passed legislation requiring the Administration to destroy old chemical weapons by 1997 as more advanced chemical weapons are deployed.
Responding to such sugestions, the President's national security adviser, Brent Scowcroft, said, ''Whether it's making a virtue of necessity, it is certainly part of a major effort not simply to wait until verification is up to the standards we have, but to proceed on parallel with the destruction as we are improving our ability to verify.''
 
Example to Third World
At a news conference after the speech, Mr. Baker said that if the United States and Soviet Union joined to eliminate chemical weapons, they could serve as an example to third world countries like Libya, which is reported to be developing chemical weapons, and Iraq, which used poison gas during its recent war against Iran.
He said the United States would consider economic sanctions against nations that refused to go along with a treaty forbidding chemical weapons, because such bans ''don't work unless you get everybody joining in.''
There is already a treaty banning bacteriological weapons.
Mr. Bush held meetings this afternoon at the Waldorf-Astoria Hotel with President Jose Sarney of Brazil, Finance Minister Shimon Peres of Israel and Presidents Vinicio Cerezo of Guatemala, Jaime Paz Zamora of Bolivia and Carlos Andres Perez of Venezuela.
In the evening, the President and Mrs. Bush were hosts at a diplomatic reception and about 400 people, including heads of government, foreign ministers, some heads of state and some delegates to the General Assembly. The reception, in the Great Hall of the Metropolitan Museum of Art, was followed by a smaller dinner in the courtyard of the museum's American Wing.
The menu for the dinner, for 120 guests, included poached salmon, medallions of veal, lamb and chicken with fall vegetables, warm apple Charlotte served with ice cream, and American wines.

PUB-DATE: September 26, 1989

LENGTH: 2210 words

HEADLINE: Companies Step In Where the Schools Fail

SERIES: SKILLS VS. JOBS: THE CLASSROOM MISMATCH - SECOND OF THREE ARTICLES.

BYLINE: By JOSEPH BERGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SCHAUMBURG, Ill.

 BODY:
At the huge Motorola Inc. plant here, assembly line workers like Carritha Wells are being paid to learn not about electronic circuit boards or cellular telephones but about how much a pound of grapes would cost if 2Z pounds cost $3.25.
The sleek computerized equipment that Motorola is installing requires Mrs. Wells, to do far less manual labor in her job, but a lot more thinking. She must read graphs and change percentages to decimals. Yet, she has not performed such calculations since her high school days in Mound Bayou, Miss., several decades ago.
''If you don't need it, you forget it,'' she said. 
So, in a small factory conference room she and a half dozen colleagues are taking a course in grade school arithmetic.
 
Companies Teach Basic
Skills Motorola, Ford, Xerox, Polaroid and Eastman Kodak are in a small but growing number of American companies spending millions of dollars a year on basic reading and arithmetical instruction because, they say, America's schools are no longer meeting the companies' needs. Their jobs are becoming more sophisticated each year and many of their workers cannot read and count well enough to fill them.
A growing number of experts believe that this trend will worsen in coming years and that the groups most affected by it are the poor and minorities.
So far, the companies are making do by retraining current workers and casting a much wider net to find competent new workers. But soon, some executives predict, American businesses may not be able to find enough qualified workers and will have to hire tens of thousands of unqualified ones and teach them to read and write. #57,000 Test for 2,100 Jobs As a case in point, Frederic V. Salerno, president of New York Telephone Company, recalled that in 1987 the subsidiary of the Nynex Corporation had to test 57,000 people before it could find 2,100 who were qualified to become operators and repair technicians.
Louis V. Gerstner Jr., chairman of RJR Nabisco Inc., warns that, if current trends continue, American businesses will have to spend $25 billion a year providing elementary instruction to 1 million workers before they can even train them to handle modern equipment.
Like a growing number of executives, Mr. Gerstner has been speaking to audiences urging a shakeup of the country's educational system.
Susan Hooker, Motorola's director of retraining, explained the changes this way: ''For many years our factory jobs did not require people to use a lot of brain power. We were really hiring people for their ability to manipulate things and put things together. Now, most of those manipulations can be performed by machinery. It isn't that they've lost skills, but the skills traditionally required have changed.''
 
Direct Involvement in Schools
Major companies are also immersing themselves in local schools in ways that would have seemed astonishing only a decade ago. Some are writing curriculums for special ''academies'' in certain high schools. These schools within schools are intended to attract students to the sponsoring companies' fields. Other companies are guaranteeing jobs to graduates, deploying executives to act as mentors, lending expensive equipment and providing college scholarships.
Companies are taking this role largely for self-preservation. Forrest P. Chisman, director of the Southport Institute for Policy Analysis in Washington, said that 20 million to 30 million adults cannot read, write or calculate well enough to function effectively at work, a situation he described as ''a cancer eating away at the American economy.''
Some companies, worried about anemic productivity at home and fiercer competition abroad, are shifting locales just to find larger pools of qualified entry level workers. Some are moving divisions overseas.
The problem, experts say, will not wither away. Motorola officials expect to keep increasing the educational levels of the company's workers just to keep up with changing technology, a trend Ms. Hooker compared to walking up a down escalator just to stay in place. Almost one in every four jobs now requires a college degree. Banks have replaced tellers with cash dispensing machines, but the new generation of tellers must be able to process loan applications.
Moreover, companies eager to energize lackadaisical workers are giving them greater decision-making authority and responsibility. The new duties require workers to reason, to follow written instructions and to articulate their thoughts to teams of co-workers.
''I believe companies are going to have to shoulder a lot of the burden of correcting adult illiteracy because that's where the adults are during the day, not at home,'' Mr. Gerstner of RJR Nabisco said in a recent interview.
 
Educators Are Responding
For the most part, the education community is not shrugging off the criticism. In early September, the Teachers College Institute on Education and the Economy brought business leaders and educators together for three days on Cape Cod.
P. Michael Timpane, Teachers College president, opened the conference by warning that the shortage of qualified workers ''strikes at the sinews of our nation'' and ''affects our hopes for social integration.'' Although he did not say so directly, a study by the college shows that black and Hispanic people are heavily represented in precisely those occupations that are lagging in growth.
Some school systems are taking dramatic steps to demonstrate their responsibility to business. The Plymouth-Carver district in Massachusetts has decided to ''warranty'' the skills of its high school graduates for three years. Bernard Sidman, the superintendent, said that if an employer reports that a graduate cannot make change at a cash register, for example, the district will enroll the student in ''evening classes for retooling'' free of charge.
Business and education are increasingly linking their worlds. Seven years ago, the American Express Company confronted hiring pools of high-school graduates whose abilities were mediocre and who often did not understand the need for courtesy and punctuality in the workplace. It decided to develop a special program to prepare students for careers in finance and helped set up academies at seven New York City high schools.
At the Academy of Finance within John Dewey High School near Coney Island, 120 students take classes in Wall Street operations, accounting, computer business applications and banking, all on top of their regular high school courses. The content was developed with with the help of Wall Street executives. Every class plays the stock market with an imaginary portfolio of $100,000.
With 150 companies now participating, the program guarantees all youngsters $6-an-hour summer jobs, and many turn those into permanent jobs after graduation. Ninety percent of the youngsters, though, pass these up and elect to go on to college because they realize that high-earning positions require advanced education.
 
An Investment Whiz Emerges
''They want to be the big guys, the guys working in arbitrage, the managers of departments in accounting firms,'' said Ada Dolch, Dewey's academy coordinator.
One student, she said, has proved to be such a canny broker that Shearson Lehman Brothers International Inc. sends a limousine to pick him up after his classes at St. John's University and drives him to work at the offices of the American Express investment service.
The American Express program is now operating in 42 schools in 18 cities and costs the company $1 million and considerable executive time a year, according to Ida Schmertz, senior vice president for corporate affairs. More than 65 percent of the students are black, Hispanic or Asian.
A similar program has been operating in 10 Philadelphia high schools, preparing 1,200 students for careers in electrical work, business, health and the automotive industry. In 1984, the business academy had a job placement rate of 86 percent, a remarkable figure for students who are often afraid to venture into corporate personnel offices.
The quality of such academies has not been consistent and, in some cases, sponsors have urged the recruitment of brighter, more ambitious youngsters rather than the dropout-prone students to which the program is directed.
Perhaps the most far-reaching effort to create a partnership between business and schools is Boston's. In 1982, 600 businesses, alarmed by the scarcity of entry-level workers, signed the ''Boston compact.'' If the city's schools would agree to improve attendance, dropout and reading rates, businesses would reserve a quota of jobs for high school graduates.
 
Results Are Mixed
A 1987 study by Public/Private Ventures, a consulting concern, found that absences dropped to about 15 percent from roughly 25 percent daily and reading scores improved for students in grades 9 and 10, though not those in grade 11. Dropout rates, however, deteriorated. Businesses had generally delivered on the jobs.
Although measurable results were mixed, the study noted that the agreement increased political support for schools, improved teacher morale and physically upgraded many schools. But the Boston experience may also be demonstrating how intractable the welter of family and school problems can be, some participants say.
''We were naive to think that in five years we could have a substantial impact,'' said Daniel Morley, chairman of the Morley Group in Wayland, Mass., one of compact's founders. ''School officials have learned that business just can't solve everything with a check.''
The idea that companies should enter the remedial education business is a relatively new approach. Mr. Chisman said that only a small fraction of the $30 billion spent on training each year is devoted to basic skills, and that it is mostly spent by companies with strong unions or severe labor shortages.
Sue E. Berryman, director of the Teachers College's Institute, complains that employers principally ''train the trainable and the trained.''
Motorola, the multibillion producer of two-way radios, pagers, and electronic parts, has been a pioneer in basic-skills training. The company's assembly-line workers once simply inserted colored resistors and capacitors into reddish circuit boards by copying a diagram. The job did not even require a knowledge of English. Indeed, many workers are immigrants from Poland, Colombia, India and other countries.
 
Fewer Workers, but Literate Ones
But Motorola has been introducing chip placement machines like the Fuji CP III that assemble 11,000 components an hour with superhuman reliability. Only a third as many employees are needed to operate the robots. But those employees must be able to monitor the robots' precision by reading computer graphs that track the location, rotation and thickness of inserted parts.
This statistical process control method of monitoring has spread through much of American manufacturing, forcing companies to hire workers who are more literate than in the past.
Motorola could have dismissed illiterate employees, but it has prided itself on loyalty to workers, and also sensed that it would not be easy to find replacements. So three years ago, it commissioned Harper College, a local community college, to provide instructors to teach basic skills. About 10 percent of the 1,800 production employees at the Schaumburg plant are currently taking the classes. In 1988, Motorola spent $5 million on such training throughout the corporation, which has 60,000 employees in the United States.
A recent visit to Motorola's Schaumburg center found a class largely of black and immigrant workers practicing division of fractions. Carritha Wells and her classmates concluded that the grapes cost $1.22 a pound.
Another class, taught by Sue Thompson, discussed the meanings of comparatives like fewer, taller, smaller, fatter and thinner by studying the different sized coffee makers pictured in a catalogue.
Ford Motor Company has established ''learning centers'' at 11 of its major national sites. Dramatic changes in the automobile business require better educated workers. Ernest J. Savoie, Ford's director of employee development, points out that 15 years ago companies produced 100 models; now they produce 300 different ones. More frequent shifts in machinery demand greater employee resourcefulness.
 
Benefits Beyond the Job
Tennessee Eastman Company in Kingsport, an Eastman Kodak division that makes plastics for soda bottles and fibers for cigarette filters, has long trained high-skilled workers, but realized two years ago that it must also train many of its machine operators in reading and calculating.
''Things that used to be monitored by gauges and dials are now monitored by computer screen, and it takes a whole different set of skills to monitor by computer screen,'' said Jan Stallard, an instructor.
Such training programs have produced benefits for workers that carry beyond the job. Kathy Mitura, a Motorola worker who immigrated from Greece 13 years ago, said Motorola's arithmetic course has made her a better parent to her 12-year-old daughter and 10-year-old son.
''When I sit down with my kids to do homework, I know how to help them,'' she said.

621 of 722 DOCUMENTS

Copyright 1989 The New York Times Company 
The New York Times

PUB-DATE: September 26, 1989

SECTION: Section A; Page 1, Column 1; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 1126 words

HEADLINE: Surge in Resources For I.M.F. Forecast; East-Bloc Role Seen

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 25

 BODY:
The member nations of the International Monetary Fund are likely to agree by the end of the year to increase the fund's resources by as much as 67 percent, or $80 billion, officials said today.
They added that the money would be used to cushion economic shocks in the 1990's and to take an expanded role in Eastern Europe and possibly the Soviet Union.
The $80 billion would be raised by contributions from members. The United States is the largest lender to the I.M.F., providing about 18 percent of the organization's funds. 
If contributions are increased by 67 percent, the United States would owe an additional $17 billion, one-quarter of which would have to be paid immediately on ratification of the agreement and the remainder covered by a letter of credit from the Treasury.
Worried about selling American participation to Congress, the Bush Administration is still voicing reservations about the plan to increase the fund's reserves. But it is doing so more softly than previously, and its posture suggests that a consensus on the increase will probably be reached this year.
The projection of the likely amount of new capital was made by H. Onno Ruding, the Finance Minister of the Netherlands, who serves as the chairman of the fund's policy-making Interim Committee, which represents 152 member countries.
In a communique today, the committee spoke of ''widespread support'' of the need for a substantial increase.
Mr. Ruding said at a news conference that although the I.M.F. staff has called for a doubling of the current $120 billion in reserves, the likely increase would be 50 to 67 percent. ''It's well possible that it will be 67 percent,'' he added.
The committee met here as part of the annual meeting of the fund and the World Bank, institutions born in the ashes of World War II that helped rebuild Europe and Japan and now lend mainly to third world countries that pledge free-market economic changes.
The chief proponents of the increased reserves are France, West Germany, the Nethlerands and the fund's staff. With a majority of its members supporting an increase, the fund's executive board is likely to discuss the proposals in coming months.
 
Changes Generate Excitement
The economic changes in Poland and Eastern Europe have generated the most excitement among the thousands of participants in the meetings here, along with changes in the Soviet Union unleashed by Mikhail S. Gorbachev's economic and political restructuring policies.
Thousands of bankers and top government officials are discussing these global problems in a festival atmosphere of dinner parties and receptions at hotels and restaurants, Georgetown homes and historic buildings.
Poland's new Finance Mnister, Leszek Balcerowicz, a 42-year-old academician, has been at the informal gatherings and formal meetings, describing his country's embryonic plans for spurring private enterprise and taming inflation. Poland's inflation rate is currently 30 to 40 percent a month. France's Finance Minister, Pierre Beregovoy, told reporters after seeing Mr. Balcerowicz that the Polish ideas sounded ''very courageous.''
 
Meeting With Director
Mr. Balcerowicz is scheduled to meet Tuesday with the fund's managing director, Michel Camdessus, in what could set the stage for a full-scale I.M.F. mission to Poland early next month. While Poland could borrow substantial sums from the fund, up to $2.5 billion over three years, a seal of approval from the organization might help Poland tap additional billions of dollars from Western banks and creditor nations.
The Group of Seven industrial nations on Saturday called on Poland to reach early agreement with the fund on a ''strong and sustainable program'' and said they ''stand ready to support such a program through bilateral and multilateral actions.''
One sign of new Western assistance came with an announcement today of a program of investments, including joint ventures, and technical assistance for Poland announced by the World Bank's International Finance Corporation, an affiliate that encourages private enterprise in the third world.
 
Dramatic Change Possible
Although it is widely agreed that the I.M.F. now has adequate funds -about $40 billion of uncommitted hard currencies - it could take up to two years before an agreement to increase its resources is ratified. By then, the fund's needs could dramatically change.
''As a monetary institution, the I.M.F. must be permanently able to have its resources liquid enough to answer all requests,'' Mr. Camdessus said at today's news conference.
Despite the American reservations about an increase in quotas, Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady, in his remarks to the meeting, went out of his way to compliment the fund on progress it has made in addressing the thorny question of overdue loans. Arrears are among the problems the United States has identified as needing attention before a quota increase is approved.
At the end of 1987 the fund had 26 countries with overdue financial obligations. Now there are 13. However, the total overdue loans, even with a smaller number of countries, is a record. Debts overdue longer than six months totaled $3 billion last April 30, according to the latest available data, compared with $2.2 billion on April 30, 1988.
 
Lack of Clarity Cited
The United States has said in the past that a lack of clarity about the fund's role, especially because of some functions that overlap with the World Bank, raises questions about the need for a quota increase.
Mr. Brady noted, however, that there was ''broad agreement that the preservation of the fund's monetary character and the revolving nature of its resources are an essential basis for its future role.''
While it is uncertain how Congress will react to the plan for increasing contributions to the fund, the last time the issue came before Congress, in 1983, an increase was narrowly apporved only after aggressive lobbying by President Reagan.
Representative Charles E. Schumer, Democrat of Brooklyn, a senior member of the House Banking Committee, said a vote to support the fund was difficult for lawmakers. The reason, he added, was that it ''reflects at least perceived support for two things neither of which are wildly popular in Congress - foreign aid and the banks.''
In his remarks, Mr. Brady said, ''We must remember that it is our taxpayers who are being asked to provide these resources.''
Actually, however, a subscription to the I.M.F., unlike American contributions to other international bodies like the United Nations or the World Bank, is not a net budgetary outlay. The American cash payment to the fund is immediately offset by the enlarged American reserves, which are held by the I.M.F.

COUNTRY: EUROPE (94%); UNITED STATES (94%); POLAND (92%); EASTERN EUROPE (88%); FRANCE (79%); NETHERLANDS (79%); JAPAN (79%); GERMANY (72%); CENTRAL EUROPE (57%); 

COMPANY: INTERNATIONAL MONETARY FUND (94%); INTERNATIONAL MONETARY FUND (92%);

HEADLINE: Vietnamese Quit Cambodia Today But Face Heavy Burdens at Home

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HO CHI MINH CITY, Vietnam, Sept. 25

 BODY:
For a country about to enjoy what could be its first prolonged period of peace in many decades, Vietnam today displays little sense of celebration.
The long Cambodian military adventure, nearly 11 years requiring much sacrifice from the common soldier and citizen, officially ends Tuesday when the last Vietnamese infantryman is scheduled to cross the border at Moc Bai in Tay Ninh Province.
But the experience in Cambodia is ending in ambivalence, with no ringing victory. Vietnam's client regime in Cambodia is established, but not quite secure. While at least 25,300 Vietnamese soldiers have died and 55,000 have been seriously wounded, the Khmer Rouge still threaten the Cambodian Government that Hanoi installed after it invaded on Dec. 25, 1978, quickly overthrowing the Khmer Rouge regime of Pol Pot. 
 
No Diplomatic Payoff
Diplomatically, too, there is no evident payoff for Vietnam for having finally heeded Western and Asian calls for a withdrawal. The United States remains standoffish, insisting on a comprehensive diplomatic settlement in Cambodia - which now seems far away - before normalizing relations with Vietnam or, it seems, lifting an embargo on aid and trade with Hanoi.
Rather than national joy, there is increased weariness as the Vietnamese, more than a million of whom died in the Vietnam War, concentrate on the many problems this tattered and still isolated nation continues to face. Among them is overpopulation, unemployment, increasing crime, intermittent hunger, party corruption, an aged leadership and even a Communist world that is no longer familiar, with cracks in Poland, Hungary and the Soviet Union seeming to sunder the bloc.
About Cambodia, a noted Vietnamese writer said: ''There is individual relief, that our fighting is over, and collective resignation. We have borne a great sacrifice, and we could bear no more.'' The Cambodians, he said, ''will just have to do the best they can.''
For most individuals here, even the leadership question is scarcely worth discussing. ''It's them and us,'' said a young man in a cafe. ''It seems that they don't often think of us, and we don't think of them.''
 
'A Chance to Rebuild'
A Vietnamese official said: ''Withdrawing from Cambodia doesn't mean we have a sigh of relaxation. But it is very important, for now we have a chance to rebuild our own society in an easier atmosphere.''
The central issue for Vietnam today is the economy. Despite improvements since recent moves away from central management and state subsidies, the economy still lags considerably behind the aspirations of the people, whose numbers are growing rapidly. Many of these economic concerns tend to be expressed as a determination to try to give decent jobs, or at least job training, to returning soldiers.
This city has already experienced difficulties caused by some demobilized soldiers no longer content with the farm who come here but find little work, and with city-born veterans who join criminal gangs.
''Spiritually,'' a senior editor said, ''all they get is a medal.''
The Government's policy is to give returning soldiers some priority in schools, hospitals and job placement, and in chances to work abroad, a program intended to ease the job pressure here and to help pay Vietnam's external debts. Officially, veterans also have the right to return to their former employers.
 
In Conflict With Reality
''The problem is the reality and capacity of the society,'' the editor said. ''That very factory doesn't have material with which to work, so what can they do? The policy and the reality are very different.''
Another senior journalist said: ''They think they have contributed to the country, and they want society to be grateful. But in this economic situation, there are many difficulties. We cannot afford what each one of them wants. That's our most difficult problem.''
There has also been some friction between those who fought in brutal surroundings, with rampant malaria and often inadequate food and shelter, and those who remained at home.
''There are people in the same generation who did not have to participate in the Cambodian war, who just stayed here and did smuggling and had a happy life,'' another writer said, ''I worry about the consequences.''
 
Crime Is on the Rise
These feelings mix with another source of national shame - the flight of Vietnamese in flimsy boats to whatever country might possibly take them. People joke bitterly, ''If the lamppost could walk, it would go, too.''
At the same time, the press is full of articles about increasing crime, of housebreaking and acts of violence.
Responsible people speak with some despair of the growing ''chaos'' of life.
Despite the domestic problems, the Vietnamese feel much pride in having rid Cambodia of Pol Pot, under whose rule more than a million Cambodians died.
Nguyen Duy, a much-admired poet who entered Cambodia with the Vietnamese Army as a correspondent, remembers the color black - ''the black of the pajamas, the black of the skin of those who were dying or very ill.''
''The long queues of the orphans have haunted me for 10 years,'' he said. ''The people had become animals. They ate anything they could find along the road - grass, leaves, frogs, insects. They were like the black ants of Africa that move through a field and leave it devastated. and it was Vietnamese soldiers who gave them their first grain of rice.''

HEADLINE: Migrant Job Program Baffles Mexico

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, Sept. 25

 BODY:
A new United States immigration program, aimed at preventing potential labor shortages in American agriculture over the next four years, is causing confusion and controversy throughout Mexico, which is expected to supply most of the workers for the plan.
Begun on Sept. 1, the Replenishment Agricultural Worker program, known as R.A.W., is intended to create a labor pool to relieve any shortages between 1990 and 1993. Hundreds of thousands of Mexican farm workers here and in the United States are expected to apply before registration closes on Nov. 30, but a bitter bureaucratic battle in Washington has made it uncertain how many of them, if any, will actually be selected to come north and work in the fields.
The program was included in the Immigration Reform and Control Act of 1986 as a safety-valve measure after farmers and growers expressed fears that the legislation could create a shortage of migrant workers. But the Immigration and Naturalization Service, American officials say, views the program as a threat to the 1986 law and has opposed carrying out the program until it can be established that such a labor shortage exists. 
''If the shortage can be documented, it will reflect the fact that the main provisions'' of the 1986 legislation ''do not reflect objective demand for this kind of labor in the U.S. economy,'' said Wayne Cornelius, director of the Center for United States-Mexican studies at the University of California at San Diego. ''It will be proof of the fallacy that simply by passing this kind of legislation you can create a stable work force large enough to meet needs and then effectively seal up the country and the labor market.''
Growers argue that spot shortages have already appeared during the current harvest season and are likely to increase in coming years. Nearly 1.3 million people, more than 80 percent of them Mexicans, applied for ''special agricultural worker'' status in 1988, but many are believed to have abandoned agriculture for less arduous work in factories and service industries as soon as they legalized their status in the United States. Will Any Be Called? ''This is a very hot issue, and we are right in the middle of calculating the final number,'' said Rick Mines, a Labor Department official in Washington who is involved in the process of determining the shortage figure. He said, ''We can't be sure that any R.A.W. people will be called at all,'' at least this year.
Because of that uncertainty, the State Department and the Immigration Service have been reluctant to publicize the program in Mexico.
But with the scarcity of official information, misconceptions about the program are spreading. Radio stations and newspapers in areas of Mexico that have long supplied workers to American agriculture are portraying the program as another chance to qualify for amnesty under the new law.
''It needs to be made absolutely clear what this program is all about,'' said Jon G. Edensword, the United States Consul General in Mexico City. ''People need to understand that this is a registration program only and not a visa, not permission to work in the United States, not a travel authorization, and not a border-crossing card.''
In addition, unscrupulous middle men are taking advantage of gullible applicants, promising them special treatment and guaranteed access to the United States. One company here, calling itself United States International Immigration Services, requires a $500 advance payment and $2,500 more if applicants are selected.
Last week, a Government official in a Mexican border state, Coahuila, was arrested, along with six other people, on charges of defrauding applicants for the migrant labor program. The police said the ring operated from a government office in the state capital.ee.
Some Mexican and American Government officials, as well as immigration experts in both countries, assert that American farmer and grower groups are actively encouraging the misunderstandings. The objective, they say, is to create a labor surplus that would drive down farm wages.

HEADLINE: Quayle Tries to Quiet Japan's Trade Concerns

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TSUKUBA, Japan, Sept. 25

 BODY:
Rushing through a series of meetings with Japan's leadership, Vice President Dan Quayle brought a message of cooperation and reconciliation to America's biggest economic rival today, declaring that the United States' alliance with Japan should not be overshadowed by trade disputes.
Mr. Quayle's efforts to soften the tone between the two countries was in sharp contrast to recent visits by other members of the Bush Administration, who have been pressing for a quick solution to problems ranging from agriculture imports to high technology.
In a tour of Japan's ''science city'' here, 40 miles north of Tokyo, Mr. Quayle talked of a ''partnership'' between the United States and Japan in space exploration, and at other events he spoke of mutual efforts in environmental issues, drugs and debt relief. 
Nonetheless, trade issues seemed to dog Mr. Quayle. Talking to Hikaru Matsunaga, the Minister of International Trade and Industry, Mr. Quayle said failure to resolve trade disputes -which he took care not to name specifically - would place President Bush in an untenable position with Congress.
And at a Government space research center here, Mr. Quayle found himself in the uncomfortable position of admiring Japanese craftsmanship on a new telecommunications satellite, just months after the United States initiated a new trade action against Japan because Tokyo was protecting its own satellite industry by refusing to purchase American-made models.
Mr. Quayle said he saw no problem balancing Japan's dual role as a partner and a competitor in space activities, including the development of commercial satellites. ''Japan and the United States will have a partnership in space,'' he said, adding that the trade issue ''dealing with the satellites will be dealt with separately.''
Earlier in the day, Mr. Quayle met Prime Minister Toshiki Kaifu and told him that the three-decade-old Mutual Security Treaty, the backbone of the Japanese-American alliance after World War II, was ''not just a piece of paper.'' His comments were viewed as significant because the Japan Socialist Party, big gainers in the summer elections for the upper house of Parliament, has often criticized the treaty.
Mr. Quayle added that the Japanese-American military alliance was the ''key to preserving stability in East Asia,'' a point he touched on over the weekend in a visit to an air force base run by American and Japanese forces.
After his meeting with the Prime Minister, Mr. Quayle took part in the signing of an agreement for a joint space project, to be launched in 1992 aboard an American Delta-2 rocket, to investigate how the energy of the sun flows to earth. But Mr. Quayle's visit came at a time of uncertainty over how much the United States and Japan will be able to cooperate in space.
Japan has staked much of its space hopes, and much of its budget, on the space station that the United States has said it will build in the mid-1990's. A vital laboratory for the station will be built by Japan at a cost of $2 billion.
Japan is worried that Congress will be unwilling to pay for the space station program, which has been criticized in the United States as unfocused. Mr. Quayle, who heads the White House Space Council, told officials that President Bush was committed to the effort, and he made no effort to press Japan to pay for more of the space station, as members of Congress have suggested.

HEADLINE: Hungarian Serenade: Wooing Voters and the West

BYLINE: By HENRY KAMM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BUDAPEST, Sept. 23

 BODY:
The Communist leadership is casting about for ways out of Hungary's deep political and economic predicaments that will appeal to two critical quarters: the West and the Hungarian electorate.
The party is responsible for a profound economic crisis that it admits it cannot solve without Western aid. The West insists that to receive the aid, Hungary must go further in turning its one-party state into a country governed by freely elected representatives.
The Communist Party has committed itself to holding free parliamentary elections by June, making Hungary the second country, after Poland, in which a ruling Communist Party accepts an open electoral challenge by opponents. 
As a result, Hungarian Communists, like the Poles before them, are beginning to act like Western politicians. Unlike the past, when an ambitious Communist leader lobbied only for the support of the party machine and Soviet comrades, they are aiming their pitch at the people they are said to represent.
Three elections will soon test the standing of Communist politicians. Next month, they will have to face the most open congress of their party in its history, where popularity might decide whether a party leader is elected to one of the leading groups. Possibly by November, Hungarians are to elect the first President of the Republic, a new and potentially powerful position. By next spring, a new Parliament is to be popularly and freely chosen.
 
Active Steps and Speeches
As a result of seeking to please both the Western creditor nations and the Hungarian public, the Communist Party and its leaders have taken more active steps and made more speeches and press statements than has been the custom in the one-party state. This month, on a visit to the United States, the President of Parliament, Matyas Szuros, has used many opportunities to assert Hungary's desire to leave the Warsaw Pact and declare neutrality.
Government and Communist Party officials and senior Western diplomats expressed surprise at Mr. Szuros's heavy emphasis on an issue that most agree has no current standing among Hungarian policy makers because they see no change in Soviet opposition to defections from the alliance.
In an interview this month, Deputy Foreign Minister Ferenc Somogyi offered the official view. ''We consider our membership in the Warsaw Pact as an integral part of the European setup,'' he said. ''Some have said officially that no single country is interested in seeing Hungary break away under the present circumstances. But to become neutral generally is accepted as an ultimate goal.''
 
Efforts to Change Bloc
Mr. Somogyi said differences existed on the conditions for neutrality, and for the time being Hungary was concentrating on staying in the alliance and doing ''everything to reform the Warsaw Pact organization.''
He did not specify the nature of the reforms, but officials and Western diplomats said Hungary was aiming for a greater emphasis on the grouping's political rather than military aspects, allowing its members to consult freely and act separately more than as a bloc.
A senior Western diplomat said he viewed Mr. Szuros's statements as irresponsible. At the moment, he said, the Soviet Union is showing considerable tolerance for Hungary's democratization and most Hungarian leaders avoid ''counterprodutive statements'' that serve to make Moscow ''nervous.''
During a party shuffle in June, Mr. Szuros, a former party secretary, was not named to the 21-member leading body. The coming parliamentary elections also put in doubt whether he will continue in his present post. As a result, Hungarians and diplomats say, Mr. Szuros appears to be running harder than some of his colleagues.
 
Ouster of Kadar
The Communist Party is seen by Hungarians and diplomats as split into almost as many factions as there are contenders for top posts since the removal of Janos Kadar in May 1988.
Since then, Hungary has been in flux, striving to adopt measures to solve its economic predicament but failing to achieve a turnaround. The measures are intended to open the country to the creation of a private economy and to ease foreign investment.
They have given the image of a country loosening the chains of an overcentralized economy. The mechanisms have been created, but the results so far have been meager. There is little domestic capital to take advantage of the possibilities to build a productive private sector, whose shares would be traded on the newly created but as-yet-inactive stock market, and foreign capital has not poured in.
A steady flow of Western, Japanese, South Korean and Taiwanese businessmen looking for opportunities descend on this lovely capital in the warm weather, but not yet their money.
The American Ambassador, Mark Palmer, considered the most active Western booster for economic and political liberalization, estimated during a visit by Secretary of Commerce Robert A. Mosbacher that the flow of foreign investment was no more than $300 million and set this against the staggering foreign debt of $18 billion, the highest per capita in Europe.
 
A 'European' System
The party, guided by the Polish example, has negotiated general terms with opposition groups for a transition from one-party rule to a more ''European'' political system. Since last year, the inoffensive term European has become the code word for all measures to undo what are now regarded as perversions of Hungary's normal ways at the behest of a domineering Soviet Union.
Diplomats note that in all these measures, Hungary is observing a principle that, with the brief exception of the national uprising in 1956, has always marked its policies. Budapest attunes its initiatives to what it judges to be the prevailing mood in Moscow.
''We have always gnawed at the borderlines of the possible,'' a senior party official said. In Mikhail S. Gorbachev's Soviet Union, Hungarian officials believe, their gnawing space is greater than ever, as is the Soviet leadership's preoccupation with domestic crises. Hungarians like Mr. Szuros feel almost free to try to get into step with the majority of their anti-Communist nation.
Policy makers are encouraged in their urge to strive for the possible, the official said, by having allowed the migration of thousands of East Germans from Hungary to West Germany through Austria without arousing more than perfunctory Soviet criticism.
