HEADLINE: Excerpts From Bush's Speech at the Opening of the U.N. General Assembly

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from President Bush's address yesterday to the United Nations General Assembly, as recorded by The New York Times:
. . . I feel a great personal pleasure on this occasion, for this is a homecoming for both Barbara and me. The memories of my time here in 1971 and 1972 are still with me today - the human moments, the humorous moments that are part of even the highest undertaking.
With your permission, let me share one story from one of the many sessions of the Security Council. I was the Permanent Representative of the United States. I was 45 minutes late getting to the meeting. And all 45 minutes were filled by the first speaker to take the floor. And when I walked in and took my seat, the speaker paused, with great courtesy, and said as follows: ''I welcome the Permanent Representative of the United States and now, for his benefit, I will start my speech all over again from the beginning.''
That's a true story. And at that moment, differences of alliance, ideology, didn't matter. The universal groan that went up around that table from every member present, and then the laughter that followed, united us all. ... 
 
Condemning Terrorism
The United Nations was established 44 years ago upon the ashes of war and amidst great hopes. And the United Nations can do great things. No, the United Nations is not perfect. It's not a panacea for world's problems. But it is a vital forum where the nations of the world seek to replace conflict with consensus and it must remain a forum for peace.
The U.N. is moving closer to that ideal. And it has the support of the United States of America.
In recent years, certainly since my time here, the war of words that has often echoed in this chamber is giving way to a new mood. We've seen a welcome shift from polemics to peacekeeping. U.N. peacekeeping forces are on duty right now and over the years more than 700 peacekeepers have given their lives in service to the United Nations.
Today I want to remember one of those soldiers of peace - an American on a mission of peace under the United Nations flag - on a mission, really, for all the world. A man of unquestioned bravery, unswerving dedication to the United Nations ideal, Lieut. Col. William Richard Higgins.
And I call on the General Assembly to condemn the murder of this soldier of peace and call on those responsible to have the decency to return his remains to his family and let us all, right now, right here, rededicate ourselves and our nations to the cause the Colonel Higgins served so selflessly. ...
 
Openness in the East
Freedom's advance is evident everywhere - Central Europe and Hungary, where state and society are now in the midst of a movement towards political pluralism and a free market economy, where the barrier that once enforced an unnatural division between Hungary and its neighbors to the West has been torn down. Torn down. Replaced by a new hope for the future, a new hope in freedom.
We see freedom at work in Poland, where in deference to the will of the people the Communist Party has relinquished its monopoly on power. And indeed in the Soviet Union, where the world hears the voices of people no longer afraid to speak out or to assert the right to rule themselves.
But freedom's mark is not confined to a single continent or to the developed world alone. We see the rise of freedom in Latin America where, one by one, dictatorships are giving way to democracy. We see it on the continent of Africa where more and more nations see, in the system of free enterprise, salvation for economies crippled by excessive state control.
 
Collapse of Despotism
East and west, north and south, on every continent, we can see the outlines of a new world of freedom.
Of course, freedom's work remains unfinished. The trend we see is not yet universal. Some regimes still stand against the tide. Some rulers still deny the right of the people to govern themselves. But now the power of prejudice and despotism is challenged. Never before have these regimes stood so isolated and alone, so out of step with the steady advance of freedom.
Today we are witnessing an ideological collapse - the demise of the totalitarian idea of the omniscient, all-powerful state. There are many reasons for this collapse. But in the end one fact alone explains what we see today. Advocates of the totalitarian idea saw its triumph written in the laws of history. They failed to see the love of freedom that was written in the human heart. ...
 
Strengthening Freedom
Our challenge is to strengthen the foundations of freedom, encourage its advance and face our most urgent challenges - the global challenges of the 21st century: economic health, environmental well-being, the great questions of war and peace. First, global economic growth. During this decade a number of developing nations have moved into the ranks of the world's most advanced economies, all of them - each and every one - powered by the engine of free enterprise.
In the decade ahead others can join their ranks.
But for many nations, barriers stand in the way. In the case of some countries, these are obstacles of their own making - unneeded restrictions and regulations that act as dead weights on their own economies and obstacles to foreign trade.
But other barriers to growth exist and those, too, require effective action. Too many developing countries struggle today under a burden of debt that makes growth all but impossible. The nations of the world deserve better opportunity to achieve a measure of control over their own economic fate and build better lives for their own people.
The approach the U.S. has put forward - the Brady plan - will help these nations reduce that debt and at the same time encourage the free market reforms that will fuel economic growth.
 
The Debt Crisis
In just two days, I will be speaking to the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank and I'll discuss there, in more detail, the steps that our nations can take in dealing with the debt problem.
But I can say now the new world of freedom is not a world where a few nations live in comfort while others live in want.
The power of commerce is a force for progress. Open markets are the key to continued growth in the developing world.
Today the United States buys over one half of the manufactured exports that all developing nations combined sell to the industrialized world.
It's time for the other advanced economies to follow suit, to create expanded opportunities for trade. I believe we'll learn, in the century ahead, that many nations of the world have barely begun to tap their true potential for development.
The free market and its fruits are not the special preserve of a few. They are a harvest that everyone can share.
 
Threat to the Environment
Beyond the challenge of global growth lies another issue of global magnitude - the environment.
No line drawn on a map can stop the advance of pollution.
Threats to our environment have become international problems. We must develop an international approach to urgent environmental issues. One that seeks common solutions to common problems.
The United Nations is already at work on the question of global warming and the effort to prevent oil spills and other disasters from fouling our seas and the air we breathe.
And I will tell you now the United States will do its part. We have committed ourselves to the world-wide phase-out of all chlorofluorocarbons by the year 2000. We've proposed amending our own Clean Air Act to insure clean air for our citizens within a single generation.
We've banned the import of ivory to protect the elephant and rhinoceros from the human predators who exterminate them for profit. And we've begun to explore ways to work with other nations, with the major industrialized democracies, and in Poland and in Hungary, to make common cause for the sake of our environment.
The environment belongs to all of us. In this new world of freedom the world citizens must enjoy this common trust for generations to come.
 
Banning Chemical Weapons
Global economic growth. The stewardship of our planet. Both are critical issues. But as always questions of war and peace must be paramount to the United Nations.
We must move forward to limit and eliminate weapons of mass destruction.
Five years ago, at the United Nations Conference on Disarmament, in Geneva, I presented a United States draft treaty outlawing chemical weapons. Since then, progress has been made but time is running out. The threat is growing. More than 20 nations now possess chemical weapons or the capability to produce them. And these horrible weapons are now finding their way into regional conflicts. This is simply unacceptable. For the sake of mankind, we must halt and reverse this threat.
Today I want to announce steps that the United States is ready to take - steps to rid the world of these truly terrible weapons. Towards a treaty that will ban, eliminate, all chemical weapons from the earth 10 years from the day it is signed. This initiative contains three major elements.
 
Three Steps to a Treaty
First, in the first eight years of a chemical weapons treaty, the U.S. is ready to destroy nearly all - 98 percent - of our chemical weapons stockpile, provided the Soviet Union joins the ban, and I think they will.
Second, we are ready to destroy all of our chemical weapons - 100 percent - every one - within 10 years once all nations capable of building chemical weapons sign that total-ban treaty.
And third, the United States is ready to begin now. We will eliminate more than 80 percent of our stockpile even as we work to complete a treaty if the Soviet Union joins us in cutting chemical weapons to an equal level and we agree on the conditions, including inspections, under which stockpiles are destroyed.
We know that monitoring a total ban on chemical weapons will be a challenge. But the knowledge we've gained from our recent arms control experience and our accelerating research in this area makes me believe that we can achieve the level of verification that gives us confidence to go forward with the ban.
The world has lived too long in the shadow of chemical warfare. So let us act together beginning today to rid the earth of this scourge.
 
Conventional Arms
We are serious about achieving conventional arms reductions as well. And that's why we tabled new proposals just last Thursday at the Conventional Forces in Europe Negotiations in Vienna, proposals that demonstrate our commitment to act rapidly to ease military tensions in Europe and move the nations of that continent one step closer to their common destiny - a Europe whole and free.
And the United States is convinced that open and innovative measures can move disarmament forward and also ease international tensions. And that's the idea behind the ''open skies'' proposal about which the Soviets have now expressed a positive attitude. It's the idea behind the ''open lands'' proposal permitting, for the first time ever, free travel for all Soviet and American diplomats throughout each other's country.
Openness is the enemy of mistrust. And every step towards a more open world is a step toward the new world we seek. 1990 Summit Meeting And let me make this comment on our meetings with the distinguished Foreign Minister of the Soviet Union, Mr. Shevardnadze, over the past few days. I am very pleased by the progress made. The Soviet Union removed a number of obstacles to progress on conventional and strategic arms reduction. We reached agreements in principle on issues from verification to nuclear testing. And, of course, we agreed to a summit in the spring or early summer of 1990.
And I look forward to meeting Mr. Gorbachev there.
Each of these achievements is important in its own right. But they are more important still as signs of a new attitude that prevails between the United States and the U.S.S.R.
Serious differences remain. We know that.
But the willingness to deal constructively and candidly with those differences is news that we, and indeed the world, must welcome.
 
Regional Conflicts
We have not entered into an era of perpetual peace. The threats to peace that nations face may today be changing but they've not vanished.
In fact, in a number of regions around the world, a dangerous combination is now emerging: Regimes armed with old and unappeasable animosities and modern weapons of mass destruction.
This development will raise the stakes whenever war breaks out. Regional conflicts may well threaten world peace as never before. The challenge of preserving peace is a personal one for all of you right here in this, in this hall.
Mr. Secretary General, with great respect, you have made it your own.
The United Nations can be a mediator, a forum where parties in conflict come in search of peaceful solutions. For the sake of peace, the U.N. must redouble its support for the peace efforts now underway in regions of conflict all over the world.
And let me assure you the United States is determined to take an active role in settling regional conflicts.
Sometimes our role in regional disputes is and will be highly public. And sometimes, like many of you, we work quietly behind the scenes. But always we are working for positive change and lasting peace.
 
The Drug Scourge
Our world faces other, less conventional, threats no less dangerous to international peace and stability.
Illegal drugs are a menace to social order and a source of human misery wherever they gain a foothold. The nations who suffer this scourge must join forces in the fight. And we are.
And let me salute the commitment and extraordinary courage of one country in particular, Colombia, where we are working with the people and their President, Vergilio Barco, to put the drug cartels out of business, bring the drug lords to justice.
And finally, we must join forces to combat the threat of terrorism. Every nation and the United Nations must send the outlaws of the world a clear message: Hostage-taking and the terror of random violence are methods that cannot win the world's approval. Terrorism of any kind is repugnant to all values that a civilized world holds in common. And make no mistake, terrorism is a means that no end, no matter how just that end, can sanctify. ...
 
The Dream of Peace
When I began today, I spoke to you about peacekeeping.
I want to speak to you now about peacemaking.
We must bring peace to the people who have never known its blessings. There's a painting that hangs on the wall of my office in the White House and it pictures President Abraham Lincoln and his generals meeting near the end of a war that remains the bloodiest in the history of my country.
Outside at that moment a battle rages - in this picture - and yet what we see in the distance is a rainbow - a symbol of hope, of the passing of the storm. That painting is called ''The Peacemakers.'' For me, it is a constant reminder that our struggle, the struggle for peace, is a struggle blessed by hope.
I do remember sitting in this hall. I remember the mutual respect among all of us proudly serving as representatives.
Yes, I remember the almost endless speeches. And I don't want this to be one of them. The Security Council sessions, the receptions, those long receiving lines, the formal meetings of this assembly and the informal discussions in the delegates lounge over here.
And I remember something more - something beyond the frantic pace and sometimes frustrating experiences of daily life here. The heartbeat of the United Nations. The quiet conviction that we could make the world more peaceful, more free. What we sought then - all of us - now lies within our reach.
 
Unity of Purpose
I ask each of you here in this hall: Can we not bring a unity of purpose to the United Nations? Can we not make this new world of freedom the common destiny we seek? I believe we can. I know we must. My solemn wish today is that here among the United Nations, that spirit will take hold and that all man and all nations will make freedom's moment their own.
Thank you. God bless you. And may God bless the work of the United Nations.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: With September's Rains Come Baby Showers

BYLINE: By FELICITY BARRINGER

 BODY:
''Teeming autumn, big with rich increase,'' is what Shakespeare called it. At maternity wards all over the country, it's called chaos.
Since 1942, when the first reliable nationwide birth records were used, a September baby boom has made the year's ninth month the demographic equivalent of rush hour in America. 
In 1987, for instance, about 11,135 children were born every day in September, 700 more children per day than the annual daily average and nearly 1,300 more per day than in January, the low point for births.
Why the annual baby boom? Does the frenzy of Christmas shopping beget other frenzies? Is it a function of New Year's Eve celebrations, of resolutions, of people making sure old acquaintance is not forgot? Does a national craving for human warmth ride in on the Canadian winds at autumn's end?
 
Hypotheses in Search of Facts
Jeffrey Miron, an economist at the University of Michigan, and a colleague there, David Lamb, a demographer, have tried these and a number of other hypotheses only to find no single explanation that matches the facts.
''The obvious conjecture is a Christmas effect,'' said Mr. Miron. ''You know, leisure time, a warm family glow. But it also occurs in India and Israel, so calling it a Christmas effect is not necessarily appropriate.''
And in northern Europe, homeland of the Christmas tree, King Wenceslas and any number of merry gentlemen, September is not the big birth month, he said. ''There's a blip upward in September in places like northern England and the Scandinavian countries,'' Mr. Miron said, ''but the big birth peak is in the spring.''
 
Temperature and Long Nights
The latest Miron-Lamb hypothesis, still being tested, assumes that the seasonal increase is governed by some interaction between temperature fluctuations, the length of nights and the intensity of daytime light. But as Mr. Miron contemplates a growing heap of discarded hypotheses, he says, he is not sure this one will work either.
Demographers first started looking at the boom-and-bust birth cycle as early as the 1930's; nobody can remember a September without many births. There were some expectations that the wide availability of birth control measures in the 1960's, and of abortion in the 1970's, would even out the seasonal peaks. But in this case, science and medicine were apparently powerless against some more primeval imperative.
''The seasonal pattern doesn't change - on births it hasn't changed for 30 years,'' said Stephanie Ventura, a demographer for the National Center for Health Statistics. ''The September thing has been there for as long as we can remember.''

LENGTH: 1633 words

HEADLINE: Poll Finds Broad Support for Bush but Skepticism About Drugs and Taxes

BYLINE: By R.W. APPLE Jr.

 BODY:
The American public thoroughly approves of the way President Bush is handling his job and supports most of his programs, but doubts whether many of them, including Mr. Bush's highly publicized plans to fight illegal drugs and clean up the environment, will really work.
Such skepticism also applies to the President's campaign pledge not to increase taxes. Most voters have read Mr. Bush's lips and endorse his opposition to new taxes, but they do not think that he will be able to stick to it. Almost three out of four Americans, an unusually large majority, expect the President to ask Congress for a tax increase sometime in his four-year term.
Those are some of the main findings of the latest New York Times/CBS News poll, a survey that includes plenty of political reassurance for Mr. Bush, but also perhaps an early warning that his Presidency has not yet generated great enthusiasm or full confidence in his leadership. 
 
Few Apprehensions Found
By the same token, the poll suggests that the electorate has relatively few apprehensions about the state of the nation.
The poll, based on telephone interviews with 1,347 adults nationwide from Sept. 17 to 20, had a margin of sampling error of plus or minus three percentage points.
Over all, 69 percent of the public approves of Mr. Bush's job performance and 18 percent disapproves, according to the new poll. The figures have been more or less steady since July, and are generally in line with other recent polls. On foreign policy the approval rating is 60 percent, up 7 points since it was last measured in April, and on the economy it is 55 percent, up 10 points since April.
 
Rating of Other Presidents
None of the last three elected Presidents before Mr. Bush - Richard M. Nixon, Jimmy Carter and Ronald Reagan - stood that tall with the nation's voters eight months into their terms. Indeed, Mr. Bush's rating is now about the same as Mr. Reagan's highest personal rating, which was 68 percent, reached in New York Times polls in December 1985 and May 1986.
But Dwight D. Eisenhower, John F. Kennedy and Lyndon B. Johnson all recorded higher scores at this stage in Gallup polls - 75 percent for President Eisenhower in September 1953, 76 percent for President Kennedy in September 1961 and 74 percent for President Johnson in June 1964, seven months after Kennedy's assassination.
All three Presidents subsequently suffered sharp declines in popularity, notably Mr. Johnson, whose approval rating fell to 35 percent in 1968. Aides to Mr. Bush say he will be subject to those same shifts of the public mood.
 
A Hint From Sununu
John H. Sununu, the White House chief of staff, recently sent to friends of the Administration copies of a graph showing that at about this stage both Mr. Carter and Mr. Reagan started to lose popularity, as if to warn people, ''Don't be too terribly surprised if the President stubs a toe some day soon.''
Taxes might turn out to be a big problem, it appears from the poll.
When asked what the President should do to pay for domestic programs, 53 percent said he should not raise taxes but only 40 percent said he should. However, when asked specifically whether they would be willing to pay $100 more a year in Federal taxes to increase spending on the war against drugs, 58 percent responded that they would.
Mr. Bush's difficulty is that he has never been able to persuade the country of his determination not to raise taxes. Last January, as he was assuming the Presidency, 73 percent of the respondents to an earlier Times/CBS poll said he would seek to raise taxes. In the current survey the number was the same despite all that he and his aides have said on the subject since he took office.
 
Good Grades on Education
In a number of areas Americans have a good deal of confidence in the President. For example, 57 percent believe he will be able to improve education, which is down a bit from January, but still encouraging for a man who says he wants to be remembered as ''the education President.'' And 79 percent think he will be able to maintain the current good relations with the Soviet Union.
But 54 percent say Mr. Bush will not be able to improve the environment significantly, and 51 percent say he will not be able to ''significantly reduce the drug problem.''
Nonetheless, 69 percent say they approve of Mr. Bush's handling of the drug issue, which suggests that the intractability of the problem and not the President's approach is what makes the public so skeptical of success. Fifty-four percent of the respondents identified drugs as the nation's No. 1 problem, down from 64 percent earlier in the month, when sentiment was measured immediately after the President's speech outlining his Administration's drug strategy. But this still demonstrates an intense public focus on this one domestic issue.
The poll indicated, however, that different people mean different things when they speak about ''the drug issue.'' What bothers 17 percent of those who named drugs as the big problem is children using drugs. But 15 percent of those citing drugs said violence and crime connected with drugs caused them the greatest concern.
 
Drugs as Crucial Yardstick
If the current intensity of concern persists, making progress on drugs could become the crucial measurement of the Bush Administration's political success or failure.
Mr. Bush's popularity extends to all segments of society. More than 55 percent of those in all age groups approve of his performance so far, as do more than 60 percent of those with each level of education, and a majority of those at all income levels. Men like him slightly more than women (73 percent vs. 65 percent), white Protestants like him better than Roman Catholics (76 per cent vs. 66 percent). Even among Democrats, liberals, blacks and Hispanic Americans, his ratings are high.
There are, nonetheless, some findings in the poll that might give the President and his aides pause.
Only 13 percent of the respondents in the survey expressed a favorable view of Vice President Dan Quayle, with 19 percent holding an unfavorable opinion. Months of intensive television and newspaper exposure have created only a faint picture of Mr. Quayle in the public mind, with 45 percent saying they know too little about him to form an opinion. That number has been rising since the election; apparently, the more people hear and see about the Vice President the less they understand him.
Such figures are sure to make Mr. Bush's political strategists think hard about whether Mr. Quayle should stay on the ticket in 1992, even if the President, for whom loyalty is a political watchword, continues to oppose a change.
 
A Split on Eastern Europe
Another soft spot in public opinion concerns Eastern Europe, an area of extremely rapid change where the Administration concedes it is still feeling its way.
On the question of American aid to countries like Poland and Hungary, which are becoming more independent of the Soviet Union, opinion is fairly closely divided, with 40 percent favoring more aid and 51 percent opposing it. Likewise, on the question of American help for Soviet President Mikhail S. Gorbachev's reforms by removing trade restrictions, 41 percent say yes and 48 percent say the United States should not get involved.
Voters see no very clear pattern emerging in Mr. Bush's relations with the Democratic-controlled Congress, a topic that apparently excites more interest inside Washington than outside. Forty percent think the Democrats are cooperating with the President, while 36 percent think they are obstructing him. In any event, only a little more than a third of the public thinks control of the White House and the Congress by the same party is better for the country than divided control.
Looking toward next year's midterm elections, the poll gives the Republicans some reason for optimism. If the election were held now, 39 percent of the registered voters said they would back the Democratic candidate in their district while 38 percent would opt for the Republican candidate. That kind of voting pattern would almost certainly greatly shrink the current Democratic majorities on Capitol Hill.
 
How the Poll Was Taken
The latest New York Times/CBS News Poll is based on telephone interviews conducted Sept. 17 to 20 with 1,347 adults around the United States, excluding Alaska and Hawaii.
The sample of telephone exchanges called was selected by a computer from a complete list of exchanges in the country. The exchanges were chosen so as to assure that each region of the country was represented in proportion to its population. For each exchange, the telephone numbers were formed by random digits, thus permitting access to both listed and unlisted numbers. The numbers were then screened to limit calls to residences.
The results have been weighted to take account of household size and number of residential telephone lines and to adjust for variations in the sample relating to region, race, sex, age and education. In theory, in 19 cases out of 20 the results based on such samples will differ by no more than three percentage points in either direction from what would have been obtained by seeking out all American adults.
The percentages reported are the particular results most likely to match what would be obtained by seeking out all adult Americans. Other possible percentages are progressively less likely the more they differ from the reported results.
The potential sampling error for smaller subgroups is larger. For example, for either men or women it is plus or minus four percentage points.
In addition to sampling error, the practical difficulties of conducting any survey of public opinion may introduce other sources of error into the poll.

HEADLINE: ON MY MIND;
The Case For Slavery

BYLINE: By A. M. Rosenthal

 BODY:
Across the country, a scattered but influential collection of intellectuals is intensely engaged in making the case for slavery.
With considerable passion, these Americans are repeatedly expounding the benefits of not only tolerating slavery but legalizing it:
It would make life less dangerous for the free. It would save a great deal of money. And since the economies could be used to improve the lot of the slaves, in the end they would be better off. 
The new anti-abolitionists, like their predecessors in the 19th century, concede that those now in bondage do not themselves see the benefits of legalizing their status.
But in time they will, we are assured, because the beautiful part of legalization is that slavery would be designed so as to keep slaves pacified with the very thing that enslaves them!
The form of slavery under discussion is drug addiction. It does not have every characteristic of more traditional forms of bondage. But they have enough in common to make the comparison morally valid - and the campaign for drug legalization morally disgusting.
Like the plantation slavery that was a foundation of American society for so long, drug addiction largely involves specifiable groups of people. Most of the enchained are children and adolescents of all colors and black and Hispanic adults.
Like plantation slavery, drug addiction is passed on from generation to generation. And this may be the most important similarity: like plantation slavery, addiction can de-stroy among its victims the social resources most valuable to free people for their own betterment - family life, family traditions, family values.
In plantation-time America, mothers were taken from their children. In drug-time America, mothers abandon their children. Do the children suffer less, or the mothers?
Anti-abolitionists argue that legalization would make drugs so cheap and available that the profit for crime would be removed. Well-supplied addicts would be peaceful addicts. We would not waste billions for jails and could spend some of the savings helping the addicted become drug-free.
That would happen at the very time that new millions of Americans were being enticed into addiction by legalization - somehow.
Are we really foolish enough to believe that tens of thousands of drug gang members would meekly steal away, foiled by the marvels of the free market?
Not likely. The pushers would cut prices, making more money than ever from the ever-growing mass market. They would immediately increase the potency and variety beyond anything available at any Government-approved narcotics counters.
Crime would increase. Crack produces paranoid violence. More permissiveness equals more use equals more violence.
And what will legalization do to the brains of Americans drawn into drug slavery by easy availability?
Earlier this year, an expert drug pediatrician told me that after only a few months babies born with crack addiction seemed to recover. Now we learn that stultifying behavioral effects last at least through early childhood. Will they last forever?
How long will crack affect neurological patterns in the brains of adult crack users? Dr. Gabriel G. Nahas of Columbia University argues in his new book, ''Cocaine: The Great White Plague,'' that the damage may be irreversible. Would it not be an act of simple intelligence to drop the legalization campaign until we find out?
Then why do a number of writers and academicians, left to right, support it? I have discussed this with anti-drug leaders like Jesse Jackson, Dr. Mitchell Rosenthal of Phoenix House and William J. Bennett, who search for answers themselves.
Perhaps the answer is that the legalizers are not dealing with reality in America. I think the reason has to do with class.
Crack is beginning to move into the white middle and upper classes. That is a tragedy for those addicted.
However, it has not yet destroyed the communities around which their lives revolve, not taken over every street and doorway. It has not passed generation to generation among them, killing the continuity of family.
But in ghetto communities poverty and drugs come together in a catalytic reaction that is reducing them to social rubble.
The anti-abolitionists, virtually all white and well-to-do, do not see or do not care. Either way they show symptoms of the callousness of class. That can be a particularly dangerous social disorder.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: OP-ED

SECTION: Section B; Page 1, Column 2; Metropolitan Desk

LENGTH: 1686 words

HEADLINE: Dinkins Gaining Support Among Business Executives

BYLINE: By SAM ROBERTS

 BODY:
New York City's business community, without an incumbent mayor to support for the first time in 16 years, is largely following the public-opinion polls and finding a familiar face in David N. Dinkins, the Democratic nominee.
Mr. Dinkins, the Manhattan Borough President, has already inherited prominent fund-raisers and contributors from the three-term incumbent he toppled in the September 12 primary, Mayor Edward I. Koch, even though there is a credible Republican candidate.
A sampling of executives found substantial, though sometimes conditional, support for Mr. Dinkins. Many business people who have dealt with him as Borough President described him as experienced and reasonable. That he would be the city's first black mayor appeals to executives who are concerned that racial polarization may crimp economic prosperity. 
 
A Degree of Wariness
His Republican rival, Rudolph W. Giuliani, achieved fame as the United States Prosecutor in Manhattan, but he is a political novice. The independent and fresh perspective he promotes as an asset is regarded skeptically by some business executives, who complain that he lacks experience in city government and a coherent platform.
Moreover, until early this year, he was prosecuting individuals and companies in the securities industry, from which he is now seeking support, with some success.
Even among Dinkins supporters, however, some executives expressed concern about what they labeled a ''liberal triumverate'' - Mr. Dinkins and two other Democratic candidates who are virtually assured of election: Elizabeth Holtzman, the Brooklyn District Attorney, who would replace a four-term incumbent as comptroller, and Ruth W. Messinger, a West Side City Council member, who would succeed Mr. Dinkins as borough president.
Some real-estate executives are nearly apoplectic about Mr. Dinkins's support for what they consider a form of commercial rent control.
 
Unease About Labor Ties
And still other executives fear that generous labor settlements with the municipal unions that are supporting Mr. Dinkins will cost them more in taxes and in demands from their own employees.
''He has to come out with a sincere and honest statement to the business community, many of whom didn't vote for him, that the new administration would understand and be receptive to what businesses' needs are,'' said Lewis Rudin, a landlord and developer who heads the Association for a Better New York.
The executives' concern was tempered, though, by their expectation that any return to profligacy would be constrained by monitors installed after the city's fiscal crisis in the 1970's.
 
Koch Supporter Backs Dinkins
Among the Koch fund-raisers who have joined Mr. Dinkins since the primary is Jerry Speyer, a real-estate developer. He called the nominee ''a reasonable person and a good listener'' whose position on commercial rent arbitration merely proved that ''nothing is perfect.''
With Mr. Giuliani, Mr. Speyer said, ''I don't think the issue is his competence; it's his lack of experience.''
Mr. Rudin's brother Jack, also a landlord and developer, said of Mr. Dinkins: ''He's a very responsible guy. David is up to calling it as he sees it for the good of the city as a whole.''
The business community has lost some of its political influence because the city's new campaign finance law limits individual contributions to $3,000 for the general election race. But an influential donor can encourage colleagues to give and, more importantly, can provide an imprimatur that money alone cannot buy. Money represents the most tangible sign of political support, though, and Mr. Giuliani has been lagging in overall contributions.
Ordinarily raising money for a Republican candidate in New York city is tough enough; none has been elected mayor on the party's line in 24 years. The moderate or conservative ideological bent of many executives often is subordinated to common sense, which holds that it is bad for business to buck an incumbent or the party in power.
 
Eyes on the Polls
''Better take the guy we know than the guy we don't know, unless he comes up in the polls,'' was how Lawrence Mandelker, Mr. Koch's campaign treasurer, put it.
Mr. Giuliani's Republican credentials may be suspect to some, since he also has the endorsement of the Liberal Party. Some real-estate executives are uncomfortable with Mr. Giuliani because he, like Mr. Dinkins, supports legislation that would prohibit landlords from keeping apartments off the market.
He also would consider limits on commercial rents in some neighborhoods, while Mr. Dinkins favors legislation that would trigger binding arbitration when a landlord seeks to raise commercial rent beyond a percentage set by law. Both say they would raise taxes only as a last resort.
Mr. Giuliani has harped on corruption to people who are worried less that public officials will do the wrong thing than that they will do nothing.
 
Support for Giuliani
''When Rudy first ran, I remember people saying that because he had cracked down on Wall Street, we were not going to have business support,'' said John H. Gross, a lawyer who is the Giuliani campaign treasurer. ''In fact, we have done very well with the financial community and with the legal community.''
Among those who have helped raise money, Mr. Gross said, are William E. Simon, the former Treasury Secretary; George L. Ball, chairman of Prudential-Bache Securities; Jeffrey B. Lane, president of Shearson Lehman Hutton Inc.; Alton G. Marshall, a banker and investor; E. John Rosenwald Jr., vice chairman of Bear Stearns & Company; and Frank Zarb, chief executive of Smith Barney Inc.
''I think he has a record of success in the things he has done, and the city needs new blood,'' said Mr. Lane, a Republican.
''I'm looking for people who are hedging their bets,'' Mr. Marshall said. ''In the old days, when a guy was putting $50,000 on the line, he wanted to know everything about a candidate down to the last clause. But now, when a guy is writing a check for $3,000, he looks at the situation, which is that this is the first time in a long time that there is at least a possibility that someone other than the Democrat might have a shot at winning, because of the unique situation we have this time.''
The situation to which he referred is Mr. Dinkins's race, which has injected an element of uncertainty when otherwise a Democrat would be considered a prohibitive favorite.
 
Unknown Factors Acknowledged
''David Dinkins is a very intelligent, amiable gentleman, but I'm not sure good guys, frankly, finish first, as far as running the city goes,'' said Charles J. Urstadt, who heads his own real-estate company. ''Dave may well stand up to the unions and his own constituencies, but he may not.''
''I've been a Republican all my life, so I naturally lean toward the standard-bearer,'' Mr. Urstadt said. ''But Giuliani is an unknown, too.''
Different components of the city's business community often have different agendas, but they share many common concerns. Moreover, said Ronald K. Shelp, president of the city's Chamber of Commerce, ''there has been a convergence of so-called business issues - the tax climate, regulation -and what might be called social issues - education and affordable housing and good race relations.''
William Prensky, president of Harry Gitlin Inc., a custom architectural design and manufacturing company in Manhattan, said his biggest concerns were the cost of doing business in the city, crime and education. He expressed reservations about both major party candidates but said, ''I don't think Dinkins will be an anti-business mayor.''
 
Concern About Crime
Crime is the major concern of Kenneth J. Buettner, chief executive of the York Scaffold Equipment Corporation in Long Island City, Queens. But Mr. Buettner, an enrolled Republican, said that while Mr. Giuliani was able to focus effectively on crime as a prosecutor, ''I don't know if he becomes mayor he would be able to direct his attention to that while running the whole city.''
Joseph H. Flom, a lawyer who was co-chairman of Mr. Koch's finance committee and now is raising funds for Mr. Dinkins, said that ''we've already seen a palpable easing'' of racial tension as a result of Mr. Dinkins's nomination.
Among others identified by the Dinkins camp as supporters were Arthur L. Liman, the lawyer; Milton J. Petrie, chairman of Petrie Stores, and Steven J. Ross, co-chairman of Time Warner Inc.
Donald J. Trump, the developer and casino operator, hedged his bets in the primaries. By contributing to Mr. Dinkins and to Mr. Giuliani, he won twice. Mr. Trump said he had been too preoccupied with celebrating Mr. Koch's defeat to choose between the winners.
 
'Reduced Tension' Stressed
Felix G. Rohatyn, a partner in Lazard Freres & Company, the investment banking concern, and chairman of the Municipal Assistance Corporation, said: ''On balance, people in the business community think that reduced tension has to be the highest priority, that it's impossible to govern with any requirement for sacrifice unless the people who are going to be asked to sacrifice feel they are being treated fairly. Dave has a lot of personal qualities that lend themselves to that kind of approach.''
''The coming to the fore at the same time of a number of elected officials of strongly liberal background and with whom labor unions have significant influence is going to be of some concern,'' Mr. Rohatyn said. ''But you can be a philosophical liberal and a financial conservative. Dave is an extremely careful man. Careful people don't go about throwing money away. And he is as conscious of the need to maintain fiscal balance and a vibrant economy as much as we in the business community are of maintaining a stable social base.''
Who would make a better mayor? Mr. Rohatyn was asked. ''Suffice it to say that I would be extremely comfortable with David Dinkins as mayor of the city,'' replied Mr. Rohatyn, who is a Democrat. ''Other than on crime and corruption issues, Giuliani is still a blank slate.''

HEADLINE: Looting on St. Croix Linked to Island's Tensions

BYLINE: By JEFFREY SCHMALZ, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SAN JUAN, P.R., Sept. 25

 BODY:
As food and other relief supplies poured into St. Croix today, residents and Federal officials began asking why, of a dozen Caribbean islands devastated by a hurricane last week, only one erupted in widespread violence and looting.
The answer, Federal officials and residents say, lies in a number of factors involving class, race, isolation and a general lack of government authority.
Unlike many Caribbean islands that have their own government, the main government controlling St. Croix is 40 miles away on St. Thomas, the capital of the Virgin Islands, a territory of the United States. As a result, St. Croix has long been a little less controlled, a little more lawless. Although the crime rate is high throughout the Virgin Islands, it is particularly high on St. Croix, with its rate of violent crime running three times the national average. Crack use is also more widespread on St. Croix than on most other Caribbean islands, Federal officials and private study groups say. 
 
Racial and Class Tensions
When many National Guard troops and police officers failed to report for duty the day after the hurricane, Federal officials said, that reinforced the feeling of many that there was no authority.
Like other Caribbean islands, St. Croix has racial tensions, with more than three-quarters of the island's 55,000 people being blacks who hold mostly low-paying labor jobs. Whites own most of the businesses and many hire whites from the mainland instead of blacks from St. Croix. And unlike other Caribbean islands, blacks on St. Croix have to compete for jobs with black and Hispanic residents from other islands who come to St. Croix to work in the tourist industry.
''I'm not surprised about the looting,'' said Ann Brittain, an assistant anthropology professor at the University of Miami who has studied the Virgin Islands. ''When you look at the isolation, the crime already there, the low wages, the fact that blacks cannot afford to rent or buy a house, it was bound to happen.
Charles Fisher, an editor at The St. Croix Avis, the only black-owned paper in the Virgin Islands, said: ''This is a class fight. The rich guys come down from New York or wherever and build a hotel, but they can't be bothered to build us a hurricane shelter or a playground.''
 
Unemployment Rate Is High
Statistics that could offer a barometer of the economic status of blacks on St. Croix were not available today. Federal figures - for unemployment, for example - are kept for the Virgin Islands as a whole, not individual islands. The islands themselves keep their own statistics, but since St. Croix officials was occupied today by the hurricane cleanup, it was unable to supply them.
But some private groups, including the Council on Hemispheric Affairs, said they believed unemployment on St. Croix is as high as 30 percent.
Larry Birns, the director of the council. a Washington-based research group, said: ''St. Croix has been a Watts or a Harlem in the waiting, The United States is not good at being a colonial power. It pays some attention to St. Thomas as a commercial center. But St. Croix is treated as backwoods. It has few paved roads, poor schools, poor medical care. If it wasn't the hurricane that touched things off, it would have been something else.''
But some white residents are not so sure that last week's incidents are part of a class fight. They point out that in separate incidents after the storm, a dozen tourists said they had been set upon in the streets by blacks, who called them ''whitey'' and told them to get out of town.
Such incidents, combined with the looting, panicked white residents. They armed themselves with shotguns and knives, and overran boats and airplanes to flee the island.
On the mind of virtually every white was the incident that has dominated race relations on St. Croix for 17 years: On the night of Sept. 6, 1972, during a time of heightened racial tensions, eight white Americans, four tourists and four St. Croix residents, were murdered by five black men at the Fountain Valley Golf Club.
 
Troops Annoy Some
''I woke up and heard Rastafarian drums right after the storm,'' said a 40-year-old woman who lived on St. Croix 10 years and was at the airport there on Friday to flee to Miami with her two small children. ''All I could think of was Fountain Valley, and I thought: ''My God! I have to get out of here.''
''White people,'' said Larry Willett, who owns a liquor store on St. Croix, ''saw what some blacks were doing looting the stores and they figured that when the stores were depleted, they would turn to the homes of the whites. That's why the whites were so desperate to get help. All they saw was Fountain Valley.''
The arrival of more than 1,000 Federal troops on Thursday brought relief to white residents, at least for now. But many blacks seemed annoyed.
Some said that blacks were not the only ones to blame for the looting, adding that they had seen white store owners tell people to come in and take what they wanted so the stores could claim a 100 percent loss on their insurance.
Other blacks said they resented the presence of the troops because it reinforced a perception by some whites that they were not accountable to the local black government and that the government of the whites is the Federal Government. Whites responded that many members of the local government are corrupt and ineffectual.
Today, St. Croix remained without electricity and telephones, but the streets were calm. Officials concentrated on cleaning up 40,000 gallons of No. 6 fuel oil that leaked into a St. Croix harbor from a hurricane-damaged 1.6-million-gallon tank.
Meanwhile, officials said that 140 of 200 convicts who escaped from prison during the storm had been recaptured, but they would not say how many people were arrested for looting. There was no indication when Federal troops would be withdrawn.
''I can't tell you what a relief it is to have them here,'' said Scott Hemeon, a white who owns the Danish Manor Hotel, where looters had climbed the walls in an unsuccessful effort to break in. ''I'm in no rush to see the Army go.''

HEADLINE: Venezuela Eases Stance On Its Debts

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 25

 BODY:
Trying to revive negotiations with commercial banks, the Government of Venezuela is dropping its demands for 50 percent debt reduction and offering other proposals it believes will be more acceptable to the banks.
The revised plan may signal a change in the way that deals to reduce debt will be negotiated under the Bush Administration strategy devised by and named for Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady.
The changes were detailed in an interview today with Miguel Rodriguez Fandeo, Venezuela's minister of planning in charge of the debt negotiations, who said an agreement was near on a $600 million interim loan from the banks. The loan would enable Venezuela to meet some interest payments missed over the last several months ''in order to create the atmosphere so the negotiating process could speed up.'' Another party to the negotiations indicated that the agreement on the interim loan could come Tuesday. 
Mr. Rodriguez said the changes were made to ''accommodate the realities of the banks'' - referring to the different legal and accounting rules in different countries. But he also said Venezuela had not changed its position in response to moves in the last couple of weeks by three New York banks to increase their cushion against bad third-world loans. Some bankers have suggested that these moves will make negotiations tougher for debtor nations.
The revision of the debt proposal comes even as the new President of Venezuela, Carlos Andres Perez, continues to criticize the banks. In a speech in Venezuela Friday, he called the negotiations a ''kind of war, of financial war, economic war in which each side wants to use all the missiles it has to defend its interests.''
The President also said, ''Banks are organizations that have electronic brains, that have no heart, that have no soul, that have business to do and transact it in the interest of their shareholders.'' He repeated that criticism today, in somewhat milder terms, in a speech to the United Nations General Assembly.
Instead of a 50 percent debt reduction across the board on its $21 billion in loans from commercial banks - a goal that banks rejected months ago - the Venezuelans will now offer the banks a broad range of options on debt reduction, said Mr. Rodriquez, who is in Washington for the annual meetings of the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank.
 
Choice of Levels
The options, he said, would give the banks a choice of levels of debt reduction, starting at 60 percent and going to below the 35 percent level negotiated in the debt restructing agreement with Mexico, which was the first package concluded under the Brady plan. The plan, announced in March, revised the Administration's debt strategy to put a new emphasis on debt reduction as part of the solution, which also includes new lending, to the $1.3 trillion debt problem of the world's less developed nations.
Mr. Rodriquez also said banks would have an option to make new loans, a proposal not in the original plan.
''We cannot be held hostage by a mighty number,'' Mr. Rodriquez said, acknowledging the difficulty of reaching an agreement on a single debt reduction level. What makes such an agreement difficult, as was discovered in the Mexican negotiations, is that the American banks and foreign banks involved have different priorities and operate under different tax and accounting rules that make agreeing to a set level of debt reduction more difficult.
''We understand the difficulty of imposing one level of discount,'' Mr. Rodriquez said. ''With this type of approach, we can get rid of the lowest common denominator.''
 
Hindering Negotiations
Since the basic Mexican agreement was reached in July, bankers, including John S. Reed, the chairman of Citicorp, have said that setting such an across the board rate hinders the negotiations. Under the Mexican plan, the banks must choose between a 35 percent debt reduction, either by a cut in interest rates or a drop in the face value of their loans, and the lending of new money.
Bankers and Government officials have said each negotiation would be different and that Mexico would not be a standard. In the Philippine negotiations, the second completed under the Brady plan, no set level of deficit reduction was agreed to.
Mr. Rodriguez said that the Venezuelans would still push for as much debt reduction as they can get but that a final agreement could be less than they had sought.
One banker involved in the negotiations with Venezuela, who asked not to be identified, responded positively to the new initiative. ''The clearing up of interest arrears and a flexible approach with respect to the medium-term package will clearly enhance the atmosphere in which the negotiations proceed,'' he said.
 
No Official Reaction
But there was no official reaction from Venezuela's bank advisory committee, which is headed by the Chase Manhattan Bank in New York and negotiates on behalf of the American and foreign banks with loans to Venezuela.
Venezuela was one of the first countries singled out by the Bush Administration as a candidate for debt reduction under the Brady plan. It was felt that attention was needed quickly after bloody riots erupted in February over the imposition of an economic austerity program by the new President.
Until now, the negotiations have been rocky. Both the demand for an across-the-board debt reduction of 50 percent and the bank advisory committee's contention that Venezuela is not in as much economic trouble as it contends have soured the talks. Bankers also did not want to negotiate while Venezuela remained behind on the interest payments on its debt.
Some bankers on the committee contend that Venezuela is just trying to take advantage of the apparent urgency created by riots and capitalize on a chance for debt reduction under the Brady plan.
 
Argentinian Stresses Growth
Separately, Argentina's President, Carlos Saul Menem, said today that his country would honor its foreign debt but needed to grow and develop to be able to pay. ''In order to be able to repay you, we must grow,'' he added in a speech to the Americas Society in New York, Reuters reported.
The burden of Argentina's foreign debt ''is a typical case of co-responsibility,'' he said, adding: ''They lent to us irresponsibly, we happily became indebted and the real interest rates on those loans increased unfairly.''
Mr. Menem is on a five-day trip to the United States to promote his largely successful economic program.

HEADLINE: Dollar Falls As Central Banks Act

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 25

 BODY:
The dollar dropped sharply today after the United States and its economic allies sent a strong message that they wanted a decline.
It ended the day down almost four Japanese yen and more than five pfennigs against the West German mark.
Traders said that the Federal Reserve and other central banks began selling the dollar in an effort to push down its value when trading began in Toyko. That provided the punch needed to back up Saturday's policy statement by the leading economic nations saying the dollar was too high. 
The statement, reaffirming a policy of coordination aimed at stabilizing the dollar, was issued after a meeting in Washington of the Group of Seven - the United States, Japan, West Germany, Britain, France, Italy and Canada.
 
Stocks and Bonds Down
Both stock and bond prices fell on the dollar's decline as traders apparently became concerned that the weaker currency would deter foreign investment in the American markets. The Dow Jones industrial average slipped by 22.42 points, to 2,659.19. And the benchmark 30-year bond fell by more than a point, or more than $10 for each $1,000 face amount of securities. [Pages D10 and D21.] But some traders were quick to predict that the dollar might not remain at the lower level unless the Group of Seven nations made broader economic policy changes, including the lowering of interest rates in the United States.
''They were trying to give a show of force that proved the statement was a strong one,'' said James T. McGroarty, a senior vice president at Greenwich Capital Markets Inc. in Greenwich, Conn.
 
'Market a Little Scared'
''They have gotten the market a little scared,'' said Richard E. Witten, vice president for foreign exchange at Goldman, Sachs & Company in New York. ''The dollar will be soft for a while.''
By the time trading began in New York, the dollar was already down five pfennigs against the mark and three Japanese yen from its close on Friday. By the end of trading, with some more Federal Reserve selling, the dollar slipped somewhat further, to levels that at least for the moment are within or fairly close to their presumed ceilings of 1.90 marks and 140 yen. The dollar ended the day at 1.8992 marks and 142.15 yen, down from 1.9530 marks and 146.05 yen on Friday.
Members of the Group of Seven were happily pointing out that the action of their central banks proved that they meant what they said in their communique and were ready to back it up. The French Finance Minister, Pierre Beregovoy, referring to the dollar's drop, smiled when he told reporters at a breakfast: ''Perhaps today may well clarify matters for you. You saw what happened, so the statement meant what it said.''
Nigel Lawson, Britain's Chancellor of the Exchequer, in a separate comment, said, ''I am perfectly satisfied with the position.''
For the Group of Seven, the drop of the dollar was a small victory after a spring and summer when the nations were unable to restrain its rise. In May the dollar burst though the 1.90-mark and 140-yen ceilings. And the dollar was soon over 2 marks and 150 yen, despite enormous selling by the central banks, including $12 billion by the Federal Reserve in May, June and July.
The dollar fell below the ceilings temporarily in July but then began rising again in August, briefly hitting 2 marks again and nearing 150 yen again. The market had expected the official communique for some time as traders waited to assess how unhappy the group was about the level of the dollar.
''The ministers and governors considered the rise in recent months of the dollar inconsistent with longer-run economic fundamentals,'' said the communique, which was issued during the annual meetings of the International Monetary Fund and World Bank.
''They agreed,'' the communique continued, ''that a rise of the dollar above current levels or an excessive decline could adversely affect prospects for the world economy. In this context, they agreed to cooperate closely in exchange markets.''
The dollar first began to drop when trading opened in Sydney, Australia, which begins while it is still Sunday in New York. Traders said it fell because of the impact of the Group of Seven communique, although there was disagreement over how strong it was. Then in Tokyo, the Federal Reserve, the Bank of Japan and the Bank of Canada were all seen selling dollars aggressively, traders said.
What added to the impact was that the Federal Reserve and the Bank of Canada rarely made such interventions outside of their national markets, which require keeping traders working at night because of the time differential. The last time the Federal Reserve intervened in Tokyo was in January 1988 as part of an aggressive, coordinated effort to support the dollar.
When the foreign exchange markets opened in Europe, traders said that European central banks, including those in West Germany, Belgium, France, Switzerland and Italy, joined in the sales of dollars. Both Mr. McGroarty and Mr. Witten said that the intervention could keep the dollar down for a while. But they suspect that after stepping back for a while traders will begin to see the dollar as a good buy again at these much lower levels. This is when the resolve of the Group of Seven will be tested, they said.
The real move to cap the dollar, traders and economists agree, will require a change in interest rates in the United States and abroad. By lowering American rates and raising them in West Germany and Japan, dollar-denominated assets would become a little less attractive for foreign investors. But such a move is not expected soon because the Federal Reserve will not lower rates until it see signs that the United States economy has slowed down.

HEADLINE: Tiffany's Lure for Japanese Retailer

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Sept. 25

 BODY:
The five models stepped down the runway in Giorgio Armani fashions, swaying to a rock beat, but they finished with a telling flourish: Arrayed around the three-story corporate symbol of the Japanese retailing giant Mitsukoshi, the women dropped their poses and bowed humbly to the audience.
The gestures provided a vivid insight into the strategy of Japan's oldest department store chain. Even with Mitsukoshi's highly visible foray into American retailing with the announcement last week that it will pay $93 million to raise its stake in Tiffany & Company to 13 percent, the retailer remains focused almost exclusively on serving its Japanese audience rather than invading Western markets.
And for good reason. Japan's affluent consumers regard the West as a rich source of exotic goods, and they have gone on something of a buying binge for luxuries. Like the Victorian emporiums that brought the bounty of Britain's colonies to London, Mitsukoshi aims to satisfy those tastes by bringing the spoils of Japan's economic success back home, be they Christian Lacroix and Chanel fashions, live Maine lobsters, Italian furniture or Coach handbags. 
 
Japan Comes First
Whether operating in Japan or through its overseas outlets, Mitsukoshi ''is catering to Japanese customers first,'' said Motoyuki Homma, managing director of the company's international division. ''We regard our overseas business as a way to enhance our image domestically,'' he said.
He added that in the next five years it plans more overseas steps; its agreement with Tiffany allows Mitsukoshi to increase its holding to 19.9 percent, Mr. Homma said. But over that time the company will still receive less than 5 percent of its sales outside Japan.
Mr. Homma said that Mitsukoshi was decidedly lukewarm on the prospect of acquiring all or part of Bloomingdale's, the New York-based department store chain. Teams of American investment bankers have been trying to peddle the company here after its parent company ran into severe financial problems several weeks ago.
 
'Interested in Consistency'
''When we heard it was for sale, our reaction was how had so many changes in ownership affected the corporate identity and the perceptions of the workers and the customers,'' Mr. Homma said. ''We are interested in consistency.''
But the acquisition of the larger interest in Tiffany does have important symbolism for the once-sleepy Mitsukoshi, which operates 17 department stores in Japan and 94 smaller satellite shops, in addition to real estate, consumer finance, wholesale and construction management businesses.
After stumbling badly in the wake of a scandal over its display of Persian art treasures that turned out to be fakes and then a divisive board room battle in 1982, the prestigious, conservative - some say dowdy -department store chain is back on its feet, trying to distinguish itself as the most stately and glamorous of Japan's big retailers.
The 316-year-old merchandiser is spending heavily to remodel and expand its stores to win back its once loyal upper-middle-class clientele.
 
Their Parents' Store
Mitsukoshi is even experimenting with new stores in the more fashionable shopping districts it has usually shunned, to wrest Japan's growing ranks of big-spending young professionals from more innovative retailers. It is a crowd that still regards Mitsukoshi as a place where their parents shop.
For instance, Mitsukoshi plans to build a store in Shinjuku, a brassy district of Tokyo with glass skyscrapers and nightclubs.
That will provide a striking contrast to its flagship store, the sober stone structure where the Armani fashion show was held, which stands in a business district across from the conservative old Bank of Japan building and in the shadows of the imposing Corinthian columns of Mitsui & Company headquarters; the Mitsui group sprang up from Mitsukoshi and the retailer is still a member of the industrial group.
Mitsukoshi Ltd. had net income of 5.932 billion yen, or $41.2 million at the current exchange rate, for the fiscal year that ended Feb. 28. That represented 40.6 percent growth from the previous year, when net income jumped 32.6 percent from the year before that. Sales grew by 10.8 percent in the last fiscal year, to 712.6 billion yen, or $5.6 billion.
That gave Mitsukoshi gross margins of 24.3 percent, average for the industry here and below the top publicly listed retailing performers; Isetan had margins of 27.6 percent and Takashimaya had 25.2 percent, said Mike Allen, retailing analyst with Barclays de Zoete Wedd.
 
'A Whole New Company'
''It's a whole new company today with all the restructuring they've done in the past five years, but they haven't reached their peak profitability yet,'' Mr. Allen said. The company's stock price rose by 10 yen today, to 2,390 yen, or $16.80 a share.
The changing tastes of Japanese consumers are expected to work to the advantage of Mitsukoshi, though. In general, these consumers are increasingly buying Western goods. But they remain discerning, analysts say, looking for status and willing to pay exorbitant sums for goods only when they feel they are of top quality. Consumers here also want to be entertained when they shop, even awed, and Mitsukoshi obliges them.
Mitsukoshi's main store, which generates 44 percent of the company's sales, assaults the senses and provides constant diversions.
 
Customers Are Pampered
The store is filled with novelties, like an antique corkscrew collection featuring one ivory-handled model at $18,000. Elsewhere, a craftsman carved traditional lacquered vases and experts demonstrated Japanese and Western cooking methods. Customers are pampered, constantly offered samples of tasty foods and other items, as well as brochures.
Almost daily there are events in front of the company's corporate symbol, the Statue of Sincerity, in the main atrium, which is surrounded by booths crammed with expensive Western goods. Or one can go to the golf department and buy a set of $9,500 golf clubs, then try them out at an enclosed driving range, where video monitors record one's swing.
There is a pet shop, Shinto shrine and children's play area on the roof and a food hall in the basement with everything from Kobe beefsteaks at $100 a pound to huge salmon being carved at a counter and trays of fresh vegetables. Rows of open buckets against a wall contained 21 varieties of muddy brown miso, the soybean paste used for soups. $35,000 Kimonos The Japanese character is, indeed, unmistakable. Upstairs from the Gucci and Tiffany boutiques, traditionally garbed saleswomen kneel on straw tatami mats, selling custom-made kimonos that can cost $35,000. And in the housewares department, there is row on row of lacquered kitchenware and the low furniture used in traditional homes.
But as those homes fill up with Western goods, Mitsukoshi is prepared to give consumers more of what they want. That is why some speculate that the Tiffany deal will be one of many similar Japanese acquisitions.
An undisclosed Japanese buyer acquired slightly more than 7 percent of Christie's, the auction house, two weeks ago and a Japanese company earlier attempted to buy into the shipper of one of Burgundy's most famous wines. As sake consumption declines and young Japanese consume more fine wine, Japanese companies are acquiring interests in California vineyards to secure supplies.
 
Tiffany's Goods Sought
Mr. Homma said Mitsukoshi wanted to use its new position in Tiffany as leverage to win the right to distribute Tiffany goods elsewhere in Asia. But the principal aim, he added, was to find more opportunities for retailing the silver, gold and bejeweled items in this country.
Even Mitsukoshi's growing number of outlets overseas - in places like New York, where there is a branch at 465 Park Avenue at 57th Street, as well as Honolulu, Paris, Rome and Munich - will continue to aim at Japanese customers. The strategy, Mr. Homma said, repeating himself to emphasize the point, is to cultivate Japanese living temporarily overseas as long-term customers when they return home.

HEADLINE: G.M. to Start '90 Model Year By Offering Large Discounts

BYLINE: By DORON P. LEVIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, Sept. 25

 BODY:
In a surprising move intended to forestall a drop in retail sales and to spur its dealers to stock cars more aggressively, the General Motors Corporation today applied substantial discounts to its 1990 car and truck lines through cash rebates and subsidized financing.
G.M.'s action, which excludes its Cadillac models, marks the first time the No. 1 auto maker will begin its model year on Monday with a wide range of discounts. Its decision is evidence of widespread apprehension that automotive demand may be softer this year and that competition for sales may be tougher.
G.M. dealers and other franchise dealers, citing high financing costs, have been saying for several months that they are less willing to risk stocking large inventories of cars and trucks unless they have a better idea of prices and market conditions. 
Unofficially, some G.M. executives acknowledge that factory orders for 1990 vehicles have been soft. The company had temporarily idled plants for up to three weeks in Arlington, Tex.; Pontiac, Mich.; Linden, N.J.; Wilmington, Del.; Moraine, Ohio, and Shreveport, La., because of slow dealer orders and swollen inventories.
''We wanted to let our buyers of 1990 models know that there was going to be an incentive program,'' said Robert E. Coletta, the general sales manager of G.M.'s Buick division. Last October, many buyers stayed away from showrooms anticipating rebates, which came at the end of the month, he said.
 
Chrysler and Ford Plans
The Chrysler Corporation and the Ford Motor Company declined to match G.M.'s discounts immediately, although the analysts predicted that the auto makers would soon follow. The companies have rebates on their 1989 models. Chrysler has announced rebates on its 1990 midsize pickup trucks.
G.M.'s rebates, ranging up to $1,000, affect most Oldsmobile, Pontiac and Buick car models and several Chevrolet car models, including the new Lumina sedan, which was introduced this spring. The GMC truck division is offering rebates of up to $1,250. Cadillac was apparently excluded because of robust sales that pushed the division 6.3 percent ahead of last year.
Ronald Glantz, an analyst for Montgomery Securities in San Francisco, said G.M.'s share of the American vehicle market had been eroding for several months, reaching a low of 33.7 percent in August. That is one reason why G.M. initiated the rebates instead of waiting for its smaller competitors do so, he said.
''They need more share, and their dealers have the worst inventory overhang,'' Mr. Glantz said.
A significant omission from the list of discounted products is General Motors' new mini-van, which is being sold as the Oldsmobile Silhouette, the Chevrolet Lumina APV and, later in the year, as the Pontiac Transport. The futuristic-looking vans are G.M.'s first true mini-vans and are meant to compete with Chrysler's successful mini-van.
 
Paying for Requirements
The Big Three have raised the retail prices on 1990 models by 3 percent to 5 percent, and more in some cases, partly to reflect the cost of mandated safety requirements like driver-side air bags. With the rebate, the 1990 prices may be less than the 1989 retail prices, although the 1989 prices were also discounted for much of the year.
The G.M. incentives are almost certain to encourage some buyers and dealers to buy G.M. cars, but the extent to which they can continue the strength of recent sales is unclear.
Mark Herrmann, a Buick dealer in Yonkers, said he had ordered about a hundred 1990 Buicks, compared with 150 to 175 cars at this time last year. But he said the new rebates did not influence his order.
''I'm going to wait and see what my customers do,'' Mr. Herrmann said. ''If they come in and start buying, then I'll order more cars.''
A Ford spokesman said the company would not announce any rebates for 1990 models until Oct. 4, when the rebates on the 1989 models expire. The auto maker is offering up to $1,500 off on various models. The spokesman acknowledged that 1990 orders from dealers were also soft and had resulted in scattered shutdowns of assembly plants.
Ford said today that it would idle its truck plant in Louisville, Ky., for a week and its plant in Lorain, Ohio, that assembles the Ford Thunderbird and Mercury Cougar for two weeks, both beginning on Oct. 2.

HEADLINE: Car Sales in Mid-September Surged Forward by 10.2%

BYLINE: By DORON P. LEVIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, Sept. 25

 BODY:
Sales of cars built in the United States surged by 10.2 percent in mid-September, as buyers continued to buy 1989 models to beat price increases for the 1990 model year.
The seasonally adjusted annual sales rate for the period of Sept. 11 to Sept. 20 was 8.4 million. The 1989 model year ends this weekend and the 1990 models officially go on sale on Monday. 
''People were out getting the best deals they could,'' said Wendy Beale Needham, an analyst for Smith Barney, Harris, Upham & Company in New York. She said the General Motors Corporation, whose sales reached 106,780, or 11.2 percent higher for the period, appeared to increase its market share at the expense of its domestic rivals.
G.M.'s market share had been sliding throughout the summer.
 
Rebates Are Planned
General Motors today announced rebates for many of its 1990 cars and trucks, and analysts have predicted that the Ford Motor Company and the Chrysler Corporation will follow, leading to widespread discounts.
''If one manufacturer is offering rebates and the others aren't,'' said Harvey E. Heinbach of Merrill Lynch and Company in New York, ''the ones who aren't will soon hear from their dealers who will scream for something competitive.''
The seven domestic automobile makers sold 211,966 cars, compared with 170,988 cars in the corresponding period last year. This year, the mid-September period had nine selling days, compared with eight last year.
Ford sold 52,347 cars, up by 2.6 percent, while Chrysler sold 28,098, up by 2.1 percent.
The Honda Motor Company, which has opened a second American assembly plant, increased its sales by 65 percent, to 11,033 cars. The Toyota Motor Corporation raised its sales by more than threefold, to 5,445. Toyota has been increasing production of its Camry model in Georgetown, Ky.
The Mitsubishi Motor Corporation reported sales of 1,422 in its first mid-September sales report, reflecting the production of its Eclipse sports coupe by Diamond Star Motors, a venture with Chrysler in Normal, Ill.
 
Sales Drop Expected
For the first 20 days of the month, domestic car sales were up by 5.5 percent compared with a year ago. Sales for the month could finish strong, Mrs. Needham of Smith Barney said, but she said she expected a drop at the beginning of October. She cited falling indicators of consumer confidence in her forecast.
The sales of light trucks for the period were also robust. Ford, General Motors, Chrysler and the Nissan Motor Company sold 129,570 light trucks, up by 17.1 percent from a year ago. G.M. showed the biggest increase of the period, with sales of 60,929, compared with 37,095. General Motors has just introduced a new mini-van, which is considered a truck.
