HEADLINE: Interest Widens in Bid To Buy Bloomingdale's

BYLINE: By ISADORE BARMASH

 BODY:
Marvin S. Traub, the chairman and chief executive officer of Bloomingdale's, has received expressions of substantial interest from foreign retailers and other investors in his quest to buy the chain he has worked for for almost 40 years.
Financial executives familiar with the process said substantial interest in investing in Mr. Traub's effort was coming from both large Japanese and European retail chains. But no specific names were mentioned by either Mr. Traub yesterday or financial sources familiar with the situation.
''Bloomingdale's is seen as perhaps the epitome of the best in American retailing,'' one investment banker said, ''and foreign retailers are showing their excitement in investing substantial sums in it.'' He noted, for example, the 10 percent stake bought by the Japanese retail chain Mitsukoshi Ltd. last week in Tiffany & Company. 
The Campeau Corporation, owners of the 17-store chain, put Bloomingdale's up for sale earlier this month when the company began experiencing a severe cash squeeze. The store is one of the nation's most innovative retailers in fashion and design presentation and had been expected to bring as much as $2 billion.
 
Weeklong Financial Crisis
Between the early September announcement that Bloomingdale's would be sold and this week, as the prospectus for the sale is being prepared to show serious bidders, however, Campeau experienced a weeklong financial crisis that may have undercut the price that Bloomingdale's may get. Analysts now expect it may bring only slightly more than $1 billion.
Mr. Traub and his backers will have to compete for Bloomingdale's with at least 10 other individuals or groups that have expressed their interest in the company to the First Boston Corporation, Campeau's financial advisers. Among them are the Crown American Corporation, the fifth-largest mall developer; the Limited Inc.; the Maus family group of Switzerland, which recently bought Carson, Pirie, Scott, the Chicago department store, and Joseph E. Brooks, chairman of Ann Taylor, in conjunction with Merrill Lynch.
For his part, Mr. Traub remained carefully noncommittal in an interview yesterday, saying, however, that ''a lot of people seem to be interested.''
Asked particularly about reports of Japanese and European interest, he replied, ''Frankly, there has been interest shown from all over the world.''
 
Investment Firms Consulted
In addition to other retailers, Mr. Traub has been looking for advice and support for his bid from certain investment houses. A spokesman for the Blackstone Group, an investment banking house that often invests in mergers and has a substantial buyout fund, confirmed yesterday that the firm had talked with Mr. Traub but added, ''Everything is in an early stage and nothing can be considered final.''
He indicated that Blackstone was considering a dual or separate role as financial adviser and/or investor. Blackstone's $800 million buyout fund could furnish some of the financing, he said. He said he understood that the investment firm of Drexel Burnham Lambert would probably be involved as an adviser.
The Wall Street Journal reported yesterday that Mr. Traub was talking to the Blackstone Group and Drexel Burnham.
Since Mr. Traub announced his own intention to develop a buying group for Bloomingdale's, the response has been ''very satisfying.'' He added: ''A substantial number of people have indicated their interest in investing in the leveraged buyout group. Many of our employees - though I don't want to seem immodest - came to me to express their enthusiasm because they respect me and they feel that only good things can happen if we take over.''
 
Support of Suppliers
Suppliers, too, demonstrated their support in a Monday meeting, he said. He declined to elaborate. But Mr. Traub, widely considered one of the country's best merchants, is now in a completely new arena where his decisions on financial criteria - trying to generate the highest bid for the 17 Bloomingdale's stores - mean more than his opinions on designer fashions or Italian furniture.
Mr. Traub plans to propose a leveraged buyout - that is, using the assets of the company to borrow to finance the management-led acquisition bid - at a time when analysts have said that such debt-laden transactions in retailing have appeared to dampen risk-taking and innovation. But he is convinced that this will not happen if he succeeds in winning Bloomingdale's. ''If we allowed that, it just wouldn't be Bloomingdale's,'' the 64-year-old executive said.
Accordingly, he said he was pursuing both short- and long-term retailing plans for the store. These include the recent opening of ''Vive la France,'' a seven-week French promotional event in all 17 stores; the imminent opening of the first Museum of the Louvre shop at 59th Street; the opening of four new stores and carrying out a plan to open two or three small stores in Moscow.

HEADLINE: Stocks Fall Broadly as Dow Drops 22.42

BYLINE: By PHILLIP H. WIGGINS

 BODY:
Stock prices plunged across a broad front yesterday on the heels of a steep decline in the dollar and in bond prices.
The Dow Jones industrial average fell 22.42, to 2,659.19, after being down as many as 28 points during the afternoon.
Declines on the New York Stock Exchange led advances by 1,031 to 445. Volume on the Big Board continued to be slow, with 121.1 million shares changing hands. On Friday, 133.4 million shares were traded. This was the lowest volume since July 3 when 68.9 million shares were traded. 
In the broader market, the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index dropped 2.82 points, to 344.23, while the New York Stock Exchange composite index slipped 1.45 points, to 191.28.
Key economic reports to be released this week include durable goods orders for August, which are scheduled to come out today and leading economic indicators for August, which will be released on Friday.
After meeting in Washington over the weekend, the Group of Seven determined that the dollar had become too strong. This spurred a precipitous decline in the United States currency and in fixed-income securities.
''Unusually harsh G-7 statements to the effect that a strong U.S. dollar is inconsistent with long-run economic fundamentals caused a crumbling cascade of dollar-denominated securities all day,'' said Robert H. Stovall, president of Stovall/21st Advisers in New York.
Yesterday, several central banks sold dollars, as did the Swiss and others.
''With the Fed apparently on hold and the German central bank muttering about rising interest rates, stocks had little reason to rally,'' Mr. Stovall said.
Transports were adversely affected by Transportation Secretary Samuel K. Skinner's comments over the weekend that he has second thoughts about foreign ownnership of American airlines and about the airline merger trend in general. This pushed the transports down by 20.09 points, to 1,428.17.
The UAL Corporation, parent of United Airlines, dropped 7 1/8, to 274, on news that the Department of Transportation is seeking details on the financial structure of the carrier's planned buyout. AMR, parent of American Airlines, dropped 1 3/8, to 77 7/8; Delta Airi Lines fell 1 point, to 70, and USAir was unchanged at 46 7/8.
The market's most notable strength came in Japanese stocks. Kyocera rose 5 1/2, to 79 1/4; Matsushita gained 4 1/2, to 164, and Hitachi added 2 7/8, to 111 1/4.
The group gained strength from rumors that a Japanese company might be holding talks with Columbia Pictures. Sony, which declined comment, was the leading candidate mentioned. The stock rose 1 3/8, to 59 1/2.
Columbia Pictures Entertainment jumped 5 1/4, to 26 3/8, as was the most active Big Board stock on a volume of 6.2 million shares.
On the American Stock Exchange, the Amex market value index edged up 0.02, to 381.40, but in over-the-counter trading the Nasdaq composite index slipped 1.36 points, to 466.71.

HEADLINE: Economists Are Optimistic

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 25

 BODY:
The economy will continue to grow through 1990, forestalling a recession until at least 1991, top business economists said today.
Nearly two-thirds of the 72 forecasting experts surveyed by the National Association of Business Economists said they thought a recession was not likely before 1991. A third of them said a recession would not arrive until 1992 or later.
The nation's economy is enjoying record peacetime growth, which will be seven years old in December. The longest expansion ever occurred in the February 1961-December 1969 period, which included the Vietnam War.
The survey was presented at the association's annual meeting in San Francisco. A copy was made available here.
In its previous quarterly survey last May, the economists group found that 63 percent of the respondents expected the next recession next year. Now, only 37 percent foresee a serious downturn by 1990.
The survey responses did imply a slowdown in economic growth in the last half of this year, however. The forecasters' median growth rate for the gross national product in the final three months of 1989 is 2.1 percent. That compares with a 3.1 percent growth in the first half of the year. The survey found a substantial majority expected growth to continue at about the same 2.1 percent rate throughout 1990.
At the same time, the economists scaled back their inflation forecast to 5 percent for the October-December quarter, down from 5.3 percent in the May survey. The median forecast for inflation in 1990 is 4.3 percent.

* Federal National Mortgage Association, also known as Fannie Mae, is offering $700 million in real estate investment conduit securities, or Remics, through Goldman, Sachs & Co. Yields range from 8.94 percent to 10.35 percent, with spreads over Treasury securities of from 60 basis points to 205. HEADLINE: High Prices In Japan Cited

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: TOKYO, Sept. 25

 BODY:
Despite their higher per-capita income, the Japanese have less purchasing power than consumers in other advanced industrial countries because Japan's prices are so high, the nation's Economic Planning Agency said today.
In an annual report, the agency estimated that prices in Tokyo were 39 percent higher than those in New York and 47 percent higher than those in Hamburg, West Germany. Its conclusion was based on a survey of prices of 400 selected items in the three cities.
The survey showed that rent and food that is subject to import restrictions were particularly costly in Tokyo, but consumer durables like automobiles were not much more expensive than in the other two cities, it said.
The agency said prices of imported brand items, which should have fallen as the Japanese yen rose in value against other currencies, had remained very high relative to their prices in New York, Paris and Dusseldorf, West Germany.

HEADLINE: Careers;
Updating The Training Of M.B.A.'s

BYLINE: By Elizabeth M. Fowler

 BODY:
GRADUATE schools of business have been struggling with change in recent years. Their administrators face problems such as how to make courses more globally oriented, what to do about foreign languages, and how to instill values like leadership and creativity in students.
''Everybody says we must change,'' Peter Veruki, a top placement officer for Vanderbilt University's graduate school of business, said last week. He was an organizer of a forum for placement officers and 80 recruiters from top companies held at the Citicorp headquarters. 
''It was agreed that we have got to make courses more relevant to what international executives will be doing in the 1990's,'' he said.''The problem is partly our tenure system, because faculty have to approve curriculum changes, and often their specialty is not globally oriented,''
H. J. Zoffer, dean of the University of Pittsburgh's graduate school of business, who was in New York last week to raise funds, said that it was not ''faculty unwillingness as much as it is the state of knowledge.'' He noted, for example, that there are not enough good international business textbooks.
Bruce Hutton, professor of marketing at the University of Denver's graduate school of business who was in New York for a meeting, said, ''Integration is the key for us - the weaving of international aspects into all courses.''
As for students, Mr. Veruki said, ''We must admit more broadly educated ones who have studied at least one foreign language and taken courses in international relations along with lots of history and courses in other cultures.''
But Dr. Zoffer said business schools should not teach language courses because curriculums were already crowded. ''We cannot make a graduate school a remedial place,'' he added. But he said that internships abroad might give students foreign language skills. He said he hoped to organize such internships at a West German business school.
Dr. Hutton said one approach at Denver had been for students to learn about foreign cultures from foreign students on campus.
There was a consensus at the forum that corporations should distinguish among the different types of M.B.A.'s - accountants, marketers, financial specilaists, economists and entrepreneurs.
''Corporations'' Mr. Veruki said, ''must do a better job of identifying M.B.A.'s they hire, because training and aspirations are so different.''
Dr. Zoffer said that with more than 70,000 M.B.A.'s graduating each year ''quality control is impossible.''
Also, Mr. Veruki said, students must realize that when some companies, like the Exxon Corporation and the General Motors Corporation, hire M.B.A.'s, they hope the new employees will spend their careers with the company. But investment banking and consulting firms often want instant traders and deal makers, and ''they do not have the cradle-to-grave philosophy,'' he said.
Such companies tend to be flat organizations, he explained, which do not have a hierarchy of managers. ''They want M.B.A.'s who can hit the ground quickly and start running with the ball,'' he said.
Just as companies differ, so do business schools. ''I urge companies to come to our campus and learn firsthand what is unique,'' Mr. Veruki said.
Another need, Mr. Veruki said, is to find more ways to develop students' leadership and managerial skills. ''We use the case method, teamwork and internships,'' he said. All help, but we need more.''
Dr. Hutton said that Denver had recently adopted survival training techniques - a method that was tried for a short time at Carnegie Mellon University in Pittsburgh. The supervised wilderness training is believed to promote teamwork and creative problem solving.
So far, three groups of Denver M.B.A. students and professors have gone through such programs, which included activities like rock climbing.
''One test involves leading a blindfolded person for a 30-minute hike, including crossing a stream on guy wires, Dr. Hutton said. The team is not allowed to speak during the hike.
The idea, Dr. Hutton explained, is that early in the M.B.A. program students are learning group skills. ''Also, they get to know the faculty in a different way from the classroom experience,'' he said.

HEADLINE: $660 MILLION PLAN OF AID TO HUNGARY AND POLAND URGED

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BRUSSELS, Sept. 26

 BODY:
The European Community's executive commission today proposed a $660 million economic aid program for Hungary and Poland next year, more than double the total already pledged by the industrial democracies this year.
The response from the 24 major non-Communist economic powers gathered here to decide how to encourage evolution in Eastern Europe was overwhelmingly positive. And many officials made it clear that the amount, which would come from all 24 countries, could be greatly exceeded.
''The 24 met today at a decisive moment in the process of democratization and economic reforms in Poland and Hungary,'' a communique issued at the end of the meeting concluded, ''but it remains fragile.'' 
 
A Floor, Not a Ceiling
The United States delegate, Deputy Assistant Secretary of State Ralph Johnson, told the group that the United States regarded the European Community proposal as a floor rather than a ceiling and that it was looking at additional commitments beyond the $169 million in food and economic aid that President Bush has already promised Poland, according to a spokesman for the community. The United States has also pledged $25 million to Hungary.
As the 24 nations met to discuss aid to Eastern Europe, in Washington the Bush Administration was reported to be working on a new aid package for Poland that would rely on the International Monetary Fund. [Page A13.] The discussions in Brussels today show that there is no longer any basic doubt among the Western governments that it is in their interest to help these Eastern European countries throw off the shackles of state-run Communist economic and political systems, several NATO diplomats here said. Bigger Issue: What Is Europe? Much more fundamental questions are coming to the fore instead, they said. Many of them go to the heart of what Europe is, how much longer its division will last and whether the United States will take the lead in devising a comprehensive Western strategy to deal with the implications of economic and political change in the Soviet Union, Hungary and Poland.
The questions concern the NATO alliance as much as the European Community, which was asked by the United States, Japan and the five other participants in the economic summit in Paris in July to coordinate the aid effort. Food aid already pledged to Poland has now totaled $298 million, the community spokesman, Claus-Dieter Ehlermann, said today.
For next year, the community proposed a package of aid including direct investment, agricultural equipment, management training programs and environmental protection measures, all on the condition that Poland and Hungary do away with the state trading monopolies and market controls imposed during 40 years of Communism. $660 Million Suggested The community suggested $660 million worth of financing for the projects, half of it to be contributed by the community and its 12 member states, and half by the 12 other participating countries.
At the meeting, Sweden, Denmark, Norway and Finland pledged development projects and credits totaling about $121 million, mainly for projects to control air and water pollution from Central Europe that are killing trees and befouling the seas of Scandinavia. Iceland pledged $330,000, most of it in herring.
''Help for Polish reforms is not just an economic issue,'' said a Polish Government appeal to the 24 that was handed over to the community's commission on Sunday. ''It will determine also the success of political changes which are of importance to Europe as a whole.''
The appeal, like those reportedly made by Polish representatives at the International Monetary Fund meetings in Washington this week, did not have a price tag. It was a vast catalogue of wishes for new technologies for eliminating pollution and for training plant managers, economists, bankers and teachers to transform its economy. #1992 Could Be Anticlimax Things are moving so fast in the Soviet Union and parts of Eastern Europe, a NATO diplomat said today, that the removal of remaining trade barriers between community members in 1992 as envisaged by the Single European Act could turn out to be almost an anticlimax.
Jacques Delors, the community's President, spoke in ambitious terms today to a group of European dignitaries in Strasbourg. ''The changes in Central and Eastern Europe and in the Soviet Union, the acceleration of European integration since the Single Act, all allow us to pursue the objective of overcoming the division of the old continent,'' he said.
After the election of a Parliament with a non-Communist majority in Poland and the opening of Hungary's border with Austria, those words no longer sound as visionary as President Bush's did after the last summit meeting, in May, when he called upon the allies and the Soviet Union ''to heal Europe's tragic division, to help Europe become whole and free.''
NATO's North Atlantic Council meeting here on Wednesday will be devoted to change in Eastern Europe, diplomats said, because it could create as many problems for the Alliance as opportunities, unless the change was managed.
 
Alliances' Reasons for Being
One NATO official, asserting that the goal of a verifiable conventional arms agreement reducing Soviet and American troops in Europe to 275,000 on each side now seemed to be something that could be achieved by the end of next year, said the problem now was to convince public opinion in the member countries that NATO would still have a reason for being - and the Warsaw Pact as well.
''If the negotiations are successful, they will also produce a new security system - with extensive verification measures, controls over how many tanks can be where, exchanges of information on troop strength and deployments - the management of which will be in the hands of the two alliances,'' he said.
NATO officials concede, however, that it will be next to impossible to get West Germany to agree to go forward with a modernized short-range nuclear missile system for the alliance, as the United States and Britain proposed earlier, if the troop reduction talks succeed.
And West Germany's intentions for aid to Poland remained unclear at today's meeting, although Chancellor Helmut Kohl is expected to bring a sizable package with him when he visits Warsaw later this year. ''The West German delegation referred to negotiations under way with Poland and Hungary and gave a positive reception to the plan of action outlined,'' the community spokesman said.
The Organization of Economic Cooperation and Development, to which all 24 countries belong, the International Monetary Fund, the World Bank and the major countries holding third-world debt all attended as observers. Poland's foreign debt amounts to $39 billion.

HEADLINE: After Hurricane, Relief Gives Way to Despair

BYLINE: By PETER APPLEBOME, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CHARLESTON, S.C., Sept. 26

 BODY:
Only today did Debra Deas stop and think about what comes next. After the worst week of her life, the future is the most frightening part of all.
Her uninsured mobile home was crushed like a matchbox by Hurricane Hugo. She has no work because the houses she cleans have been destroyed. Her husband has no work because the commercial fishing boats he works on no longer exist. And their four sons do not know where they will go to school.
''I guess it just hit me,'' she said. ''I can't describe how sad all this is. I have no idea what we'll do.'' 
Like a photograph slowly developing before people's eyes, the full extent of the devastation from the hurricane has finally become clear, leaving most of South Carolina and much of North Carolina staggered and dazed.
The storm did vast damage, from the graceful streets of Charleston to the seaside arcades and T-shirt shops of Myrtle Beach to towns a hundred miles from the shore, like Sumter, Camden and Bishopville. Its victims ranged from Charleston's upper crust living in Colonial-era homes to the working class and the poor living in small houses, apartments, trailers and rickety shacks.
Officials say the economic toll of what may be the nation's costliest storm could take a decade to overcome. Estimates for South Carolina alone have ranged from $1 billion to $3 billion.
The human cost to one of the nation's most evocative cities and one of its poorest states will never be known. But it is clear that rather than a short-term catastrophe, the storm will have a lasting impact here.
 
Symbol of Nature's Wrath
For those close to it, it has already become an indelible symbol of nature's wrath, much like the storm that virtually destroyed Galveston and killed 6,000 people in 1900 or Hurricane Camille's rampage across the Gulf Coast in 1969 that killed 256 people and did $3.8 billion in damage.
''There are so many people that have lost a part of themselves, and for them this is something that will last forever,'' said Bob Linville, who just six weeks ago became Mayor of Folly Beach, a barrier island community that took the full force of the storm's fury when its 135-mile-per-hour winds hit late Thursday night. ''If we ever saw anything to top old Hugo, we would be gone from the face of the earth. We would be nothing.''
For many people, certainly for most of them in Charleston and the surrounding South Carolina Low Country, it is far too early to think about Hugo's place in history.
 
Still Without the Basics
Much of the area remains without electricity and running water. Thousands of volunteers have descended on the city bringing food and supplies, but life remains a obstacle course of appliances that do not work, traffic lights that hang uselessly in clogged intersections, leaking roofs with giant oak trees resting on top of them and debris-strewn streets.
In Charlotte, N.C., more than 200 miles from here, about half the city remains without power and National Guardsmen carrying M-16 rifles are still guarding the streets against looters. President Bush today declared Mecklenburg County, which includes Charlotte, as well as three other nearby counties disaster areas. Twelve counties in South Carolina had already received disaster designations.
The continuing force of the storm as it moved away from the coast is still a source of shock to people.
In Sumter, 100 miles inland, 109-m.p.h. winds destroyed houses and businesses. Damage was estimated at $100 million in the town, which had only expected to get the last gusts of a dying storm.
''I was talking to someone who lived in Florida for a number of years, and he said he had been through a number of hurricanes, but he had never seen anything like this,'' said Talmadge Tobias, assistant city manager of Sumter.
 
Loss in Historic Areas
But because of the storm's path through historic Charleston and some of the most popular islands nearby, the toll goes far beyond dollar figures for damages. Although most of the city's most historic buildings survived, the storm's powerful winds brought with it extraordinary sorrow as well as pain.
''There's no doubt in my mind that our greatest loss is in terms of beauty,'' said Bob James, who lives in one of the historic homes downtown. ''We've lost almost everything that gave me such great joy of living in the Low Country.''
While the cleanup continues, people throughout the region are slowly turning to the long-term questions of putting their lives back together.
For some, the problems are so large they do not know where to start.
Mr. and Mrs. Deas spent the night of the storm in a shelter and have been staying with relatives since then. But now they and their four sons, aged 9, 11, 14 and 16, have to find a place of their own.
''Right now, all we have is the clothing on our back,'' Mr. Deas said.
 
In the Shelter
At a shelter nearby, Laura Oliphant, sat looking blearily into space while her 81-year-old mother, Irene Clark, rested in a cot reading.
Mrs. Oliphant, who lives with her mother, daughter and two grandchildren, said they cannot stay in their house because of water damage. She does not know when she will be about to return to her work as a seamstress because her shop, the Stitching Post, was also damaged. She has insurance on both, but does not know what will be covered, when she will get the money and who will be available to help her rebuild. Nor does she know when her daughter will be able to get back to work.
''When it started to rain yesterday, it felt like the bottom had fallen out,'' she said, as she smoked a cigarette and surveyed the half-filled shelter. ''You don't really know where to start. You don't know what you can do.''
For the poor the burdens are more crushing still.
At the same shelter as Mrs. Oliphant was Frank Green, who is blind and 89 years old and does not know when he can go back to his home in a Charleston housing project. Andrea Taylor sat nearby. She had been living in a homeless shelter that was badly damaged in the hurricane and working in a convenience store. Now she has one baby with her and another in a hospital and does not know if she will be able to return to her job.
Most homeowners expect that insurance will cover their losses. But so far, few residents have been able to reach their insurance agents or get estimates. And many without insurance are trying to figure out if they will be able to rebuild.
 
Once a Landmark, Now Rubble
Tommy Browne lived in a 60-year-old frame house facing the ocean on Folly Beach, where houses blown off their foundations now sit in the middle of what were island roads. Just down the street was the Atlantic House restaurant, once a landmark eating place, now a pile of rubble.
Mr. Browne's house is still largely intact, but it was blown off its foundation and knocked back 10 feet. Mr. Browne, who had no wind or flood insurance, estimates the house will need $20,000 to $40,000 in repairs.
''I guess I'm going to go to the bank, and see if they'll give me a loan,'' he said. ''If they won't, I guess I'll try to do it myself, which is probably impossible.''
Today brought more bad news for island dwellers. Many residents of the exclusive Isle of Palms and Sullivans Island returned to their homes for the first time.
There was serious property damage on Sullivans Islands and large portions of the Isle of Palms were all but destroyed by the storm. On the Isle of Palms, returning homeowners found pools of raw sewage and piles of splintered wood where stylish island homes once stood.
 
Trees Still Rest on Roofs
The concerns about rebuilding are not limited to those who lived near the coast. On Highway 378, for example, which runs east to west through the state, trees still rest on rooftops from Conway near Myrtle Beach to Columbia. Parts of Columbia, the state capital, are still without power.
For Pavrin Patel, who bought the Sharolyn Motel near Sumter two years ago, the damage was hard to fathom.
He said his 20-unit motel, valued at $300,000, was a total loss.
The story was the same across much of the eastern part of South Carolina, where many victims are poor and uninsured.
State officials said the storm had thrown a quarter of a million people out of work. Some will be back relatively soon as businesses reopen. Others could be out for months or longer, officials said.
Governor Campbell asked Federal officials for help in putting the unemployed back to work rebuilding the areas destroyed by the storm.
 
Damage to Tourism Industry
The damage to the state's tourism business, however, provides more long-term concerns.
Tourism is now the second largest business in South Carolina, behind textiles. It is centered on the 300 miles of coastline, of which about half was badly damaged in the storm.
Tourism provides 98,000 jobs, $4.7 billion in direct spending and $7 billion to $8 billion in overall economic impact, officials say.
''We're badly wounded, but we're not in danger of death,'' said Robert Liming, director of the tourism division of the South Carolina Department of Parks, Recreation and Tourism.
Some experts estimated that the storm could cut the business by about 10 percent next year.
''A 10 percent change would have $150 to $160 million impact on the state's output,'' said Douglas Woodward, research economist for the University of South Carolina. ''That is aside from property damage and everything else. The tourist dollar, in turn, would have a ripple effect on the rest of the state. We are in for rough times, rough seas I guess, for several years.''
He said part of the problem was that the state must now use its resources to get back to where it was, instead of where it wants to be. South Carolina ranks 42d nationally in per capita income.
''The state obviously is going to have to spend a lot of money on recovery instead of enhancement,'' Mr. Woodward said. ''For example, they need to build a new theater to handle the Spoleto Festival. It is going to be some years before they are going to be able to look at that project.''
The damage could hurt in other ways.
In Charleston, for example, $50 million of the $125 million for the school district comes from property taxes. If destroyed buildings are taken off the tax rolls and damaged ones are assessed at lower levels, it could cripple financing for local schools, officials warned.

HEADLINE: Excerpts From the Speech by Shevardnadze Before the General Assembly

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from the address yesterday by Foreign Minister Eduard A. Shevardnadze of the Soviet Union to the United Nations General Assembly. The speech was translated by a United Nations interpreter and recorded by The New York Times through the facilities of Cable News Network. . . . Yesterday we listened with close attention to the speech of President George Bush of the United States, a statement which in our view contained a number of very important and interesting ideas.
As always during these days of the General Assembly's regular session, representatives of the world community have together been recreating a panorama of the last year in the life of mankind. Its overriding idea is that of peace and security. Its ideal composition is harmony of universal human values and national interests.
In making our own national contribution to this grand mural, each of us, I am sure, wants it to become a part of an organic and unitary whole. Unfortunately, in some places the overall composition is still marred by cracks that impair its integrity. 
 
U.S.-Soviet Dialogue
I would permit myself to touch upon the U.S.-Soviet dialogue only because I am convinced that its importance far transcends the frontiers of the two states.
In the view of the Soviet leadership, this is not a kind of privilege, but a clearly understood responsibility toward the world community. Therefore my account of the results of our talks with President Bush and Secretary of State Baker is my report to you.
These talks demonstrated increasing awareness by both sides of the need to cooperate for the benefit of mankind and growing confidence that such cooperation, such interaction is possible.
Extraordinary efforts at the highest level will be needed to attain the goal of concluding treaties. I am referring particularly to the preparation of the agreement on 50 percent reductions in strategic offensive arms.
We have proposed to the U.S. side options for resolving the key issues in that agreement, the antiballistic missile treaty and space and long-range sea-launched cruise missiles. Our partners have accommodated us on mobile I.C.B.M.'s and positions on other outstanding problems have become closer to each other.
In our view, by the time the summit meeting is held next year, late spring or early summer, we may have passed the last turn on the road toward a treaty reducing strategic offensive arms. These are real prospects.
 
The Bush Proposal
The protocols to the 1974 and 1976 treaties on nuclear explosions could also be signed at the summit so that they could enter into force very soon after that. We are confident that the summit will give powerful momentum to the talks on major reductions in armed forces and conventional arms in Europe.
We welcome the proposal concerning chemical weapons put forward yesterday by President Bush. Generally consistent with the initiatives of the Soviet leadership, it indicates to us that we share the same desire to rid mankind of these barbaric weapons.
The Soviet Union is ready, together with the United States, to go further and assume mutual obligations prior to the conclusion of a multilateral convention; cease the production of chemical weapons, as we've already done - I'm referring here to binary weapons - and on a bilateral basis radically reduce or completely destroy Soviet and U.S. chemical weapons, viewing it as a step toward the global destruction of chemical weapons; renounce the use of chemical weapons under any circumstances, and institute rigorous verification of the cessation of production and elimination of chemical warfare agents.
I think there is no need to state how beneficial all this would be for the overall world climate.
 
The Nuclear Threat
I would say that the world community has as yet no reason for complacency or euphoria. The nuclear threat has only been reduced by the U.S.-Soviet treaty eliminating intermediate- and shorter-range missiles. We believe that reliance on nuclear weapons serves the national interests of none. It is also an obstacle to more democratic international relations. Only the elimination of nuclear capabilities would help us to attain real security.
The advocates of nuclear deterrence do not believe that this will be possible in the foreseeable future. They respond with concepts of so-called minimum nuclear deterrence. . . .
But first we have to define what we mean by minimum nuclear deterrence and what capabilities should be considered sufficient. The Soviet Union proposes that these questions be discussed at a meeting of representatives of nuclear powers and the states on whose territories nuclear arms are placed.
What is the way out of the vicious circle? it lies in asserting glasnost and openness and building a wide-ranging infrastructure of pervasive verification . . . To that end the Soviet Union is proposing that all nuclear powers conclude multilateral agreements on measures to reduce the risk of outbreak of nuclear war. Detailed discussions to work out such an agreement could be held in consultations among the permanent members of the Security Council.
 
Nuclear Testing
Guided by its obligations to the world community, the Soviet Union has revised its nuclear testing program by reducing the number and yield of explosions.
Let me point out, however, that the efforts of one state are not sufficient for a comprehensive solution to this problem. We see a number of possibilities here.
First of all, the U.S.S.R. is ready to reinstate its moratorium on all nuclear explosions any day and hour, if the United States reciprocates.
Second, it is time finally to end the procedural impasse at the Conference on Disarmament and start concrete discussions there on the problem of a complete test ban.
Third, as one possible way toward such a ban, we are now considereing the possibility of extending the 1963 test-ban treaty to cover underground nuclear explosions.
 
Plutonium Production
There is an urgent need for verifiable cessation of the production of fissionable material for weapons purposes. We have declared that this year we are ceasing the production of enriched uranium and that in 1987 we closed down one reactor producing weapons-grade plutonium and that we plan to close down a few more such reactors in 1990.
By the year 2000, all remaining reactors will have been shut down. In addition, the Soviet Union is proposing that all nuclear powers should begin preparing to conclude an accord on the cessation and prohibition of the production of such material.
In response to our alliance's initiative, the NATO countries have made some meaningful proposals that go a long way toward accommodating the positions of the Warsaw Treaty countries. . . .
Going back to the topic of the obligations of individual countries to the community, I feel it my duty to emphasize that reluctance to engage in active talks on reducing naval forces is a sign of neglecting those obligations. Leaving naval forces outside the process of reducing armed forces and arms would be harmful to universal security.
 
On Disarmament
If NATO countries agree to start talks on tactical nuclear weapons, the U.S.S.R. will respond by further unilateral cuts in its tactical nuclear missiles in Europe. The problem of missile proliferation is also ripe for multilateral discussion. The number of countries possessing such weapons is approaching 20, while the trend toward further geographical spread is still strong.
The approach to this issue could be two-pronged: barriers could be erected that, first, would preclude the proliferation of combat missiles and associated technology across the globe and, second, would not impinge on the legitimate interests of countries in gaining peaceful access to outer space.
No one in the world can yet bid farewell to arms but we can abandon once and for all, and do it now, the practice of unconstrained and uncontrolled international arms sales.
To that end, the principles of glasnost and openness should be asserted here as well. The U.S.S.R. reaffirms its willingness to take part in the establishment of a U.N. register of sales and transfers of weapons, including work on parameters.
Along the same lines, the Soviet Union and the People's Republic of China have agreed to start talks shortly on putting an end to their military standoff. The Soviet Union expresses the hope that other states, too, will engage in this process.
 
'Open Skies,' and More
A civilized world is an open world. Breaking the seals on many secrets, the Soviet Union has revealed to the international community detailed data about all reductions in its armed forces and arms, their strength, and the size of its military budget and so on. We reaffirm here the position of the Soviet Union: our ultimate goal is not to have a single Soviet soldier outside our country.
But speaking of equal responsibilities, let us roundly condemn the continuing encirclement of our country - and not only our country - by military bases of other states.
The Soviet Union attaches fundamental importance to a transition from individual measures of confidence building, openness and glasnost in international affairs to a global policy of openness that would become an integral part of comprehensive security and international peace.
The President of the United States, Mr. Bush, has proposed the idea of ''open skies.'' We like that idea. In welcoming and supporting this initiative, the Soviet Union calls for opening up the lands, the waters and outer space. Let's also have open land, open seas and open space. Only then shall we attain absolute transparency and the necessary level of confidence.
 
No Threat in Poland
It is no secret that we were not enthusiastic about the election setback of the Polish Communists. Nor should it be a secret that we hope that they can overcome the crisis. Nevertheless, we see nothing threatening in the fact that in accordance with the will of the Polish people a coalition government has been formed.
We are in no way prejudiced against that Government. We wish it every success and are ready to cooperate with it most actively. Tolerance is the norm of civilized behavior. But if it is obligatory for us in our attitude toward the Government of Poland, why are others so intolerant toward, for example, Cuba? And if a non-Communist Prime Minister is possible in a socialist country, why should the appearance of a Communist as head of a Western Government be perceived as heresy? That's something we shouldn't exclude.
 
Socialism Is Not Spent
And I would like to take this opportunity to say just a few words about my own country. We are building a new model of society, a new model of relations among people and nations, a new model of socialism.
As a great concept, socialism is by no means a spent force. Indeed, it is revealing its humanistic potential in the bitter and often dramatic confrontation with the forces and flaws that are organically alien to it.
We in our country are not just repainting the facade, but rebuilding the entire structure in which the rules of living together must and will be based on the supremacy of law, people's power, openness to the outside world, inter-ethnic harmony.
In every sphere of the common life of our state and our people, the national economy, the political system, the intellectual endeavor of our people, rejection of the ossified relics of the past goes hand in hand with the enthusiasm of new construction.
And even though in the words of a poet, there may be a gloomy day or two, we are confident that perestroika, which began as a revolution of hopes, will keep these hopes alive. Our people, our nation will keep perestroika going for it embodies the aspirations that they cherish.

GRAPHIC: The Soviet Union linked President Bush's proposal for reductions in chemical weapons to a halt in production of new binary weapons by the United States. Foreign Minister Eduard A. Shevardnadze outlined the Soviet position yesterday in his speech to the United States. (NYT/Don Hogan Charles)

HEADLINE: White House Plans to Aid Poland Through I.M.F.

BYLINE: By BERNARD WEINRAUB, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
The Bush Administration is working on a new ''package of support'' for Poland that relies strongly on the International Monetary Fund to channel money to the new Government in Warsaw as part of a major multilateral effort, White House officials said.
In agreeing with plans by the International Monetary Fund to expand its role in Eastern Europe, the Administration has apparently committed itself to more ambitious aid to Poland than the direct assistance previously proposed by the White House. The new idea for aid through multilateral institutions was discussed today by the National Security Council, officials said.
Administration officials say there could be a substantial difference between direct aid from Washington to Warsaw - which some Democrats have criticized as paltry - and international aid provided by the I.M.F. But they could not say exactly how much the fund might actually offer. 
The Administration has been under pressure to help the Government of Prime Minister Tadeusz Mazowiecki, the Solidarity supporter who is the head of the first Government in the Eastern bloc not led by Communists. Mr. Mazowiecki took charge of a nation reeling from a deteriorating economy that is crippled by numerous problems, including obsolete industries, consumer-goods shortages, and a staggering foreign debt.
 
'More Clout' in New Approach
President Bush plans to address the fund on Wednesday morning, and State Department officials said he might use the forum to generally endorse an I.M.F. aid package to the economically desperate Polish Government. But they said he was not likely to offer details of the new package in the speech.
Officials said Mr. Bush and his Administration were now convinced that the most effective political, diplomatic and economic vehicle for aid to Poland is not a nation-to-nation approach but an international program that would involve Western donors and Japan. That applies to Hungary as well, they said.
Such an approach, the officials said, would carry specific conditions, like calling for a reduction in inflation, cuts in the budget deficit, a slower growth in the money supply and a competitive exchange rate.
Administration officials also said aid through the fund provides ''more clout'' and ''international discipline'' than bilateral aid. The United States is the largest lender to the I.M.F., providing about 18 percent of the organization's money.
 
Democrats Fault Direct-Aid Plan
Administration officials said the United States, by working through the fund, can better insure that Poland's Government will take steps needed to restructure the economy. Administration officials assert that a preference for a multilateral approach does not represent a change in policy. Nor, they said, would it circumvent Washington's concerns that American aid would permit Poland to postpone the fundamental economic changes that American officials consider crucial to rebuilding Poland's economy.
In political terms, the Administration could use aid through the I.M.F. to counter Democratic criticisms that Mr. Bush's nation-to-nation direct-aid program is paltry. A ranking White House official indicated that more aid to Poland was likely in some form, given the more than $1.2 billion in aid over three years proposed by Democrats, as compared with $146 million proposed by Mr. Bush.
Polish officials have told the I.M.F. that Poland is seeking $700 million a year over the next three years from the fund.
Perhaps most significant, the American contribution to agencies like the fund is not an outlay in budget terms, so it does not contribute to the deficit. Essentially, the funds are United States reserves that are made available to the fund but are always at the disposal of the United States if it wants the money returned.
 
Expanded I.M.F Role in Poland
''The U.S. does not want to spend budgetary resources in Eastern Europe,'' said Richard Feinberg, executive vice president of the Overseas Development Council, a leading economic-policy research organization. ''That's the beauty of the multilateral agencies. It enables us to participate in lending to Eastern Europe, but only paying less than 20 percent of the bill.''
Officials at the I.M.F. meeting in Washington said Monday that the fund is likely to agree by the end of the year to increase its resources as much as 67 percent, or $80 billion. That would be used, in part, to play an expanded role in Poland as well as Hungary, Yugoslavia and Rumania - the four Eastern European nations that are members of the fund. The Soviet Union is not a member of the fund.
I.M.F. and Administration officials said it was virutally impossible to calculate how much Poland would receive if the agency increases its resourtces - although the $700-million-a-year figure over three years was a ''starting point,'' said one official.
Economists and former Administration aides say that international agencies, like the World Bank and I.M.F., are the most viable way of lending abroad, and that the White House strategy is a sensible one.
 
Avoiding U.S Budget Process
''There's the simple point of financial leverage - by operating through the I.M.F. where the U.S. is a large capital holder, more money can flow into Poland than by operating through the U.S. budget process,'' said Lawrence A. Kudlow, chief economist of Bear, Stearns & Company, and former chief economist at the Office of Management and Budget in the President Ronald Reagan's first term. ''There's another agenda here too. The Administration wants the I.M.F. to guide Poland's economic development. They also want the I.M.F.'s monitoring.''
Meanwhile, Finance Minister Leszek Balcerowicz has called on Western governments to provide a $1 billion ''stabilisation loan'' to back a tough anti-inflation program in his country and build up its international reserves.
Western officials said today that Mr. Balcerowicz, in a series of talks with finance ministers from major industrial nations over the last days, had set out an ambitious timetable to transform Poland's crippled economy into the free-market mold.

HEADLINE: Nearly Half of U.S. Rural Bridges Found Lacking

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
Nearly half the bridges in rural America are ''structurally deficient or functionally obsolete,'' an Agriculture Department report says.
The department's Office of Transportation said its analysis was derived from the national bridge inventory by the Department of Transportation. As of last Dec. 31, there were 578,094 highway bridges, with 81 percent classified as rural. 
According to the report, which was issued Monday, 26 percent of the 468,095 rural bridges were classified as ''structurally deficient'' and 20 percent as functionally obsolete.
The remaining 54 percent were listed as ''neither structurally deficient nor functionally obsolete.''
 
Reassurance on Safety
Wesley R. Kriebel, deputy administrator of the Office of Transportation, said the classification of bridges as structurally deficient or functionally obsolete ''does not necessarily mean the bridges are unsafe.''
''The definition of structurally deficient is a term the Federal Highway Administration uses in their definitions,'' Mr. Kriebel said. ''A structurally deficient bridge is either closed or restricted to lighter vehicles only because of deteriorated structural components.''
In response to questions, Mr. Kriebel added that functionally obsolete refers ''to the age of the bridge, and that, too, doesn't necessarily mean the bridge is unsafe.''
The report showed that Missouri has the most structurally deficient rural bridges, and Texas has the most that are functionally obsolete. Arizona ''appears to have the most adequate rural bridges,'' the report said.
On average, the age of all United States bridges was found to be 35.5 years - 36.6 years for rural bridges and 30.9 years for urban.
''Half of all rural bridges are less than 50 feet in length, while half of urban bridges are less than 123 feet,'' the report said. ''Their average lengths, however, are 111 and 257 feet, respectively.''
 
Responsibility for Bridges
The report said counties and other local governments were responsible for 56 percent of all rural bridges but responsible for only 38 percent of the city bridges.
Nationwide, 59 percent of the 468,095 rural bridges are off the Federal highway system. A third are in six states: Iowa, Onio, Kansas, Texas, Illinois and Oklahoma.
In contrast, only 23 percent of all urban bridges are off the federally subsidized road system.
The report said the 41 percent of the rural bridges that are on Federal highway systems carry nearly 90 percent of the average daily traffic.
But average daily traffic, ''in and of itself, does not measure the economic value of a bridge,'' the report said. ''A third of all rural on-system (Federal) bridges are in seven states - Texas, Kansas, Ohio, Illinois, California, Missouri and Mississippi.''
Martin Fitzpatrick Jr., administrator of Office of Transportation, said that although reponsibility varied, most responsibility for the below-standard bridges lay with local governments.
''Various and complex bridge funding and management arrangements exist throughtout the United States,'' Mr. Fitzpatrick said. ''This means that future improvements will require continued intergovernmental support, along with policies that acknowledge the importance of rural bridges to the nation's economy.''
In June, the National Association of Counties issued a survey of county bridges, reporting that three-fourths of them need to be repaired or replaced, at a cost of billions of dollars. But the survey did not take into account bridges under state or Federal jurisdiction and used different criteria to evaluate the structures.

HEADLINE: Once Again, Louisianians Get Chance to Vote on Tax Increase

BYLINE: By FRANCES FRANK MARCUS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NEW ORLEANS, Sept. 26

 BODY:
For the second time this year, Louisiana is trying to reinvent prosperity. The state's voters, who rejected a tax referendum in April, will go to the polls again on Oct. 7 to consider a tax increase intended to lift the state's nearly flat economy.
The new proposal, a modified version of the earlier one, came out of a recent session of the Legislature that political analysts here said was notable for the mounting tensions between the lawmakers and the Governor, Buddy Roemer.
Last fall Mr. Roemer, a 45-year-old Democrat and former Congressman from Shreveport, saw his first fiscal program crushed by the Legislature, and Louisianians voted down a less ambitious package last spring. The Governor, who fought hard for both programs, has been uncharacteristically quiet about the upcoming vote. 
The tax proposals are designed to create jobs by building roads, expanding ports and airports and helping to restore wetlands. Through an amendment to the State Constitution, the proposals would also nibble at the edges of a tax system that the Governor says is in need of an overhaul.
 
A Legacy of Huey Long
Louisiana has an unemployment rate of 8.3 percent, the highest in the nation, and its tax system is one of the country's most archaic. The former partly reflects a decline in the oil industry; the latter is a populist legacy of Huey P. Long, who was Governor 60 years ago but has had a great and enduring effect on Louisiana. Today, by constitutional fiat, few homeowners pay property taxes.
Economists say the proposed constitutional change, called Amendment 1, will energize the economy with a $1.4 billion construction program. The program would be financed by an increase in the gasoline tax of 1 cent a gallon, and the money would be used to build highways and expand and improve ports and airports in an effort to attract industry. The amendment would also create a trust fund similar to those in other states that confine the use of gasoline taxes to highway and other transportation systems.
The amendment was part of a larger tax package that voters rejected last April. Voters next month may choose among 13 amendments, some of them on issues unrelated to the state's economy.
Another proposal, Amendment 2, would set up a trust fund to curb coastal erosion. The state's wetlands, which have been estimated to be vanishing at the rate of about 40 square miles a year, yield a billion dollars annually in fisheries, fur trapping and related commerce.
 
Federal Matching Funds Sought
The trust fund could raise as much as $25 million a year from oil and gas revenue to help provide a start in rebuilding the marshes, environmentalists said.
''It's a downpayment on what we think we need from the Federal Government,'' said Paul Kemp, director of the Coalition to Restore Coastal Louisiana, a group that hopes to attract matching Federal dollars.
While business leaders, economists and others are speaking out in favor of Amendments 1 and 2, Governor Roemer has maintained a low profile this time. Last spring he campaigned aggressively throughout the state for the tax proposals.
The Governor, who has an M.B.A. from Harvard, spent much of last week in Manhattan courting potential investors and bond raters. Louisiana's bond rating is the lowest in the nation.
''Personalizing referenda is not a good idea,'' said P. J. Mills, a former state legislator who is the Governor's new chief of staff. ''This time we want it to pass.''
 
'Frayed Feelings' Conceded
Mr. Mills, who also is a New Orleans businessman, said Mr. Roemer's problems with lawmakers stem from the state's financial crises and ''almost continuous'' sessions of the Legislature in an effort to balance the budget.
He said the Governor had been ''addressing problems head on,'' and added, ''It's logical you're going to leave some frayed feelings.''
A recent statewide poll has found voters unhappy with both the Governor and the Legislature, and dispirited about the direction of the state.
''They're saying, 'A plague on both your houses,' '' said Edward F. Renwick, a political analyst who conducted the poll of 900 voters after the Legislature adjourned in July.
Proponents of Amendment 1 said a positive vote would revive the optimism in Louisiana.
''I'm tired of sending my members out of state all over the country to go to work,'' said Peter Babin, business manager of Local 406 of the International Union of Operating Engineers, who is a member of an unusual business-labor coalition working for the amendment. In two years, from July 1986 to July 1988, 167,000 Louisianians moved out of the state.
Mark Drennen, president of the Public Affairs Research Council in Baton Rouge, the state capital, said legislators have patched the budget with temporary taxes for four years.
''October 7 doesn't deal with basic budget problems,'' Mr. Drennen said. ''Unless the price of oil more than doubles, next spring the state will be facing budget cuts and-or tax increases of between $700 and $800 million. Nobody's talking about that.''

HEADLINE: A Drug War, With Little Ammunition

BYLINE: By Robert M. Morgenthau; Robert M. Morgenthau is District Attorney of New York County.

 BODY:
The key to understanding the Administration's national drug strategy lies not in reading President Bush's lips but in reading his proposal's fine print. The closer one looks, the clearer it is that the budgeteers have gutted the President's and drug czar William Bennett's strategy.
Take state and local law enforcement. The report states that the ''first challenge facing [us] is to reclaim neighborhoods that have been rendered unsafe by drugs.'' That is an understatement. 
For many citizens, domestic tranquility has become a forgotten ideal. They feel imprisoned in their homes, in fear of crack dealers who own their street corners - or worse, their apartment buildings. Their parks are havens for drug trafficking and their protests are increasingly met with violent retaliation.
The media quickly reported that the Administration's plan responded to this challenge by requesting $350 million for state and local law enforcement for fiscal 1990 - a 133 percent funding increase.
Hidden in the report's fine print, however, is the real truth: The $350 million figure is for ''budget authority'' - but actual spending in 1990 is slated at merely $149 million. That represents a paltry $39 million increase over this year's expenditures. Spread over 50 states, it is hardly the sum needed to reclaim our streets.
Worse still, to finance its new initiatives the plan would cut other state and local grant programs, including monies for economic development, housing and juvenile justice.
A soon-to-be-released study estimates that if the proposals become law, state and local governments will be net losers. Overall, they would receive $95 million less in Federal assistance in 1990 than they now are scheduled to receive. New York State would be a lucky winner: It would gain all of $4 million in Federal funds if the new plan is enacted.
Most harmful would be the proposed cuts in U.S. grants for juvenile justice. Most of that money goes to programs for delinquents and ''at risk'' youth - the very youngsters most likely to abuse drugs if not given some alternative.
In New York City, the cuts would eliminate programs such as the South Bronx AIDS Outreach Program, which works with adolescent intravenous-drug users, and the Educational Workshop for Homeless Youth, which offers tutorials for children living in welfare hotels. To fund a war on drugs by cutting such programs is as cruel as it is absurd.
There is an even more fundamental problem. The new drug strategy details the costs incurred in fighting the war on drugs but fails to calculate those associated with treating casualties of the war: It nowhere reckons the costs of caring for crack-addicted babies and abused children. It figures in the strains on the prison system -but not on our health care and educational systems.
Those sums are staggering. Florida estimates it will spend $700 million for health care, social services and special education to prepare for kindergarten 17,500 infants born in 1987 and exposed to cocaine. Nationwide, the costs of intensive hospital care for crack-addicted babies will total $2.5 billion this year. These figures starkly demonstrate that if we do not pay up front to stem the drug epidemic, we will pay far more later to attend to its victims.
We need $500 million a year (the cost of one Stealth bomber) for five years, not $149 million for one year, to enable state and local law enforcement to regain control of our neighborhoods.
Whatever else our priorities, we must take back our streets. It is simply unacceptable to plead budget deficits as an excuse for abandoning the law-abiding people who live in drug-infested neighborhoods.
We also need a national commitment to the education of our children, especially those in inner-city schools. In New York City, the dropout rate is 32 percent; in other cities, the figure is higher. We cannot permit that to continue. As long as there are legions of young people disaffected from society, there will be a ready supply of drug dealers and a ready market for their product.
If we try to fight the war on drugs on the cheap, we are bound to lose -and, in the future, will be condemned to pay far more in dollars and human misery.

HEADLINE: $900 Million Tax Rise Leaves Voters in Connecticut Upset

BYLINE: By KIRK JOHNSON

DATELINE: HARTFORD, Sept. 26

 BODY:
Business leaders, politicians and poll-takers say that across this state both Republicans and Democrats, employees and employers, are unhappy with what happened under the Capitol dome in the spring when Connecticut confronted its budget deficit.
They say the solution to the deficit - $900 million in new and increased taxes enacted by the Democrat-controlled General Assembly and signed by Gov. William A. O'Neill -has combined with other concerns to produce a potent brew of voter discontent.
One such voter is Scott Ledbetter, a 38-year-old Greenwich entrepreneur who has invested in television programming and cable TV companies and whose political activity had been confined to local issues. Now, however, he is concerned that an increase in the top bracket of the state's tax on capital gains, to 14 percent, could discourage people from investing in new companies like his. 
 
'A Mediocre State Government'
''We've had a mediocre state government for years, but people didn't get alarmed because the mediocrity didn't affect them,'' said Mr. Ledbetter, who was spurred by the tax increases to contribute time and money to Joel Schiavone, a New Haven developer who hopes to run as the Republican opponent to Mr. O'Neill, a Democrat. ''People have got to stand up and do something about it if they can,'' Mr. Ledbetter said.
Surveys showing Mr. O'Neill's job performance rating at or near its lowest ebb have also encouraged rivals in his own party - notably Representative Bruce A. Morrison of New Haven, who announced on Monday the formation of a committee to raise money for a campaign to challenge the Governor at the Democratic convention.
Underlying the various challenges is Mr. O'Neill's own longevity as Governor. Sworn into office in 1980 upon the resignation of Gov. Ella T. Grasso, Mr. O'Neill, who is 59 years old, is already among the longest-serving governors in Connecticut history. He has said he will run again next year, if his health permits, and become the first three-term governor since Colonial days.
The question being asked among hopeful Republicans and ambitious challengers like Mr. Morrison is whether Mr. O'Neill, having ridden a wave of popularity during the boom years of the 1980's, can overcome his association with the era's end.
Some of the Governor's supporters say a turnaround in his image has already begun, however slowly.
 
Governor's Performance Rated
The most recent poll conducted by the Institute for Social Inquiry at the University of Connecticut, for example, showed 29 percent of the 500 people surveyed rating Mr. O'Neill's performance as ''poor,'' compared with 34 percent three months ago, just after he had signed $900 million in new and increased taxes into law.
The Governor's performance was rated ''fair'' by 36 percent of those surveyed, and ''good'' by 29 percent, up from 26 percent. Few people, however - 3 percent in both June and September -thought Mr. O'Neill was doing an excellent job.
The results, published today in The Hartford Courant, came from randomly selected people who were questioned between Sept. 12 and 20. The poll has a margin of error of plus or minus 5 percentage points.
 
Federal Government Blamed
''People in Connecticut are not fools,'' John F. Droney Jr., the state Democratic Party chairman, said in defending Mr. O'Neill's performance. ''They know that the Federal Government has backed away from its commitments. He raised taxes to pay for those programs.''
Mr. Droney said that before the general election in November 1990, Connecticut residents would realize that raising taxes was necessary to maintain the state's commitments to things like public education and local town grants and that they would once again vote for Mr. O'Neill.
But in interviews with business leaders, union officials, and citizens groups, many people said the issues troubling voters go far beyond an aversion to taxation.
The issues they cite range from an explosion of drug-related crime and a resulting wave of prison construction in 14 communities across the state to changes in the international economy that could threaten Connecticut exporters. There is an increased sense of vulnerability to forces beyond the small state's control.
''I give the Governor and the legislature high marks for maintaining services, but I'm still uneasy,'' said George C. Springer, president of the 20,000- member Connecticut Federation of Teachers. ''The world we live in today is very, very different from the world we lived in when Governor O'Neill took office - what we did over those years may not be good enough for what we will face.''
John R. Rathgeber, executive vice president of the Connecticut Business and Industry Association, said: ''There's a real feeling that we're at a crossroads. Our economy is primarily manufacturing, insurance and financial services, and all are facing severe global pressures.''
 
Crisis in Jobs Foreseen
Mr. Rathgeber said that expected declines in Federal defense spending were likely to hurt major employers like United Technologies, while the cluster of giant insurance companies, mostly in Hartford, would probably find it increasingly economical to decentralize their thousands of employees rather than expand or pay for relocations to Connecticut.
''The question is, do we act in advance of the crisis or wait for the crisis to be upon us?'' he said. ''We have that option now.''
For Republicans, who have controlled the governor's office for only 10 years since World War II, failing to re-elect any governor during that time, the apparent weakness of Mr. O'Neill and the possibility of a split in the party, because of Mr. Morrison, has already raised hopes.
 
Republican Challengers Available
Although four major contenders have expressed interest, the current front-runner appears to be Representative John G. Rowland, who - like Mr. Morrison -was in Washington during the state's debate on taxes and played no role in raising them.
Two other possible Republican contenders, in addition to Mr. Schiavone, who has never before run for political office, are State Representative Robert G. Jaekle of Stratford, the House minority leader in the General Assembly, and State Senator Reginald J. Smith of New Hartford, the minority leader in the Senate.
''The Democrats are divided and split, and generally people think they're worse off than they were,'' said Richard Foley, the state Republican Party chairman. ''Things like that bode well.''

HEADLINE: CAMPAIGN STOP: GREAT KILLS, S.I.;
Great Kills Favors Koch But Supports Giuliani Bid

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM

 BODY:
In Great Kills, S.I., a three-bedroom town house sells for $140,000, but a round-trip drive to Manhattan can cost $10 in tolls. Well-tended lawns slope gently toward tidy marinas, but the main roads running past them are clogged with cars and flood at the first heavy rain. It is a place where peace comes at a price.
Now people in Great Kills see that peace threatened by the prospect of a new face in New York's City Hall. For 12 years this has been Koch country, and despite their disaffection from a city they feel forgets them, more than a few voters here seem reluctant to lose the Mayor whose sympathy they say they had.
''I'll probably vote for Koch - I guess he's pretty good,'' said Anne Griffiths, 81 years old, as she paused with a shopping cart on Amboy Avenue last week. Told that the Mayor had lost the Democratic primary and was no longer a candidate, she gave a start and said, ''Oh - I don't know anything about the other guys.'' 
Indeed, voting for Mayor Edward I. Koch is an option many people interviewed in this conservative community midway down the island's south shore said they wished they still had.
More than half of the voters surveyed in this area by The New York Times on Primary Day said they had chosen Mr. Koch, but might vote Republican or stay home Nov. 7 if David N. Dinkins won the Democratic nomination. Now that Mr. Dinkins is the nominee, a number of voters stopped on the street last week said they would do just that, and some did not disguise the role race played in their choice.
 
'I Hate to Say Color, But'
''I tell you, I'm a Democrat, but I think I'm going to go for the other guy,'' said Fred King, a 69-year-old retired grocery store produce manager as he passed the Lucky Fortune Chinese restaurant. ''I've seen what people of the opposite sex have done. I hate to say color, but you know what I mean. I've seen what they've done in Brooklyn and other places, you know.
''I mean, I don't have anything against the guy personally,'' he said of Mr. Dinkins, the Manhattan Borough President, who is black. ''He may be a very good man, you know.'' Nick Petrie, 63, makes pizzas at the Village Maria restaurant down the street. He is a Republican who has voted for Mr. Koch in the past, but he said this year he would probably vote for Rudolph W. Giuliani.
''I think he's a good man,'' he said, quickly adding: ''I think Dinkins is a good man, too.'' He said he thought the race might be close. ''They vote here the same way they vote in Harlem,'' he said. ''I think it's a biased vote, personally.''
No precise demographic snapshot of Great Kills is available. But figures from the City Planning Department based on the 1980 census show that the population of Community Board 3, which covers Great Kills and a dozen other communities, is 94 percent white, 1 percent black, 3.3 percent of Hispanic origin and just under 2 percent of Asian descent. Nearly half of the population is Italian-American.
It is a solidly middle-class area, home to many police officers and other civil servants. Its median household income is $24,645, compared with $13,855 for the city as a whole. By far the most prevalent crime is car theft, with 1,502 cases reported in the 122d Precinct - which cuts a broad swath through the middle of the island - in the first six months of this year.
''If there's an overriding thing, it's quality of life issues, youths hanging out, people complaining,'' said Assistant Chief Joseph R. Borrelli, the borough police commander. ''There are some burglaries, but street robberies and all that, thank God, that's not a real big problem.''
 
Early Waves of Immigration
Politically, the area is mixed. In the 60th Assembly District, which includes Great Kills, there are just under 27,000 registered Democrats, just over 20,000 Republicans and about 10,000 Independents, according to the Board of Elections.
Many residents of Great Kills moved from Brooklyn or Queens after the building of the Verrazano-Narrows Bridge 25 years ago, but the early waves of immigration were small compared with the growth of the last decade. Last year, 2,000 new building permits were issued in the Board 3 district, more than in any other community district outside Manhattan, according to Maxine Spierer, the board's chairman.
''The major issue here is the fact that there's been so much development over the past decade that the infrastructure hasn't been able to keep pace,'' Ms. Spierer said. ''There are not enough good roads, not enough mass transit, terrible traffic congestion, not enough storm or sanitary sewers.''
Moreover, Ms. Spierer said, as the one place in the city with substantial amounts of vacant land, ''we're petrified that we're going to be the repository of a lot of these unwanted facilities'' from jails to homeless shelters. Already Staten Island has the city's last remaining large landfill, where there are plans to build a pyramid of trash.
 
Future Policies Are Uncertain
Some residents seem concerned that if such problems persisted under the Koch administration, which they considered sympathetic, they might be worse under a new mayor, whose future policies are uncertain. Mr. Dinkins, for example, has mentioned Great Kills as one of several areas in the city ripe for further economic development. Mr. Giuliani has said he does not favor building a proposed new jail in the nearby Rossville section of the island, but has not said where it should go.
''I'm a Democrat, but it's a tossup between Giuliani and Dinkins,'' said John Colon, 32, an operations supervisor at Morgan Stanley, the investment house, in Manhattan. ''Experience-wise, both of them seem to lack that.''
''Mayor Koch, a lot of times we disagreed with what his proposals were,'' Ms. Spierer said. ''But at least we knew more about him, more about his priorities. David Dinkins and Giuliani are sort of unknown quantities to us.''
Ginny Paladino, a 25-year-old Tupperware saleswoman who was shopping with her 21-month-old son, Mikey, said she had wanted Mr. Koch to win, but would now vote for Mr. Giuliani.
 
'I Think He's an Honest Man'
''I like his policies,'' she said. ''I think he's an honest man.'' She said she was disturbed by Mr. Dinkins's failure two decades ago to pay income taxes for four years. He has said the lapse was the result of inattention, not evasion, and he eventually paid the taxes with interest and penalties.
''I don't like the idea of his income tax,'' Mrs. Paladino said. ''I don't think he's so honest.''
Of some two dozen people stopped on the busy commercial strips and quiet residential side streets of Great Kills, only one 29-year-old woman said she definitely intended to vote for Mr. Dinkins. She would give her name only as Nancy.
''I think maybe he can do something with some of the racial stuff that's going on,'' she said. ''I think maybe he can do something to make people be a little kinder to each other.''
 
'They'll Take Over the City'
Others offered much harsher assessments.
William Malast, 63, a retired accounting manager for an insurance company whose parents emigrated from Czechoslovakia, is a registered Republican who favored Mr. Koch but will vote for Mr. Giuliani.
''Not to be prejudiced,'' he said of Mr. Dinkins. But he added: ''If he gets in, we're dead. They'll take over the city. I've got nothing against the man, but it might get out of hand.''
''Liberalism just gets out of hand,'' added Mr. Malast's neighbor, Joseph Passalaqua, also 63, who stood chatting with him in the shade in front of the trim houses on Bay Terrace, a quiet street near the water.
Mr. Passalaqua, a retired processor in a motion picture film laboratory in Manhattan, said he was concerned that government spending for welfare and social programs was undisciplined. ''For people who need it, O.K.,'' he said. But too many people get it who don't need it.''
 
Changes in City Charter
Islanders are also concerned about proposed changes in the City Charter that would abolish the Board of Estimate - where the Staten Island borough president has the same vote as the Brooklyn borough chief, who represents six times the population. Many of the board's powers would go to the mayor and an expanded City Council of 51 members, in which Staten Island would have three seats.
An uneasy sense about the future pervades many residents' comments.
''I'm worried,'' said Peggy Glensor, who stopped outside the local post office and described herself as ''55 plus.''
''I don't like the whole thing,'' Mrs. Glensor said. ''I just don't see how the city can get pulled together again.'' She, too, said she would be voting Republican.
''If I lived in New York, I would probably go the other way.'' she said. ''But living on Staten Island, I like Giuliani.''
