HEADLINE: Taiwan Picks Yonkers Company for Subway Cars

BYLINE: By JAMES FERON

DATELINE: YONKERS, Sept. 26

 BODY:
A Japanese-owned rail car manufacturer here that has built subway cars for the New York City and PATH systems was awarded a $170 million contract today to build the first 132 subway cars for a new mass-transit system under construction in Taipei, Taiwan.
The company, U.R.C., enlisted leaders of the Yonkers government as well as of the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey, which leases the rail car manufacturing plant, to travel to Taiwan as part of an intense campaign for the subway car contract.
The contract, with the first cars to be delivered in 1992, will mean the employment of up to 250 skilled laborers in a city that has seen its once-vibrant manufacturing diminished by the movement of companies to the South. Mayor Nicholas C. Wasicsko said he was happy to hear ''some good news for a change in Yonkers.'' 
He was referring to the city's division over a housing desegregation plan imposed by a Federal judge. City officials have feared that the dispute, which attracted national attention for its constitutional implications, would keep new business away.
 
'Too Excited to Sleep'
Mr. Wasicsko announced the awarding of the subway car contract flanked by beaming officials of the Nissho Iwai American Corporation, a subsidiary of the Japanese conglomerate Nissho Iwai, and Kawasaki Rail Cars, a division of Kawasaki, which jointly own U.R.C., which stands for United Rail Car.
News that U.R.C. had won the contract arrived in Yonkers late Monday night, according to Japanese company officials who had arrived for what they hoped would be a celebration. Noboru Sakamoto, a vice president of Nissho Iwai, said ''we were too excited to sleep.''
Hats Kageyama, general manager of the Transit Systems and Equipment Division of the Nissho Iwai American Corporation, said two companies, U.R.C. and an American-Canadian consortium that included a Canadian company, ''had qualified as American companies'' for the purpose of the contract Taiwan has an $18 billion trade surplus with the United States, and the State Commerce Departments have been pressing Taiwan to reduce that imbalance. Taiwan indicated that it would do so, virtually eliminating foreign rail car makers as bidders.
Yonkers itself has been seeking to reverse an economic drain, most recently exemplified by the move of the manufacturing division of the Polychrome Corporation, which makes printing supplies, to Atlanta. Mr. Wasicsko said he hoped U.R.C. would be able to hire some Polychrome workers and retrain them to build subway cars.
 
Space Is Leased
The contract awarded to Yonkers by the Department of Rapid Transit in Taiwan is for the Tamshui-Hsintein line, one of the radial spokes on the semicircular system being built in Taipei. Two other rail car contracts will be put out for bid before the system is finished.
U.R.C. leases space in a structure that was the original home of the Otis Elevator Company and the site of the first safety elevator. It was purchased by the Port Authority in 1985 from United Technologies Corporation of Hartford, which had acquired Otis. The city helped United Technologies modernize the structure and its leaders were furious when the company moved to Connecticut.
Kawasaki's first contract for subway cars in the United States came in the 1970's with the export of 125 units from Japan to Philadelphia for its subway system. The Yonkers plant was opened for rail-car production in 1986 and its first job was the manufacture of subway cars for the PATH (Port Authority Trans-Hudson) system.
It built 95 cars in the Yonkers plant. U.R.C., under the Kawasaki name, assembled 325 R-62 cars used on New York City's Lexington Avenue IRT line.
Company officials said a ''cadre'' of 100 skilled workers was now employed in the Yonkers plant, rehabilitating cars for the New York City Transit Authority and building ''people mover'' rail cars, which are much smaller than subway cars, for the Las Vegas airport.
The U.R.C. officials said while engineering studies were being completed for the Taiwan system, U.R.C. would be producing 75 double-decker rail commuter coaches for the Massachusetts Bay Transit Authority in a $105 million contract. The company also expects to build ''people movers'' for Kennedy International Airport, when that system is introduced there.

HEADLINE: Survey Says Mid-Sized Companies Debate Leaving

BYLINE: By SUSAN CHIRA

 BODY:
One in every five medium-sized businesses in the New York metropolitan area is considering leaving New York, and most of those would move to New Jersey, according to a survey released yesterday by Bank Leumi.
The survey of 599 businesses in New York City, Long Island and Westchester County with annual revenues from $10 million to $250 million suggested that, although most executives believe their New York locations help their companies, many fear that an economic slowdown and urban social problems like crime and drugs will hurt their companies.
Studies of larger corporations have yielded similar findings, but the survey by Bank Leumi, an Israeli bank, whose clients are largely medium-sized companies, is one of the few to concentrate on the medium-sized businesses. 
''The findings present a glum outlook for the New York regional economy,'' said Emanuel Tobier, a professor at New York University's Graduate School of Public Administration, who commented on the survey at a news conference yesterday at the Plaza Hotel. ''Drugs and crime are a mask for our unease about what's going to happen. We're in for a period of restrained growth.''
 
Beyond the Predictable
Daniel S. Bayer, vice president of the New York Partnership, a business group formed to address social issues, said that this and other surveys' findings suggested an agenda for state and city officials concerned about keeping businesses in New York. They must address crime, drugs, education and other urban problems, he said, as well as respond to predictable business concerns about taxes and housing costs.
In a study of the financial services industry conducted this spring, the partnership found that advances in telecommunications enable many large companies to move substantial numbers of employees out of their New York headquarters without business disruptions.
But it is difficult to say how many companies would actually go, or how many are simply considering leaving. Half the medium-sized companies surveyed said that more than half of their sales are within New York, a strong incentive to stay in this area.
Nicholas J. Tortorello, chairman of Research and Forecasts, the company that conducted the survey, said that in his experience, about half of those who said they would move their businesses would be likely to do so. Of the 23 percent of executives who said they would be ''very likely'' or ''somewhat likely'' to move, most were heads of manufacturing or wholesale businesses. Service businesses were more likely to stay, a good sign because services are becoming more important in the New York economy, Mr. Tortorello said.
 
New Jersey Is Preferred
Fifty-six percent of those businesses considering leaving would go to New Jersey. New York City officials have promoted the boroughs outside Manhattan as a low-cost alternative to New Jersey. But Mr. Bayer said the survey indicated that executives were also concerned about security and drugs in these boroughs.
While several of the findings were similar to those of other studies, some defied conventional wisdom. Executives overwhelmingly pointed to crime and drugs as their most serious concerns about living and working in New York, but a relatively low 24 percent said that local governments' top priority should be improving public education. By contrast, Mr. Bayer said, the executives he sees name education as the most important problem.

LENGTH: 1890 words

HEADLINE: EDUCATION;
Schools Trying to Link Good Jobs and Skills

SERIES: SKILLS VS. JOBS: THE CLASSROOM MISMATCH - LAST OF THREE ARTICLES

BYLINE: By WILLIAM E. SCHMIDT, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT

 BODY:
Teachers used to tell Gaylon Jefferson he should stay in school if he wanted a good job, but he says he did not see the point then. He knew too many people who had high school diplomas and were still working at McDonald's.
Besides, it was dangerous at Mumford High School here, and he said he was often robbed. ''They took my shoes once,'' said Gaylon, a tall, quiet-spoken 17-year-old. ''They got my coat. They stuck a gun in my back and took it from me, on the corner right outside school. It was the middle of winter, and it was freezing.''
So Gaylon dropped out, joining more than four million other Americans 16-to-24 years old who have failed to complete high school. Instead of completing his freshman year in 1988, he took on the Detroit job market. 
 
Failure, Then Hope
It was a mismatch. Armed with junior high skills in math and reading, and no high school diploma, he soon found himself, like tens of thousands of young men and women, most of them black or Hispanic, in large urban areas, out of school and out of work.
''There was nothing but fast-food restaurant jobs,'' Gaylon said. ''Jobs that paid maybe $5 or $6 an hour.''
But he turned out to be luckier than most. He was accepted into a machinists' training program run by Focus Hope, a nonprofit community group, and is learning to operate the kind of computer-operated equipment that makes up the city's new factories.
For decades, the students who dropped out and the high school graduates whose diplomas did not necessarily mean they could read, write or do basic arithmetic had a refuge of sorts. They could usually find work in factories in Detroit and many other cities. That was when industry needed a steady supply of raw labor, unskilled workers who, if they just kept showing up, might eventually earn enough to raise a family, buy a car, often even a house, and hope for something better for their children.
But in the last decade, Wayne County, which includes Detroit, has lost more than 124,000 manufacturing jobs, most of them for unskilled labor. The remaining factories have trimmed their work forces and invested in automation and new technology, robots and computer-aided machinery that require workers who not only can read, but also can think on their feet.
That is exactly what companies are not getting, at least not nearly enough of it, business executives around the country say.
 
A 50 Percent Dropout Rate
Of the nearly 14,000 students beginning ninth grade in Detroit's public schools this month, it is estimated that about 40 percent will drop out well before graduation. Nationally, according to a 1986 study on urban dropouts, about 50 percent of inner-city youths never graduate from high school.
But even among those who do finish, only a relatively small number leave school with the kind of academic and social skills that will make them attractive to college recruiters or employers. According to a study by the Business Education Alliance of the Detroit Chamber of Commerce, about 6,000 of 7,000 recent high school graduates had math and reading skills below 11th-grade levels.
School districts, states and the Federal Government use various methods to calculate dropout rates, making direct comparisons difficult, but however it is stated, the nation's dropout rate and ways to improve the skills its graduates are sure to be raised today or tomorrow at the education meeting that President Bush is holding with the nation's Governors in Charlottesville, Va.
''We are at a point where we have to realize the traditional way of educating young people doesn't work for everyone,'' said Dr. Thomas Steele, an Assistant Superintendent in Detroit who heads the city's dropout prevention program. ''How do we motivate these young people to see a direct relationship between reading and math and the jobs on the outside?''
In the last two years educators, community groups and business people, alarmed about the declining skills of the city's pool of young workers, have started several pilot projects and collaborative programs intended to redeem some of these young people.
One is the Detroit Compact, organized by the Detroit Chamber of Commerce in cooperation with the city's schools. It enrolls sixth graders, who sign an agreement in which they pledge to maintain a C average and at least a 90 percent attendance rate in return for a guaranteed job or a college scholarship when they graduate.
The Detroit Compact is among more than a dozen collaborative projects involving the city's schools and local universities, businesses and community groups. The projects are aimed at providing incentives, counseling, tutoring and other assistance to young people at risk of dropping out. Similar projects exist in many other cities as well.
 
Building Pride and Confidence
''The problem is not just academics,'' said Dr. Steele, who oversaw a new program this summer that offered six weeks of intensive academic and social counseling to 240 elementary and junior high school students who were identified as potential dropouts. ''We have got to build pride, self-esteem and confidence.''
Charles Grenville, an official with Focus Hope, which recruits students from the Detroit schools to enter job training, said the schools and the community as a whole had failed tens of thousands of youths.
''A lot of teachers, consciously or unconsciously, reinforce the idea that education is pointless unless you are going on to college,'' he said. ''For starters, the schools need to do better representing the new reality of the job market to these kids. We need a kind of cultural change all across the system, to sell young people on the relationship between good jobs and skills.''
But to these young people already struggling in the system, the insistent pleas by educators and business people to stay in school in the hope that it will prepare them for a good job often seem badly out of synch with the gritty reality of their day-to-day lives.
Dia DeBoest is a 14-year-old eighth grader at Cleveland Junior High School on the city's West Side, Like his twin brother, Diabate, he says he wants to go to college and become a lawyer. He also recalls how others in his school, in a tough inner-city neighborhood, would dress up at Career Day, wearing doctor's gowns or suits, even carrying briefcases, like the business executives and lawyers they aspired to be.
But for many of Dia's classmates Career Day had little meaning. For them, he said, the job that holds the greatest immediate appeal is selling drugs. ''At my school, lots of kids sell drugs. They can make $250 a week right now, being a lookout for a crack house, or working there,'' he said. ''When the teachers talk about staying in school, they don't pay attention. They are making money already, so why do they need to learn to read?''
 
Blaming the System
Inner-city schools here and around the nation not only reflect the social pathologies of their neighborhoods - the crime, the drugs, the hopelessness - but in many ways also perpetuate them.
Frederick Wallace dropped out of high school when he was 15, not long after he learned he was going to be a father and needed to work to help support the baby. The child's mother dropped out, too. Detroit school surveys have found that 42 percent of the young women who drop out do so because they are pregnant.
Like many young people, Mr. Wallace blames the system for not trying to intervene more on his behalf. ''I was drinking and smoking and carrying on,'' he said. ''But nobody tried to talk me out of dropping out. They just let me slide.''
Others blame themselves. Maurice Moore was expelled from Cooley High School for truancy. ''The principal sat me down and told me about life and about jobs,'' Mr. Moore said. ''He said that even the Army wouldn't take me unless I got a degree. But I didn't listen. Maybe I was just stupid.'' Many dropouts say now that they might have stayed in school if they had had more vocational opportunities, more real work experience, while still in high school.
For example, Gaylon Jefferson said he had always been interested in electronics. But when he tried to enroll in one of the city's special vocational high schools, he was told he could not, because his grades were too low.
Detroit school officials like Dr. Steele say the city's five vocational schools have not done a good job in dealing with students like Gaylon who are at risk at dropping out. ''We have to do a better job of exposing these young people to work experiences, in the hopes of catching their interest and keeping them in school,'' Dr. Steele said.
 
Students' Recommendations
Two years ago, the school sytem here asked a panel of students to offer advice on what could be done to convey the message that staying in school was necessary for anyone hoping to get a good job.
The students offered a long list of recommendations. Teachers needed to be more caring, they said. The system needed to create more positive role models. Students needed more field trips and better career programs, to give them a clearer picture of the work place.
''A lot of students here just need someone to look up to,'' said Trina Williams, a 16-year-old junior at Redford High School, where she is a member of the varsity tennis team and a good student. ''I went back to my eighth-grade class one day and saw the same thing there. How do you talk about jobs and school when the person they look up to most is a drug dealer?''
To help address the growing shortage of skilled workers in Michigan, the Governor's Commission on Jobs and Economic Development formed a study group of employers last spring, who then composed an inventory of the kinds of skills that businesses say they will require of young people coming into the labor market.
While the foundation of the report involved academic skills, like the ability to read graphs, charts and displays, and to write, read and understand the language in which business is conducted, most of it the report addressed the developmment of better personal management and teamwork skills.
It said schools must do a better job turning out workers who exercise a sense of responsiblity, are fair and honest, demonstrate self control, show pride in their work and are able to function well in groups.
 
Broad Changes May Be Needed
To develop these kinds of skills better, said Gary R. Bachula, a co-author of the report, public schools may have to make changes that go well beyond tougher academic standards and more time in the classroom.
''We may eventually have to move toward more flexibility, more site-based management in the classroom, the same way the American manufacturing process has changed in recent years,'' he said.
But among some of the products, and the failures, of the urban public school system, there is a great deal of doubt about how much substantial change is possible. James Snow is 25, a high school dropout who, after four years in the Marines and a succession of jobs as a security guard, is now enrolled in the machinists' training program with Gaylon Jefferson.
''I have a 3-year-old daughter of my own,'' Mr. Snow said. ''I've been in the Detroit public schools, and I think I know what the answer is. I'm going to put my child in Catholic schools. There is no way she will go to the schools I did.''

HEADLINE: EDUCATION;
Meeting on Education Starts Today in Virginia

BYLINE: By EDWARD B. FISKE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CHARLOTTESVILLE, Va., Sept. 26

 BODY:
President Bush will face the most important test yet of his pledge to be ''the education President'' here Wednesday when he assembles Cabinet members and the nation's governors to a two-day conference on education.
The meeting, to be held on the campus of the University of Virginia, is only the third time that a President has convened governors to discuss an important national issue. Theodore Roosevelt did so in 1908 to discuss the ravaging of forests, and Franklin Delano Roosevelt did so in 1933 to deal with the Depression.
Mr. Bush called for the meeting in August, explaining that it would provide an opportunity ''to strengthen our schools, to enlarge opportunities, and to improve our nation's educational performance.'' After a series of working groups Wednesday afternoon and Thursday morning, he will address the forum on Thursday morning. 
The White House has not released any agenda for the education meeting. Many governors have said, though, that they would like to see Mr. Bush use the conference to start developing a set of performance goals aimed at public schools that are struggling - many say failing - to equip young people with the reading, calculating and other skills needed in the emerging global economy.
 
Governor's Bad Dream
Gov. Bill Clinton, Democrat of Arkansas, said targets were needed to sustain the school improvement efforts made at the state level in the past six to eight years. ''I wake up at night with a bad dream that the education reform movement will go the way of the Hula Hoop, twist and Edsel,'' he said.
Education Secretary Lauro F. Cavazos said in an interview that his office was prepared to coordinate efforts to set national goals for education ''by the first of the year.''
Such targets, he said, might involve reducing the high school dropout rate, increasing the number of minority teachers and improving the math and science performance of American students compared with those from other countries. Other goals might specify what students in certain grades should know and be able to do.
At a meeting last August, the National Governors' Association set up its own study group on national goals, with Governor Clinton and Gov. Carroll A. Campbell Jr. of South Carolina, a Republican, leading it. It was not immediately clear how the governors' group, which expects to spend a year on the project, might be coordinated with Administration efforts.
 
Some Privacy Is Desired
Much of the work of the conference here will be carried out by small discussion groups of governors and Cabinet members out of the public eye. Mr. Bush will drop in on the groups.
Some governors, as well as educators around the country, expressed irritation that these meetings will be closed. But the meeting planners reply that the President wants participants to be free to express themselves candidly. Marlin Fitzwater, the White House spokesman, promised ample press access to plenary sessions, which will include reports of the working groups.
The first of the working sessions will be held Wednesday afternoon after welcoming remarks by Mr. Bush. One session each will be held on school governance, the improvement of teaching and problems like drugs that affect learning.
The second set of working sessions on Thursday morning will focus on increased parental choice in where children go to school, lifelong learning for workers and access to higher education.
 
Dinner at Monticello
Dinner Wednesday will take place at Monticello, the home of Thomas Jefferson, founder of the University of Virginia.
In addition to the setting of goals, participants are likely to push for two other outcomes of the conference, starting with an easing of Federal regulations to permit more flexible use of Federal aid to schools.
''If a school buys a computer with funds for special education, teachers cannot use it for, say, an adult literacy program in the evening,'' said Gov. Gary Carruthers of New Mexico, a Republican. ''That doesn't make any sense.''
Some governors are also pushing for a redefinition of the relationship between Washington and the states in some areas of education. They argue, for example, that the Federal Government might take responsibility for programs for prenatal nutrition in exchange for more state spending for early childhood education.
Officials say the conference is being held in Charlottesville, rather than the White House, to avoid any suggestion that education is a national responsibility - and thus something requiring lots more Federal spending.
In his decision to convene the conference but keep the agenda - and expectations of what it might accomplish - diffuse, Mr. Bush runs the political risk of being perceived as waffling on his commitment to be ''the education President.'' On the other hand, some governors argue that simply by calling attention to the national stake in improving education, the main benefit will have already been accomplished by the time the gavel comes down for the opening session.
''Most Americans still believe that when we talk about a crisis in education, we are talking about someone else's kid in some other schools,'' said Gov. Thomas H. Kean, Republican of New Jersey. ''If the Presidential summit can turn that attitude around and highlight the need for a national focus on education, that will be a major achievement.''

HEADLINE: EDUCATION;
No One Leading Football Cheers For School Team

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: MONTPELIER, Vt., Sept. 26

 BODY:
When the Montpelier High School football team takes the field this year, something will be missing: cheerleaders.
No one tried out for the cheerleading squad, said Peter Clark, the school's principal. Instead, some signed up for field hockey, track and field, and the flag team, which accompanies the band at football games.
''There are a lot more choices for young women now than when I was in high school,'' Mr. Clark said. ''I think the real issue is that there are lots of different ways for girls to get involved in school affairs. Cheerleading is not so much a way of gaining social status.''
Donna McAllister, a home economics teacher who coached the cheerleading squad last year, agreed, saying, ''The girls who did it last year are doing different things this year - working or going out for field hockey.''
In fact, several girls on last year's squad have decided to manage the football team.
About 200 girls attend Montpelier High School. Ms. McAllister says cheerleading for basketball games is still planned for winter.

LENGTH: 804 words

HEADLINE: EDUCATION;
About Education

BYLINE: By Fred M. Hechinger

 BODY:
If President Bush wants to be known as the education President, he competes with Lyndon B. Johnson, his only predecessor with that title. The way Mr. Johnson gained that distinction seems relevant to the education meeting that begins today.
The Johnson agenda recognized that education and the whole range of children's needs are inseparable. This was expressed by the simultaneous attack on inequality and poverty and on their impact on children's learning. Thus, after the Civil Rights Act of 1964, the Elementary and Secondary Education Act to send Federal money to schools with poor children, and the creation of Project Head Start in 1965, which moved the care of poor children down to the pre-school level. 
But these programs lost momentum during the Nixon Administration. The Child Development Act, the first major attempt to help children at an even younger age, was vetoed by President Richard M. Nixon in 1971. Head Start has long suffered from inadequate financing and now serves only about 20 percent of the children who are entitled to it.
This may be relevant background when the White House and the governors meet today. Many school-reform leaders say the meeting will be of little use if it deals only with fragments of the education scene. It could be most effective if it looked at the total picture - from birth to graduation, at least graduation from high school.
Experts attribute the failure of past school reforms to a lack of comprehensive policies for children and youths - the division of the first 18 years into separate fiefs: child care, pre-school education, kindergarten, elementary, junior high and high school. Each fief is left with cleaning up the unfinished business of its predecessor, thereby building up the ultimate fief of remediation.
Since the Johnson education Presidency, many crucial discoveries have been made, none more persuasive than the magic effects of sound child care and early childhood education. Better and less painful ways have been found to measure students' progress and teachers' performance than by standardized tests.
Current reformers want to put an end to running the schools through top-down orders to principals and teachers. The last attempt to improve the system through orders from above was the 1983 report, ''A Nation at Risk,'' but it at least alerted the public to the need for change.
Its recommendations have long since been superseded. Among the later blueprints are: Ernest L. Boyer's ''High School,'' published by the Carnegie Foundation for the Advancement of Teaching; ''A Nation Prepared: Teachers for the 21st Century'' and ''Turning Points,'' aimed at improving the education and development of adolescents, both by the Carnegie Corporation of New York; ''Children in Need,'' by the Committee for Economic Development, and ''The Forgotten Half: Non-College Youth in America,'' by the William T. Grant Foundation.
The National Governors' Association has supported the proposals in these works, thus sending its members well prepared to the meeting. And the White House is no longer tied to the role of scolder-in-chief held by former Education Secretary William J. Bennett.
So the meeting's promise may be the chance to use wide-ranging reform proposals to draw a road map toward continuity in education from birth on.
But the education meeting faces two powerful movements that may seem in conflict: demands, at the same time, for national standards and for greater autonomy at the schoolhouse level. In reality, these movements need not clash. It would be foolish to give principals and teachers more power without letting them know what is ultimately expected of them and their students.
Of course, national standards already exist, set by such yardsticks as the Scholastic Aptitude Tests, the National Assessment of Educational Progress and a variety of national reading tests. But many critics see these tests as either basically flawed or as dictating what teachers must teach. Reformers say: Let's have national goals but let teachers reach them in their own way. They want no national curriculum but clearly spelled-out goals for an intellectually, socially and economically sound and internationally competitive nation.
The Bush Administration's theme of letting parents choose their children's school fits easily into this pattern. If choice is to have any meaning, principals and teachers must be able to give their schools distinctive content and character. Competition can improve the system.
These issues, not educational details, are before today's and tomorrow's meeting in Virginia. The meeting itself cannot proclaim Mr. Bush the education President; it can commit the President and the governors to school reforms which, as all polls show, are demanded and supported by parents, teachers and business.

HEADLINE: FILM FESTIVAL;
A Twainlike Twist for Flint, Mich.

BYLINE: By VINCENT CANBY

 BODY:
America has an irrepressible new humorist in the tradition of Mark Twain and Artemus Ward. He is Michael Moore, the writer, producer and director of the rude and rollicking new documentary feature ''Roger and Me.'' Much in the manner of those 19th-century forebears, Mr. Moore celebrates the oddities of the American frontier, once defined by the historian F. J. Turner as ''the meeting place of savagery and civilization, where democracy is strengthened.''
The American frontier of tall tales and garrulous fables has long since been developed into extinction. Mr. Moore's frontier is his hometown, Flint, Mich., population 150,000, the birthplace of General Motors. As a result of the closing of various G.M. plants and the elimination of 40,000 jobs, Flint has become one of the more embarrassing eyesores in the landscape of what is supposed to be a booming American economy. 
All sorts of attempts have been made to save Flint. ''Just when things were beginning to look bleak,'' Mr. Moore recalls on the soundtrack, ''Ronald Reagan arrived in Flint and took 12 workers out for a pizza.'' Somebody walked off with the pizzeria's cash register, though it's unclear whether the two events were connected.
When Money magazine named Flint ''the worst place to live in America,'' ABC planned to devote an entire ''Nightline'' show to the subject. The program was canceled at the last minute. The television power truck had been stolen.
Depressing figures and nutty anecdotes bubble out of ''Roger and Me'' nonstop, leaving the frequently appalled audience roaring with laughter, the kind of response that Twain would cherish.
''Roger and Me,'' which does not yet have a commercial distributor, will be shown at the New York Film Festival today at 9:30 P.M. and tomorrow at 6:15 P.M.
The film takes its title from Mr. Moore's attempts to reach Roger Smith, the G.M. chairman. Mr. Moore's plan is to take Mr. Smith on a tour of Flint and to persuade him of G.M.'s responsibility in attending to the problems of the unemployed. A modest goal, it seems, though Mr. Moore knows as well as anybody that it's not a goal that stands any chance of being achieved. It is, however, a wonderful premise for an angry, biased, witty movie.
The portly, beady-eyed Mr. Moore, as sharp and sophisticated a documentary film maker as has come on the scene in years, manifests a down-home wonder at the world's idiocies. With a toothpick stuck in the corner of his mouth, wearing a down jacket, jeans and the sort of cap that should have the name of a feedlot on it, he stalks the G.M. chairman in the assorted sanctuaries of the seriously rich and powerful.
He shows up at the G.M. offices in Detroit, where he has some hilarious, comparatively polite arguments with security guards and public relations people, who bite their lips as they try desperately to to hang onto their well-paid cool.
He is hustled out of both the Grosse Point Yacht Club and the Detroit Athletic Club. He attends the annual G.M. stockholders' meeting and successfully gets the microphone, only to be cut off. On the dais, Mr. Smith brags to an associate about the fleetness with which he managed to avoid the embarrassment, not realizing that his microphone is still on.
''Roger and Me'' is stuffed with such remarkable ''found'' moments, which are not really found at all. They may be unplanned, but only a film maker thoroughly at ease with his subject, and aware of various possibilities, is going to be in a position to find those moments.
They include Mr. Moore's not terribly warm encounter with Mr. Smith at a reception following the G.M. chairman's annual ''Christmas message,'' a scene cross-cut with the Christmas Eve eviction of an unemployed G.M. worker.
To save their city after the G.M. pullout, the Flint city fathers approve a series of schemes that sound as if they'd come out of the head of Evelyn Waugh, reborn as a Flint booster. They spend $13 million to build a Hyatt Regency Hotel and $100 million or so more for a theme park called AutoWorld, both of which quickly go broke. They attempt to boost morale by bringing in Pat Boone and Anita Bryant to perform for the tired masses. The television evangelist the Rev. Robert Schuller is paid a reported $20,000 to tell his audience, ''You can turn your hurt into a halo.''
After photographing a radiant Miss Michigan in a Flint parade waving to crowds standing in front of closed stores, Mr. Moore tries to get her reaction to Flint's ever-present poverty. Her smile vanishes as she tries to show concern. Does she have any message for the people of Flint? The smile returns. ''Just keep your fingers crossed for me as I go for the gold!'' Miss Michigan did, indeed, become Miss America that year (1988).
Mr. Moore is clearly someone who believes that poverty and corporate neglect are sins, and he doesn't pull his punches. He doesn't appeal to easy sentiment. He demolishes the television personality Bob Eubanks, of ''The Newlywed Game,'' just by letting him talk on and on.
Mr. Moore makes no attempt to be fair. Playing fair is for college football. In social criticism, anything goes, as it goes triumphantly in ''Roger and Me.''

HEADLINE: U.S. and Its Allies Sell Dollars

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER; Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
The central banks of the leading industrial democracies sold dollars aggressively again today but the dollar held its ground, after falling sharply on Monday.
The dollar fell slightly today against the West German mark and some other currencies and rose against the yen. The dollar's resilience signals persistent underlying support despite the new coordinated effort to drive it down. And it may mean, traders said, that changes in interest rates, along with more intervention, will be needed to cap the dollar's rise. 
The United States and its economic allies began their concerted intervention Monday after stating on Saturday that the dollar was too strong.
 
Drops Against Mark
The dollar ended today at 1.8918 West German marks and 142.35 yen in New York, down from 1.8992 marks Monday but up from 142.15 yen. But the currency is still down six pfennigs and is almost four yen lower than it was on Friday, before the communique was issued by the Group of Seven - the United States, Japan, West Germany, Britain, France, Italy and Canada.
The intervention came as the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank formally opened their annual meeting here and as bankers, finance ministers and other officials focused on the debt problems of the world's developing nations.
At a reception he hosted today, President Bush called on the 65 bankers and various foreign officials present to support the plan advanced by Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady to alleviate third world debt burdens.
''He strongly urged adherence to the Brady plan,'' Alixe Glen, a White House spokeswoman, said tonight. Mr. Bush addresses the I.M.F. meeting on Wednesday.
 
Sign of Progress
In one development that reflected some progress, a member of the delegation from Argentina, said an agreement with the I.M.F. on a standby loan of about $1.5 billion over 18 months ''is almost ready.'' He said signing could come this week.
The agreement would be another major step in Argentina's effort to overhaul its economy and negotiate a debt reduction and restructing deal with its commercial bankers.
Argentina has been unable to get financial aid since it stopped making interest and principal payments on most of its debt in April of 1988 and is about $4.4 billion in arrears.
The new President, Carlos Saul Menem, initiated an economic plan after his inauguration in July that will sell state-owned businesses, slash Government subsidies, improve tax collection, cut the budget deficit and restrain wages and prices.
An agreement with the I.M.F. would allow the release of $150 million from the World Bank and open the door to a longer-term agreement with the fund. The fact that the I.M.F. also approved of the economic reform program would set the stage for new debt negotiations.
Venezuela said it had received a $600 million interim loan from 15 commercial banks that would allow it to bring its interest payments up to date by the end of 1989.
Its Government will also draw $400 million from its reserves to make interest payments. At present, Venezuela is about $700 million behind in payments, said Miguel Rodriguez Fandeo, the Minister of Planning who is in charge of the debt negotiations.
The interim loan and Venezuela's decision to drop its demand for a 50 percent across-the-board debt reduction should revive negotiations over the restructuring of its $21 billion in debt to commercial banks.
At a news conference announcing the change in the proposal, Mr. Rodriquez said the country would offer an array of debt-reduction alternatives ranging from a discount of 60 percent to below 35 percent. The new plan will also include an option for new loans.
Sir Kit McMahon, the chairman of Midland Bank of Britain and a member of the bank advisory committee negotiating with Venezuela, praised the move. ''I'm glad they have dropped their initial demand,'' he said.
In another development, Mexican officials, including Finance Minister Pedro Aspe, met with more than 300 bankers to discuss the country's recently concluded debt reduction and restructuring plan. Under the agreement with a committee representing creditor banks, each bank must decide whether to accept a 35 percent reduction in the value of existing loans or provide new loans.
In foreign exchange trading today, the central bank intervention began in Tokyo and continued in Europe and New York.
Traders said as many as 10 central banks, including the Bundesbank of West Germany, the Federal Reserve, the Bank of Japan and the Bank of England, joined in the dollar-selling, which one trader said might have totaled $1.5 billion.
But the dollar did not fall immediately. It rose at first in Tokyo. After intervention in Europe and in New York, the dollar fell below the key 1.90 mark level, which is believed to be the ceiling the United States and its allies had set for the dollar. The dollar also fell against the yen in New York but rebounded to end higher.
''I think they mean it,'' said Liliana Nealon, vice president of foreign exchange at the Union Bank of Switzerland in New York. ''The issue is will they continue and will they couple it with a change in interest rates.''

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
Olympians Play, U.S. TV Pays

BYLINE: By Peter Passell

 BODY:
ABC paid $309 million for broadcast rights to the 1988 winter Olympic Games in Calgary, Alberta. Western European television paid a total of $5.7 million.
No, that is not a misprint: The United States, with roughly the same income and fewer viewers than Europe, forked over 50 times as much money to the International Olympic Committee.
In the latest issue of The Brookings Institution Review, Robert Z. Lawrence and Jeffrey D. Pellegrom explain why the American networks always pay an olympian share of the Olympic television bill. More important, the two Brookings researchers offer a clever plan to right the balance - and in the process raise hundreds of millions to support amateur athletics in the United States. 
According to the International Olympic Committee, there is no conspiracy to force American television to underwrite most of the cost of the Games. The committee negotiates the best deal it can with the networks in each country. American rights have simply proved to be far more valuable because the Games are shown here on commercial television. In Japan and Europe, most Olympic broadcasts are commercial-free.
Mr. Lawrence is unconvinced. ''When it comes to the rest of the world,'' he says, ''the I.O.C. extends its hand with a kid glove rather than a tight fist.''
In any event, Mr. Lawrence argues, the key to understanding why United States networks shell out so much lies in the bargaining process. Western Europe negotiates through a single entity, the European Broadcast Union. Once the E.B.U. cuts a deal, it apportions the cost among its members.
The three American networks, by contrast, compete with each other for rights. Bidding for the 1988 winter Games pushed the price far above the price expected by the International Olympic Committee. And in only slightly less frenzied competition, NBC paid $300 million for the 1988 summer Games in Seoul.
One consequence, Mr. Lawrence asserts, is that American viewers are stuck with advertising overkill. In what proved to be an unsuccessful attempt to recoup its costs, NBC larded 179 hours of summer coverage in 1988 with 5,000 commercials. ABC, similarly pressed in the winter Games, chose to interrupt live hockey coverage in order to slip in more advertising.
Mr. Lawrence would increase American bargaining leverage by adopting a variation of the European system. Washington - or the U.S. Olympic Committee - would set broadcast standards for the Games, limiting commercial time and forbidding interruption of live action. Rights would be auctioned to the highest bidder, with the cash going to the U.S.O.C. The Committee would then bargain directly on the amount paid to the I.O.C. During some transition period, Mr. Lawrence reckons, the United States might cover half the total television bill. Thereafter, he argues, America ought to hold out for a formula in which payments were tied to population, per capita income and prime time viewing hours. The millions left over after the U.S. Olympic Committee paid its share would be funneled into training for American athletes.
The proposal has already received a potent political boost in the form of an endorsement in principle from Bill Bradley, a former Olympic athlete and - more important - a senior member of the Senate Finance Committee.
Any plan that imposed legal limits on commercial time would buck a 10-year trend toward deregulation and would certainly be opposed by the owners of television stations. If that opposition proved unshakable, Mr. Lawrence would opt for a less ambitious auction alternative that placed no limits on commercials but did capture the bulk of television revenues for American athletes.
Stripped to basics, Mr. Lawrence notes, such an approach would not actually require the involvement of the Government or even much help from the U.S. Olympic Committee. If, for example, the networks merely pledged in advance to match, dollar for dollar, any payments to the International Olympic Committee with donations to U.S. athletes, their maximum potential bids for television rights would automatically be halved.
Indeed, the act of threatening to circumscribe competition between the networks would increase American bargaining leverage.
The International Olympic Committee has already agreed to rebate 10 percent of U.S. television revenues from the 1992 and 1994 Games. And in negotiations set to begin next month on the percentage to be returned to the U.S. Olympic Committee in 1996, the I.O.C. might respond to pressure for tough, unilateral action by the United States by offering a substantially larger share of the pie.

PUB-DATE: September 27, 1989

SECTION: Section D; Page 8, Column 3; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 523 words

HEADLINE: Dow Closes Up a Bit After Losing Big Gain

BYLINE: By PHILLIP H. WIGGINS

 BODY:
Stocks ended slightly higher yesterday after losing substantial early gains on a late wave of futures-related trading.
Fears that a possible rise in West German interest rates might spark similar moves by Japan and other major economic powers also contributed to the selloff, traders said.
The Dow Jones industrial average finished 4.75 points ahead, at 2,663.94 points, after being up about 22 points at midday, virtually erasing its 22.42-point drop on Monday. 
But in the last hour before the close, arbitragers sold shares represented in the Standard & Poor's 500 index and simultaneously bought futures based on that index. This was done because the futures toward the close were cheaper than the stocks in the index.
The S.& P. index finished up 0.10, to 344.33, while the spot, or nearest, futures on this index, December, closed at 350.25, up 1.30. The New York Stock Exchange composite index added 0.19, to 191.47.
 
Volume Climbs
Advances on the New York Stock Exchange outnumbered declines by 740 to 634. Volume was moderate, but at 158.4 million shares was solidly ahead of the 121.1 million that exchanged hands on Monday.
''The dollar's stabilization at slightly lower levels provided a trampoline for equities throughout most of the day,'' said Robert H. Stovall, president of Stovall/21st Advisers in New York.
''The bond market rallied off of the U.S. currency's firmness but later weakened after a senior German official said the West German central bank was considering a small interest rate rise,'' he added.
Mr. Stovall pointed out that stocks that had been damaged by Hurricane Hugo recovered some of their recent losses. The Travelers Corporation rose 7/8, to 40 3/4, and Aetna Life and Casualty was up 3/8, to 56 1/4. Sears, Roebuck, which also owns an insurer, Allstate, was unchanged at 42 1/2.
Among individual issues, the spotlight remained on Columbia Pictures, which slipped 1/8, to 26 1/4, on a volume-leading total of nearly 4.2 million shares. The entertainment company is expected to accept an offer worth more than $3 billion from the Sony Corporation, although neither concern would comment on details. Sony rose 2 1/8, to 61 5/8.
Coca-Cola, which owns 49 percent of Columbia and stands to gain substantial cash that might be used for expansion overseas or share repurchases, rose 1 1/8, to 64 1/8.
 
Japanese Stocks Gain
Other Japanese stocks benefited for the second straight day from the Columbia Pictures activity. Matsushita Electric jumped 3 1/2, to 167 1/2, and the Kyocera Corporation added 2 3/4, to 82.
In a sympathetic reaction and on substantial Japanese buying, several foreign funds were among the bigger net gainers. The Spain Fund soared 12 points, to 38 3/4; India Growth Fund jumped 3 3/8, to 19 3/4; Thai Fund rose 3 1/2, to 26 1/2, and Germany Fund rose 3, to 15.
Paramount Communications, which Harold Vogel, an analyst at Merrill Lynch, rated yesterday as a long-term buy, was up 1 1/2, to 56 1/4.
On the American Stock Exchange, the Amex market value index added 0.79, to 382.19, while in over-the-counter trading, Nasdaq advanced 1.13 points, to 467.84.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
U.S. Issues Rebound Somewhat

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities rose modestly yesterday, rebounding from Monday's big selloff.
The gains, which were recorded in fairly light trading, were started by better-than-anticipated demand for $9.75 billion worth of two-year Treasury notes that were auctioned yesterday.
''The yield on the two-year backed up enough so that investors came back,'' said William M. Brachfeld, an executive vice president at Daiwa Securities America Inc.
 
Japanese Bids Scant
Although rumors circulated through the market that one Japanese client had bid for $1 billion of the securities, Mr. Brachfeld said bids placed through Japanese firms for the new notes were light. 
''We have seen a backup in the market, and conceivably we could see some more,'' he said. ''If the economy is still chugging along and the Fed is not going to ease policy, then yields might have to back up a bit more in order to deal with the new supply that still has to be sold.''
At the auction, the two-year notes were sold at an average yield of 8.39 percent, the highest yield level since the two-year note sale in May and slightly above the average 8.37 percent yield on two-year notes auctioned on Aug. 22.
Today the Treasury is expected to sell $7.75 billion worth of four-year notes. The notes were offered on a when-issued basis late yesterday at a price to yield 8.32 percent.
Mr. Brachfeld and other market participants said foreign investors were likely to show more interest in the four-year issue.
 
Durables Report Cited
Most market participants had little reaction to the news that durable goods orders had posted a bigger-than-expected 3.8 percent increase in August. Some analysts suggested the report may have warranted more attention than it got.
''Conditions at most durable goods industries are moving from being soft to clear-cut weakness,'' said Donald J. Fine, chief market analyst at the Chase Manhattan Corporation. ''This report does not bode well for the manufacturing sector, and it bolsters the argument that the Fed should lower interest rates to help manufacturing.''
Prices of Treasury bonds and longer-term notes posted the biggest gains yesterday, recouping at least some of Monday's losses. By late in the day, the Treasury's 8 1/8 percent bonds were offered at a price of 98 3/4, up 1/2 of a point, or $5 for each $1,000 face amount of securities, to yield 8.23 percent. Monday's big selloff pushed yields on the closely watched bellwether bond above 8.25 percent for the first time since late June. 10-Year Notes at 8.27% Among secondary Treasury note issues, the 8 percent 10-year notes were offered at 98 5/32, up 1/4 of a point, to yield 8.27 percent. And the 8 1/4 percent five-year notes were offered at 99 27/32, up 5/32, to yield 8.27 percent.
Short-term Treasury bills were mixed. In late trading, three-month bills were offered at a discount rate of 7.75 percent, up five basis points, or hundredths of a point. Six-month bills were offered at 7.79 percent, up two basis points. Meanwhile, one-year bill rates dipped by one basis point, to a late offered rate of 7.71 percent.
Elsewhere, investors in the high-yield ''junk bond'' market snapped up a $375 million offering of Petrolane Gas Service. The senior subordinated debentures, which mature in 2001, were priced at par, or $100, to yield 13.25 percent. By late afternoon, commitments for the entire issue had been received.
The First Boston Corporation acted as lead manager for the underwriting, which is rated B-2 by Moody's Investors Service and B by the Standard & Poor's Corporation.
A $125 million offering of Petrolane senior secured notes was pre-placed, and not reoffered.
 
Corporate Activity Light
Activity in the secondary market for investment-grade corporate bonds was light yesterday, dealers said, with prices of most issues down by 1/8 of a point to 1/4 of a point.
Prices of secondary tax-exempt municipal bonds ended a two-week slide as prices of most issues rose by 1/2 of a point to 5/8 of a point.
The rally in the municipal market occurred despite the offering of a slew of new issues, including a $175 negotiated deal for the San Diego County Regional Transportation Commission.
The issue includes serial bonds with maturities ranging from 1991 to 1999. The bonds were preliminarily priced with coupons from 6.30 percent in 1991 to 7.10 percent in 1999.
First Boston acted as lead manager for the transaction, which is rated A-1 by Moody's and A+ by S.&P.

HEADLINE: FINANCE/NEW ISSUES;
General Obligation Bonds Priced by New York City

 BODY:
New York City yesterday tentatively priced $700 million in general obligation bonds through a group of underwriters led by Goldman, Sachs & Company.
The offering is the second for the city in its current fiscal year, which ends June 30. The first was a $750 million general obligation sale in mid-July. Since then, the cost of borrowing has increased and interest rates on tax-exempt issues have risen about one-half of a percentage point.
Yields offered in yesterday's issue include 6.40 percent on bonds maturing in 1990, 7 percent for 1994 maturities, 7.40 percent for 1999 and 7.70 percent for 2003 and 2004.
The financing includes a group of A-series bonds, which are insured by the Financial Guaranty Insurance Company, with maturities running from 2005 to 2008 and priced to yield 7.25 percent. There is also a series of B bonds, maturing from 2009 to 2015 at a price to yield 7.80 percent, and a group of C bonds with final maturites running from 2016 to 2019 and priced to yield 7.75 percent.
Underwriters said that becuase bonds in group A were insured by the F.G.I.C., they were rated triple-A by Moody's Investors Service and the Standard & Poor's Corporation. All other bonds carry an A rating by Moody's and an A- rating by S.&P. For New York City residents, the bonds are exempt from local, state and Federal taxes.
The yields being offered in this bond issue range anywhere from 25 to 70 one-hundredths of a percentage point higher than those initially offered at the July sale. The Bond Buyer's weekly index of 20 actively traded general obligation bond issues is currently at 7.33 percent, compared with 6.92 percent in mid-July.

HEADLINE: Durables Orders Jump 3.8%

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
Durable goods orders jumped a surprising 3.8 percent in August for the best gain in eight months, the Commerce Department said today, but fresh signs of weakness in the industrial sector crept into the monthly report.
Led by rising orders for transportation goods and non-electrical machinery, orders for durable goods reached a seasonally adjusted $126.7 billion, up from $122.1 billion in July.
Although the rise appeared healthy on the surface, economists said manufacturers were now receiving far fewer orders than they were shipping, signaling slower growth for United States industry ahead. 
 
'On the Soft Side'
''Over all, the report characterizes the durables sector as being on the soft side,'' said Jerry Zukoski, an economist with the Paine Webber Group.
Products built to last three years or more, durable goods ranging from baby carriages to battleships, represent a key part of the nation's manufacturing sector and are closely watched as an indicator of the economy's health.
The rise in durable goods orders was broad based, with gains in all sectors except for metals and electrical machinery, such as communication equipment.
The August gain was led by a 9.2 percent rise in machinery orders, where the increase was broad based. The 6.7 percent gain in transportation equipment was pushed up by autos, which more than offset a decline in aircraft and parts.
 
Shipments Rise 9.5%
Shipments of durable goods also rose a strong 9.5 percent after a 3.7 percent fall in July.
But in a troubling sign, unfilled orders fell by three-tenths of 1 percent, the first decline in that category since February 1987. The decline showed manufacturers were failing to win enough new orders to sustain the current robust shipment rate.
''New orders are not keeping pace with shipments, and thus some weakening is occurring in the industrial sector,'' said Cynthia Latta, an economist at Data Resources.
Economists are largely convinced the Federal Reserve Board has engineered a ''soft landing'' of moderate growth and low inflation, and the durable goods report gives further evidence that the economy has avoided a recession so far.
 
Home Sales Rebounding
Despite the apparent industrial slowdown, employment growth remains strong, personal income and consumption are healthy, home sales are rebounding and inflation has been tamed by falling gasoline and lower food prices.
The National Association of Business Economists projected on Monday that the current economic growth cycle would continue through next year and that a recession was likely to be avoided until 1991 or beyond.
Economists said the slump in new orders might be due to the higher dollar, which has made it more difficult for American manufacturers to continue achieving large export gains.
The Group of Seven industrialized democracies - the United States, West Germany, France, Britain, Japan, Canada and Italy - pledged at meetings held over the weekend to seek a lower dollar to correct the world's trade imbalances, especially the gaping American trade deficit. The dollar has since weakened after a heavy round of dollar sales.
Excluding military-related orders, which can fluctuate wildly from month to month, durable goods orders rose 3.9 percent in August, the department said. Orders for military capital goods rose 1.9 percent.

HEADLINE: LOSS OF $4 BILLION IS FOUND IN AUDIT OF MORTGAGE FUND

BYLINE: By JEFF GERTH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
An independent audit of several Federal funds that insure mortgages shows that they had a record loss of $4.2 billion last year.
The audit, released today at a Senate hearing, suggests that the troubles with the Federal Housing Administration funds are far more serious than had been thought. And the losses that the audit found at the F.H.A., part of the Department of Housing and Urban Development, provide the most costly indication yet of management problems at the department in the Reagan Administration.
Comptroller General Charles A. Bowsher, who released the new audit, said the F.H.A. losses would significantly affect the Federal budget deficit. He said Congress would have to appropriate at least $4 billion to $5 billion over the next few years to make up for losses in two F.H.A. insurance funds that rely on Congressional subsidies. 
 
Two Broad Problems
Congressional and H.U.D. investigations of the department's workings in the Reagan years have uncovered two broad problems: the more publicized political manipulation of housing money and the more costly mismanagement of housing programs. Today a former top assistant to former Housing Secretary Samuel R. Pierce Jr. refused to answer questions from a House panel investigating these problems. [Page A18.] Mr. Bowsher said the audit provided considerable evidence that H.U.D. did not properly oversee the work of the private lenders who gained significant control over loans in the early years of the Reagan Administration. The Administration promoted the division of risk between private lenders and the Government as part of its move to shift more Government functions to private contractors. But the Government retained most of the risk.
Investigators have said that many of the F.H.A. losses stemmed from some lenders' practice of vastly overvaluing property that was being mortgaged. Mr. Bowsher, who heads the General Accounting Office, Congress's investigative arm, also attributed some of the losses to economic downturns, especially in the Southwest, that caused a rising number of defaults. He added that a sluggish housing market cut the F.H.A.'s premium income.
 
Audit by Independent Firm
The audit was performed for the General Accounting Office by the accounting firm Price Waterhouse.
Only in the last two years have the F.H.A.'s financial statments been audited by an independent accounting firm. Both audits, conducted by Price Waterhouse, have uncovered large losses, most of them in 1988.
The audits were requested by Congress even before the problems at H.U.D. were revealed.
 
Difference in Loss Figures
The latest audit's findings are starkly different from what the F.H.A. itself has reported: while Price Waterhouse found a $4.2 billion loss for 1988, the F.H.A.'s audit found a loss of $858 million. A Price Waterhouse auditor attributed the difference in the figures to varying auditing techniques rather than an attempt by the F.H.A. to cover up the extent of its losses.
The Price Waterhouse audit found a dramatic decline in the value of the Government's equity, or stake in all of its funds combined. Just two years ago the equity was more than $3 billion, but, according to the new audit, there is now a deficit of $2.9 billion.
This $6 billion deterioration ''starts to show where the dollars went'' under H.U.D. management in the Reagan era, Mr. Bowsher said in an interview after his testimony before the Senate Banking Subcommittee on Housing and Urban Affairs.
 
Growth in Defaults Hits Peak
Mr. Bowsher offered some good news from the audit findings, namely that the growth rate in defaults seems to have peaked in 1988. But he tempered that by noting there will be more defaults in the future and the amount of 1988 losses reported in the audit may turn out to be a conservative figure.
The Price Waterhouse audit raises the possibility that there may be additional losses beyond what is contained in its report. The firm declined to give an unqualified opinion as to the accuracy of F.H.A.'s financial statements because of continuing investigations into possible diversions of F.H.A. money and misstatements of foreclosed porperties held in inventory.
Today's developments came as Congress is considering proposals to expand F.H.A.'s main insurance fund, which insures mortgages for single-family houses. Mr. Bowsher told the committee that a preliminary analysis showed that lowering downpayment requirements ''would be likely to increase F.H.A.'s claims and losses.''
Mr. Bowsher praised current efforts to reform the F.H.A. The head of the F.H.A., C. Austin Fitts, an Assistant Secretary of H.U.D., told the subcommittee that while the losses were ''extremely heavy,'' they were ''manageable and fixable and we're committed to solving the problems.''
 
Breakdown of Losses
The accounting firm's audit gave this breakdown of the F.H.A.'s losses:
* The mutual mortgage insurance fund, which is self-sustaining and F.H.A.'s largest and most conservative fund, incurred a loss of $1.4 billion in 1988, causing its equity to drop to $1.8 billion.
* The general insurance fund, which requires subsidies from Congress, incurred a loss of $2.6 billion, causing its equity, which was already negative, to worsen to a deficit of $3.1 billion. The largest loss in this fund was $960 million for the co-insurance program, which was overwhelmed by mismanagement. Another $275 million was set aside for losses in a hospital insurance program.
* The special risk insurance fund suffered losses of $300 million. The deficit in this fund, which also requires subsidies from Congress, widened to $1.6 billion.
Tom Craren, a partner at Price Waterhouse who supervised the audit, told the panel that the F.H.A.'s loss figure of $858 million was determined by looking at actual cash outlays to cover defaults, while the Price Waterhouse audit ''tried to look for real incidents of loss'' that more realistically reflect required ''outflows of cash in the future.''

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL AID SOUGHT FOR POLES

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
President Bush called for greater international economic assistance for the new Government of Poland today, pledging that the United States would be ''out in front of this effort'' to insure success. But he offered few details about what the United States would do.
His speech, to the annual meeting of the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank, signaled a reinforced Administration effort to help officials of the new Polish Government, who have said that they will move toward freer markets.
The address was also an attempt to rebut accusations that the Administration help to the Poles has so far been paltry. The Administration is supporting a $169 million package of economic and food assistance that many in Congress deem inadequate. 
Last week, the Senate Foreign Relations Committee recommended passage of a $1.2 billion package of American aid for Poland and Hungary, and on Tuesday, the executive commission of the European Community proposed a separate $660 million program for the two countries.
 
Few Specifics Provided
Speaking to finance officials from the governments of the two international institutions, President Bush urged both lending agencies, which help countries carry out free-market economic reforms, to ''work rapidly with Poland.'' But he laid out no specific initiatives. ''In light of clearly growing needs, the recent accession of a Solidarity-led Government, and our self-evident stake in its success, we must do more,'' Mr. Bush said. Administration officials said that they had considered using the speech today to announce some specific new aid programs, but that no consensus had emerged, chiefly because of Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady's reluctance to move forward until Polish economic reforms are in place. Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d has been the principal proponent of greater aid. The 20-minute speech was politely received. It was not interrupted with applause, but Mr. Bush received a 25-second ovation.
 
Polish Finance Chief Speaks
Meanwhile, Poland provided fresh details today on the kind of assistance it wants. Leszek Balcerowicz, the Polish Finance Minister, said he had asked his counterparts in the seven leading industrial countries for $500 million in aid to cover an expected payments deficit in the fourth quarter that could keep Poland from importing essential industrial products, food and medicine.
Mr. Balcerowicz said Poland was asking for a $1 billion stabilization fund to help defend the exchange rate of the zloty and to make the currency convertible into Western currencies. The zloty is now practically worthless in much of the world.
The President praised work in the International Monetary Fund to increase its capital, which could be used to support economic change in Eastern Europe. ''The United States recognizes that the I.M.F. must have adequate resources to fulfill its critical role,'' he declared.
The fund, which helps countries meet immediate debts, is now considering assistance for Poland that, over three years, could total as much as $2.5 billion. Its managing director, Michel Camdessus, a former official of the French central bank, is expected to visit Warsaw next month.
 
A Big First Step
The World Bank, which helps countries with longer-term development projects like fertilizer plants and dock facilities, intends to lend Poland $300 million as the first part of a major commitment. The bank's International Finance Corporation affiliate, which supports private enterprise, already has a modest program, helping a Polish agricultural cooperative, and has recently offered to help Poland create a new financial system.
The President also called for more collaboration to ease debt burdens on third-world countries and remove trade barriers. With specific reference to the growing reluctance of commercial banks to lend money to third-world countries, he said that banks ''have a special role'' in furthering the Administration's debt strategy.
The strategy calls for commercial banks to forgive some existing loans and continue to provide new loans to debtor countries. Large American banks have recently been writing off some third-world debt and taking losses, raising concerns that they will not soon want to make new loans.
 
Bush Anticipates Progress
The President defended the strategy, saying that it has provided hope for countries like Mexico and the Philippines, which have agreements with the banks on debt-reduction plans, and urged that the effort be broadened.
Treasury Secretary Brady, in his speech, said ''unrealistic expectations'' among banks and debtor countries were among the main problems that the debt strategy aims to meet. He also disclosed that Costa Rica, Venezuela, Morocco, Uruguay and Chile are also seeking to negotiate debt-reduction packages.
In commenting on the Polish reform plan, Finance Minister Balcerowicz said that Poland was still hoping to maintain a stable trading relationship with the Soviet Union, especially in the supply of raw material. ''We are neighbors. There are advantages to both sides to trade,'' he said.
He also said that that one important feature of the reform will be the auction of some state enterprises to private businessmen. Such auctions, he added, would provide the Polish Treasury with badly neded funds.

HEADLINE: Latest Pentagon Report Lifts Shadow of Soviet Menace, a Bit

BYLINE: By ANDREW ROSENTHAL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
It is not quite a paean to perestroika. But the Pentagon issued a new report on the Soviet military today that is a far cry from the catalogue of the Soviet empire's evils published annually by the Reagan Administration - and even a bit more muted than the public statements of Defense Secretary Dick Cheney.
Gone are blurry satellite photos of Soviet radar installations and drawings of submarines firing nuclear missiles. Instead, a photo of smiling Soviet soldiers leaving Afghanistan is on the cover of ''Soviet Military Power 1989.'' 
Talk of ''world domination'' has been soberly rewritten as the Soviet Union ''striving to increase its power and influence at the expense of the West.'' Even the subtitle, ''Prospects for Change,'' shows how the Pentagon's view of the Soviet Union has changed since 1988, when the book was subtitled, ''An Assessment of the Threat.''
It also is not nearly as gloomy about the political future of President Mikhail S. Gorbachev as Mr. Cheney was when he got in trouble with President Bush for suggesting that the Soviet President might not last long.
 
A Warning by Cheney
To be sure, the book says that the Soviet military remains a ''major threat'' to American security and that the extent of Mr. Gorbachev's changes are still unproven. Mr. Cheney, who is fighting off budget cuts on Capitol Hill, made a point of warning today that Soviet nuclear missiles ''threaten the very survival of the United States.''
In remarks to reporters today, President Bush said: ''I want to have a prudent defense policy. I don't want to do something naive or silly in defense just because we are working more closely with the Soviets.''
Some critics of the Pentagon said the new edition of ''Soviet Military Power'' contains inaccuracies and exaggerated figures that they said were intended to help get more money for military programs.
But, just as milder commentary on the United States in the Soviet press reflects the Kremlin's changing view of Washington, the new book is a graphic illustration of an evolution that has come hard to the Pentagon, where military planners once cut their teeth on ''the Soviet menace.''
Now, the Soviet military has direct contacts with the American military and arms control talks are moving forward. Next week, Mr. Cheney will be host to the Soviet Defense Minister, Dmitri T. Yazov, at the Pentagon.
 
Book Delayed for Months
Still, officials said there was a struggle over the 1989 book, which was delayed for several months and reflected compromises between those who wanted to recognize the changes in the Soviet Union and those who feared the Defense Department might appear naively optimistic.
There also was some concern that a too-rosy analysis might encourage lawmakers who want to cut money for weapons programs intended primarily to deter Soviet force.
''We are faced with a situtation in which Congress seems to be determined to take cuts in the defense budget now in anticipation of a kinder, gentler Soviet Union,'' Mr. Cheney said in releasing the new book.
Phillip A. Karber, vice president of the B.D.M. Corporation, a concern that does Pentagon consulting work, said the new book ''is a historic document in that it reflects the new ambiguity.''
''Two years from now,'' he said, ''if Gorbachev succeeds, we are going to look like a bunch of pikers for not forecasting how radical his changes turned out to be. If he fails, all of our hopes are going to look terribly naive. No matter what we say now, it's probably going to be wrong, so the ambiguous position is probably the most realistic.''
 
First Publsihed in 1981
''Soviet Military Power'' was first published in 1981 by Defense Secretary Caspar W. Weinberger. Over the years, it has stressed different aspects of military technology, depending on the hot issues of the day - space weapons, conventional armed forces in Europe, or, this year, long-range nuclear weapons.
''In 1981, all the public could think about was deployment of American medium-range missiles in Europe,'' said Richard Perle, who helped create the book as Assistant Secretary for International Security Policy. ''At a NATO meeting, we gave the ministers a briefing on Soviet capabilities and they pleaded with Weinberger to free up some of the information.''
The book rapidly became part of the Soviet-American debate, spawning Moscow's own version, titled ''Whence the Threat to Peace.''
The writing style heated up over the years, along with the Soviet-American war of words. In 1984, asked to sum up the report's findings, Mr. Weinberger said, ''World domination, it's that simple.''
Even in 1988, Soviet reforms were given little credence. The Soviets ''experience feelings of inferiority when they are not accepted as full participants in world affairs,'' the book said.
''Their national goals and objectives require that the Soviets expand their military power and political influence beyond their own borders to insure their security and satisfy their imperial urges.''
 
A Book of Facts
Mr. Perle disputed the suggestion that such alarming comments were intended to drum up Congressional support for big military budgets, but he added, ''It started out as a book of facts and that's what it should have remained, without the editorial comment.''
This year, Mr. Cheney's introduction warned that the United States ''cannot react unilaterally to Soviet initiatives that are not yet implemented or to proposals, which, if implemented, can easily be reversed.''
But he also spoke of the ''dramatic changes'' under way in the Soviet Union. The book is replete with upbeat, if cautious, assessments of those reforms.
At one point, it says: ''At present, the Soviets do not seem to anticipate war in Europe in the near future and are stressing political, diplomatic and economic alternatives to achieve their strategic goals.''
Mr. Cheney sought to play down the change in the book's tone, simply smiling when asked what had happened to the old references to ''world domination.''
''The publication I will bear responsibility for is the one we put out today,'' he said. ''I take no responsibility for ones that were put out before.''
