HEADLINE: East Europe: Superpowers Quietly Shake Hands

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN

 BODY:
The Bush Administration and President Mikhail S. Gorbachev of the Soviet Union appear to have come to at least a tacit understanding about how their countries should relate to the Eastern Europe now emerging from the cold war.
Judging by recent remarks by the Soviet Foreign Minister, Eduard A. Shevardnadze, and Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d, both countries seem for the moment to feel they can pursue their interests in a rapidly evolving Eastern Europe without antagonizing the other or stifling the process of change there.
The United States has signaled Mr. Gorbachev that it will not exploit the loosening of Moscow's grip over countries like Poland and Hungary to entice them out of the Warsaw Pact or, through either words or deeds, foment anti-Soviet activities. As President Bush put it, Washington will aid the Eastern Europeans without poking ''a stick'' into Moscow's eye. 
 
'Least Worst Option' for Moscow
At the same time, the Soviets have signaled Washington that they intend to allow Eastern Europe to take a more liberalized economic and political course, with whatever financial aid the West wants to provide, as long as the process of change there does not threaten either the solidity of the Warsaw Pact or the security of the Soviet Union.
''I think there is such a tacit undertanding, and I think the Bush Administration has handled it quite deftly,'' said Michael Mandelbaum, an expert on Eastern Europe at the Council on Foreign Relations.
''We must keep in mind, though, that the Soviets have not come to such an understanding for our sake. It is the least worst option for them, because the cost - both to their own reform programs and physically - of keeping Poland and Hungary in line are too great. Yet, having made that calculation, Gorbachev wanted to be assured that the United States was not going to rush into Poland and rope it into NATO. It is now clear that we have given the Soviets that assurance.''
Whatever tacit understanding Moscow and Washington have achieved on the future of Eastern Europe, it is likely to be only temporary, in contrast to the understanding that governed the last 45 years. As long as change in Eastern Europe is confined to Poland and Hungary, the Kremlin can easily live with the new order without its fundamental security being brought into question, provided the Americans play along. But what happens when this process of change spreads to East Germany or the Baltic republics?
 
Germany 'the Real Test'
''The real test is Germany,'' Mr. Mandelbaum said. ''If you have a Polandization of East Germany, you are not only talking about a change of regimes, you are talking about the very existence of the state. The end of Communism in East Germany immediately raises the prospect of German reunification. And since East Germany is both at the center of Europe and the strategic anchor of the Warsaw Pact, these changes would be extremely difficult, perhaps impossible, for Moscow to accept.''
Administration officials, of course, are loath to even hint that there is any sort of even a temporary or tacit understanding with the Kremlin on Eastern Europe since this evokes images of Yalta, the 1945 conference in which the United States and Britain were accused of giving Stalin their tacit consent to dominate the region.
Nevertheless, the signs of at least an informal understanding abound. For instance, Administration officials say, Mr. Baker told Mr. Shevardnadze explicitly and repeatedly that an American objective in the new Eastern Europe was ''stability.''
In the same breath, though, Mr. Baker emphasized that Washington believed that the only way to insure a stable Eastern Europe after an easing of Soviet control was through gradual, but steady, change to more free-market systems and popularly elected governments.
A recent description by Mr. Baker of the Administration's policy toward the Baltic republics could easily apply to all of Eastern Europe.
''We hope that whatever happens with respect to the desire on the part of the people in the Baltic states for more autonomy and more self-determination and more freedom happens peacefully,'' Mr. Baker said.
''Should there be self-determination? Should there be freedom? Should there be more autonomy? Yes - but it should not take place in the context of major instability, bloodshed and that sort of thing. That's our policy.''
Referring to the aspirations of Eastern Europeans to be free, Mr. Baker added, ''I think there is a way to speak to that without, at the same time, inciting rebellion and without, at the same time, creating instability which could have a significant adverse result, in terms of our relationship with the countries of Eastern Europe and, for that matter, our relationship with the Soviet Union.''
 
'Very Courageous' Choice
In private, though, Mr. Baker has not hesitated to adopt a more outspoken approach. On Tuesday, when the Secretary of State met with Hungary's Foreign Minister, Gyula Horn, he began the meeting by congratulating him on his Government's political and economic changes and its recent decision to open its borders to East Germans trying to go to the West.
''You're in a difficult position,'' aides quoted Mr. Baker as having told Mr. Horn. ''Your Government made a very courageous and correct choice.''
Speaking generally of Hungary's opening to the West, Mr. Baker added, ''We want to be helpful in a way that does not contribute to instability.''
An Administration official who briefed reporters after Mr. Baker's meetings on Tuesday with the Hungarian and Polish Foreign Ministers said:
''The President's course in Eastern Europe has been well received by all the parties. From the Eastern European point of view, obviously they don't want to have something that causes them trouble with either other Eastern European countries or with the Soviet Union. And from the Soviet's perspective we have not had any complaints in any form.''
 
Baker Meets Czech
Indeed, Mr. Baker felt comfortable enough to initiate a meeting yesterday with the Czechoslovak Foreign Minister, Jaromir Johanes, the first such contact between an American Secretary of State and a Czechoslovak Foreign Minister since 1978.
Aides said Mr. Baker told Mr. Johanes that the United States was prepared for a closer relationship with Czechoslovakia provided Prague first improved its human rights record. The Secretary also pointed out to him that at a time of great change in Eastern Europe, the Czechoslovaks had not kept pace with Poland or Hungary in either the economic or political spheres.
As for the Soviet side of the understanding, that was given explicit expression by Mr. Shevardnadze in his speech before the United Nations on Tuesday.
''It is no secret that we were not enthusiastic about the election setback of the Polish Communists,'' Mr. Shevardnadze said. ''Nor should it be a secret that we hope that they overcome the crisis. Nevertheless, we see nothing threatening in the fact that in accordance with the will of the Polish people a coalition Government has been formed. We wish it every success and are ready too cooperate with it most actively. Tolerance is the norm of civilized behavior.''

HEADLINE: $20 Million Fine Sought for Indiana Pollution

BYLINE: By WILLIAM E. SCHMIDT, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Sept. 27

 BODY:
The Environmental Protection Agency is seeking a new and much tougher round of penalties against polluters it says are still dumping chemicals and untreated wastes into the Grand Calumet River in northwest Indiana, one of the nation's most highly industrialized and contaminated waterways.
The agency announced Tuesday that it would seek $20 million in fines against the City of Gary on the ground that the city's Sanitary District is releasing raw sewage and chemical wastes into the Grand Calumet. The river, which leads into Lake Michigan, is so dirty that neither swimming nor fishing is allowed anywhere along its five-mile length.
Three weeks ago, the E.P.A. proposed that the Gary Works of USX Corporation, the steel company's flagship mill, be barred from doing business with the Federal Government because the complex continues to dump sewage, oil, ammonia, cyanide, metals and other pollutants into the river. 
If the E.P.A. prevails in its proposal, contractors who do business with the Federal Government would be barred from using steel produced in the Gary Works in the manufacture of their wares, including military equipment like tanks and ships.
 
One-Year Ban Sought
The E.P.A. is seeking a one-year ban that would bar any Federal agency from entering into, renewing or extending any contract with the Gary Works. George Kuebler, a spokesman for the Gary Works, said the company did not know how much business it does with the Federal Government, either directly or indirectly.
Officials in both Gary City Hall and at the Gary Works say they intend to contest the E.P.A. moves. They argue that progress has been made in dealing with the pollution in the river, which ties Gary and its surrounding steel mills, refineries and other industrial plants to Lake Michigan.
But officials at the agency's regional office in Chicago say the E.P.A. has lost patience with the situation after nearly two decades of trying to force local governments and private industry to act.
Charles H. Sutfin, the water division director in Chicago for E.P.A.'s Region 5, which oversees Indiana and four other states in the Middle West, said the agency had decided to toughen its enforcement strategy because pollution from the river continued to threaten the waters of Lake Michigan.
 
'Very Little Improvement'
''Northwest Indiana is one of the most polluted areas on the Great Lakes, but we have seen very little improvement in the situation along the river in recent years,'' said Mr. Sutfin.
He said Gary's sewage treatment plant continued to dump untreated raw sewage into the river, in direct violation of a 1987 Federal court decree, which in turn had been filed to resolve repeated violations dating to 1978. Last year, in a similar action, the agency filed a motion in Federal court to collect a $1 million fine from the city. That case is pending.
''There has been absolutely no improvement even though the Federal Government has invested $122 million in Federal money to improve the city's sewage treatment plant,'' said Mr. Sutfin. ''But the facility is not managed well. In fact, it is not managed at all.''
The agency said, for instance, that the city does not properly staff the treatment plant, which is supposed to have 15 operators working over a 24-hour period, in addition to administrative and maintenance workers. E.P.A. inspectors said they had driven past the plant at times and not found any cars in the parking lot. Disagrees With E.P.A. Jackie Shropshire, a lawyer with the Gary Sanitary District, said the district had hired a private company to take over management of its sewage treatment plant within the next two weeks. ''I don't deny we have problems, but they are not as bad as the E.P.A. says they are,'' he said.
He said the city's efforts to run the plant had been hampered by the city's depressed economy and declining operating revenue because of a growing problem in collecting water and sewage fees from residents.
The E.P.A. enforcement strategy has cheered local environmentalists and conservation groups. Glenda L. Daniel, the executive director of the Lake Michigan Federation, an environmental organization, sent a letter earlier this month to William K. Reilly, the Administrator of the E.P.A., commending him for his ''forceful'' action directed at USX.
Mr. Sutfin said the action against USX and the Gary Sanitary District were part of a larger strategy to clean up the Grand Calumet River and the Indiana Harbor and Canal, which ties the river into Lake Michigan.
Much of the waterway's bottom already has up to 10 feet of highly toxic sludge, the legacy of decades of industrial dumping and sewage overflows. In some places, freighters carrying ore to local steel mills cannot reach their docks because of the accumulation of sludge. Dredges have not been able to remove the material because it is so toxic no one has been able to agree how to dispose of it safely.

HEADLINE: War: 1 Percent. Drugs: 54 Percent.

 BODY:
What do you think is the most important problem facing this country today?
The New York Times/CBS News Poll asked that question of a cross-section of Americans in January 1985. The proportion who answered war, nuclear war and defense was 23 percent. Last week, in response to the same question, the figure was 1 percent. 
In January 1985, 16 percent said the most important problem was unemployment. Last week the figure was 4 percent.
In January 1985, less than 1 percent said the most important problem was drugs. Last week the figure was 54 percent.
There's dramatically undramatic news in these figures, news that recalls Conan Doyle's story ''Silver Blaze,'' in which Sherlock Holmes called attention to the curious silence of the dog in the stable. In 1989, the poll figures call attention to a period of extraordinary peace and prosperity.
The low war figure is incredible, nearly as hard to accept as the stream of astonishments that flows almost daily from Mikhail Gorbachev's Kremlin. Not many months ago, this was the Strangelove generation. Teachers and parents were shaken by the nuclear nightmares experienced by anxious children. In June 1982 a nuclear freeze rally drew 600,000 people to Central Park. Compare: 1982, 600,000 people. 1989, 1 percent. The high drug figure is equally incredible - not because it's unreliable but because of comparative dangers. Crack, heroin and other narcotics daily imperil the health and welfare of millions and promote crime harming millions more. They undermine the integrity of institutions. That drugs has become an issue of such concern to so many may finally presage some effective responses.
But even so, the comparison is worth dwelling on. What does it mean when Americans fear drugs more than war, and by a margin of 54 to 1? The likely answer is that issues usually on the B list now clang loudly because the A list has fallen silent. Peace, knock wood, seems to proliferate; prosperity, at least for many citizens, endures.
All that can change in an instant. If unemployment rose, President Bush's high popularity figures would sink within weeks. Teflon or no Teflon, Ronald Reagan's standing dropped like a bobsled during the 1982-83 recession. The non-barking dog can, in short order, not only bark but bite.
The test that this period of extraordinary grace creates for Americans is not whether they stop to notice it, but what they do with it. Peace and prosperity stretch on, but so does homelessness; so does the need to give poor children a fair chance in life; so does rampant drug abuse. The test is how to array the invisible good news against the oh-so-visible bad - how to turn the blessings embodied in that 1 percent against the curses signified by the 54.

HEADLINE: ESSAY;
Who Lost Gorbachev?

BYLINE: By William Safire

DATELINE: LONDON

 BODY:
The most resentful political question asked by American conservatives after World War II was ''Who lost China?'' The imputation was that Western irresolution, inspired by subversives in high places, enabled Mao to seize control from Chiang. Liberals hotly replied that China was never ours to lose, but the accusation-laden question bedeviled them for a decade.
Now the tables are turned. Hard-liners around the world are nibbling their nails at the possibility that Mikhail Gorbachev will fall or turn mean, and that accommodationists will bedevil us for years with ''Who Lost Gorbachev?'' We will stand charged with sourpussism - failing to help the Soviet leader with trust and trade, thereby losing the glorious moment of opportunity for peace with freedom. 
People like me don't trust the new Soviet dictator. We see the success of a cult of personality that concentrates power in one man's hands; we note the ease with which he manipulates the levers of Kremlin power, orchestrating permission to criticize so as to force out his critics; we gape at the skill with which he winkles West Germany out of the Western military alliance while ridding the Soviet Union of economic responsibility for the basket cases of Eastern Europe.
We are not persuaded that he is motivated by the ideal of democracy, or that his K.G.B. faction has permanently abandoned the goal of Communist supremacy. He is pouring weaponry into Cambodia, showing surprising strength in Afghanistan, doing nothing to restrain his Syrian client in the bloody occupation of Lebanon, and maintaining his investment in totalitarian regimes in our hemisphere. These are not confidence-building measures.
Nor are we taken in by arms-control stunts. His latest maneuver is to temporarily unlink a missile treaty from his previous insistence that we abandon a space shield but - in the fine print - reserve the right to tear up the treaty if we deploy a missile defense. We would be fools to negotiate such an ''iffy'' treaty; we are developing space defenses to use them, not to give the Soviets an excuse to resume all-out missile production when they can again afford it.
Though we are not dazzled by the footwork, we are not blind to major tactical retreats by our superpower opposition. The promised dismantling of the Krasnoyarsk radar, a flagrant ABM treaty violation, is significant. Flat on its economic back, the Soviet Union is making genuine concessions.
The Soviet motive may be solely to save money, to provide a safety valve for the pressures of dissent and to reinvigorate a system that is failing to feed the populace, but the daring and scope of the changes cannot be minimized.
Mr. Gorbachev has permitted Poland to install a non-Communist Prime Minister (and Solidarity leaders promptly installed the world's most telegenic spokeswoman). He has encouraged Hungary to open its Western border, beginning the exodus that will lead to German reunification - and Europe is holding its breath as the Kremlin boss prepares for a speech in East Berlin next week.
This is heady stuff. Boris Yeltsin is free to criticize, Rupert Murdoch is free to buy half of a newspaper in Hungary, and all the publicized movement is in the right direction. We have to give him credit, though we don't have to give his nation credits.
But the Soviet market is due for an anti-freedom correction; logic suggests crackdowns in store. In a private letter to Margaret Thatcher before her visit with the Soviet leader, President Bush warned of the adverse affect any reversal would have on American opinion toward the Soviet leadership; he urged a steadiness of Western policy in the face of these likely ups and downs.
When the bear finally turns and snarls, what will happen to Mr. Gorbachev? He is more Deng than Zhao, more survivor than victim; if he chooses not to fall, if he follows his practice of rushing to the forefront of the furious, then his will be the hardest of the hard faces.
At that point, our soft-liners will issue a keening geshrei: it was basically our fault, we are the devils who made him do it, our failure to embrace him cost us the magic moment. We did not recognize Mr. Good Guy; as a result, we lost the glasnostic opportunity for which the word Gorbachev has come to stand.
But the same internal pendulum that brought him forward will turn him out or turn him mean. Who lost Gorbachev? Gorbachev was never ours to lose. (See? that question already has me on the defensive.)

HEADLINE: Koch Orders Hiring Freeze, Citing a $400 Million Gap

BYLINE: By DAVID W. DUNLAP

 BODY:
Facing what he said was a $400 million budget gap, Mayor Edward I. Koch yesterday imposed a municipal hiring freeze that is to remain in effect until the budget can be balanced.
The Mayor attributed $247 million of the gap to the fact that New York City may be prevented from selling the Coliseum site to the real-estate developer Mortimer B. Zuckerman in time to use the revenues in the current fiscal year. Mr. Zuckerman plans to build an enormous twin-towered complex known as Columbus Center on the site.
The freeze does not immediately affect the Police Department, because no hiring was planned in any case until January, when a new class will be mustered at the Police Academy. Nor does it affect firefighters, teachers, code-enforcement inspectors and workers in Special Services for Children, a division of the Human Resources Administration. 
The director of the Office of Management and Budget, Paul L. Dickstein, could not say yesterday afternoon exactly how many positions would not be filled while the freeze was in force.
 
Refusal to Drop Suit
The jobs that will not be filled he said, were those of clerks, administrators, park maintenance workers, street cleaners and pothole crews. The city's operating budget for the current fiscal year, which began July 1, is $27 billion, and a revised financial plan is to be issued in the third week of October.
Both Mr. Dickstein and Mr. Koch said there was an immediate need for the freeze, given the chance that lawsuits pending against the Columbus Center Development might delay or prevent the project.
''If the Coliseum suit proceeds, it's very serious,'' Mr. Dickstein said. ''There will either be a mid-year tax increase of substantial proportions, somewhere in the quarter-of-a-billion- dollar range, or there will be severe service reductions and layoffs.''
In a meeting last night, the Coalition Against Columbus Center refused to drop the suits, its counsel, John Van Der Tuin, said. He said coalition members ''discussed the political pressure that has been brought to bear by the current administration and vowed to press forward,'' although he did not rule out the possibility of a settlement.
Mr. Dickstein said that had a freeze not been put into effect, agencies would have accelerated their hiring in anticipation of budget cutbacks.
As a member of the Board of Estimate, Borough President David N. Dinkins of Manhattan, who is the Democratic candidate for mayor, voted for the Columbus Center project.
Mr. Dinkins's chief of staff, Barbara Fife, said yesterday that although he believed that ''including a one-time cash revenue in the expense budget was not a responsible fiscal practice, and he does not expect to use that device himself, nonetheless it is in the budget and we need to address the current gap.''
State Senator Manfred Ohrenstein, a Manhattan Democrat and a member of the Coalition Against Columbus Center, said he had told Mr. Dinkins it ''would be very difficult'' to persuade the coalition to give up the lawsuits.
''There is a lot of deep feeling involved,'' Mr. Ohrenstein said, ''and there seems to be no ready answer. When the Mayor of the City of New York tells me there are budget problems, I've got to listen. On the other hand, I have a sense of responsibility to my community.''
Mr. Dickstein said that if the lawsuits were dropped, the remaining $150 million budget gap could be overcome ''without layoffs and, we think, without any dramatic action or severe, sharp service reductions.'' However, he said, ''there would be an attrition program.''

HEADLINE: Hope and Dissent Blend In Education Conference

BYLINE: By BERNARD WEINRAUB, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CHARLOTTESVILLE, Va., Sept. 27

 BODY:
President Bush opened his education conference with the nation's governors today in an atmosphere of hope tinged with division and uncertainty over how best to improve the nation's ailing schools.
Many of the Democratic governors and some of the Republicans distributed a document listing ''summit objectives'' and intended to serve as their own framework for the meeting.
Among the goals for the Federal Government and states in the memorandum were seeing that ''by the year 2000, all children will be ready for the first grade,'' largely by assuring that every eligible poor child is enrolled in Head Start, a Federal preschool program. Experts say Head Start now serves only about 20 percent of those eligible. 
Another goal was to raise American student achievement ''to internationally competitive levels, especially in math and science,'' where tests show the United States generally lags far behind many other nations.
 
Staking Out Turf
The memorandum on goals, while not announced, seemed to be an attempt by the governors to assert themselves in what has been a White House show. Nearly all of the nation's governors are attending the two-day conference at the University of Virginia.
Perhaps the sharpest criticism of Mr. Bush came tonight from Gov. Mario M. Cuomo of New York. While other Democratic governors generally refrained from publicly criticizing Mr. Bush's approach, which seeks to restrain Federal spending on education, Mr. Cuomo was unabashed in his criticism.
Mr. Cuomo said tonight: ''You can't do it with poetry. You need a little prose. You can't do it with garnish. You need a little meat and potatoes. You can't build a University of Virginia with a speech. It takes bricks, that means dollars and cents and let's be honest. If you really are for these things, you really are going to have to start producing resources.''
Mr. Bush, from his arrival at midday on the leafy campus ablaze with autumn colors until a dinner tonight in Monticello with the governors, proclaimed that the nation's schools were lagging and that ''measurable national goals'' were crucial.
 
'Get On With It'
''Accountability, flexibility, tougher standards and a results-oriented system - all of these have got to be on the table,'' Mr. Bush told the governors shortly after his arrival by helicopter from Washington. ''What I'm seeking at this summit is not just dialogue but a new sense of direction.
''We've got to challenge the education system if we're to meet the challenge of educational excellence,'' Mr. Bush said. ''It's time to stop debating over commissions and studies and set priorities, and it's time to get on with it.''
As Mr. Bush spoke in a packed 970-seat auditorium in Old Cabell Hall, the governors applauded unevenly. Also attending were most of President Bush's Cabinet.
Although some governors insisted that more Federal education aid was essential, Democrats generally refrained from calling for more money.
Two Southerners Comment ''What is needed most critically from the President and the Federal Government in the area of education is leadership,'' said Gov. Joe Frank Harris, Democrat of Georgia.
''Federal funding is not the major issue,'' said Gov. Bill Clinton, Democrat of Arkansas, one of the organizers of the event. ''It is not the main subject and is only a small part of what we want to achieve.''
Mr. Clinton said accountability to taxpayers on how education money was spent was a more important topic. Besides, he told an opening news conference, the bulk of the more than $350 billion spent annually on education comes from state and local sources, not from Washington.
The governors' document listing objectives mentioned these goals:
* ''Disparities in achievment levels of students of different races and economic backgrounds will be dramatically reduced.''
* ''The dropout rate will be reduced dramatically.''
* ''The percentage of high school graduates going on to some form of post-secondary education will be increased to a point sufficient to give them opportunities to get good jobs.''
* ''Illiteracy among adult Americans will be virtually eliminated.''
By inviting the governors to Charlottesville, Mr. Bush has drawn attention to the education issue - an issue he highlighted in the Presidential campaign, when he said he would become the ''education President'' and pledged to tackle such problems as school drop-out rates, adult illiteracy and many students' ignorance of geography, mathematics and science.

HEADLINE: Senate Votes $9.4 Billion for Drug War

BYLINE: By DAVID JOHNSTON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
After a week of deadlock, the Senate agreed today on a $9.4 billion financing plan for President Bush's anti-drug strategy, more than $1 billion above the amount requested by Mr. Bush.
By a vote of 97 to 2, the Senate accepted a compromise negotiated by Senate Republicans and Democrats that would increase Federal spending on drug education, prevention and treatment programs while also providing more money for local law enforcement.
''The failure to commit these resources is to fight only half the war,'' the chief Democratic negotiator, Senator Robert C. Byrd of West Virginia, said on the Senate floor.
The chief Republican negotiator, Senator Mark O. Hatfield of Oregon, said of the compromise, ''We may differ on funding, but there is not an iota of difference in our desire to fight the war.'' 
 
House Speaker Optimistic
The House of Representatives has yet to act on the plan. Speaker Thomas S. Foley, Democrat of Washington, said it ''looks promising,'' but Congressional aides said some House members might not go along with the financing arrangement embodied in the compromise.
The plan would require a cut of 0.43 percent on all ''domestic and international discretionary programs'' run by Federal departments and agencies. But it would provide a flexible formula for cuts in the military budget, while imposing a more restrictive across-the-board cut on domestic programs. Programs like Social Security or Medicare, which provide automatic benefits to those who are eligible, would be exempt.
The compromise, approved as an amendment to a transportation appropriation bill, will be considered by House-Senate conferees with jurisdiction over various parts of the bill.
The two Senators who voted against the compromise were Jake Garn of Utah and Steve Symms of Idaho, both Republicans. Senator Jim Sasser, Democrat of Tennessee, was absent.
After voting for the drug financing plan, the Senate approved another amendment to the transportation bill that would add alcohol testing to the random drug testing required by Transportation Department regulations for flight crews, train crews and professional drivers. Those regulations, which have been held up by court challenges, are scheduled to take effect later this year.
The Senate also approved an amendment that allows state and local officials to spend money under the drug plan on voluntary drug testing for students who participate in extracurricular activities.
The vote on the drug plan cleared the way for the Senate's consideration of spending bills that had been stalled by Republicans until the drug dispute was resolved.
Votes in the House, meanwhile, demonstrated the political strength of businesses and well-to-do taxpayers, and the Democratic leadership continued to fight an uphill battle against a cut in the maximum tax rate on capital gains.
With the capital gains vote scheduled for Thursday, the House voted today to repeal a part of the 1986 tax law, known as Section 89, that prohibited businesses from giving more generous tax-free medical insurance to their executives than they provide for rank-and-file workers.
 
Pension Proposal Rejected
The House also rejected a proposal sponsored by the Ways and Means Committee that would give employees more say in the operation of company pension plans.
Of the drug plan, Senator George J. Mitchell of Maine, the majority leader, said the compromise gave greater emphasis than Mr. Bush's plan did to education and rehabilitation. ''The proposal submitted by the President was painfully inadequate for the task that we face in this country in dealing with drugs,'' Mr. Mitchell said. ''Now we have the opportunity to improve it.''
But Republican Senators credited Mr. Bush with stimulating action to combat drug use. ''I think the President's strategy is sound and going to be effective,'' said Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the minority leader.
Supporters of the compromise from both parties acknowledged that the plan was unlikely to win the war on drugs and represented merely the beginning of a broad Federal commitment that would ultimately consume billions of dollars more for years to come.
 
Concessions on Both Sides
Each side ceded ground in negotiating the financing plan that some Senators said would not increase the deficit or require new taxes. Republicans agreed to cuts in military spending, along with cuts in domestic programs, to help finance the expanded anti-drug campaign. Democrats accepted an increase in anti-drug spending that was half the amount they originally sought.
The White House accepted the compromise on Tuesday, but only on the condition that the Senate agree to consider at a later date several highly contentious anti-crime proposals backed by the Reagan Administration, including an expanded application of the death penalty.
Senator Byrd had demanded that $2.2 billion be added to the President's anti-drug proposal. The ensuing discussions involved the settlement of several disagreements over how to cut the Federal budget to pay for the expanded drug war and how the extra money would be spread among prevention, education, treatment and law-enforcement programs.
Mr. Byrd, the chairman of the Senate Appropriations Committee, said the compromise plan envisioned spending $1.1 billion more than Mr. Bush requested. The Bush plan was initially estimated to cost $7.9 billion, but that did not include $400 million for prison construction already requested by the Adminstration and considered by Senate negotiators as drug-related. The increase approved today, therefore, would bring total spending for the plan to $9.4 billion, some of which would be spent over several years.
 
Too Close to Call
In the House, the capital gains measure and the legislation involving Section 89 and pensions are part of a catch-all bill that was written to bring revenue and expenditures in line with overall budget targets worked out earlier in the year. The decisions made by the House will go before the Senate next month.
Both sides on the capital gains issue said publicly that the vote on Thursday was too close to call.
Capital gains are the profits from the sale of stocks and other property. The President and others who favor a reduction in the top tax rate on those profits to 19.6 percent, from 33 percent, maintain that the cut would stimulate investment and therefore the overall economy. Democratic leaders say it would be nothing but a tax break for the wealthy.
The Democrats have offered an alternative to the proposed cut. It would restore tax-deductible individual retirement accounts to upper- and upper-middle-income taxpayers who are now ineligible if they are enrolled in company pension plans. The Democratic measure would also raise to 33 percent from 28 percent the marginal tax rate paid by the 600,000 wealthiest taxpayers - those with taxable income above about $200,000 a year.
Under the rules, the House must approve one plan or the other in its entirety. It cannot accept both or neither.
The House rejected the Section 89 rules on medical benefits 390 to 36. The section was originally adopted as part of the 1986 tax law, but the rules were never put into effect.
The proposal to give workers equal representation with employers on the boards of most pension plans was rejected 250 to 173. Republicans were solidly against the plan; Democrats were divided.

LENGTH: 1353 words

HEADLINE: A Self-Taught Film Maker Creates a Comic Hit

BYLINE: By GLENN COLLINS

 BODY:
Judging from the frozen smile on his face, the standing, cheering ovation caught Michael Moore by surprise. The film maker, a solitary figure wearing a nylon-mesh baseball cap, stood awkwardly before an audience of 500 cheering filmgoers. They had just seen his first movie, ''Roger and Me,'' at the Toronto International Film Festival this month.
''I wish that everyone back home could be here to enjoy this response,'' the 35-year-old director said into a microphone, his eyes tearing. But Mr. Moore, like his film, never lets a tear go unaccompanied by a laugh: ''I never made a film before - I think I'm going to keep doing this.''
Mr. Moore's ''Roger and Me'' is a highly personal, darkly comic vision of the closing of the General Motors Corporation plants in Flint, Mich., his hometown. The documentary depicts the Rust Belt blues in a way that is so original that now, two weeks after that giddy night in Toronto, American critics have celebrated the film as a comic masterpiece following its debut at the New York Film Festival yesterday. The film will be shown again tonight at 6:15 P.M. in Alice Tully Hall. 
This dark-horse entry was a hit at the Telluride Film Festival in Colorado last month and it also won the Most Popular Film award in Toronto - the first documentary to do so since ''Best Boy'' in 1979. That film, about a cheerful mentally retarded 52-year-old, went on to win an Academy Award. $1 Million Bids Currently, Mr. Moore said, the bidding war for rights to distribute the film has already reached $1 million, a high level for a documentary. After an unusually successful theatrical run, for example, ''The Atomic Cafe,'' the tragicomic 1982 compilation of film footage from the 1940's and 1950's extolling the glories of the atomic bomb, grossed $1 million.
The sudden arrival of this unlikely film maker at the locus of a media and movie-distributor be-in is a story ''that's a bit hard to believe,'' as Mr. Moore puts it. ''But I never doubted that the film would succeed,'' he adds. In fact, he sold his house, held yard sales and set up hometown Bingo games to pay for the film, which was completed so recently it nearly missed the deadline for consideration at the major film festivals.
Mr. Moore's 85-minute film is a documentary about what he calls ''the unemployment capital of America,'' his hometown, the birthplace of General Motors, which lost 30,000 jobs in the last decade through plant and assembly-operation closings. ''Roger and Me'' shows the effects of G.M. plant closings that began in 1986, and derides the calamitous attempts of civic boosters to pump life back into Flint by initiating a parade of failed schemes and cheer-up visits from dignitaries ranging from Pat Boone to Anita Bryant. ''It's as if someone was trying to use a cup to bail out the Titanic,'' he said. ''In fact, we once thought we'd call the film 'The Dance Band on the Titanic.' ''
''Roger and Me'' was done ''on the run and on the cheap,'' Mr. Moore said. The film cost $260,000, of which $100,000 is still owed. Mr. Moore sold his house in Flint for $27,000 to help finance the film, and he held two yard sales that raised $5,000. He also sold his bed.
 
'It's Not That Strange'
Part of the film's financing -$50,000 over the last three years -came from a weekly Tuesday-night Bingo game in Carman Plaza Hall in Flint, where 180 faithful players showed up last week. ''It's not that strange a fund-raising idea for one who grew up Catholic and spent time in a seminary,'' Mr. Moore said.
Mr. Moore, the film's writer and narrator, is also a sometime protagonist in ''Roger and Me.'' On camera, he is a natural deadpan comedian; in person, the bearlike 6-foot-2 film maker is a gentle presence, and before film-festival audiences he is a folksy and practiced public speaker. He was born and raised in Flint, and his father worked on a G.M. assembly line for 33 years.
As an 18-year-old, Mr. Moore began his lifelong civic involvement with Flint by being elected to the city's school board; at the age of 22, he started his own alternative newspaper, The Flint Voice, and kept it going for 10 years, until he went to San Francisco in 1986 to edit the muckraking magazine Mother Jones.
Mr. Moore lasted only four and a half months, until a falling out with the magazine's owner that he describes in ''Roger and Me.'' ''There I was, out in San Francisco, depressed, unemployed and eating chocolate croissants,'' he said in an interview. ''I went to a lot of movies to relieve my depression - I'd go to anything. I realized that if I set out to make a movie about Flint, I couldn't do any worse than the ones I was seeing.''
 
On-the-Job Training
Subsequently, Mr. Moore's lawsuit against Mother Jones for wrongful discharge - settled for $58,000 -helped to finance the film he began to make when he returned to Flint. So amateurish was his film-making group, he said, ''that we didn't know f-stop from F-Troop.''
''It was the first time any of us had made a film,'' said Kathleen Glynn, a production assistant in the Flint crew. ''We learned as we went along - and it shows.'' For the first week of shooting, she said, the Flint crew got some help from Kevin Rafferty, the co-director of ''The Atomic Cafe,'' as well as from experienced the documentary makers Anne Bohlen, Christopher Beaver and Judy Irving.
''We didn't want to go to film school for three years,'' said Mr. Moore, ''so they were our film school.'' At times, the crew's inexperience proved to be helpful, he said, because his subjects ''relaxed a lot when they realized that we looked like we didn't know what we were doing.''
At one point, they were filming an interview with the Rev. Jesse Jackson, one of the dozens of public figures who visited ailing Flint. Mr. Jackson told them that a knob on their tape machine was in the wrong position for recording, ''and he was absolutely right,'' said Mr. Moore.
''When Jesse Jackson is teaching you film making, you know you're in really bad shape,'' he added.
 
'We Wanted to Name Names'
''I made this film for personal and political reasons,'' he said. ''See, the homeless didn't just drop out of the sky, and we wanted to find out who was responsible. We wanted to name names.''
The principal name named in the film is that of Roger B. Smith, chairman of G.M. Through a spokesman, Mr. Smith issued the following statement: ''I haven't seen the film, but from what I have read, it does a great disservice to the community of Flint and the thousands of G.M. employees who are making a positive contribution to the city and to General Motors. How I personally feel is unimportant. It is unfortunate that people who have pride in the community and the products they build are subjected to public embarrassment at a time when they are producing some of the finest products in America.''
In the film, Mr. Moore says that ''my mission is a simple one: to convince Roger Smith to spend a day with me in Flint.'' Indeed, a subplot of the film involves the ''Roger and Me'' crew's attempt to video-ambush Mr. Smith in his office, his home and his clubs. ''This man eliminated 30,000 jobs and devastated the town and we wanted him to see just what that meant,'' Mr. Moore said.
To Mr. Moore, ''Roger and Me'' is the story of his life, of his city and ultimately of America as it portrays the human consequences of bottom-line decisions. ''Thanks to the decisions that big businesses make now, the majority of us don't have the simple things our parents had,'' he said. ''It's not the American dream anymore; it's a nightmare.''
Is the film a call for action? ''If people do see it as a call for action, I hope they're smiling at the time,'' he said, adding after a pause: ''The film doesn't provide solutions, or tell people what to do. We think people can figure that out for themselves.''
At the end of ''Roger and Me,'' Mr. Moore finally has an encounter with Mr. Smith, though certainly not the one he'd expected. ''I would still love for him to come to Flint,'' said Mr. Moore with a dreamy smile. ''I'd show him around. You know - to see the sights.''

HEADLINE: Cypress Opposes U.S. Memories Concept

BYLINE: By LAWRENCE M. FISHER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Cypress Semiconductor Inc. said today that its president, T. J. Rodgers, had offered an alternative to the proposed joint venture that is intended to revive production in the United States of the most commonly used memory chips.
The venture, called U.S. Memories, would produce dynamic random access memory chips, or D-RAM's, using technology from the International Business Machines Corporation. It would be financed by large semiconductor and computer manufacturers, which would receive a guaranteed supply of D-RAM's. 
With the exception of Texas Instruments and Micron Technology, United States chip manufacturers all left the D-RAM business in the late 1980's after stiff competition and price cutting by Japanese makers.
Cypress, based in San Jose, makes static random access memory chips, a faster type of memory.
Mr. Rodgers has been an outspoken opponent of U.S. Memories, which he believes will imperil smaller entrepreneurial chip companies such as his own to bolster a few large players.
The Cypress announcement comes a day before the beginning of the third round of hearings before the House Judiciary Committee's Subcommittee on Economic and Commercial Law, which is reviewing the formation of U.S. Memories and related antitrust legislation.
Mr. Rodgers said he had made a written request to I.B.M. to transfer its 4-megabit and 16-megabit D-RAM and associated technologies to Cypress for Cypress to manufacture them in return for royalties and a guarantee of supply.
''I believe that if the goal is to get an American-manufactured, 4-megabit D-RAM into volume production as quickly as possible, an I.B.M. partnership with Cypress Semiconductor should be an attractive option,'' Mr. Rodgers said.
In contrast to the $1 billion that U.S. Memories is seeking to finance a totally new production company, Mr. Rodgers said he could begin production for only $5 million to $10 million by utilizing unused capacity at a Cypress production facility in Round Rock, Tex.

HEADLINE: Weakness In Prices Widespread

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Across the country, companies are reporting difficulty in raising prices - providing strong anecdotal evidence that inflation, for the moment, is not a problem.
Fresh stories of price weakness were told this week at the annual convention of corporate economists. Often they fret about inflation dangers. But this year many said that weak demand and strong competition had forced their companies to hold down prices. 
''Raising prices is something we'd like to do and can't,'' said Robert E. Hayes, corporate economist at the K Mart Corporation, one of the nation's five largest retailers. ''Price increases this year have averaged less than 3 percent. If we try to get more, the consumer says goodbye.''
 
Same Story in Other Areas
Similar reports came from economists employed by manufacturers, among them companies that make paper, lumber, steel, chemicals, cars and other consumer products. Two railroad executives, at Conrail and the CSX Corporation, said car loadings were also off, putting a damper on freight rates.
''It's much harder to get price increases than it was a year ago,'' said John J. McDevitt of the Minnesota Mining and Manufacturing Company. He said 3M's domestic sales volume for an array of products is growing now at a 3 percent annual rate, down from 6 percent in the first quarter.
The stories of price weakness and sluggish sales suggested that the Government's inflation measures, particularly the Consumer Price Index, might continue to decline.
The C.P.I. in recent months has fallen to an annual rate of less than 5 percent, from more than 6 percent earlier this year. In August, the C.P.I. did not rise at all.
 
No Recession Expected
Nevertheless, with only a few exceptions, the more than 300 people at the National Association of Business Economists Convention, which ended today, did not see a recession in the offing.
The association's annual forecast, based on a membership survey, said that the nation's current economic expansion would last two more years, although the economy would grow only weakly, and that the C.P.I. would decline next year to 4.3 percent.
Some economists argued that the current price weakness was temporary, concentrated in manufacturing rather than the larger service sector and brought on by special factors.
Robert T. Parry, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco, listed these factors as falling energy prices, a rainy summer that pushed down food prices and a stronger dollar.
 
Business Economists Agree
But whatever the future holds for inflation, more than a dozen business economists said in interviews and presentations that for the present prices are not going up and some are coming down.
Even in insurance, a major non-manufacturing industry, premiums on many policies are not rising, said Francis H. Schott chief economist at the Equitable Life Assurance Society of the United States.
''Rather than premiums going up, companies are lowering the interest income that consumers earn on some policies,'' Mr. Schott said.
Bernard Lashinsky, chief economist at Inland Steel Industries, said that while his company is getting new orders, they were not bunched together, as they were in 1987 and 1988. In addition, he said, there are not as many big new projects involving an expansion of manufacturing capacity for petrochemicals and paper, appliances and food processing. That means steel production is exceeding demand for the moment, he added.
John D. Walter of Dow Chemical said prices of silicones, used for things like textiles, cosmetics and food processing, started to rise last year but had leveled off. ''In the absence of special supply shortages, there is no way to push up prices,'' he said.
Finally, the convention was told that monthly surveys of 2,000 independently owned companies, usually with fewer than 40 employees, show that 36 percent of these companies had raised prices in the first quarter. But in the second, the ratio plunged to less than 30 percent.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasuries Dip in Light Trading

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
As dealers struggled to absorb a new $7.75 billion issue of four-year notes, prices of Treasury notes and bonds dipped in light trading yesterday.
Almost all the price movement came after the results of the Treasury auction were announced. For much of the session, traders kept busy watching movements in the dollar but did little actual business.
''The market completely lacks direction, and there doesn't seem to be any catalyst that will change that,'' said Ward McCarthy, a managing director at Stone & McCarthy Research Associates, a Princeton, N.J., firm that provides consulting on the credit and foreign exchange markets. 
''At this stage,'' Mr. McCarthy added, ''customers aren't in any hurry to invest.''
 
Demand Called Light
According to market participants, demand for the new four-year notes was light here and abroad. ''It looks like the dealers ended up buying most of the issue,'' one government securities trader said.
The notes were sold at an average yield of 8.35 percent, up from the average rate of 8.19 percent at the last four-year note auction on June 28. It was the highest yield on a four-year issue since the 9.70 percent at the March 29 auction.
Prices of Treasury bonds and longer-term notes slipped after the auction results were announced. In late trading, the 8 1/8 bonds that mature in 2019 were offered at a price of 98 5/8, down 5/32, to yield 8.24 percent.
Meanwhile, the 8 percent 10-year notes were offered at 98 2/32, down 1/8 point, to yield 8.29 percent. And the 8 1/4 percent five-year notes were offered at 99 23/32, down 1/8, to yield 8.30 percent.
 
Treasury Bill Rates Rise
Short-term Treasury bill rates moved higher. By late in the day, three-month bills were offered at a discount rate of 7.81 percent, up six basis points, or hundredths of a point. Six-month bills inched two basis points higher, to a late offered rate of 7.82 percent. And one-year bills were offered at 7.76 percent, up five basis points.
''The market is likely to remain in a narrow trading range at least until Oct. 6, when we get employment data for September,'' said Robert Chandross, chief economist at Lloyds Bank.
Activity in the tax-exempt municipal bond market was highlighted by a large negotiated offering of approximately $860 million worth of capital outlay bonds from Florida's Board of Education. The bulk of the issue consisted of three term bonds with maturities from 2023 to 2025. The bonds were reoffered with coupons ranging from 7.50 percent in 2023 to 7.35 percent in 2025.
Lazard Freres & Company, the underwriting's lead manager, said that the investor demand for the issue was very strong.
 
Florida Bonds Rated AA
The issue is rated AA by both Moody's Investors Service and the Standard & Poor's Corporation.
The big Florida issue did not have an adverse effect on the secondary market, traders said. Prices of most secondary issues rose by an eighth to a quarter of a point in slow trading.
In the taxable corporate bond market, Salomon Brothers acted as lead underwriter for a $65 million subordinated note issue from the Merchants National Corporation, an Indianapolis banking company.
The 9 7/8 percent notes, which mature Oct. 1, 1999, were priced at 99.282 to yield 9.99 percent, 1.70 percentage points more than the prevailing yield on the 8 percent 10-year Treasury notes at the time of pricing. The issue, which is noncallable for life, is rated Baa-2 by Moody's and BBB+ by S.&P.

HEADLINE: FINANCE/NEW ISSUES;
Offering by New York City Is Repriced to Lower Yields

 BODY:
New York City's issue of $700 million in general obligation bonds, which was initially priced on Tuesday, has been revised and repriced to offer slightly lower yields in the longer maturities. Serial bonds running from 1990 to 2002 remained unchanged. 
The underwriting was led by Goldman, Sachs & Company.
Bonds due in 2003, formerly uninsured, are now insured by the Financial Guaranty Insurance Company and priced to yield 7.40 percent.
The Group A bonds, which were insured by F.G.I.C., now include a 2004 bond and maturities have been extended to 2006. The interest rate on these securities was lowered a quarter of a percentage point and they were repriced to yield 7.40 percent. Maturities of the Group B bonds now range from 2007 to 2013 at a price to yield 7.775 percent, while Group C bonds now range from 2014 to 2019 at a price to yield 7.75 percent.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Ends Slightly Lower On Selling by Central Banks

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Central banks sold dollars for a third straight day yesterday, pushing the currency lower after an agreement last weekend that it had risen too much in recent months.
In New York, the dollar fell a little more than two Japanese yen and one pfennig against the German mark.
The losses left the dollar 4 percent lower against the West German mark and Japanese yen than it was late last week, back to its levels of late July and early August. 
 
Early Rally Fizzles
The central banks squashed a rally attempt at midmorning in New York. The brief rebound in the dollar signaled to some traders that the central banks had succeeded for now in their efforts to influence the $300 billion-a-day currency market, but whether that influence would continue over the long term remained in doubt.
A strong dollar tends to swell the United States trade deficit by making imports cheap and pushing up the price of American goods in foreign markets. At a meeting in Washington, finance leaders of the Group of Seven industrial nations agreed Saturday that the dollar's rise was ''inconsistent with longer-run economic fundamentals.''
Traders said the frequency of sales rather than their size was pushing down the dollar.
The dollar fell against all major currencies except the Canadian dollar, which often moves in tandem with the dollar, and the British pound, which was weighed down by a worse-than-expected trade deficit report.
In London, the pound fell to $1.6155, from $1.6215 late Tuesday. In New York the pound fell to $1.6167 from $1.6190.
In Tokyo, the dollar fell 1.45 Japanese yen to a closing 141.35 yen. Later, in London, it dropped further to 139.45 yen. It was quoted at 140.32 yen in late New York trading, down from 142.35 late Tuesday.
In West German marks, the dollar ended the trading day in New York at 1.8805, down from 1.8918.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late rates on Tuesday, included: 1.6305 Swiss francs, down from 1.6415; 1.1771 Canadian dollars, up from 1.1756; 6.3655 French francs, down from 6.4110, and 1,361.7 Italian lire, down from 1,366.9.
Other late dollar rates in Europe, compared with late Tuesday's rates, included: 1.8830 West German marks, down from 1.8885; 1.6305 Swiss francs, down from 1.6390; 6.3485 French francs, down from 6.3990; 2.1215 Dutch guilders, down from 2.1480; 1,359.50 Italian lire, down from 1,377.00, and 1.1770 Canadian dollars, up from 1.1750.
 
Gold Prices Edge Higher
Gold bullion edged higher in active European trading. In London, the metal rose to $368 a troy ounce, from $367.75 bid late Tuesday.
In Zurich, gold rose to a late bid price of $368, from $367.75 bid late Tuesday. Earlier, in Hong Kong, gold rose $2.09 to close at a bid $369.92.
On the Commodity Exchange in New York, gold bullion for current delivery fell to $367.60 an ounce from $368.50. Later, the Republic National Bank of New York said gold was bid at $366.20 an ounce, down from $367.30 late Tuesday.

HEADLINE: Arnold Johnson Is Dead at 84; A Leading American Communist

BYLINE: By JOAN COOK

 BODY:
Arnold Johnson, a member of the national leadership of the Communist Party for many years, died Tuesday at St. Vincent's Hospital after a long illness. He was 84 years old and lived in Manhattan. 
Mr. Johnson was born in a lumber camp in Seattle and began working at the age of 12 in a lumber mill. He worked his way through college and later received a master's degree from Teachers College at Columbia University and a bachelor of divinity degree from the Union Theological Seminary.
Mr. Johnson joined the Socialist Party in New York in 1929 while he was still a student. In 1931 he was arrested in Harlan, Ky., at a miners' strike. He later became a leader of the National Unemployed Leagues and the Workers Alliance.
 
Ran for the Senate
After joining the Communist Party Mr. Johnson moved to Ohio, where he was the party's state leader from 1940 to 1947. In the late 1940's he came to Manhattan, where he served as national legislative director, public-relations director and filled other posts in the party's national and New York State organizations. He also ran for the United States Senate on the Communist line.
In 1953, Mr. Johnson and a group of other party leaders were convicted in Manhattan of violating the Smith Act, which made it a crime to advocate the violent overthrow of the Government. Sentenced to a three-year term, he was released after two years and four months.
In 1970 he refused to testify before a subcommittee of the House Committee on Internal Security, which had subpoenaed him in an investigation of the New Mobilization Committee to End the War in Vietnam. He was cited for contempt of Congress, but the charge was later dismissed.
There are no immediate survivors.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Obituary

HEADLINE: U.S. TELLS ISRAEL IT PLANS TO SELL SAUDIS 300 TANKS

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN

 BODY:
The Bush Administration has told Israel that it intends to sell Saudi Arabia about 300 United States Army battlefield tanks and will take steps to meet Israel's security concerns if Jerusalem does not actively oppose the sale, American officials said yesterday.
The officials said Defense Secretary Dick Cheney raised the plan to sell the M1-A1 tank to the Saudis with Israel's Defense Minister, Yitzhak Rabin, when they met in Washington three weeks ago. Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d followed up on the plan in talks this week with Israel's Foreign Minister, Moshe Arens.
The Administration has decided to try to reach an understanding in principle with the Israelis that they not use their influence with Congress to try and block the sale, before taking up the issue with Congress and giving it the required legal notification of the proposed transaction.
 
Each Costs $2.6 Million
Each M1-A1 costs about $2.6 million, so if the Saudis decide to buy all 315 under discussion, the deal could be worth between $700 million and $1 billion, depending on the associated equipment purchased, officials said. 
Military analysts say Washington is particularly interested in this sale not only because it would come at a time when many countries are simply upgrading older models, but also because the cash it would bring in would allow the manufacturer, General Dynamics, to keep its production lines open longer to make even more advanced models.
While certainly not enthusiastic about such a deal, the Israeli officials have not flatly rejected the compromise formulas sketched out by the Administration. But once the incendiary issue of arms sales becomes a matter for public and Congressional debate, no one can predict where it will end.
The Saudis have been considering buying M1 tanks, the principal tank of the United States armed forces, since the early 1980's. The Saudi Army has about 550 tanks, a mixture of French-made AMX-30's and American-made M-60's, both of which are based on designs of the Vietnam War era.
The M1-A1 is an improved version of the M1, and has a 120-millimeter cannon instead of a 105-millimeter cannon, thicker armor and more advanced computers and communications gear, making it what many believe to be the best tank in the world today.
Israel makes a tank of its own, the Merkava, that is roughly comparable to the M1.
The ideal arrangement for the Bush Administration appears to be the kind that the Reagan Administration worked out in July 1988 when it sold 40 F-18 fighter jets and 300 Maverick air-to-ground missiles to Kuwait. Congress and the Administration agreed to downgrade the technical level of the Mavericks, while the Administration gave Israel military sales benefits to help its military high-technology industry. Israel then perfunctorily opposed the Kuwaiti sale, but did not encourage its allies in Congress to block it.
In an effort to avoid a major battle with Congress and Israel over the prospective Saudi tank sale - the kind of battle that occurred over the 1981 sale of five AWACS advanced radar planes to Saudi Arabia and over several other sales - Mr. Cheney laid out the following arrangement to Mr. Rabin during his recent visit.
The United States, it was noted, has been pre-positioning weapons and other war materiel in Israel since the early 1980's, but most of the weapons involved are ''single use'' armaments, meaning that they do not fit with the parallel equipment used by the Israeli Army. They are for use by the American military only, in the event that it has to fight a war in the Middle East.
In South Korea, by contrast, the United States has long pre-positioned weapons which are of dual-use capacity, suitable for both the South Korean and American forces. The South Korean Army can draw on these weapons stocks on a ''pay as you use'' basis, if the United States approves.
According to officials, Mr. Cheney has decided ''in principle'' to begin a similar arrangement with Israel. The draft plan is for the United States to pre-position up to $100 million worth of military equipment in Israel that would be suitable for either army. Israel could draw on these stocks if Washington approves.
 
Israel Short of Arms Funds
The cash-starved Israeli Defense Ministry, for budgetary reasons, has allowed certain categories of weapons and ammunition to be reduced to below-normal levels of inventory. Thus, the idea that the United States would maintain some weapons inventories in Israel, in effect on Israel's behalf, has a great deal of appeal.
Mr. Cheney is said to have suggested to Defense Minister Rabin that the pace at which this prospective $100 million stockpile would be built up by the Pentagon might be affected by the degree of Israel's cooperation on the proposed Saudi tank deal.
Mr. Cheney also broached some other financial offsets, most involving complex bookkeeping arrangements by which the Administration could pay out Israel's annual $1.8 billion in military aid in a way that compensates Jerusalem for erosion from inflation.
Mr. Cheney told Mr. Rabin that the Saudis will buy the tanks from some European nation if they do not get them from the United States. And, Mr. Cheney added, while there might be a marginal weakening of Israel's security by the fact that the Saudis received higher-quality American-made tanks rather than slightly less advanced European models, that would be more than offset by the increase to Israel's security through American pre-positioning and better financing.
 
Compromise Is Possible
Mr. Rabin answered that Israel's official policy was to oppose any such arms sale to any Arab nation still technically at war with it, which applies to Saudi Arabia. Neverthless, while Mr. Rabin made clear that this would always be the official position, he did not rule out some sort of unspoken, unwritten compromise along the line that was reached in Kuwaiti arms deal. Foreign Minister Arens took the same line with Mr. Baker, Israeli officials said.
In Congress, there appears to be some sympathy for the long-expected Saudi deal, although the Administration may have antagonized some key pro-Israeli legislators by floating the idea by the Israelis before consulting with Congress.
''I think there is room for negotiations on this tank sale, but it would help if the Administration bothered to consult with the Congress, which it has promised to do and repeatedly failed to do on questions related to the Middle East,'' said Representative Mel Levine, the California Democrat who has been a central figure in several Administration-Congress battles over arms sales to Arab nations.
Mr. Levine said he would look at several factors in deciding whether or not to oppose the Saudi tank sale. He said he would want to know if the tanks would be equipped with depleted-uranium armor, an advanced technology that deflects penetrating shells, the type of weapons the tanks would carry, where in Saudi Arabia they would be stationed - that is how close to the frontier with Israel - and what type of overall threat they might pose to Israel's security.
 
Egyptian and Israeli Meet
On the Middle East diplomatic front, Secretary Baker played host yesterday to a meeting in his Waldorf Astoria suite between Mr. Arens and Egypt's Foreign Minister, Esmat Abdel-Meguid. Earlier in the day Mr. Arens met with the Soviet Foreign Minister, Eduard A. Shevardnadze. The four men were in New York for the United Nations General Assembly session.
After his meeting with the Israeli and the Egyptian, Mr. Baker told reporters that there ''may be some possibility for progress'' in getting Israelis and Palestinians to discuss Israel's proposal for Palestinian elections.
The Secretary explained, however, that on the two key issues - which Palestinians Israel would talk to and on what basis - no breakthrough was forthcoming yesterday.
He said Mr. Arens had told him that next week the Israeli Cabinet would consider Egypt's 10-point proposal for bridging Israeli and Palestinian differences over how to organize elections in the occupied territories to choose Palestinian representatives who would negotiate peace with Israel. Mr. Arens said he could not commit himself until the Cabinet had made a decision.
 
The M1-A1 Tank at a Glance
 
Performance
The M1-A1 is the latest version in the M1 series of tanks developed by the United States in the 1970's. The $2.6 million tank has improved computers and communications gear. It can travel 41.4 miles an hour on the road, 30 miles an hour cross-country and four miles an hour up a 60 percent grade. It has a maximum road range of 300 miles. The tank is powered by a Lycoming Textron AGT 1500 gas turbine engine.
 
Armor
To deflect penetrating shells, the thicker hull is designed with low-level radiation uranium encased in steel, which is 2 1/2 times the density of steel, and reiinforced with depleted uranium mesh on the front and on the turret.
 
Weapons
The main weapon on the M1-A1 is a German Rheinmetall 120mm smooth-bore gun. To its right is a 7.62mm machine gun with another mounted on the left side of the turret. A total of 11,400 rounds of 7.62 mm machine gun, with 1,000 rounds of ammunition available, is mounted at the commanders station. It can be aimed and fired from within the turret in 360-degrees radius and at an angle up to 65 degrees. On each side of the turret is a six-barrelled smoke grenade gun.
 
Dimensions
The M1-A1 tank's hull is 26 feet long and 11.4 feet wide. Overall height is 8 feet.
 
Source: Jane's Armor and Artillery, 1988-89
