HEADLINE: HOUSE, 239 TO 190, VOTES BILL TO CUT CAPITAL GAINS TAX

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
The House of Representatives today approved a reduction in the capital gains tax rate, giving President Bush a significant legislative victory that wounded the new Democratic leadership.
After weeks of arm-twisting and button-holing by both sides, the vote was not even close: 239 legislators voted to cut the tax rate through the end of 1991, while 190 opposed the plan. Sixty-four Democrats joined 175 Republicans in supporting the tax cut. One Republican voted against the proposal. [Roll-call, page D4.] The vote came on a Democratic alternative that would have restored tax-deductible individual retirement accounts to all taxpayers and raised the overall tax rate of the 600,000 taxpayers with the highest incomes. A vote for the alternative was a vote against reducing the tax rate.
 
Another Fight Ahead
The capital gains measure is part of a huge budget bill. Once the overall legislation is approved by the House, probably next week, the bill will go to the Senate. The Senate Democratic leadership is strongly opposed to lowering the capital gains rate, but proponents of a capital gains cut seem to have the upper hand. 
If the capital gains measure becomes law, it would represent a serious unraveling of the monumental Tax Reform Act of 1986. The basic principle behind that law was that tax rates would be lowered significantly in return for the abolition of dozens of tax breaks, including the differential for capital gains.
Proponents of reducing the tax rate have maintained, in the words of Representative Bill Frenzel of Minnesota, the senior Republican on the Budget Committee, that it would ''induce more savings,'' which in turn would lead to ''more investment, more jobs and more economic activity.'' Democratic leaders have countered that the cut would provide a tax break for the well-to-do and be a serious drain on Federal revenues.
 
Not All Assets Included
The plan approved today lowers the top rate on capital gains - profits from the sale of stock and other assets - to 19.6 percent, from 33 percent. It applies to income from sales of stocks, bonds, real estate and some raw materials. Income from sales of works of art, coins and other collectibles would continue to be taxed as ordinary income.
President Bush made lowering the capital gains rate a centerpiece of his election campaign last year. In a campaign with few concrete issues, this was one on which he and Gov. Michael S. Dukakis of Massachusetts strongly disagreed.
In the last week, Mr. Bush campaigned hard for the tax cut, and he was still calling lawmakers Wednesday from Virginia, where he was attending a meeting with governors on education matters.
After the vote, Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady praised the House for ''strengthening America's economy, creating new jobs and giving America's small businesses and entrepreneurs a fair chance to compete internationally.''
The vote was the House Democratic leadership's first real test on a highly partisan matter. The Speaker of the House, Thomas S. Foley of Washington, and the majority leader, Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, gained their posts in the summer. Both men had spoken strongly against the tax cut, saying it was a gift to the rich. In their alternative plan, the income tax would have been raised on the highest income taxpayers, like couples with income of more than $200,000 a year.
After the vote Mr. Foley said: ''We're proud of the position we took. We think it was the right position.'' He continued: ''This vote should not be read as going beyond the confines of the particular issue. We're going to win some and lose some.''
While the Democratic leaders tried to put the best face on their defeat, it was clearly a serious blow for them to lose the first time they took on President Bush on a matter of party principle. They were unable to maintain strict party discipline in the face of pressure from important constituents who stood to receive a tax break and the prevailing view that Republicans wanted to reduce taxes while Democrats wanted to raise them.
Most Democrats who broke ranks with the leadership were Southerners or from districts with timber interests. Under the proposal, timber investors would be allowed to count all their profits as capital gains while receiving other favorable tax treatment.
The only Republican to vote against the tax cut was Representative Doug Beuter of Nebraska.
 
The Political Reality of Taxes
''It's always popular to be seen as reducing anyone's taxes,'' Mr. Foley said. ''People who are going to get a tax break know about it and respond to it. Those who are not respond less.''
Representative Newt Gingrich of Georgia, the Republican whip, said the vote demonstrated the ''waning influence of the liberals in the Democratic party.''
But Mr. Gingrich more or less agreed with Mr. Foley's analysis, saying, ''It's hard for a politician to vote against a tax cut.''
The tax cut provision would be in effect until the end of 1991. After that the top rate would become 28 percent, but in calculating taxable gains a taxpayer would be permitted to adjust them to reflect inflation during the time the assets were held.
Until 1986, capital gains were taxed at a lower rate than other forms of income. But the sweeping tax law that year eliminated the differential in favor of a lower overall tax rate.
 
'A Foot in the Door'
The sponsors of the lower rates said they hoped the rate reduction would become a permanent one, and they characterized the temporary reduction as a ''foot in the door.''
According to the nonpartisan staff of the Congressional Joint Committee on Taxation, 60 percent of the benefits from a capital gains cut would go to taxpayers earning more than $200,000 and 80 percent to those earning more than $100,000.
The committee staff calculated that while the cut would enhance revenue for the first two years as taxpayers sold assets to take advantage of the lower rate, it would lose billions of dollars in subsequent years. Republicans disputed those figures and argued that the economic growth created by the tax cut would lead to a permanent increase in revenues.
In the financial community, the prospect of a capital gains tax cut has been favorably received although concerns have been expressed about the temporary nature of the measure before Congress.
 
An Economist's Reservations
''If it is certain that the capital gains tax rate will go back up, then it is hard to see how it would trigger any capital formation,'' said Jerry L. Jordan, the chief economist of First Interstate Bank and a member of the Council of Economic Advisers under President Reagan. ''I'm for any tax cut I can get. But the motivation of this one, to generate revenue for the next fiscal year, is certainly short-sighted. Having a permanent capital gains tax reduction would be far superior.''
A person involved in venture capital financing, which supplies money to new businesses, said a capital gains cut would probably not significantly increase the money available for that, because much of the financing comes from pension funds and other tax-exempt institutions.
But Standish O'Grady, a general partner of Hambrecht & Quist Venture Partners, said the reduction might encourage people to start businesses.
''While it is a temporary cut,'' he said, ''for entrepreneurs thinking of starting a business, it would certainly make the economics more compelling, so long as they believe there is a possibility it will be extended. ''
In the debate today, both sides asserted that the issue was not so much one of tax policy as of political principle.
 
Philosophical Arguments
''The struggle is not between two policies but two philosophies,'' said Representative Bob Michel of Illinois, the House Republican leader. ''The Democratic Party believes in the redistribution of wealth. The Republican Party believes in creating wealth.''
Mr. Gephardt agreed that the issue was one of philosophical differences. Urging the House to ''do what's right for the country,'' he declared, ''The choice is between two different views of what it will take to make our country strong.''
In Democratic circles, much of the blame for today's defeat was placed on Representative Dan Rostenkowski, chairman of the Ways and Means Committee. Mr. Rostenkowski let it be known last spring that he might favor some form of capital gains tax reduction. By the time he reversed his position, he had lost his normally firm control of his committee.
Indeed, six Democrats on the panel, led by Ed Jenkins of Georgia, were in the forefront of the effort to reduce the gains tax.
 
The Honeymoon Factor
After the vote, Mr. Rostenkowski said he did not have the means to counter President Bush's honeymoon popularity.
''I have served in this House during the first term of six popularly elected Presidents,'' the chairman said. ''My experience has shown that they enjoy their greatest power and influence in their first year in office. George Bush is no exception.''
Mr. Rostenkowski criticized the President for not facing up ''to his responsibility to lead the deficit-reduction effort.''
''Make no mistake,'' Mr. Rostenkowski said, ''it is his job, and his alone.''
Under the House rules, lawmakers could not cast a vote for or against both provisions.
 
More Options in Senate
The situation will be different in the Senate, where the rules are more flexible and where the Finance Committee will begin drafting a bill next week.
Senator Lloyd Bentsen of Texas, the committee chairman, favors making deductible I.R.A.'s available to all taxpayers and permitting withdrawals for college tuition payments or first-home purchases. Interest and dividends earned in retirement accounts are not taxable until the money is withdrawn.
The I.R.A. proposal is quite popular. Moreover, Senator Bentsen's measure does not include a tax increase for the wealthy. The I.R.A. proposal rejected by the House today would raise, to 33 percent from 28 percent, the tax rate on the last dollar earned by couples making more than about $200,000 a year.
Some senators have held out the possibility that the Senate might approve both the capital gains reduction and more generous I.R.A.'s.
A quick assessment of the Senate showed that about 15 of the 55 Democrats were sympathetic to a capital gains reduction while only 3 or 4 of the 45 Republicans could be counted on now to oppose one. Unless some votes are changed, that means the tax cut stands a good chance of being passed.
 
Tax Plan Highlights
 
Capital Gains Tax: Would drop to 19.6 percent for those in the highest tax bracket, who now pay 33 or 28 percent. Would drop to 10.5 percent from 15 percent for those in the lower bracket.
 
Assets Affected: Stocks, bonds, real estate, tmber, livestock and some other raw materials.
 
Assets Excluded: Works of art, coins and other collectibles.
 
Effective for: Assets held at least one year and sold between Sept. 14, 1989, and Dec. 31, 1991. Tax jcut would not be pahsed in.
 
Expiration: In 1992, the capital gains rates would become 28 percent and 15 percent, but taxpayers would be allowed to deduct an amount attributable to inflation since the asset was purchased.

HEADLINE: Bush and Governors Set Education Goals

BYLINE: By BERNARD WEINRAUB, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CHARLOTTESVILLE, Va., Sept. 28

 BODY:
President Bush and the nation's Governors agreed today on the need to overhaul the nation's education system by creating a set of goals that will focus on eliminating illiteracy, reshaping curriculums and holding teachers accountable for their performance.
''We believe that the time has come, for the first time in U.S. history, to establish clear, national performance goals, goals that will make us internationally competetive,'' said the joint statement issued here at the end of a two-day meeting called by Mr. Bush to discuss education. The statement was written by the White House staff, Administration officials and a bipartisan group of governors.
Earlier today, in a speech to the governors, Mr. Bush said: ''The American people are ready for radical reforms. We must not disappoint them. ''Education is our most enduring legacy, vital to everything we are and can become,'' Mr. Bush said. ''And come the next century - just 10 years away - what will we be? Will we be children of the Enlightenment or its orphans?'' 
 
'The First Step'
Specific goals and details will be shaped by the White House and governors to be ready for a meeting of the National Governors' Association in Washington in February.
''This agreement represents the first step in a long-term commitment to reorient the education system and to marshal widespread support for the needed reforms,'' the statement said.
Mr. Bush and the governors said that the caliber of the education system and the nation's economic future were inextricably intertwined. Developing a national strategy that includes new directions for education, Mr. Bush said, is pivotal to the nation's well-being.
The overall proposals carried few surprises, but they were hailed as the first time any President had outlined a framework for the Federal Government and states to work together to reform the nation's ailing schools.
 
Triumph for Bush
For Mr. Bush, who pledged in his campaign to serve as the ''education President,'' the agreement represented a triumph and clearly achieved one immediate goal, focusing attention on education, not just for the two days of meetings here but with the many special reports on education appearing all week.
''This is a major step forward in education,'' said Mr. Bush, standing near the sun-drenched steps of the rotunda on the University of Virginia campus. ''We've reached agreement on the need for national performance goals, on the need for more flexibility and accountability, the need for restructuring and choice.''
Obviously bowing to pressure from the Democratic governors, Mr. Bush added that the Federal Government was committed to ''more Federal support'' for preschool programs like Head Start for poor children.
Overall, said the beaming Mr. Bush, ''This has been historic.''
 
Test of Financing
But some Democratic governors, including Michael S. Dukakis of Massachusetts, said it was too early to forecast the agreement's scope and effectiveness.
''There's good news in the sense that 50 governors and the Administration seem to be committed to improving the quality of our schools,'' said Mr. Dukakis, the President's opponent in the 1988 campaign. ''But the test for all of us will be over the next couple of months. It means not only deciding who does what, but who pays for it.'' But most of the Democratic governors spoke positively, even warmly, about the agreement.
Mr. Bush won praise from several union leaders.
Albert Shanker, president of the American Federation of Teachers, said Mr. Bush's speech ''defined a vision of education that was not public relations.''
Mr. Shanker said he was surprised that Mr. Bush had offered some ''radically different proposals'' that departed from the ideas of conservative educators, notably in emphasizing the notion of diverse, nonrigid curriculums to meet the needs of students of differing ethnic and geographic backgrounds.
 
Hailed as a First
The joint agreement was announced hours after Mr. Bush addressed the governors, Cabinet officers, education officials and students and faculty members at the university.
The agreement was also the first time that the nation's governors, Democrats and Republicans, had agreed to forge a national education strategy.
''This is the first time in the history of this country that we have ever thought enough of education and ever understood its significance to our economic future enough to commit ourselves to national performance goals,'' said Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas, a Democrat who helped draw up the agreement. ''It has never happened in over 200 years.''
With state and local governments providing more than 90 percent of the money spent on education, Mr. Bush squarely left the details and the burden of substantial education changes in their hands.
Mr. Bush had called the rare meeting with the governors largely because of the consensus with the Government and the education establishment that American schools were in turmoil and that the education system was increasingly lagging behind those of other industrial democracies.
 
More Than Three R's
In his speech at midday, Mr. Bush said his Administration envisioned ''tradition-shattering reform in five areas.''
''First, I see the day when every student is literate,'' he said. ''But literacy should mean more than the 'three R's.' We must be a reading nation. We must grapple with the hard sciences.''
Mr. Bush also said students ''must do more than identify names on a multiple-choice question. They must understand the generosity of Andrew Carnegie, the genius of Alexander Graham Bell and the heroism of Rosa Parks.''
Mr. Bush's second proposal centered on ''diversity'' in classroom curriculums and standards. Current lessons and procedures are often too rigid to meet the needs of the diverse ethnic groups of students around the nation, White House officials said.
''Of course all schools in a state will share a core curriculum and minimum standards of achievement,'' Mr. Bush said. ''But the means by which that curriculum is taught, and those goals met, should be as diverse and varied as America.''
These were the President's other proposals:
* Giving parents more choice in selecting the schools they want their children to attend. ''Children differ in their interests, learning styles and capabilities,'' said Mr. Bush. ''I see the day when choice among schools will be the norm rather than the exception.''
* Developing more accountability, where teachers, principals and administrators must clearly answer for poor performances. ''We must now evaluate ourselves on a tougher grading curve, one that includes that other major industrial nations,'' Mr. Bush said.
* Exploiting the potential of every student, not only those who are gifted, but also the ''average students'' and the disadvantaged.
''Some of our reforms and experiments are sure to come up short,'' said Mr. Bush. ''But for too many of our schools, experimentation is preferable to the status quo, because the status quo could scarcely be worse.''
''After two centuries of progress,'' Mr. Bush told the governors, ''we are stagnant.''

HEADLINE: Groups Sue to Stop Shuttle Flight Because of Radioactive Payload

BYLINE: By WILLIAM J. BROAD

 BODY:
Three private groups filed a suit in Washington yesterday to stop the next space shuttle mission, which is to take aloft a nuclear-powered space probe that the three groups say could cause thousands of deaths by cancer if it is involved in an accident. The probe's power source is radioactive.
The suit, filed in Federal District Court in the capital, is the first meant to stop a civilian space launching, the space agency said. The groups charge that the National Aeronautics and Space Administration, the Energy Department and the White House suppressed public releasing of information about health and safety risks that the probe poses. 
James W. McCulla, NASA's director of media relations, yesterday quoted agency lawyers as calling the charge ''totally inaccurate and untrue.''
 
Using Radioactive Decay
The probe, code-named Galileo, is to be lofted Oct. 12 on an eight-year $1.4 journey to investigate Jupiter. It must be launched by Nov. 21 or wait two years for proper planetary alignment. It is powered by nearly 50 pounds of radioactive plutonium 238; it will swing too far from the Sun to be energized by solar cells.
Inside the Galileo probe, the heat of decaying plutonium is to be turned into electric power. Unlike a nuclear reactor, in which atoms are actively broken apart in a chain reaction, this power source is a kind of nuclear battery that uses natural radioactive decay to produce power. A similar power source was used on the recent Voyager probe of Neptune.
Disputes over the safety of the Galileo mission grew after the 1986 Challenger explosion and the subsequent estimate by the space agency that the risk of ''catastrophic failure'' for the shuttle in its fiery ascent is one mission in 78; that estimate's range of reliablity extends as high as one flight in 168 and as low as one in 36.
The nuclear risk is not unique here. The Soviets have sent dozens of nuclear reactors into orbit over the years.
 
Danger Substance Is Shielded
The Galileo plutonium is surrounded by multiple layers of shielding, including thick graphite shells NASA says are ''designed to withstand the extreme impact and temperatures associated with a mission abort.'' NASA said the greatest chance of a release of plutonium in a launching accident was less than one in 2,500.
The plaintiffs in the suit are the Florida Coalition for Peace and Justice, the Christic Institute and the Foundation on Economic Trends. The first group is based in Orlando, the others in Washington.
''Given what we know about the risks involved in the Galileo mission, it is our responsibility to stop it,'' said Lanny Sinkin, litigation director for the Christic Institute.
The groups estimate that an explosion on liftoff could shatter the nuclear-power pack and cause thousands of fatal cancers. But the space agency says so little plutonium would be released that there would be no deaths. When Plutonium Is Released . . . A Federal interagency panel that evaluated the Galileo probe's risks reported that at worst, a launching pad accident could release enough plutonium to cause an eventual 80 cancer deaths. The panel said that if the probe re-entered the atmosphere as it swung by the Earth, up to 2,000 cancer deaths could be caused by released plutonium. The report was prepared by 50 experts headed by officials of the Energy Department, the Defense Department and NASA.
The private groups contend that the release of that report was illegally delayed, preventing them from using the conclusions in comments for the environmental assessment of the mission that the space agency made in May. The suit names President Bush; the NASA Administrator, Richard H. Truly, and Energy Secretary James D. Watkins.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (93%); 

STATE: JUPITER (92%); FLORIDA, USA (87%); 

CITY: ORLANDO, FLORIDA, USA (79%); 

COMPANY: NATIONAL AERONAUTICS & SPACE ADMINISTRATION (91%); CHRISTIC INSTITUTE (59%); CHRISTIC INSTITUTE (59%);

HEADLINE: ON MY MIND;
Soldier in the Wall

BYLINE: By A. M. Rosenthal

 BODY:
The Prime Minister of Poland is strolling about his office, looking.
It is all new to him. He had been in other official Polish establishments, like jail, but until Solidarity chose him to head the new Government, Tadeusz Mazowiecki had never set foot in the large building in central Warsaw that houses all the Government ministries that are now supposed to report to him.
So after a day or two, he is walking around, inspecting the furnishings. He sees a button he had not noticed and gives it a little push. 
The wall suddenly parts. Out leaps a soldier with an automatic rifle. When they both recover from the shock, Mr. Mazowiecki is told a soldier is always behind the wall, to protect him.
The Prime Minister calls the head of police, a Communist minister, and says he would appreciate knowing about other soldiers and other breakaway walls.
That story was told to me in New York by a visiting Solidarity official to make a couple of important points. One was that although Solidarity holds the prime ministry and other important posts, it still does not know where the Government buttons are or who will leap out of the wall when they are pushed.
The second point is that almost the entire Government bureaucracy is still in the hands of tens of thousands of Communist apparatchiks.
And, of course, some of the most important ministries, like the police, are headed by Communists under the agreement setting up the new Government. Assuming every single one has not been converted to democracy, the possibilities for sabotage or slowdown are immense.
These points are important for Americans, who tend already to take Solidarity and its hold on power for granted, a political and psychological mistake.
The Bush Administration apparently will be more openhanded than it first intended. But in the United States, it has not yet come across that if Solidarity is to thrive, even survive, it will not only have to meet the savage economic crisis it inherited from the Communists but at the same time create an entirely new system of government.
Meanwhile, Solidarity has to keep a close eye on a man who is supposed to be its friend. Jozef Cardinal Glemp dealt the new Government a hard blow by making a carefully crafted anti-Semitic statement in effect proclaiming Jews to be enemies of Poland and its freedom.
Happily, it took Solidarity only a day to repudiate that statement and only a couple more days to organize a direct appeal to the Pope, who then distanced himself from the Cardinal. The Solidarity Government is also talking about recognizing Israel, ending four decades of Polish Communist enmity.
But Cardinal Glemp probably will be heard from again to add to Solidarity's troubles. Men of hatred are rarely silent for long.
One important American does understand the full range of Solidarity's problems and needs - Lane Kirkland, head of the A.F.L.-C.I.O., which has befriended Solidarity before, during and after the time it was outlawed.
Publicly and privately, Mr. Kirkland is giving members of Congress some suggestions specifically tailored to help Poland move from the failed Communist system to a free economy.
Among many, one Kirkland suggestion is a fund of $250 million a year to provide small grants to start businesses in light industry and services, where the Communists left a desert.
He also suggests an annual $100 million job-retraining fund for those Poles who will be thrown out of work when inefficient, corruptly run Government plants are closed down.
While it is struggling to keep alive economically, the new Government will be dealing with something Mr. Kirkland points out has not happened since the creation of Israel - transforming a labor movement into a Government, separating one from the other and allowing for democratic challenges to both.
Mr. Kirkland agrees with American financial experts who say Poland will have to wipe out many subsidies and artificially low prices. That is likely to bring a period of hyperinflation and unemployment.
But he warns that the Solidarity Government must not be left to deal alone with this heritage of Communism. With luck, Mr. Bush will listen to that point.
America and Western Europe must extend help before and during the new Government's period of economic trial, not wait until it is over and then look to see if Solidarity has managed to survive - or whether somebody else has jumped out of the wall.

HEADLINE: THE LAW;
Harvard Plans to Close a Public Service Placement Office

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CAMBRIDGE, Mass., Sept. 28

 BODY:
The new dean at Harvard Law School, the first law school to set up a separate placement program to help students find jobs in public service, plans to close the office, The plan by the new dean, Robert C. Clark, has led to protests by students worried that the school will abandon its commitment to support public interest law. Mr. Clark is considered an expert in corporate law who holds conservative views.
In an interview this week, Dean Clark called the planned closing a streamlining measure in which the general placement office would take on public interest counseling and placement. One new adviser will be hired to do some public interest placement. 
Last week, 1,055 of the 1,600 undergraduate law students signed a petition asking the dean to retain the 12-year-old Public Interest Placement Office. The office, which has been financed by the law school and run by a lawyer, encourages students to seek alternatives to working in large private firms. About 300 also attended a rally last week.
''We want to be the next generation of Harvard Law School graduates to do public interest law, and we're asking our school to help us,'' said Lori M. Wallach, a third-year law student who works with the Harvard Legal Aid Bureau, which assists low-income people in civil cases.
 
Assistance on Loans
Dean Clark said students facing high tuition costs and low-paying public service jobs would be better served by economic help than by counseling. Harvard's law school costs $62,200 for three years, and public service jobs begin at about $25,000 a year. The school's growing loan forgiveness program would assist such students, he said, since it reduces the tuition debt of graduates who work in some types of public interest law.
''This was not intended to downplay or challenge the school's support of public interest,'' said the dean. He added that he would consider suggestions made by the Alliance for Public Interest Alternatives, a group that joined the protest. The group wants a full-time lawyer working in a separate office, and it says public interest law should be further integrated into the law school curriculum.
Barry J. Fisher, a member of the alliance, said the plan to close the special placement office was ''negative, both practically and symbolically.'' He said eliminating the services would make it harder to find public service jobs, which have dwindled with government budget cuts, and diminish the value placed on public service at Harvard and at other law schools as well as law firms.
Geoffrey R. Stone, dean of the University of Chicago Law School, which opened a public interest placement service in July, said that while private law firms were able to recruit students on campuses, public interest groups were not.
''Students interested in public interest must profoundly buck the trend, and without support and assistance they simply give up,'' Dean Stone said. ''Why worry about loan forgiveness if you don't have people taking these jobs?''
Harvard set up the separate public interest placement program in 1977. In the past few years, 15 other law school have done so, according to Michael Caudell-Fagan of the National Association of Public Interest Law in Washington. Harvard's office not only encouraged its students and alumnae, it set up a national clearinghouse for 117 other law schools.
''If this goes through, other schools will think twice about establishing such services,'' Mr. Caudell-Fagan said.

LENGTH: 2596 words

HEADLINE: Autocrat With a Regal Manner, Marcos Ruled for 2 Decades

BYLINE: By ERIC PACE

 BODY:
Ferdinand E. Marcos was as tough as he was debonair, and he came to rule the Philippines with an iron hand before popular unrest forced him to flee in 1986 after two decades in power.
Mr. Marcos was first elected President in 1965 and converted his country's sputtering democratic system into a personal fief, with his luxury-loving wife, Imelda, as virtual co-ruler. He came to control most of the apparatus of power and acquired the right to rule by decree. For most of that time, he had the firm support of the United States.
While he was President, Mr. Marcos was not apologetic about departing from the norms of Western democracy. ''What we ask of the developed countries,'' he wrote in 1982, ''is to let the third world find a third way. We must now create a political and economic system responsive to our unique character and our special realities.'' 
A skillful lawyer and orator, Mr. Marcos entered public life in 1949 and served successively as a member of the Philippine House of Representatives, Senator and president of the Senate before he was first elected President in 1965. He was re-elected in 1969, later proclaimed that the governmental machinery was not operating and set about transforming it.
He entrenched himself as the Philippines' autocratic ruler during the nine years, from 1972 to 1981, in which he imposed martial law. He held on to sweeping powers afterward, and his regal manner and sumptuous way of life seemed to enhance his authority over his poverty-plagued, largely rural nation of 60 million people, a former American colony that became independent in 1946.
Defending his right to rule by decree, if he chose, he asserted that otherwise ''you will have Communists going back and forth, causing the dastardly ruin of our country, the killing of people and the rape of women.''
Over the years, Mr. Marcos's hand was strengthened by the support of the armed forces, whose size he tripled, to 200,000 troops, after declaring martial law in 1972. The forces included some first-rate units as well as thousands of unruly and ill-equipped personnel of the civilian home defense forces and other paramilitary organizations. In the martial-law years, he also consolidated his parliamentary power and curbed civil liberties, harassing or exiling political opponents.
Eventually, in the 1980's, corruption and mismanagement left the Philippine economy in obvious trouble, and Mr. Marcos's prestige and power shrank. His health faltered, the United States moved away from him and political opponents and Communist insurgents grew more assertive.
And he was put on the defensive after Aug. 21, 1983, when Benigno S. Aquino Jr., the main opposition leader, was shot and killed at the Manila airport as he returned home after three years of self-exile in the United States.
The indignation caused by the killing, coupled with longstanding discontent, led to demonstrations and rioting that undermined his authority and ultimately brought Corazon C. Aquino, Mr. Aquino's widow, to power.
 
Corruption Within
Mr. Marcos's aura was also tarnished by accusations and problems concerning his country's business and economic life. Under his rule, the Philippines was afflicted with widespread corruption and vast unemployment. There was widespread poverty, along with a few sectors of great wealth.
Mr. Marcos was said to suffer kidney problems, and 1984 there was speculation that he was close to death. A passionate golfer and physical fitness buff who was proud of his physique, he disputed the accounts.
With Mr. Marcos ailing, his long-powerful wife emerged as the Government's main public figure, meeting high-ranking visitors as though she were the chief of state. Her actual position was Minister of Human Settlements, which gave her cabinet rank.
Over the years, the life that the Marcoses came to lead in their riverside palace became regal indeed. When Pope John Paul II visited the Philippines in 1981, Mr. Marcos had bronze medals struck depicting himself and the Pope.
Ferdinand Edralin Marcos's early years were rather more austere. He was born Sept. 11, 1917, in the town of Sarrat in a rice- and tobacco-growing area in Ilocos Norte in northern Luzon. His father, Mariano R. Marcos, was a politician and educator. His mother, the former Josefa Edralin, was a teacher from a well-to-do landowning family.
The Marcos family moved to Manila in 1925, and the future President graduated from secondary school in 1934 and went on to study law on a scholarship at the University of the Philippines.
While he was a student, he was convicted of the murder of a political rival of his father, but the verdict was reversed by the Philippine Supreme Court after the young man argued his own appeal.
He then became a trial lawyer in his father's Manila law firm. In World War II, he was a much-decorated officer in both the Philippine and United States Armies.
 
Question of Medals
The decorations that Mr. Marcos claimed to have earned for military service against the Japanese became a campaign issue four decades later, when Mrs. Aquino denounced what she called his ''false medals.''
The Government's count of his war decorations ranged from 26 to 33. When their validity was challenged by American and Philippine journalists, his Government argued that documentation of his exploits as a wounded army officer and guerrilla leader had been destroyed by fire.
In 1946 and 1947, Mr. Marcos was special assistant to President Manuel Roxas. He was a member of the Philippine House of Representatives from 1949 to 1959 and of the Senate from 1959 to 1966, serving as president of the Senate from 1963 to 1965.
As President of the Philippines, Mr. Marcos ran the country jointly with a disputatious two-party Congress until he imposed martial law. He suspended Congress, declared himself absolute ruler and had a constitutional convention draw up a charter that gave the Philippines a Prime Minister as head of government. He then named himself Prime Minister under the new Constitution as well as President under the old.
Mr. Marcos remained Prime Minister from 1973 until 1981, when he stepped down from that post and, while retaining the presidency, also served as leader of the New Society Movement, his political party. He was easily elected again as President, Early Support, Then Furor Early in the martial law period, Mr. Marcos enjoyed widespread support, in part because crimes of violence decreased. Other successes of his presidency, over the years, included rural irrigation and electrification projects and increased rice harvests.
Yet the boasts of Mr. Marcos and his supporters seemed to be drowned out, in his final years, by the furor over the Aquino slaying, the Communist insurgency and the clamor of his emboldened political foes.
In the months after the killing, he made concessions to his opponents under pressure from the United States, which had provided hundreds of millions of dollars in aid. That assistance had been important over the years in buttressing his rule.
But by late 1984, there were signs that the Reagan Administration was distancing itself from his Government somewhat. And Mr. Marcos also had to contend with opposition from his country's Roman Catholic Church.
Many Filipinos came to believe that Mr. Marcos, a shrewd political tactician, had no hand in the killing of Mr. Aquino but that he was involved in cover-up measures.
A civilian investigative panel issued a report in October 1984 naming Gen. Fabian C. Ver, the armed forces Chief of Staff and a close friend of Mr. Marcos, and two dozen others, mostly soldiers, as ''indictable for the premeditated killing'' of Mr. Aquino and of Rolando Galman, who was earlier said to have been the lone assassin killed by airport guards. An indictment was handed up in February 1985.
Mr. Marcos asserted that the evidence backed the military's contention that Mr. Galman had been hired by Communists and had acted on his own. But some opposition politicians contended that Mr. Marcos had ordered the slaying, while others accused the military and Mrs. Marcos.
In late 1985, when the court found the defendants in the Aquino murder trial not guilty, the verdict was widely seen as a miscarriage of justice.
While the Aquino controversy continued in the capital, the Communist insurgency, which began in 1969, was making political as well as military inroads around the country.
Mr. Marcos estimated that the insurgent regulars numbered 10,000 to 12,000 by early 1985. But the rebel organization, the New People's Army, said it had around 20,000 armed fighters. And its popularity and influence were growing.
As a result, by May 1985, many opposition figures had begun striking a more nationalistic and anti-American note - partly in reaction to leftists' charges that the Marcos Government was dictatorial and was being sustained by United States aid.
His ties with Washington were of long standing. Early in his presidency, Mr. Marcos was a strong defender of American involvement in Vietnam, and he maintained close relations with Washington in the years that followed.
Those relations soured somewhat under the Carter Administration, which included the Philippines among the targets of its human-rights campaign. But Vice President George Bush appeared to signal a different tack in 1981 when he visited Manila and told Mr. Marcos: ''We love your adherence to democratic principles and to democratic processes. We will not leave you in isolation.'' Mr. Bush said the next day that he had been speaking generally of American allies in Southeast Asia, but his remarks were associated in the public mind with the Philippines and came to be quoted with ironic intent.
 
The Bases Factor
As criticism of the Marcos Government grew in the United States, American officials let it be known that the Administration was particularly worried that instability in the Philippines might impede use of the two major American military bases there, Clark Air Base and Subic Bay Naval Station. Accordingly, they indicated, the Administration wanted to prepare to deal with a Philippine power struggle after Mr. Marcos's rule.
By October 1984 the Reagan Administration had altered its attitude toward Mr. Marcos, and was prodding him to relax his authoritarian rule.
Mr. Marcos was evidently nettled by the American appraisals. Shaking his fist, he declared in one speech, ''I am surprised why some people would classify us with the Central and Latin American countries.''
''We don't want to appear to our Asian brothers as if we were the pet dogs of any Western ally,'' he said, suggesting that his handling of the Aquino assassination case was not meant just to satisfy Washington.
Criticism from the Philippine business sector, having flared up after the Aquino killing, continued, with many company executives urging that the economic power of Mr. Marcos and his entourage be reduced to permit decentralization and to bolster investor confidence.
After the assassination, the Philippine economy worsened so markedly that the nation was not able to keep pace with the payments due on loans from overseas. The country's foreign debt soared to more than $25 billion by the beginning of 1985, and international lending organizations and banks, ascribing the economic travail to cronyism and heavy-handed Government control of the economy, pressed for changes.
In the embittered political climate, Mr. Marcos found himself confronted by Mrs. Aquino.
The widow of his leading foe became a rival candidate for President in a special election that the confident Mr. Marcos, in a decisive political misstep, called for February 1986. The date was 16 months before the end of his six-year presidential term. The Marcos-controlled National Assembly declared him the victor, on the basis of a vote tally that was marked by pervasive violence and fraud by his side.
Resentment simmered, and later that month, Juan Ponce Enrile, the Defense Minister, and Gen. Fidel V. Ramos, the army deputy chief of staff, led an anti-Marcos military rebellion that was noteworthy for its lack of bloodshed. Hundreds of thousands of Filipinos turned out into Manila's streets, forming a vast human wall to protect Mr. Enrile, General Ramos and others who had barricaded themselves in a military installation.
The human buffer made it impossible for the Marcos forces to strike at the rebels without causing many civilian casualties, and it added to the manifold pressures on Mr. Marcos to cease trying to rule.
Finally, after asking in vain for Reagan Administration help in staying in power, he fled the Presidential Palace in the evening of Feb. 25, accompanied by his family, and a United States Air Force plane flew the Marcoses to Guam en route to Hawaii, where they took up residence in Honolulu.
In the months after his ouster, details emerged about the enormous wealth Mr. Marcos had amassed in his years in power.
 
Recovering the Plunder
A Manila commission, trying to regain what it called the concealed Marcos fortune, took over 268 companies, in many industries ranging from banking to mining to steel-making, by early 1987. Not long after Mrs. Aquino took power, Philippine officials estimated that the fortune built up by Mr. and Mrs. Marcos totaled $5 billion or more, and the Aquino Government estimated that as much as $10 billion in stolen wealth was involved. Mrs. Marcos was found to have left behind 1,060 pairs of shoes and slippers at the Presidential Palace, along with 508 floor-length gowns.
As time went by, the Aquino Government filed several civil suits before a Philippine court asking damages of as much as $100 billion against Mr. Marcos, his relatives and ''cronies.''
In 1986, the Government filed suit in the Honolulu Federal District Court to recover $100 million in assets that the Government charged the Marcoses had stolen. New legal dcouments were filed on June 20 in Los Angeles charging that the Marcoses stole $5 billion during the presidential years. The suit has not come to trial and is still pending.
Investigations were also carried out by American officials, and in October 1988, Mr. Marcos, his wife and eight associates, including Adnan M. Khashoggi, a Saudi businessman, were indicted by a Federal grand jury in New York in a racketeering case. It included charges that Mr. Marcos and the others had embezzled tens of millions of dollars during his presidency.
On April 6 Federal prosecutors said Mr. Marcos had become too ill to stand trial on the criminal charges pending in New York. But on June 9 they moved to guarantee claims against his fortune in the event of his death, filing a civil suit against him and his wife in Federal District Court in Manhattan.
Yet while Mr. Marcos remained of keen interest to investigators and prosecutors, as his years of exile went by, he became less of a political factor in the Philippines.
The mass support Mr. Marcos had once enjoyed mostly faded away, and his remaining supporters had come to be seen as a minority in his homeland. Indeed, even though his political legacy continued to trouble the nation, Mr. Marcos came to be widely seen in the Philippines as largely irrelevant.
He is survived by his wife, of Honolulu; a brother, Dr. Pacifico Marcos, and a sister, Fortuna Barba, both of Manila; two daughters, Maria Imelda Manotoc, who has been living in Morocco, and Irene Araneta, who lives in Menlo Park, a suburb of San Francisco; an adopted daughter, Aimee Marcos, of Honolulu; a son, Ferdinand, Jr., and five grandchildren.

Shortly afterward, he was forced to flee the country. (AP); Mr. Marcos with President Reagan during a visit to Washington in 1982. PUB-DATE: September 29, 1989

SECTION: Section C; Page 1, Column 1; Weekend Desk

LENGTH: 1902 words

HEADLINE: The Infinite Variety of Velazquez at Met

BYLINE: By JOHN RUSSELL

 BODY:
''VELAZQUEZ'' at the Metropolitan Museum of Art is a show that no one ever expected to see. The first exhibition ever to be devoted to Velazquez, whether in Spain or anywhere else, it includes many great paintings by the pre-eminent master of the Spanish school. From the tiny head and shoulders of the poet Gongora (done when Velazquez was only 23 years old) to the tranquil mix of landscape, narrative and sainted portraiture in ''St. Anthony Abbot and St. Paul the Hermit,'' we know exactly where we are - in the presence, that is to say, of a painter who can make us feel both secure in the world and at one with it.
We believe in him completely. We recognize the tight, closed, wary features of Gongora as those of a difficult and idiosyncratic master of verse. We also recognize the economy, the rigor and the feeling for fact above all things in the way that Velazquez handled this tricky and very early assignment.
And when the raven brings the daily bread to St. Paul the Hermit in his rocky fastness, we recognize it as having come from the best baker in Madrid. 
Many visitors will prefer to most everything else in the show the irresistible portrait of the Infanta Margarita. Blessed with a charm not universal among members of the Spanish royal family at that date, the little princess could not be more winningly rendered. To the unerring helterskelter of the brush across her elaborate court costume there is added, as if by an afterthought of genius, the still life of roses, irises and daisies that is one of the supreme achievements of its kind in European art.
That particular white magic of his is wrought over and over again at the Met's show, which opens to museum members today and can be seen by the public starting Tuesday. Complete worlds are set before us with the casual-seeming aplomb of which Velazquez alone had the secret. (See, for that, ''Prince Baltasar Carlos in the Riding School,'' an elaborate outdoor subject in which every figure is portrayed with an offhand brilliance that is extended impartially across the whole gamut of humankind, from crowned head to dwarf.) To statesman and licensed buffoon, putative Sibyl and imaginary Aesop, Velazquez brought that noblest of attributes, the style that is no style. He did not rely, that is to say, on any one way of setting about his work to carry him along. He reinvented the act of painting every time he took up the brush.
Perhaps it was in part for that reason that his work is full of pentimenti - second thoughts, taken up on the instant. He put down some marks. He looked at them - so we dare to guess - as if they had forced themselves upon him. Did they have precisely the burden of fact that he wanted? If not, other and alternative marks then presented themselves. He tried them out, kept some and scrapped others until the final, definitive, inimitable image was right there on the canvas.
It is for the end result of all these commotions that just about everyone would rank Diego Velazquez (1599-1660) among the half-dozen greatest painters of all time. (Some of them would go further and higher.) But to pin Velazquez down! To say exactly what he did, and when he did it, and what kind of a person he was - these are terrible tasks. No two people agree as to how many paintings he produced - not much more than 100 is the current estimate - and within that small body there is an astonishing variety.
All visitors will have their own idea of a ''typical Velazquez,'' and they will find it fulfilled in close on 40 paintings at the Met, though not perhaps always by the examples that they most hoped to see. The royal portraits, to begin with. The court portraits. The occasional and sometimes enigmatic biblical scenes. The images of everyday goings-on in kitchen and street, with their incomparable passages of still life.
All those may qualify. So do the little paintings done in the garden of the Villa Medici in Rome - so transparently natural and unpretentious to our eyes, and so quietly adventuruous in their own day. But a Velazquez that summed him up once and for all? That would be hard to find, unless it were ''Las Meninas,'' now at the Prado in Madrid and not available for this show.
His nature was both complex and incomparably direct. As he came from nowhere, socially speaking, and rose in society until he was ennobled by his patron and friend, King Philip IV of Spain, he might be expected to take more trouble with grandees than with people of humbler station.
Perhaps the most immediately spectacular painting in the present show is, for instance, the equestrian portrait of the Count-Duke of Olivares. Even in the context of the comparable portraits by Titian and Rubens, with which Velazquez was familiar, this painting stands out for its brilliance and swagger.
The curvetting horse, the armor, the stupendous sash, the ostentatious hat, the luxurious boots, upheld baton (which might be a field-marshal's, but isn't) and the backward glance of the sneaky little eye - what are these, if not the marks of a great lord among lords? Even the wraith of a burning city in the distance might be related to a great feat of arms.
These traits were perfectly adjusted to the sitter. Olivares was not the man to play down either his personal attributes or his status in society as the favorite of King Philip IV. And in this portrait by Velazquez, sitter and style were one.
Or were they? All his life long, Velazquez saw right through everything and everyone. He did this not in a censorious way but with a limpid and impartial gaze. Is it not possible that the most discreet element of irony entered into this equestrian portrait, and that what he saw before him was not only a prepotent lord, but also an overdressed, overweight, self-satisfied courtier who was getting, in every sense, too big for his boots?
This was, after all, the man who was lately described by the Spanish art historian Camon Aznar as ''a prideful count-duke, more terrible to enemies from within than from without, and a general who was first and foremost a politician.'' Anyone who sees in this great painting a pre-echo of Olivares's fall from favor in 1643 may well be quite right.
But, as every visitor to the Met already knows, Velazquez did not need the apparatus of power and glory to be in his best form. He was never better, after all, than when he painted the slave of Muslim origin, Juan de Pareja, whom he met in Rome and brought back to Spain with him. The beautiful ease with which Velazquez addressed himself to all his sitters could hardly be better exemplified than in this perennial favorite at the Met.
When Velazquez looked at another human being he gave his whole self to the task, unconditionally and without any notion of formula or high style or spectacular effect. Turn to ''An Old Woman Cooking Eggs'' and ''The Water Seller of Seville'' at the Met, and you will see what resulted from that.
Already in the two big pictures that he painted in 1630, during his first stay in Rome, Velazquez had that capacity. Both in ''Joseph's Bloody Coat Brought to Jacob'' and in ''The Forge of Vulcan'' we feel a total confidence in his handling of gods, men and dogs. We stare in wonder at the appurtenances of every day, from a hammer that someone has dropped in amazement to a perfectly plain white jug.
We peek at the lavender-colored landscape in ''Joseph's Bloody Coat'' as if we had never seen anything like it before. The world is remade, redefined and reanimated by this painter. And what a storyteller, too! That storytelling faculty was used on occasion to biblical or mythological ends. But fundamentally, in his art Velazquez was a man of this world. Painting that great soldier of the church, Mother Jeronima de la Fuente, he produced what Julian Gallego in his catalogue entry neatly calls ''a memento mori in Franciscan serge, rather than a revered and beloved mother superior.''
In the domain of myth, likewise, his outlook was down-to-earth. Painting Mars, he portrayed the god of war as a beat-up military man who wonders if the time is not overdue for him to hang up his armor for good. Painting the boy-god Apollo, he presented him as an adolescent tease who took an urchin's delight in bringing Vulcan the news of his wife's infidelity.
If in this show there are one or two paintings that do not show Velazquez at his best, they too have their place, for Velazquez remains one of the more mysterious of the great European painters, and we need to see him at first hand as much and as often as we can.
In matters of this sort, New Yorkers are terribly spoiled. If, in any given exhibition, they don't get everything, prudence and reason notwithstanding, they feel affronted. If they can't have the ceiling of the Sistine Chapel peeled off and shipped over for their enjoyment, they sulk and stay home. Nothing, they feel, should be beyond their grasp and immune from their whim.
With this in mind it has to be said that as a comprehensive survey of Velazquez's greatest work, the present show has its lacunas. How could it be otherwise, given the limited number of Velazquez's finest paintings and the irresponsibility of sending them out on loan?
There will never be a substitute for a visit to the Prado, where ''Las Meninas,'' ''The Surrender of Breda'' and ''The Fable of Arachne'' would alone establish Velazquez as an artist of supreme stature. The ''Rokeby Venus'' and the ''Boar Hunt'' in London demand to be seen. So does the portrait of Pope Innocent X in Rome. Neither ''Philip IV'' in the Frick Collection in New York nor ''A Woman With a Fan'' in the Wallace Collection in London can ever be lent. The royal portraits in Vienna gain by being seen together.
But what should amaze us is, first, that we have this show in New York at all and, second, that it really does mark off, one by one, the domains in which Velazquez excelled. For that, we have to thank above all John M. Brealey, the Met's chairman of conservation. It was he who cleaned Velazquez's ''Meninas'' in the Prado - in a room that, at his request, was open to the public at the time - and thereby revealed its full splendor for the first time in many, many years.
Without his exceptional services to the Prado, both on that first occasion and during his later yearlong consultancy, the Met could never have hoped to secure 17 loans from that source alone and one from the Escorial near Madrid. This show is dedicated to Mr. Brealey, and the dedication is amply deserved,
''Velazquez,'' at the Metropolitan Museum of Art, opens to museum members today and can be seen by the public starting Tuesday. The show, which runs through Jan. 7, was sponsored by Banco Hispano Americano. The exhibition will not travel.
 
New Hours At Met Museum
Beginning next week the Metropolitan Museum of Art will extend its Friday and Saturday hours until 9 P.M., and will no longer be open late on Tuesdays.
The new hours will be 9:30 A.M. to 5:15 P.M. Sunday, Tuesday, Wednesday and Thursday, and 9:30 A.M. to 9 P.M. Friday and Saturday. The extended Friday hours are supported by a bequest from the estate of Ruth Lapham Lloyd, a Connecticut philanthropist.
In addition to extending gallery hours, the museum plans to present free lectures, gallery talks and films on Friday and Saturday evenings, and the museum's restaurant will serve dinner from 5 to 8 P.M. Reservations: 570-3964.

HEADLINE: Review/Opera;
'Porgy and Bess': Catfish Row at the Met

BYLINE: By DONAL HENAHAN

 BODY:
The contemporary world does not often intrude on the Metropolitan Opera, but there it all was on Wednesday evening: a hurricane battering Charleston, S.C.; a cocaine dealer plying his trade on the street; racially biased police officers; sex, violence and evangelism flourishing side by side . . . but, of course, you have guessed that the opera was ''Porgy and Bess,'' Gershwin's tuneful investigation of the folkways of Catfish Row in the 1930's. First produced by the company in 1985, ''Porgy'' has returned in a restaging by Arvin Brown, artistic director of the Long Wharf Theater.
Mr. Brown, making his Metropolitan debut with a cast that included 10 singers as new to the house as he, makes extensive revisions in the original Nathaniel Merrill production. The hurricane scene, for instance, shifts from a shack on the beach back to its proper site, Serena's room. It is one of many differences that make no difference. The total effect remains as before: this uncut ''Porgy,'' which exhumes virtually every scrap of music that the composer wrote for the work, continues to look and sound as overblown as ever. The affecting simplicity and directness of the famous songs (''Summertime,'' ''Bess, You Is My Woman,'' and the rest) still is smothered under the blanket of grand-opera pretensions. 
Part of the problem traces to Gershwin himself. His large-orchestra version of the score does singers no favors, regularly blotting them out when comprehension of words and clarity of vocal melodies should be uppermost. Very little of the text could be made out at Wednesday's performance. In this first ''Porgy'' of the season, that basic difficulty of comprehension was compounded by James Levine's insistence on thrusting the orchestration into prominence at just those moments when voices and text should have taken precedence. His conducting, which generally pays attention to such matters, was bluff, unidiomatic and insensitive.
Strange to say, however, considering the composing source, not many of the voices in this performance sounded good enough to deserve more sympathetic treatment. The Porgy, Michael Smartt, seemed vocally out of sorts, as if suffering from a cold. Though a reasonably adept actor, he swallowed or crooned many notes and rarely projected a tone out into the large house. As the bootlegger and dope pusher Sportin' Life, Ben Holt danced athletically and strutted in the appropriately raffish fashion, but his vocal inadequacies were hard to overlook even in a largely declamatory version of ''It Ain't Necessarily So.'' The community's female bulwarks (Camellia Johnson as Serena, Marjorie Wharton as Maria and Karla Burns as Lily) had little opportunity to do more than deliver accompanied recitative in a shrill, presumably humorous, style. Marvis Martin, as Clara, lent her excellent soprano to ''Summertime,'' but the song's evocative appeal was dissipated by a heavily dramatic, rather ponderous treatment.
On the plus side, Priscilla Baskerville's Bess could be listened to with pleasure, though her acting was tentative when not actually awkward. As for Porgy's nemesis, the brutal stevedore Crown, it would be hard to imagine a more formidable villain than Gregg Baker, who looked like a cross between Magic Johnson and Arnold Schwarzenegger. Among the cast's more promising newcomers were Raymond Bazemore as the divorce-peddling Frazier, Gordon Hawkins as Maria's husband Jake and, in cameo appearances, Linda Thompson as the Strawberry woman and Lynn Randolph as the Crabman.
The performance's flaws and virtues could be dwelled upon at more length. Such scrutiny would not change, however, the basic objection to the Metropolitan's ''Porgy'': this is a well-meaning inflation and hence distortion of a pioneering work in American musical theater. Mr. Brown, essentially a theater director, can hardly be blamed for the lingering musical misjudgments, but his restaging does little to bring the work into sharper dramatic focus. That probably will only be possible when ''Porgy'' is trimmed back to sensible proportions and performed as the durable, though minor, opera that it is, not as Wagnerian Gershwin.
 
The Cast

SECTION: Section C; Page 16, Column 3; Weekend Desk

LENGTH: 1218 words

HEADLINE: Film Festival;
A Blissful 'Mystery Train' From Jim Jarmusch

BYLINE: By VINCENT CANBY

 BODY:
It's a slow night at the Arcade Hotel in Memphis ($22 a night for a double). Says the bellboy, as he reads from a tabloid, to the night clerk, who presides over a majestic account book, ''At the time of his death, Elvis Presley would have weighed 648 pounds on Jupiter.''
Elvis Presley - the myth, the music and (in one scene) the man -hovers over Jim Jarmusch's blissful new comedy, ''Mystery Train,'' providing the bridges between the film's three separate but vaguely interrelated vignettes. The Arcade doesn't offer its patrons television (not that the usual Arcade patrons would be interested in it anyway), but every room does have its own portrait of Elvis. One can't be sure, but the portraits look to be tinted.
What makes this night different at the Arcade is that these three most particular sets of patrons would prefer television to Elvis. That is, all except Mitzuko, the effervescent Japanese teen-ager who has traveled to Memphis on a pilgrimage with her dour teen-age-suave boyfriend, Jun. 
In the film's opening story, Jun and Mitzuko are first seen on the train taking them into Memphis, each in the private world of a Walkman headset, riding past small mountains of worn-out automobiles, most probably Japanese.
The Memphis that Mr. Jarmusch elects to show us in ''Mystery Train'' is no Memphis that any hometown booster would want to recognize. It's a place of abandoned movie houses, eerily empty streets and ailanthus trees growing like weeds in otherwise vacant lots.
To Mitzuko, who has come to pay her respects to Elvis at his shrine of Graceland, Memphis is magical. She admires the ''antique atmosphere'' of the dilapidated railroad station. Mitzuko and Jun walk through the city, each holding an end of the bamboo pole on which their huge red suitcase dangles.
Her eyes are saucers. Jun, who prefers Carl Perkins and Roy Orbison to Elvis, wants to visit the legendary Sun Studios where his idols recorded their masterworks. He remains unimpressed by the Memphis landscape. ''If you took away 60 percent of the buildings,'' he says, ''Yokohama would look just like Memphis.''
When, at the end of the day, they are finally ensconced at the Arcade, Jun starts taking pictures of their room. Why? He'll remember the sights outside, he says, but he'll need the snaps to recall everything else, including the airports they've passed through.
Jun and Mitzuko are tourists, serenely voyaging on the cheap as they make the grand tour of America's holy places. The second segment of ''Mystery Train'' is about Luisa, a pretty Italian widow, forced to spend a night in Memphis while accompanying the body of her husband back to Rome. In the lobby of the Arcade, she befriends DeeDee, a chattering young woman who, nearly broke, is running away from her boyfriend. Luisa offers to share her room with the younger woman.
DeeDee is fleeing Memphis while Luisa is just passing through. For her, Memphis is an anonymous stopover until, while sitting in a coffee shop, she is gently accosted by a tall, skinny man with a strange story. The gist of his tale is that the ghost of Elvis has entrusted him with a crummy pocket comb to be given to Luisa. She listens, amused and rather pleased, until the man suggests they continue their dialogue elsewhere.
Luisa has a startling vision in the night. The next morning, as she and DeeDee are packing to leave, they hear a sharp report from the next room. ''What's that?'' says DeeDee. ''A gunshot?'' ''Maybe a .38,'' says Luisa.
The third vignette, which more or less brings all the elements of the narrative together, involves the fellow DeeDee is leaving, an unemployed, working-class Englishman who is named Johnny and nicknamed Elvis, and his two pals. They are Will Robinson, a laid-back truck driver, and Charlie, DeeDee's brother, a fastidious barber who can't hold his liquor.
In the course of a feckless spree, Elvis holds up a liquor store for two bottles of whisky, and a gun goes off, forcing the three to seek a hideout at the Arcade. They wind up in a barren room equipped with two beds, an Elvis Presley portrait and a table to which a pair of joke-handcuffs are attached. This is the Arcade's kinky-sex room. It's not easy to describe the dizzy gaiety of Mr. Jarmusch's films, each set in a universe that seems to be even more torpid than that of the film before. ''Mystery Train'' is buoyantly funny from start to finish. One doesn't laugh out loud all that often. One grins and, as the odd events and eccentric information accumulate, one begins to recognize an enchanted vision within the seediness.
Mr. Jarmusch is an American original who, for all of his hipness, is remarkably benign. Like ''Stranger Than Paradise'' and ''Down by Law,'' the first two Jarmusch films to be commercially released, ''Mystery Train'' has something of the pragmatic craziness of the great Krazy Kat cartoons.
Nobody tosses bricks in ''Mystery Train,'' which, unlike Krazy Kat and the two earlier comedies, has been photographed (by Robby Muller) in brilliant colors. Yet there is a bluntness to the Jarmusch method that gives substance to the seemingly fragile narratives.
It's now obvious that calling his comedies shaggy-dog stories isn't good enough. They aren't really stories at all. Each is an amalgam of attitudes, images and music, especially music (blues, jazz, rock), which evoke something most cheering and comic about the human condition, even in extremis.
Watching ''Mystery Train'' for the second time, I was especially struck by what a fine screenwriter Mr. Jarmusch has become. The dialogue sounds as if it had been gathered by means of microphones hidden in diners, buses, waiting rooms, restrooms, motels and park benches. Sometimes hilariously banal, with never a word wasted.
He's equally good with actors. The ''Mystery Train'' performers fly. There isn't enough space to give them all their due, but those who must be mentioned include Masatoshi Nagase and Youki Kudoh, as Jun and Mitzuko, Nicoletta Braschi as Luisa, Elizabeth Bracco (who looks a bit like a young Rita Tushingham) as DeeDee, Joe Strummer as ''Elvis,'' Rick Aviles as the truckdriver Will, and Steve Buscemi as the unfortunate barber.
Presiding over these hapless folk are Screamin' Jay Hawkins, who plays the Arcade's unflappable night clerk, and Cinque Lee (Spike's younger brother), who plays the bellboy. John Lurie, the actor-musician star of ''Stranger Than Paradise,'' wrote the film's fine score, which is punctuated by Elvis hits.
''Mystery Train'' will be shown at the New York Film Festival today at 6:15 P.M. and tomorrow at 9:30 P.M. It will open its commercial engagement here Nov. 17.
 
Gaiety Amid Torpor
 
MYSTERY TRAIN, direction and screenplay by Jim Jarmusch; director of photography, Robby Muller; edited by Melody London; music by John Lurie; production design by Dan Bishop; produced by Jim Stark; released by Orion Classics. At Alice Tully Hall, as part of the 27th New York Film Festival. Running time: 110 minutes. This film has no rating.

The Economy
The House of Representatives approved a reduction in the capital gains tax rate, giving President Bush a significant legislative victory that wounded the new Democratic leadership. The Senate has not yet acted on the measure, which would reduce the gains tax through the end of 1991. [Page A1.] The vote was 239 to 190, as 64 Democrats joined 175 Republicans in supporting the tax cut. One Republican voted against the proposal. [D4.] 
 
Federal officials will permit an experimental AIDS drug, DDI, to be widely distributed while it is still being tested to determine its safety and effectiveness. The decision is a major depature from the agency's cautious approach. DDI is made by Bristol-Myers. [A1.] The F.D.A. said Bolar Pharmaceutical had failed to demonstrate that its generic version of the anti-high-blood pressure drug Dyazide was biologically equivalent to the original product. [D3.]

Braniff Airlines filed for bankruptcy protection, caught between the high cost of an aggressive expansion and the difficulty of securing financing in a troubled ''junk bond'' market. The airline said it would try to rebuild as a smaller carrier serving 11 cities, down from 36. Two important ceramics companies agreed to merge. The Kyocera-AVX deal is believed to be the first merger of a Japanese company and an American company in an exchange of stock. [D1.]

LENGTH: 652 words

HEADLINE: Anheuser Is Buying Parks From Harcourt

BYLINE: By THOMAS C. HAYES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DALLAS, Sept. 28

 BODY:
The Anheuser-Busch Companies, the nation's largest brewer, said today that it would pay $1.1 billion in cash to acquire four Sea World theme parks and other properties from Harcourt Brace Jovanovich Inc.
The sale price is below the $1.5 billion expected by some Wall Street analysts after the parks were put up for sale in June. Some bond analysts, who asked not to be named, said Harcourt, the nation's second-largest book publisher, may now be forced to sell its profitable insurance business as well to meet heavy debt payments.
The low price also indicates that some companies that loaded up on debt through buyouts and recapitalizations in recent years may not be able to collect as much as anticipated from asset sales, analysts said. 
 
High-Priced Deals 'Are Suspect'
''I think you're getting some deflation in asset sale prices,'' said Michael F. Holland, president of Salomon Brothers Asset Management. The high-priced deals ''predicated on asset sales are suspect.''
Harcourt eluded a takeover by Robert Maxwell, the British publisher, two years ago by taking on $1.6 billion in high-yield ''junk bonds'' and paying much of the proceeds to shareholders as a special $40-a-share dividend. Bond analysts said the company, which paid $91 million in interest and lost $61 million in the second quarter, remains in a bind.
The publisher, based in Orlando, Fla., released a two-paragraph statement shortly after its directors approved the sale in New York, and it refused to comment further.
In the statement, the company said it would use cash proceeds of about $930 million after taxes to reduce long-term debt, which at the end of June amounted to $2.8 billion, including $1.1 billion owed to banks.
On the New York Stock Exchange, trading in Harcourt stock was halted in late afternoon shortly after the announcement, and it did not reopen. Around 4 P.M. one securities firm that trades stocks in the ''third market'' - in which brokers match trades between institutions outside of an exchange - handled a sale of more than 50,000 shares at $14, down from the last posted price of $16.25.
In the junk bond market, Harcourt bonds traded lower shortly after the announcement, although dealers said volume was light.
The sale will triple revenues of Anheuser's existing theme-park business to approximately $600 million a year, said Emanuel Goldman, an analyst with Paine Webber Inc., and may give it potentially valuable marketing rights to Sea World characters, like Shamu, the killer whale.
The agreement with Anheuser gives Harcourt the right to half of any money in excess of $100 million that Anheuser receives from land sales in the next 10 years involving property included in the transaction.
Most of the undeveloped acreage is in Florida, including prime land near the Orlando International Airport, but analysts said Harcourt was unlikely to recover much from that provision of the sale.
Some analysts criticized the transaction, saying August A. Busch 3d, chairman and chief executive, could best exploit the company's widely acknowledged prowess in consumer marketing by acquiring packaged food companies instead. $61.9 Million in Profits Anheuser said it paid $975 million for the theme parks and $125 million for undeveloped land. The parks earned $61.9 million last year on $388 million in revenues.
But attendance at the Orlando Sea World declined last summer because of competition from the new Disney-MGM Studio Tour at Walt Disney World. Attendance fell in San Antonio in the second year of operations at the newest and largest Sea World.
Other Sea World parks are in San Diego and Cleveland. Anheuser also acquired Cypress Gardens in Winter Haven, Fla., and the new Boardwalk and Baseball Park in Orlando.
W. Randolph Baker, an Anheuser group vice president and president of its family entertainment unit, said Anheuser does not plan to sell any of the parks.

HEADLINE: Borden to Cut 7,000 Jobs And 65 Plants

BYLINE: By ROBERT J. COLE

 BODY:
After a three-and-a-half-year buying binge in which it spent $1.7 billion and more than doubled the number of its plants, Borden Inc. reversed course yesterday. The nation's No. 1 dairy company said it will close or sell 65 of its 265 plants in the next two or three years and cut worldwide employment by 7,000 people, bringing its payroll down to 39,000.
Borden said the streamlining would involve mostly food operations in the East and Southeast: 20 dairy plants and another 45 making grocery products and snack foods. Lawrence O. Doza, Borden's chief financial officer, said severance payments to workers and plant-closing costs would produce a third-quarter loss as high as $305 million and a loss for the year of between $47 million and $58 million. To meet the costs, Borden said it had set up a pretax reserve of $571 million, or $404 million after taxes, which it would take as a third-quarter charge to income. 
 
Sharp Cut in Dairy Business
After the announcement, Borden's share price jumped $2.25, to $36.75, on the New York Stock Exchange.
Under the plan, Borden's dairy business would shrink by a third, or as much as $750 million a year, from $2.2 billion in 1988. The plan would not affect ice cream. Some consumer plants abroad would also be affected, the company said. It declined to specify the plants that would close, until it had notified workers and customers. It said it would announce the first closings within 10 days.
Identified everywhere by Elsie the Cow, the corporate symbol that was introduced in the Depression years and popularized at the New York World's Fair in 1939, Borden will remain the leading dairy concern even after the plant closings.
The company also makes well-known brands like Cracker Jack popcorn, Wise potato chips, Creamette macaroni, Elmer's glue, Krazy Glue and Realemon juice. Borden is popular on Wall Street because it is so widely diversified, thus reducing its vulnerability to downturns.
 
Intense Competition Cited
Romeo J. Ventres, chairman and chief executive, attributed the downsizing in part to a record increase in raw milk costs and to intense price competition. Without naming the competitors, he described them as leveraged-buyout companies ''hunting volume and cash at any cost.''
He was understood to be referring to Morningstar Foods Inc., a Dallas-based company that last year bought the dairy business of the Southland Corporation, operator of 7-Eleven convenience stores. Morningstar has 15 regional dairy brands and has been conducting a price war in some areas.
''Our dairy earnings have been severely depressed,'' Mr. Ventres said, adding, ''We've concluded there are certain markets where recovery is years away.''
Food companies, including Borden, are rightly considered takeover targets. But Mr. Ventres denied that the revamping was a defensive move. Explaining what he called a streamlining, consolidation and dairy-restructuring program, he said Borden's rapid growth since 1986 ''has opened economies of scale we've never had before - opportunities to consolidate production into fewer and larger units and become low-cost producers in every business.'' Many of the closings, he said, involve smaller or older plants.
He said the programs were expected to improve total pretax earnings over the coming four years by more than $340 million but that little impact was expected through 1990.
Dairy-plant closings were attributed to heavy competition in Eastern and Southeastern markets, the proliferation of supermarket chains with their own dairies, the development of strong regional and farmers' cooperative dairies - plus no growth.
Borden said it planned to increase its advertising and promotional spending by $75 million to $100 million over the next two years, from $507 million this year.
