HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
A Champion Of Fiscal Principle

BYLINE: By Leonard Silk

 BODY:
WITH the showdown in Congress over the proposed cut in the capital gains tax fast approaching, Joseph A. Pechman, the economist who for four decades was regarded by both liberals and conservatives as the nation's leading authority on fiscal policy, was not here to play his usual role as ''happy warrior'' in the final phase of the battle. The House voted yesterday to support President Bush on cutting the tax, but the issue must still be fought out in the Senate.
But before his death last month, Mr. Pechman had made known his opposition to cutting the tax. He calculated - and his strength was not in ideology but in clear and forceful reasoning and the use of the best available data - that after a one-shot gain in revenues the proposed cuts in the tax would lose revenues for the Treasury for years, worsening the grave problem of inadequate national savings. He also feared that cutting capital gains taxes would start the process of undermining the entire Tax Reform Act of 1986 and return Congress to the course it loves so well: granting special favors to special interests. 
He further concluded that the tax cut would be inequitable in giving its principal tax breaks to the rich, rather than the middle class or the poor. Presenting the data to support that finding, in response to data and arguments in editorials in The Wall Street Journal, which asserted the contrary, Mr. Pechman said, ''The simple truth is that low-income people don't own many assets on which to earn capital gains and that a capital-gains tax cut would be a bonanza for high-income people.''
That was Joe Pechman: He called them as he saw them, and he saw them very sharply, as his opponents knew as well as his allies. Speaking last week at the Brookings Institution in Washington, President Bush's chief economic adviser, Michael J. Boskin of Stanford University, said that though he was often an ''antagonist'' of Mr. Pechman's on policy issues, ''we all respected and loved Joe.''
Mr. Boskin recalled how, after every national election, Mr. Pechman would send out word to selected economists, including Mr. Boskin, who had wound up on the losing side: ''Come to Washington,'' by which he meant ''Come to Brookings.'' Mr. Pechman regarded Brookings as a ''government-in-exile,'' not just for Democrats but also for Republicans of high intellectual quality. He felt that liberals and conservatives needed to confront each other in tough and honest argument, if the truth were to emerge.
He loved a good fight - in tennis, touch football or tax policy. A young fiscal economist at the Congressional Budget Office this week, while talking about the tax bill, remembered Mr. Pechman's boundless excitement over economics, and he said that Mr. Pechman's enthusiasm for what some people thought was dismal stuff had led him to his own career on the Hill.
Mr. Pechman never had any doubt that he was made for economics, and economics for him. After graduating from the City College of New York in the Depression, the mother of many economists of his generation, he went to the University of Wisconsin in Madison for his graduate studies. He quickly became assistant director of a major Wisconsin tax study under Prof. Harold Grove. During World War II, he did a brief stint in the Office of Price Administration as an economist, then went into the Army as a meteorological officer and saw action in Europe during the invasion and the Battle of the Bulge.
After the war, he joined the Treasury as a tax adviser, first under President Harry Truman, then in the Eisenhower Administration. A liberal in his own political philosophy, he respected conservatives with integrity. He served on the staff of President Eisenhower's Council of Economic Advisers under Arthur F. Burns. Later, during the Kennedy and Johnson Administrations, while at Brookings, he was a key ''outside'' adviser to Walter W. Heller, a close friend from Wisconsin days, and Gardner Ackley and Arthur Okun, other close friends. Economics is not a monologue art. But Mr. Pechman never took a full-time job in Government again; some officials regarded him as too hot a potato to handle. But he found a home at Brookings, where he spent the rest of his career.
There he led the longest and deepest investigation ever of the nation's tax system: It eventuated in dozens of books and scores of journal articles, which, through Congressional testimony and press reports, heavily influenced public fiscal policy.
The Tax Reform Act of 1986, built on the cardinal principle of eliminating favored treatment of particular classes of income and thereby making possible deep cuts in tax rates, was the finest flower of Mr. Pechman's studies. In his garden, the capital gains tax cut would be a weed.
Tax policy was not the whole of his contribution. He directed, and partly wrote, the series of volumes called Studies in Government Regulation that paved the way for the deregulation movement.
With the crucial help of his colleague Charles L. Schultze, he led the Brookings program called Setting National Priorities, which tried desperately, brilliantly but so far unsuccessfully to give greater order and coherence to the way the United States Government spends the public's money. He hadn't finished his work.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Campeau Halts Dividends

 BODY:
The Campeau Corporation's board said yesterday that it would not pay dividends scheduled to be paid tomorrow on the company's series B preference shares, series C preference shares, series D preference shares and convertible subordinate shares.
The board said the decision was made because of the company's cash shortage. The company said that the board would reconsider the payment of dividends before the end of the year. 
Separately, Reuters reported that a senior management group of Bloomingdale's, one of the department store chains put up for sale by Campeau to raise cash, said yesterday that it had hired the Blackstone Group and Drexel Burnham Lambert Inc. to assist ''in evaluating its alternatives.''
Marvin S. Traub, the chairman of Bloomingdale's, added that Gotshal & Manges was also hired as legal counsel to help the managers.
Campeau's stock, which is traded on the Toronto Stock Exchange, fell $1.875, to $9.50, after it said it was suspending dividends after trading had closed for the day.
Campeau has been struggling with a financial crisis all month. It said on Sept. 19 that it had reached an agreement for a $250 million loan to help ease a cash shortage at its nine department store divisions in the United States. The agreement significantly reduced the power of Robert Campeau, the chairman and founder of the Toronto-based development and retail company who borrowed heavily to take over the American stores.
Campeau has denied reports that some of its department stores will soon cut prices to raise money.
The sale of Bloomingdale's and other Campeau retail stores is not the only one announced recently. The Hooker Corporation of Australia is selling its Bonwit Teller and B. Altman & Company chains, and earlier this week, B.A.T. Industries P.L.C. of Britain said it was putting its Saks Fifth Avenue and Marshall Field & Company chains on the block to escape a hostile $21 billion takeover bid from a group led by Sir James Goldsmith, the British-French financier.
Philip B. Miller, the chairman of Marshall Field in Chicago, said he and at least 14 senior executives would try to buy the 26-store chain with the help of ''a well-financed partner, a company with a strong capital base.''

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Is Stock Swap Good for AVX?

BYLINE: By Floyd Norris

 BODY:
THE ''go go years'' of the late 1960's may be coming back, but with an Asian flavor.
The Wall Street of those years featured a favored group of conglomerates that had been awarded high price-earnings ratios by a stock market entranced by the supposed synergy of mergers. The companies were able to buy up other companies for stock that eventually came to be seen as ridiculously overvalued.
Yesterday, Kyocera, a Japanese electronics concern, agreed to buy the AVX Corporation, an American company, in what Kyocera said was the first stock swap involving companies from the two countries. 
Whether the AVX holders are getting a good deal depends on what numbers you look at. By market price, there is no doubt that they are benefiting. But book values and earnings tell a different story. AVX holders will be trading a share with a book value of $15.85 for stock with a book value of about $6.97. The share they give up represented earnings of $2.07. compared with profits of $1.08 for the stock they are to get, based on the most recent fiscal years of each company.
AVX shares leaped $8, to $29.625, in New York Stock Exchange trading yesterday as the offer was made to exchange each share for 0.39 American depository share of Kyocera, which rose 12.5 cents, to $80.625. At that price, the indicated value of the Kyocera stock being offered is $31.44.
Such stock swaps seem like a great idea for all concerned, and it will be no surprise if a trend is born. AVX holders get a big premium for their shares, and Kyocera holders get assets cheaply, considering the relative book values. Kyocera's earnings per share should rise as a result of the deal, although not by a large amount because Kyocera is a much larger company.
The go-go years of the 1960's were fun while they lasted, but eventually the bubble burst, and more than a few of the conglomerates went under. Some of the ones that survived did so in part because their managers had the wisdom to use their overvalued stock to buy real assets. If the stock market thinks a piece of paper is worth $5, when it is really worth $1, it is a good move to use that paper to buy assets worth $2.50. The seller thinks he is getting a great deal, and the buyer has improved the real value of the stock.
With a world economy that is becoming more integrated, and with companies increasingly owning productive assets in many countries, it is not so clear as it once was that companies that happen to be based in one country should be valued by widely differing standards from companies based in another. But that has happened as relates to Japan and almost every other country in the world, and Kyocera is taking advantage of the fact.
For now, AVX holders are clearly ahead. When the deal is completed later this year, they will have to decide whether to hold onto the Kyocera stock or cash in. In the American go-go years, the biggest winners were those who sold the overvalued shares while the investment fever was still raging. It is a precedent the AVX holders might wish to recall.
 
Viacom's Borrowing Success
The investment world views Viacom International Inc. as one of the success stories among leveraged buyouts. Yesterday, Viacom announced a deal with its banks, led by Citibank, that raises the amount of money it can borrow to $1.5 billion from $1 billion, reduces the rates it must pay and converts the loans to unsecured status, thus freeing assets to be pledged for other loans.
''The banks have given us more freedom to operate the company,'' said Thomas Dooley, Viacom's treasurer. For now, Viacom plans to leave $300 million of its new credit line unused. It could raise $400 million or more by pledging assets the banks have freed, Mr. Dooley said, adding that the company was looking at possible acquisitions.
Viacom will retire some of the payment-in-kind bonds it issued to help finance the buyout. By keeping $300 million of such bonds outstanding, Mr. Dooley said, it will increase its flexibility.
Viacom, which owns MTV, Showtime, the Movie Channel and distribution rights to ''The Cosby Show,'' ''Roseanne'' and ''A Different World,'' as well as 14 cable systems, 5 television stations and 8 radio stations, has also fared well in the stock market. The 1987 leveraged buyout, in which Sumner M. Redstone's National Amusements Inc., a movie theater owner, took control, left 17 percent of the stock in public hands, and those who bought then have seen the price more than double, to $56.25 a share yesterday, on the Big Board.
It may be worth noting that, if asset sales are excluded, Viacom has not reported a quarterly profit since the buyout, as interest payments have overwhelmed rising operating profits. That a company can be deemed a success under those circumstances shows the new era in finance is still with us, ''junk bond'' troubles notwithstanding.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Prices Ease, and Trading Is Light

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury notes and bonds eased slightly in thin trading yesterday, as dealers reacted to the sale of a large list of mortgage-backed securities by selling Treasury issues.
As much as $800 million worth of the mortgage-backed issues were rumored to have been sold. Bigger amounts have been sold in the past, and the impact on Treasury issues has been slight.
But activity was so light that the mortgage-backed sale had a ripple effect. 
''The fact that the market went down when some mortgage-backed issues hit the Street suggests that it is still vulnerable to new supply,'' said James R. Capra, a senior vice president at Shearson Lehman Government Securities. ''The market's psychology continues to be fragile, and can be affected by the most minor development.''
 
Tracking the Dollar
Traders also tracked movements in the dollar, which moved lower early in the day but recovered ground later.
By late in the day, the Treasury's 8 1/8 percent bonds of 2019 were offered at a price of 98 5/8, down 2/32, to yield 8.24 percent.
Prices of secondary note issues also registered small declines.
In late trading, for example, the 8 percent 10-year notes were offered at 98, down 2/32, to yield 8.29 percent. And the 8 1/4 percent five-year notes were offered at 99 19/32, down 6/32, to yield 8.33 percent.
Yields on two- and four-year notes auctioned earlier this week rose in when-issued trading.
The 8 3/8 percent two-year notes were offered on a when-issued basis at a price to yield 8.44 percent late yesterday. And the 8 1/4 percent four-year notes were offered at a price to yield 8.40 percent. 3-Month Bills at 7.87% Treasury bill rates were mixed.
By late in the day, three-month bills were offered at a discount rate of 7.87 percent, up 6 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point. And six-month bill rates were unchanged, at a late offered rate of 7.82 percent.
Word that the House had voted to cut the top capital gains tax to 19.6 percent from the current level of 33 percent had no impact on trading.
Analysts said lowering the capital gains tax, which is subject to approval by the Senate, was a mixed blessing.
 
'Favorable for Bonds'
''Obviously, the capital gains cut is favorable for bonds, but much less so than for other assets like stocks and real estate, because most of the return in bonds is in ordinary income,'' said Louis v. B. Crandall, chief economist at R. H. Wrightson & Associates, an economic consulting firm.
''But the economic impact of the change would be negative, because it dispels the notion that the 1986 tax act was the bill to end all tax bills,'' he said. ''This battle over capital gains, whether it passes or not, reminds every individual that the tax code is very changeable, and that you can't make any long-term planning.''
Issues that could be sold for capital gains purposes include Treasury bonds issued early in the 1980's, when interest rates were at double-digit levels, as well as corporate and tax-exempt municipal bonds that were purchased at a deep discount and have subsequently risen in value.
 
T.V.A. Dispute Resolved
In other developments, the Tennessee Valley Authority said that a four-month dispute with the Treasury over its ability to borrow from the Federal Financing Bank had been resolved, and that it intended to offer $1 billion or more of debt securities in the public market early next month.
The T.V.A. has been seeking to refinance $6.5 billion worth of high-interest debt it borrowed from the Federal Financing Bank in the early 1980's.
Until yesterday, the bank's position had been that if the T.V.A. sought to refinance the bonds in the public market, it would lose its status as a future borrower.
After an intense lobbying effort, the Treasury's position changed yesterday, said William F. Malec, the T.V.A.'s chief financial officer. $2 Billion Credit Line Mr. Malec said the Treasury had agreed to provide the T.V.A. with a $2 billion line of credit for the next two years. After that, the relationship will be reviewed again.
''What we now have is the best of all worlds,'' Mr. Malec said. The T.V.A. has been seeking to refinance its debt because with interest rates at current levels it will save up to $100 million a year in interest costs.
Mr. Malec said the coming issue, which could be offered during the week of Oct. 9, might be offered in several parts.
''There is a good possibility that it will be for more than one maturity,'' he said.
 
Tokyo Bond Issues
Elsewhere yesterday, two taxable bond issues, including a $175 million offering from the Metropolis of Tokyo, were priced in the new-issue market.
The 8.70 percent Tokyo notes, which mature on Oct. 5, 1999, were priced at $99.90 to yield 8.75 percent, 42 basis points above the prevailing yield on 8 percent Treasury notes at the time of pricing.
Goldman, Sachs & Company acted as lead manager for the issue, which is rated AAA by Moody's and Standard & Poor's. The offering is the fifth sale of Tokyo debt in the United States.
Morgan Stanley & Company acted as lead manager for a $100 million offering of floating-rate notes from the BankAmerica Corporation.
The notes, which have a final maturity of Oct. 21, 1992, are noncallable for life.
The one-month London interbank offered rate will serve as the coupon on the notes, which will be set monthly. The first rate will be set on Oct. 3. The issue is rated A-3 by Moody's and BBB by S.&P.
In other developments, the Federal Reserve Board reported yesterday that two of the three measures of the nation's money supply fell during the week ended Sept. 18. The third measure, M-2, rose by $3.9 billion.
The report had no impact on prices in the credit markets.

INDUSTRY: NAICS523110 INVESTMENT BANKING & SECURITIES DEALING (56%); SIC6211 SECURITY BROKERS, DEALERS, & FLOTATION COMPANIES (56%); 

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (79%); 

COMPANY: LEHMAN BROTHERS HOLDINGS INC (56%);

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Closes Mostly Up; Central Banks Still Active

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar moved mostly higher yesterday although central banks again put pressure on the currency, acting on a belief that the dollar's advance this year could have unpleasant economic consequences.
Gold prices finished with minor changes in the United States after moving lower overseas. The Republic National Bank of New York quoted a bid of $366.90 for an ounce at 4 P.M., up 70 cents from Wednesday's late bid. 
John McCarthy, chief currency dealer at the New York office of the Amsterdam Rotterdam Bank, said trading volume was moderate and punctuated by rounds of central bank intervention.
 
Dealers Unwinding Positions
Activity has slackened in recent days with dealers unwinding positions as the month ends. Trading maneuvers tied to the close of Japan's fiscal year on Sept. 30 have also reduced liquidity in the currency markets, he said.
''The central banks intervened three or four times,'' Mr. McCarthy said. ''It did have the desired effect -the dollar is closing lower than where it opened in New York'' but above Wednesday's late levels.
The United States and its allies in the Group of Seven have resolved to keep a tight leash on the dollar. They agreed at a weekend meeting in Washington that the dollar's rise this year threatened to hurt the delicate balance of economic growth among major trading partners. The other nations are Japan, West Germany, Britain, France, Canada and Italy.
In late New York trading, the dollar was quoted at 140.50 yen, up from 140.32 yen Wednesday. A pound fetched $1.61065, compared with $1.6167 late Wednesday.
 
Late New York Rates
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Wednesday's late rates, included: 1.88175 West German marks, up from 1.8805; 1.6308 Swiss francs, up from 1.6305; 1.17995 Canadian dollars, up from 1.1771; 6.376 French francs, up from 6.3655, and 1,370.50 Italian lire, up from 1,361.7.
Gold prices were mixed in the United States after declining in light trading overseas.
On the Commodity Exchange in New York, gold for October delivery rose to $368.10 an ounce, up 50 cents from Wednesday's settlement. The September contract closed at $367.60 on Wednesday, its final trading day.
Silver for October delivery rose to $5.265 an ounce, from Wednesday's settlement of $5.252.

HEADLINE: Hispanic Groups Organizing Drive for a Full Census Count

BYLINE: By ROBERTO SURO, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 23

 BODY:
In the largest effort ever undertaken by Hispanic Americans to gain political power, advocacy groups are organizing a campaign to turn out the highest possible count in the 1990 census.
Many court actions are also being fought to insure that census figures are translated into electoral muscle. And Hispanic groups, among others, are challenging a provision adopted by the Senate and now before the House of Representatives that would exclude illegal aliens from the population figures used for reapportionment.
The Bureau of the Census estimates that the 1990 count will show a 34.5 percent increase in the number of Hispanic Americans over the 1980 figures, while the total population is expected to grow by only 9.9 percent.
 
Could Take a Long Time
Hispanic politicians are already planning to use this census data in support of their case for carving out electoral districts with predominantly Hispanic populations when the districts are redrawn to account for the population shifts. 
But Hispanic Americans want to be sure that the huge increase is accurately measured, and then it might take much of the next decade before they become an effective force at the polls.
For now, a disproportionately large number of Hispanic residents are not eligible to vote because they are either too young or because they are immigrants who are not yet citizens.
Organizations representing constituencies as diverse as the Puerto Ricans of Northeastern cities and Mexican-Americans of the rural Southwest are trying to overcome problems that have produced substantial undercounts of Hispanic residents. These include a language barrier, unstable housing arrangements due to poverty and the fear of Government agents.
Hispanic celebrities are now recording advertisements, census information centers are being established and neighborhood leaders are preparing to go door to door to spread the message that being counted brings benefits. Hispanic political leaders in several parts of the country say the census campaigns are the most concerted efforts made in the political arena by this diverse ethnic group,which is estimated to total more than 20 million people, or about 8 percent of the total population.
''Making sure that every Hispanic gets counted in this census is probably the biggest political challenge we have ever faced,'' said State Representative Lena Guerrero, a Texas Democrat from Austin. ''It is potentially a very important step on the long road to getting just political representation.''
Census data can be as important as vote tallies in the power struggles over redistricting that follow every census. And Hispanic political leaders view the 1990 census as an extraordinary opportunity because of the confluence of two trends: the Hispanic communities grew, and population statistics became a major weapon in civil rights battles that have already forced changes in the ethnic makeup of many state and local governments.
 
Helped by Voting Rights Act
In 1982 Congress made it easier to attack racial gerrymandering when it amended the Voting Rights Act of 1965; under the new rules, plaintiffs were no longer required to prove any intent to dilute minority voting strength. Since then it has been easier for black and Hispanic voters to file suits demanding the creation of electoral districts in which census data show they would make up a majority of the population.
A series of recent Federal court decisions, like the one that forced the revision of the New York City Charter this year, have been applied to order the reapportionment and redistricting of elected offices favoring minority representation.
In addition to providing the ammunition for redistricting, census counts help determine the distribution of Federal funds. Last month in Dallas, for example, the American Jewish Congress gathered representatives of every major ethnic group to begin a campaign to encourage participation. State Comptroller Bob Bullock of Texas told the meeting that his state had lost more than $30 million a year in Federal programs for low-income citizens because of the undercount of black and Hispanic residents in the 1980 census.
The census and the subsequent redistricting will open a period of increased emphasis on race and ethnicity as political battlegrounds, some political scientists say.
Tucker Gibson, a professor at Trinity University in San Antonio, said, ''Following the next census, minority groups are going to launch a more concerted effort than ever before to attack alleged inequities in political representation.''
He said that although the goals are important, differences among minorities could be heightened ''rather than promoting a community of interests.''
 
Hispanic Groups Stand to Gain
Although black and Asian groups in many cities are also mobilizing to get a full census count, Hispanic groups appear to have the most to gain because of the huge increase in population and its lack of representation.
Unlike blacks, who have frequently rallied around leaders or issues, Hispanic citizens have never shared a common agenda, except perhaps an interest in language issues. In fact, Hispanic political and civic leaders have often been divided by internal rivalries as well as by differences in national origins, class and political orientations. But now they appear to share a perception that the census is important to their various interests.
The Census Bureau says that at least 25 Hispanic organizations with national or regional constituencies have begun campaigns to encourage people to make themselves available to be counted. President Bush offered his support to the Hispanic effort earlier this month in an address to the United States Hispanic Chamber of Commerce as it began a census campaign.
''Across the board and all over the country Hispanics understand that higher census numbers mean more opportunities for political office,'' said Guarione Diaz, president of the Cuban American Planning Council in Miami.
In Houston, for example, Hispanic politicians say a good census count and aggressive application of the Voting Rights Act could produce a predominantly Hispanic Congressional district as well as new slots in the State Legislature and City Council.
But electing Hispanic politicians might not be easy, particularly in the early 1990's. In Texas, for instance, only about 26 percent of the total Hispanic population was registered to vote in 1988 while the figure for non-Hispanics was 56 percent, said Robert Brischetto, executive director of the Southwest Voter Research Institute.
 
Change Won't Come Overnight
A few Hispanic leaders are concerned about unrealistic expectations. Leonel J. Castillo, a candidate for the Houston City Council, said: ''Census numbers are not going to translate into political power or economic benefits overnight. In the short term, anyway, we are not going to have the votes to elect people even in some nominally Hispanic districts, and the 1990's as the decade of political empowerment could become a big disappointment.''
Arturo Vargas, who heads a $1 million census campaign for the Los Angeles-based Mexican American Legal Defense and Education Fund, argued for a long view of the process. ''The demographic forces are on our side,'' he said, ''and as we get closer to the end of the century Hispanic political power will begin to snowball.'' He noted not only that a large number of Hispanic people will reach voting age in the next decade but also that three million illegal aliens will become eligible for citizenship in the 1990's under immigration amnesty programs.
''If we don't get the numbers in the census next year, however, all the rest is meaningless,'' Mr. Vargas warned.
The battle of the census has already begun. In addition to the wrangling in Congress over the inclusion of illegal aliens in the count, New York City has led a court fight demanding that the Census Bureau commit itself to making statistical adjustments of its 1990 tallies to correct the inevitable undercount of minority groups. After the 1980 census the bureau acknowledged an undercount of almost 6 percent of the black and Hispanic populations, although many demographers said the losses were actually much higher.
So far the Census Bureau has agreed only to consider a statistical adjustment, but it is working with Hispanic groups and others to maximize the initial tally next spring. The basic count will take place by mail with forms sent out only in English. Subsequently, some 200,000 census takers will visit as many as 30 million households to count some of those who have not responded.
''Fear and apathy are our greatest enemies, and we have to count on local leaders to help us overcome them,'' said Paula Wright, who directs promotional activites in the Census Bureau's Dallas office.
Many Hispanic leaders complain that the census methodology is flawed because so many in their communities lack permanent addresses, speak little or no English and frequently have low reading skills even in Spanish. The census campaigns are focusing mainly on a media blitz that will start early next year and on recruiting neighborhood leaders who can help census takers find residents who do not respond by mail.
''There is still going to be an undercount,'' Mr. Diaz said, ''but we will do everything we can to minimize it.''

HEADLINE: SENATE DEMOCRATS WORKING TO DEVISE THEIR OWN TAX CUT

BYLINE: BY DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
Democrats in the Senate are searching for a less sweeping reduction in the capital gains tax than the one approved Thursday by the House of Representatives.
The party's leaders remain strongly opposed to giving a tax break on capital gains, one of the most highly partisan issues of the year. Still, they apparently have concluded that they do not have the votes to block a cut, and they are looking for a more palatable alternative than the House version.
A preliminary survey of senators indicates that at least 15 of the 55 Democrats and nearly all 45 Republicans are committed to voting for a reduction in some form, a senator said.
 
A Presidential Priority
President Bush has made a capital gains cut a top legislative priority, arguing that a lower tax on profits from the sale of stocks, bonds, real estate and other assets would stimulate investment and lead to a stronger economy. The lopsided vote in the House Thursday gave the President and his supporters momentum as the fight shifts to the Senate. 
As so often happens on tax questions, those who would benefit most - primarily well-to-do investors - are trying to exert influence on lawmakers, while most people express little interest in the matter.
''We're getting rolled,'' said Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan of New York, a Democrat who is against a tax cut. ''We're being jerked around by a vocal, affluent constituency.''
Senator Lloyd Bentsen of Texas, chairman of the Finance Committee, said today that he still planned to fight for more generous individual retirement accounts as an alternative tax cut that would benefit more people.
 
Playing the Variations
Nevertheless, behind the scenes, a group of Democratic senators has been meeting regularly to fashion a capital gains proposal that would not be perceived as a giveaway to the wealthy.
''There are a lot of variations and a lot of plans but nothing like a consensus yet,'' said one member of the group, Senator David Pryor of Arkansas. Others attending the meetings include Alan Cranston of California, Bob Graham of Florida, Dennis DeConcini of Arizona, David L. Boren of Oklahoma, Richard Bryan of Nevada and Joseph I. Lieberman of Connecticut.
Senator Cranston is the party whip and Senator Pryor is the chairman of the Democratic Conference, making them the second- and third-ranking members of the leadership.
''I would like to see something that makes us look responsible,'' said Senator Pryor, his face reflecting exasperation.
Mr. Lieberman said he and the others who have been meeting consider it important for Senate Democrats to learn lessons from the party's defeat in the House.
One measure being examined by the group would restrict the cut in the capital gains rate to investments in high-risk start-up businesses.
Another would create two categories of assets: capital assets, like a direct interest in a business, for which a lower tax rate would be allowed, and trading assets, like stocks and bonds sold on the market, for which there would be no tax differential.
A tax specialist on a Congressional staff said the distinctions in either of those proposals might be impossible to draw.
''We would have trouble designing those kinds of provisions, and I'm sure the people on Wall Street would find loopholes they could drive trucks through,'' the specialist said.
The senators are also considering linking a cut in the capital gains rate to the length of time an asset has been held. One idea, Senator Lieberman said, is to give no tax break when an asset is sold in the first year after it is acquired, and then, over a six-year span, lower the rate in stages from a maximum of 28 percent to 14 percent.
 
A Suggestion on Timber
Senator Pryor, whose home state, Arkansas, is a leading timber producer, suggested that many timber growers might prefer an income-averaging rule to a lower capital gains rate applying to timberland. That would allow them to pay taxes on the average income over, say, three years, rather than having a large tax liability in the years they cleared their land.
Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady, testifying before the Finance Committee today, maintained that much of the benefit of a capital gains tax cut would be realized by people with a one-time-only gain from the sale of a business, farm or home.
But a new analysis by the nonpartisan staff of the Joint Congressional Committee on Taxation seemed to dispute that contention.
The study showed that in 1985, the last year for which figures are available, 82 percent of the money claimed on tax returns as capital gains was received by taxpayers who sold more than one asset during the year.
 
Dispute Over I.R.A. Proposal
In his testimony, Mr. Brady, the Bush Administration's chief spokesman on economic matters, took exception to Senator Bentsen's plan to allow tax-deductible I.R.A.'s to all taxpayers and to allow penalty-free withdrawals to pay for college expenses and the purchase of first homes.
Mr. Bentsen argued that such a plan would assist middle-income families and lead to more personal saving.
Mr. Brady said he had no conclusive evidence that the plan would generate more savings but he knew that it would be too costly.
The House was not in session today, but telephone calls around the country found plenty of recriminations among House Democrats in the aftermath of Thursday's vote.
 
Details of the Bill
With 64 Democrats breaking party ranks, the House rejected, 239 to 190, a Democratic alternative and approved a cut in the top rate on capital gains to 19.6 percent. That is a 30 percent cut for those in the 28 percent tax bracket, but those in the 33 percent marginal bracket would also pay capital gains taxes at a 19.6 percent bracket rate. The low rate would be in effect through the end of 1991. After that, the top rate would rise to 28 percent.
The Democratic alternative would have made all taxpayers eligible for deductible I.R.A.'s and would have extended the 33 percent bracket to cover the 600,000 taxpayers with incomes above about $200,000, who are now subject to a 28 percent rate.
Lawmakers said the capital gains cut was politically appealing because it could be characterized as a tax cut while the Democrats' plan could be protrayed as a tax increase.
A senior staff member said party leaders had to learn that, however right on the merits, they could not pass a tax increase, even for the very wealthy.
''It's a battle of the soundbites,'' the aide said, using the television term for a quick phrase with instant appeal. ''It's a soundbite you can't win if you're proposing a tax increase and not a tax cut.''
 
Appealing to Voters
Another reason for the political appeal of a capital gains cut, several lawmakers said, is that while they had relatively few constituents with capital gains, many people hope to have them in the future, and thus hope to benefit from the tax cut.
Both sides agree that the House measure would generate up to $4 billion a year in additional revenue for the Government over the first two years as investors sold assets to take advantage of the new rate. The Bush Administration maintains that it would not create a significant long-term loss of revenue. The rate cut would end in 1991, but after that taxpayers would be permitted to adjust their taxable gains to reflect inflation. The Joint Tax Committee staff has estimated that the inflation-adjustment provision would lower revenues by more than $5 billiion a year.
''What we have had is a serious flaw in the positioning of the party,'' said Representative Les AuCoin of Oregon, who favors a capital gains cut but voted against the proposal Thursday partly out of loyalty to his party's leaders and partly because he thought a temporary reduction was ''short-term instant gratification that did nothing to look out toward the horizon.''
 
'The Size of the Pie'
Mr. AuCoin said he feared that ''our party is being seen as quarreling over how the pie is sliced, when we ought to be talking about expanding the size of the pie.''
Republicans in the Senate do not like the temporary cut in the House bill, and they were working today on legislation that would make the reduction permanent.
Finance Committee members said Senator Bentsen might be able to win committee approval for his I.R.A. plan as an alternative to the capital gains cut. But most who were interviewed said they recognized that they did not have the votes on the Senate floor.

HEADLINE: Bogota Chief, at U.N., Asks U.S. to Curb Drug Demand

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, Sept. 29

 BODY:
President Virgilio Barco Vargas of Colombia called on the United States and other nations today to curb the demand for drugs and to cut other assistance that he said the countries were giving to drug producers in the third world.
As speeches at the United Nations General Assembly continued for the fifth day, the Colombian President outlined a plan for his war on drug trafficking that emphasizes the role of drug-consuming nations.
Describing President Bush's national drug-control strategy as a step in the right direction, Mr. Barco echoed the statements of others from drug-producing nations, calling on the United States to do more to restrict demand for drugs.
''Every tactic and every weapon in the war against narcotics pales into significance compared with the need to reduce demand,'' he said, speaking in Spanish. ''Those who consume cocaine are contributing to the assassination of my people by the criminal drug cartels.'' 
 
Receives Standing Ovation
The Colombian President, who received the first standing ovation at this year's General Assembly, called for tighter controls on the manufacture and sale of chemicals used in cocaine production, which, he said, ''come mainly from North America and Europe.'' He also called for limits on the sale of arms to drug traffickers, steps to curb the foreign mercenaries who help Colombian traffickers and an end to the laundering of drug profits through the Western banking system.
''Somehow our sense of justice is warped when a poor farmer who feeds his family by growing coca is seen as the greater villain than the wealthy international banker who illegally transfers millions of dollars of drug money that finances terrorist acts against innocent people,'' he said.
Mr. Barco's suggestions reflect a widespread feeling among third world countries that produce the ingredients for cocaine and other hard drugs. The fact that his Government is engaged in a violent struggle against Columbia's powerful drug cartels gives him moral authority to argue the third world's viewpoint, diplomats say.
 
Chinese Criticizes West
As the 44th session of the General Assembly ended its first week today, the Chinese Foreign Minister, Qian Qichen, criticized Western nations he did not identify for accusing China of human rights violations in its suppression of the democracy movement in June.
''The overwhelming majority of the countries of the world have expressed their understanding of this action by our Government,'' he said.
He addressed the Assembly on the day Secretary General Javier Perez de Cuellar received a detailed report, entitled ''Massacre in Beijing,'' that sets out charges against the Chinese authorities from the International League for Human Rights, a New York-based watchdog group.
Mr. Qian joined the foreign ministers of the other four nations with permanent Security Council seats - Britain, France, the Soviet Union and the United States - at the five nations' traditional working lunch with the Secretary General today.
Those at the lunch discussed United Nations efforts to resolve regional conflicts around the world. In a statement, they reaffirmed support for the Secretary General's attempts to settle these disputes as well as for current Arab mediation efforts in Lebanon.
 
Address by Mubarak
Speaking of other developments in the Middle East, President Hosni Mubarak of Egypt said in his speech to the Assembly that ''conditions are now favorable'' for opening a Palestinian-Israeli dialogue without conditions. Such a dialogue, he said, would seek to eliminate conflict in the region once and for all ''on the basis of applying the principle of territory for peace, and attaining co-existence and cooperation among all peoples of the region.''
Mr. Mubarak, who also spoke as chairman of the Organization of African Unity, denounced the existing ''imbalances in the international economic order,'' and said he had been given a mandate by the African leaders to press for an international conference on the debt question.
''The problem for many third world countries, especially the African countries, is no longer one of growth and development, but rather one of survival in the literal sense of the word,'' the Egyptian President said.
Other speakers at the Assembly session today were Foreign Ministers Oskar Fischer of East Germany, Maj. Gen. Ike Nwachukwu of Nigeria, Gerard Collins of Ireland, Sheik Mohammed bin Mubarak al-Khalifa of Bahrain, Air Chief Marshal Siddhi Savetsila of Thailand, Jacques Poos of Luxembourg, Ali Akbar Velayati of Iran, and Ali Ahmed Sahlul of the Sudan. Also speaking was Josevata Kamikamica, Minister for Finance and Economic Planning of Fiji.

HEADLINE: U.S.-China Falling-Out: Much Smoke but No Fire

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BEIJING, Sept. 29

 BODY:
At almost every opportunity, Chinese officials like Teng Teng, the Deputy Minister of Education, like to fulminate about American interference in China's affairs.
So Mr. Teng's wife raised a few eyebrows this month when she showed up at the United States Embassy seeking a visa. She said she wanted to visit the couple's three children now studying in the United States.
This seeming paradox - official attacks on the United States at every turn and private visa applications -characterizes the Chinese-American relationship these days.
Both sides have criticized the other; the United States has deplored the Chinese Army's violent suppression of the democracy movement and the subsequent crackdown, and China has attacked what it describes as American hypocrisy and interference in its internal affairs. But beneath the smoke, diplomacy and exchanges continue. 
The recriminations of June and July have been toned down. Although the overall coolness could continue indefinitely, perhaps even as long as the present Chinese leadership is in place, both sides are eager to keep talking and to try to work out the problems gradually.
 
Meeting With Baker
The latest sign of the continuing diplomacy was Foreign Minister Qian Qichen's arrival in New York on Wednesday for a weeklong visit including an address today to the United Nations General Assembly and a meeting on Thursday with Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d. He is also meeting an array of other foreign ministers at the United Nations and influential private citizens in New York.
''Beneath all the rhetoric, the Sino-American relationship pretty much continues,'' said a Western diplomat in Beijing. ''The fundamentals of the relationship haven't altered.''
The widespread perception in Beijing is that both Governments are trying to maintain contact beneath the icy surface. Chinese officials regard President Bush as making a good-faith effort to rebuild relations within the constraints of American public opinion and Congressional scrutiny.
On June 5, a day after the killings of hundreds in Beijing, President Bush suspended military exchanges and arms shipments to China. Since then, the United States has also banned high-level non-diplomatic exchanges and used its leverage in the World Bank and Asian Development Bank to defer new loans to China.
But the Bush Administration has allowed waivers of the sanctions to ship commercial planes to China, and meetings continue between Mr. Baker and Mr. Qian. The Administration has continued talks with Beijing about China's admission into the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade.
 
France Is Now Denounced
In China, Ambassador James R. Lilley, a longtime friend of President Bush, has re-emerged, appearing at signing ceremonies for American investors doing business with China.
In addition, Chinese dissidents in exile say they were discouraged from holding their organizational meeting in the United States, leading them to choose Paris instead. The headquarters of the dissident organization will now be in Paris, with a subsidiary branch in the United States.
These days it is France that is being denounced in ever-tougher terms, both because French officials have been less conciliatory than Americans and because China needs France less than it needs the United States. The perception here of French intransigence is helping Chinese-American relations by setting up a contrast with the United States and diverting attention from disagreements between Washington and Beijing.
''France is a much easier target,'' an Eastern bloc diplomat said. ''It's a small country, not so important to China's economy. But the United States plays an indispensable role in China's development.''
China also appears to have continued to cooperate with United States intelligence in providing information from listening posts that can monitor Soviet communications. Leaders' Children in U.S. Furthermore, despite the frostiness in Chinese-American relations, several Politburo members, including the Communist Party leader, Jiang Zemin, and the security chief, Qiao Shi, have children studying in the United States. They are among more than 70,000 Chinese students or scholars who have gone to the United States and not yet returned.
This academic exchange is part of the vast web of Chinese-American relations determined less by diplomatic negotiation than by the emotions and yearnings of ordinary people on both sides of the Pacific Ocean.
These kinds of unofficial ties, ranging from tourism to business investment, have been affected by the Chinese crackdown in a very lopsided way.
Reacting in horror to the bloodshed and the ferocity of the subsequent crackdown, Americans have been less enthusiastic about informal contacts. Many fewer business executives want to invest in China, American tourists have mostly vanished and probably fewer than a third as many Americans are studying in China this fall as a year ago.
Ordinary Chinese have reacted in precisely the opposite way. The more strains in the official relationship, the more the denunciations of ''bourgeois liberalism'' and United States interference, the more eager they are to go to the United States.
 
'China Will Be Dead'
On Chinese campuses, students these days are forced to spend much of their time studying political ideology; in their free time they study much more frantically than ever for the Test of English as a Foreign Language so that they can apply to go to America.
''China will be dead for the next few years,'' said a Communist Party official who is now trying to visit the United States on business and then overstay his visa indefinitely. Another trusted official who has long been near the center of power in China is quietly arranging an extended visit to escape the heartache of the crackdown.
Despite official calls for ''self-reliance,'' Chinese companies are also ever more eager for American partners to escape the desperate shortage of both foreign exchange and local investment capital.
Voice of America's Chinese-language broadcasts are mostly jammed, but they are still intelligible and immensely popular among officials and students alike. China's bookshops are obliged to stock various Communist works, but the books that sell are autobiographies of President Bush and Lee Iacocca, along with guides to American universities and Nabokov's ''Lolita.''
While the diplomatic relationship has thawed somewhat in the last two months, many analysts say it could deteriorate further before the end of the year. One reason is that Chinese dissidents in America may attract more attention soon when they set up a branch office in the United States. The second and more important reason is that the United States may approve further economic sanctions against China.
 
Congress Plans More Sanctions
''The big unknown right now is Congress,'' said Kenneth G. Lieberthal, director of the Center for Chinese Studies at the University of Michigan. Both the House and Senate have approved further economic sanctions, and a conference committee is trying to work out a compromise version that is expected to pass and be signed into law.
For American diplomats and business representatives in China, relations with counterparts are sometimes complicated by a sense of betrayal. No longer do most of them regard China as a trusted friend.
To some extent, their emotions reflect a generational split. Older diplomats in particular often appear less shaken by what happened and more interested in maintaining official ties; a repressive, suspicious China seems normal, for that is the kind they remember from their early days as China watchers. Younger diplomats frequently appear to feel more surprise and outrage at the crackdown, more of a sense that bilateral diplomacy should not be business as usual.

HEADLINE: Bush Defends Carolina Relief Efforts

BYLINE: By BERNARD WEINRAUB, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CHARLESTON, S.C., Sept. 29

 BODY:
President Bush today defended his Administration's handling of relief efforts for hurricane victims as he traveled past a vista of wrecked buildings, shattered pine forests and mangled vehicles in this state.
''I do know there's been a critic or two - that's less than I would have expected,'' Mr. Bush said at Charleston Air Force Base at the end of a two-hour visit to one of the areas devastated by Hurricane Hugo. ''But to the critics I simply say: 'I understand. We are trying very hard.' '' 
Aboard Air Force One on the flight to Charleston, Mr. Bush signed a $1.1 billion relief measure approved by Congress on Thursday. The flight here was hastily arranged Thursday night after criticism, largely by South Carolina Democrats, that Federal disaster relief was mired in bureaucracy and that Mr. Bush had shown scant interest in the disaster.
Mayor Joseph P. Riley Jr. of Charleston said, for example, said the Federal Emergency Management Agency's decision to open only two disaster aid offices in the Charleston area was ''a disgrace'' and ''absolutely ridiculous.'' Mr. Riley was not invited to join Mr. Bush, Gov. Carroll A. Campbell Jr. of South Carolina and several members of the state's Congressional delegation on today's tour.
 
More Relief Offices Planned
Marlin Fitzwater, the White House spokesman, said on the flight here that the President would expand the number of Federal disaster relief offices in South Carolina to nine from five. The 200-member staff of the Charleston centers of the Federal emergency agency would be increased by ''as many as it takes,'' Mr. Fitzwater said.
In Washington, the Senate today approved and sent to a House-Senate conference another $1.05 billion earmarked for disaster relief loans from the Small Business Administration. The funds, separate from those approved Thursday, will be used to provide low-interest loans to individuals or businesses ravaged by the hurricane.
And Commerce Secretary Robert A. Mosbacher announced that $5 million would be sent immediately to the Charleston area for rebuilding projects.
In Summerville, 22 miles northwest of Charleston, Mr. Bush stood near a school bus demolished by a pine tree uprooted in the storm and said, ''I'm here trying to mobilize the resources of the Federal Government.''
 
'It'll Never Be the Same'
Berlin Myers, the Mayor of Summerville, showed the President some of the storm damage near the Dorchester County Courthouse - trees snapped in half, roofs torn apart, vehicles crushed. ''This town is known for its pine and flowers,'' Mr. Myers said. . ''It'll never be the same again.''
Mr. Bush arrived in Charleston at daybreak and was greeted by Governor Campbell, a Republican, and Mayor Riley, a Democrat.
Before the President's arrival Mr. Riley had complained that the Federal Emergency Management Agency was engulfed in red tape and kept requesting an assessment of damage before supplying emergency food, electrical generators and temporary homes for the victims of the storm.
But Governor Campbell strongly defended the pace of relief efforts and brushed off criticisms. He told Mr. Bush that ''most people didn't realize how broad this devastation was.'' The Governor also said that 60,000 people were left homeless, 550,000 were displaced and 270,000 were out of work.
''There is frustration,'' Mr. Campbell added. ''They're tired. They're worn out. They're begging for help.''
The death toll from the hurricane was 29 in the United States, including 18 in South Carolina. The storm also caused 33 deaths in the Caribbean.
 
Hollings Assails Relief Agency
Senator Ernest F. Hollings, a South Carolina Democrat who has been one of the critics of the Federal effort, declined to fly with the President to Charleston, White House officials said. In a speech on the Senate floor Thursday, Mr. Hollings assailed the Federal Emergency Management Agency, which is responsible for coordinating Federal assistance to storm victims.
On the flight here from Washington, Mr. Fitzwater, the White House spokesman, said in response to the criticism, ''Everything the state's asked for, they've gotten.''
Poor visibility forced the President to travel by motorcade from Charleston to Summerville, but the weather cleared by midmorning, allowing a brief helicopter tour of the coast. A scheduled stop to view the devastation at Myrtle Beach was canceled because of the weather.
 
Praise for Storm Victims
At the end of the trip Mr. Bush told a small crowd at the air base, ''You couldn't help but be impressed to hear the people say, 'We're going to bounce back.' ''
Mr. Bush spent slightly more than two hours in South Carolina, squeezing in the visit before attending a previously scheduled ceremony in Annapolis, Md. The Presidnet participated in a retirement ceremony at the United States Naval Academy for Adm. William J. Crowe Jr., who is stepping down Monday as Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff.
Mr. Bush apologized that his visit was so short. ''I commend the people of South Carolina,'' he said, adding that he was also proud of the response of hurricane victims in North Carolina, Puerto Rico and the Virgin Islands.
On Puerto Rico, where local and American Red Cross officials estimate that 50,000 people were left homeless by the hurricane, the Federal Emergency Management Agency said today that it would greatly expand the number of areas on the island eligible for direct aid and loans. Originally, 16 municipal areas were declared disaster areas. Today, after further review by Federal officals, the number was raised to 56.

HEADLINE: In Carolina Fishing Hamlet, Tired Cleanup Amid Doubts

BYLINE: By KEITH SCHNEIDER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: McCLELLANVILLE, S.C., Sept. 29

 BODY:
A heavy fog lifted late this morning, and weary townspeople once more began hauling furniture out in the sun to dry and taking up shovels and hoses to remove the muck from their homes.
A week after Hurricane Hugo struck this town with 150-mile-an-hour winds and a storm surge that left homes in 10 feet of water, McClellanville's 500 residents are still without electricity, water or telephones.
Food is available from emergency shelters, but nobody is sure how long that will last. But everybody knows that the damage to this town and the many smaller, poor and mostly black communities around it is going to take months to repair. 
President Bush had intended to stop in McClellanville this morning on his tour of South Carolina, but the fog prevented his helicopter from landing. People here who had heard from visitors and the police that Mr. Bush would visit were eager for him to understand that their lives had been turned upside down and were likely to remain upside down for months.
 
Complaints About Federal Aid
Some homeowners said they were dissatisfied about not yet receiving financial assistance from insurance companies or the Federal Emergency Management Agency. Most insurance adjusters are focusing on the damage to Charleston, 37 miles south of here, and have not yet investigated conditions in tiny McClellanville.
The emergency management agency, which is providing loans, has established five offices in South Carolina, but the two closest are 30 miles away in Georgetown and in Mount Pleasant.
''And even if you go, the lines and confusion, it's hardly worth the trip,'' said Sally Warren, an office manager in Charleston who lives here in a 119-year-old house caked with mud. Ms. Warren's son, Bill, 29 years old, helped her today to lift furniture out of the house and remove the muddy carpets. And he comforted his mother. ''This is one of the few houses hit this bad that's still on its foundation,'' he said. ''You can see the watermark. It's above her head.''
 
The Flow of Support
This fishing village 37 miles northeast of Charleston was probably brutalized more than any other in South Carolina by the hurricane. At least 500 people in this region along the Santee River are homeless and living in shelters in Georgetown and on Highway 17 just south of town.
Red Cross volunteers at the St. James Santee Elementary School served 6,000 hot meals Thursday, said Anne Pilkering, the shelter's director. Food is being distributed to outlying churches and community centers. A volunteer worker said today that there were not enough people helping to do all the work. And in some cases, food is going undistributed.
''The storm did not leave anyone untouched in this area,'' said Ms. Pilkering. ''It affected all the lives of all the people here. It's the worst I've ever seen.''
McClellanville was settled about 1822, after a hurricane destroyed the prosperous rice-growing economy along the Santee. In the decades that followed, McClellanville existed apart from the rest of South Carolina as a fishing port and a summering village full of airy, handsome homes with wide porches. Tall oaks shaded the streets. Breezes washed in from the ocean five miles east across a green marsh-grass plain.
Hurricane Hugo jangled the rhythms of life here, once soft and gentle as the brush of Spanish moss. For homeowners living in town under martial law, there are no warm baths, no home-cooked meals, a scarcity of clean clothes and hours of shoveling mud out of kitchens, living rooms and dens.
People say they have enough to eat. Food has been distributed by the Red Cross and hot meals are available at shelters. Nobody has expressed certainty, though, just how long the kindness of strangers will last.
 
Peril for the Shrimp Harvest
''I'm so thankful so many people are helping,'' said Lyda Graham, a retired bank executive who lives with her brother, R. E. Graham Jr., and his wife, Maszy, in a flooded house on Pinckney Street, the town's main thoroughfare. ''The Marines are helping people take furniture out of the house and cleaning up. People are bring water and food. You just never imagined people could be this kind.''
More than 100 fishermen, some of them from North Carolina, moor their 80-foot shrimp boats along Jeremy Creek, where they supply seafood to two processors based here. Hurricane Hugo left about 40 of the boats on high ground. Some of them smashed against trees and buildings.
Shrimpers said they did not believe that the storm had ruined the shrimp beds in the estuaries where the fry develop in the winter before heading to the open sea in summer and fall; those are the prime harvesting seasons.
But the fishermen say they face financial ruin unless a crane is found to lift the boats into the water in time to lower their nets for this year's shrimp season.
''We need help getting these boats back in the water - we need it now,'' said Nathan Smith, a shrimp boat owner who spent his day by the docks commiserating with friends who had lost their boats. ''These guys in Washington need to know we're in trouble here. We're not looking for handouts. We're looking for a crane!'' The Strangers Who Help Two hundred marines from Camp Lejeune, N.C., are in McClellanville to operate heavy equipment to clear roads, cut trees and perform other cleanup duties. The National Guard also sent troops here to man outposts along the roads for security and provide transportation for equipment and supplies. In addition, people from Myrtle Beach and other towns were here as volunteers today to help townspeople to cope with the mess.
''We weren't as bad off as they were down here,'' said Kathryn Tilghman, an advertising saleswoman from North Myrtle Beach, about 80 miles north of here, during a break from cleaning a home on Pinckney Street. ''Ten of us came down and we'll be back tomorrow. You should see this woman's house. She lost antiques. Her bedding. Pictures. It's an awful lot of damage to cope with.''
Precisely. The mud oozed into every corner of every home in McClellanville. In one night last week, a way of life may have died here.
 
In the Middle of the Cleanup
''This is our family home since 1906,'' said Marian Standifer, a nurse standing in muddied clothes on the porch of her family's muddied home. The front yard was a tangle of dead marsh grass, pieces of piers and other debris washed in by the flood.
Her furnishings, covered in mud, were drying in the yard - a table, cabinets, chairs.
Her family's clothes dried on the railing, bright splashes of color amid the gray and brown.
''We're going to get this cleaned up,'' she said. ''We will.''

GRAPHIC: In McClellanville, S.C., weary residents are still sifting the debris left by Hurricane Hugo. Arch Van DeVender, who came from Maryland to help relatives, rested yesterday amid the rubble of their home. (NYT/Jose R. Lopez)

HEADLINE: How the G.O.P. Can Nail Down the Black Vote

BYLINE: By Roger Stone; Roger Stone, a partner in the public affairs company of Black, Manafort, Stone and Kelly, has worked for Presidents Bush, Reagan and Nixon.

DATELINE: ALEXANDRIA, Va.

 BODY:
In 1985, New Jersey's Governor, Thomas H. Kean, won 62 percent of the black vote in his re-election bid. He swept the state's urban areas and crept close to carrying the cities of Newark and Camden. Was this an aberration or the beginning of a trend that will dissipate the Democratic Party's monopoly on black votes? 
Such a shift could have a profound impact on our political system, even putting control of the House within the Republicans' grasp.
Representative James Courter, who hopes to succeed Mr. Kean; the Republican national chairman, Lee Atwater, and even President Bush can learn and profit from Governor Kean's sweeping success.
Republicans have taken the position that spreading the message of economic opportunity is the key to winning urban blacks' votes. While it is true that the rising black middle class has the same concerns as the white middle class - housing, jobs, safe streets, high-quality education -there are threshold issues that must be addressed before the Republican message will find receptive ears in the black community.
Mr. Kean first established himself as an unbending proponent of civil rights. Then he possessed the credibility to seek votes in the cities on a broader scope of issues of concern to the black community.
If the Republican Party's economic strategy is going to have an opportunity to sell in the black community, the party must first reclaim its birthright as the party of Lincoln.
President Bush holds out this real possibility. A more aggressive Civil Rights Division at the Justice Department, a forthright activist stand on discrimination, even a position to provide a legislative remedy to the court decision that would allow individuals to sue institutions for discrimination - all are the pieces of a necessary new G.O.P. commitment to civil rights.
Attorney General Dick Thornburgh, no slouch at winning black votes as Governor of Pennsylvania, possesses the power to help lead this change in direction, and should capitalize on his unique opportunity.
The time is ripe. The emerging black middle class will find little to appeal to them in the Rev. Jesse Jackson's message of economic empowerment: They have already achieved this goal. Black voters will buy the message of expanded opportunity if the Republicans can meet the test of a real commitment to equality.
It took the Democratic Party 100 years to get beyond the racist baggage of the Civil War. In 1932, more blacks voted for Herbert Hoover than for Franklin D. Roosevelt.
Jack Kemp, the Secretary of the Department of Housing and Urban Development, has said that Republicans were absent in the 60's struggle for civil rights. We must be in the frontline of the second struggle - the struggle for economic empowerment.
We Republicans must shed our racial baggage, including tax breaks for segregated schools and the policy of benign neglect. It may take 100 years to achieve a majority of the black vote nationally, but we must do it.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

STATE: NEW JERSEY, USA (92%); PENNSYLVANIA, USA (79%); 

CITY: NEWARK, NJ, USA (73%);

HEADLINE: Under Attack, Giuliani Calls His Ads 'Fair'

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM

 BODY:
Rudolph W. Giuliani yesterday angrily defended his criticism of David N. Dinkins's association with the Rev. Jesse Jackson and said their economic policies and support for unions would bankrupt New York City.
And last night, Mr. Giuliani broadcast his first television commercial of the general election campaign, faulting Mr. Dinkins for failing two decades ago to file income-tax returns for four years, and for filing papers indicating he had filed his returns on time.
Together, they were Mr. Giuliani's harshest attacks yet on his Democratic opponent. Yet the 30-second television spot began, ''Some people will try to tell you this is a negative commercial. But it isn't. Because it's fair, and the facts are true.'' 
David Fishlow, a spokesman for Mr. Dinkins, said this morning that he hadn't seen the commercial, but that the two candidates had met in February to discuss ''certain principles to govern the campaign,'' and at that time, Mr. Giuliani had told Mr. Dinkins the tax returns should not be an issue. ''Presumably now because of what the polls think of his chances of success . . . , he now feels obliged to raise yet again this tired old issue,'' Mr. Fishlow said.
Earlier, Mr. Giuliani, the Republican and Liberal nominee, said his newspaper advertisement picturing Mr. Dinkins with Mr. Jackson was ''perfectly fair.'' He said he was the victim of a double standard of scrutiny that was not applied to Mr. Dinkins, who had called him a Reagan Republican.
 
Some Jewish Groups Criticize Ads
''It has nothing to do with the color of your skin, your religion or ethnicity,'' Mr. Giuliani said at a campaign stop in Manhattan in midday. ''If you subscribe to the philosophy of Jesse Jackson, the people of this city should know about that.''
At the same time, several prominent rabbis and Jewish groups criticized the advertisement in this week's issue of The Algemeiner Journal, the nation's largest Yiddish newspaper, as an effort to frighten Jewish voters uneasy about Mr. Jackson. The ad, a holiday greeting for Rosh ha-Shanah, the Jewish New Year, also pictured Mr. Giuliani with President Bush. Its headline read, ''Let the people of New York choose their own destiny,''
In the commercial, white lettering appears on a black screen, spelling out the words intoned by a solemn narrator. Mr. Giuliani's name is never spoken, and a small picture of him appears only in the last frames, with the notice that the ad was paid for by his campaign.
''For four years, David Dinkins failed to file his tax returns,'' the voice says, as a picture of Internal Revenue Service forms comes on the screen. ''Twelve returns in four years, Federal, state and city. He also filed false papers claiming he did pay taxes. In fact, he didn't. Until he was caught. Haven't we had enough city officials who wait until they get caught to obey the law? Think about it. Would you be a candidate for mayor if you didn't pay your taxes?''
 
Dinkins Blamed His Inattention
A review by The New York Times this year of Mr. Dinkins's tax history showed that while he sought and received extensions for filing returns, those documents sometimes erroneously stated that he had filed returns on time the previous year, or significantly understated the total amount of taxes he owed.
He has repeatedly said the failure was the result of inattention, not evasion. He attributed the erroneous information to a filing made by his accountant that he had not reviewed. He disclosed the failure to file when investigators asked routine questions about his taxes as he was about to be named deputy mayor in 1973. He withdrew from consideration and paid the taxes with penalties and interest.
Earlier yesterday, surrounded by television cameras, Mr. Giuliani lashed out at critics of the newspaper ad. He said that Mr. Dinkins had repeatedly attacked him as a ''Reagan Republican,'' and that he was responding in kind.
''David Dinkins himself says that he's proud to be a Jesse Jackson Democrat, so how could it possibly be in bad taste?'' Mr. Giuliani said of the ad. ''And was it in bad taste when David Dinkins for four or five months went around the city talking about 'Reagan Republican' and 'reactionary?'
 
'Double Standard' Is Charged
''And where the heck was the American Jewish Committee when that was being done?'' he asked, referring to a group that had criticized the advertisement. ''Or where were they when I was being tainted with Nazism, racism and lots of other charges? They were nowhere. I say there's a double standard.''
Mr. Giuliani said: ''Jesse Jackson stands for views and policies, that's what it's all about, that some people may think - I do - are antithetical with the good of New York City.''
''Unless you want to say you cannot campaign in this election, I've got to be able to raise issues regarding policies and programs,'' he added.
Mr. Fishlow responded: ''If Rudy Giuliani wanted to discuss the issues in an ad, he should have discussed the issues in an ad.
 
Dinkins: Jackson Is Not Running
''My objection to those ads is there are no issues in them,'' he said. ''They just say David is a friend of Jesse Jackson's. The fact is, they are different. They agree on some things and disagree on others. But Jesse Jackson is not running for the mayor of New York, David Dinkins is.''
Mr. Jackson's office in Washington said he was traveling on a hurricane-relief mission in Puerto Rico and unavailable for comment yesterday.
Mr. Giuliani said Mr. Jackson's ''policies and programs'' would ''bankrupt this city.'' Later, asked if Mr. Dinkins's policies would bankrupt the city, he replied: ''Absolutely. I think David Dinkins's policies, to the extent there are economic policies, would mean fiscal disaster for New York City and I think a lot of other people think that.''
Mr. Fishlow replied that Mr. Dinkins had voted on the budget for four years as a member of the Board of Estimate and ''has a record on which the public can judge his capacity to handle city government, Giuliani's opinion to the contrary notwithstanding.''

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST: SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 30, 1989

 BODY:

 
 
The Economy
 Senate Democrats are searching for a less sweeping cut in the capital gains tax than the one approved Thursday by the House of Representatives. The party's leaders remain strongly opposed to any tax break on capital gains, one of the most highly partisan issues of the year, but apparently have concluded that they do not have the votes to block a reduction. [Page 1.] The Government's index of leading economic indicators rose three-tenths of 1 percent in August. The second consecutive monthly gain, it suggests modest growth ahead without recession. [32.] Prices that farmers received for raw products dropped seven-tenths of a percent from August to September, the Agriculture Department reported. Lower prices for cattle, hogs and potatoes were responsible for most of the decrease. [33.] 
 
 
 
Markets
 The dollar fell again, putting it below the ceilings believed set for it by the leading industrial nations against both the yen and the mark. The dollar is now at its lowest since July. Since last Saturday, when the seven top industrial nations issued a communique urging a lower dollar, the dollar has fallen 4.2 percent against the mark and 4.8 percent against the yen. [31.] American companies seeking to borrow money found Wall Street a fertile environment in the third quarter because of declining interest rates, so long as the companies were investment grade. But the quarter was not particularly good for investment banks, because of a declining appetite for new issues of common stock and junk bonds, two normally lucrative areas. [31.] Stocks ended mixed. The Dow Jones industrial average fell 2.09 points, to 2,692.82, but advances on the Big Board led declines. For the third quarter, the Dow was up 10.4 percent, the best performance since it rose 21.6 percent in the first quarter of 1987. [34.] Many banks borrowed excess reserves from other banks and institutions to show lower liabilities on their third-quarter statements, sending the Federal funds rate well above 9 percent briefly. Prices of Treasury securities slipped early in the day, dealers said, but were little changed by the end. [34.] Wheat futures prices reached three-month highs on the Chicago Board of Trade. [39.]
 
 
 
Companies
 The group that has taken over Northwest Airlines agreed to reduce the equity held by KLM, the Dutch airline, and to restrict KLM's power on Northwest's board. The decision came in response to pressure from the Bush Administration. In exchange, the Transportation Department dropped its objections to the deal. [31.] Control Data has set up a committee to select a chief executive to succeed, Robert M. Price, who will retain the chairmanship of the struggling company. [31.] Japanese financial institutions are losing top merger specialists to the American institutions they had looked to as role models. The head of mergers at Dai-Ichi Kangyo Bank, is quitting to join Goldman, Sachs & Company, investment bankers said, while his counterpart at Sumitomo Bank is job hunting in America. [31.] Campeau's American retail units said they should be able to meet their needs through the end of the year. Federated Department Stores and Allied Stores said they were paying for merchandise with cash from operations and borrowed money. [33.] Spelling Entertainment Inc. said it might put itself up for sale. The television production and distribution company is responsible for such hit series as ''Dynasty'' and ''Love Boat.'' [33.]
 
 
 
Today's Columns
 Investors have visions of capital gains windfalls dancing in their heads. But before selling any assets in the expectation of benefiting from a capital gains tax cut, investors are well advised to consider the economics of holding versus selling. Your Money. [32.] I.B.M. researchers have won patent approval for superconductor materials. But the patent's reach remains unclear, partly because researchers at the University of Arkansas maintain they discovered the fundamental composition. Patents. [32.]
