HEADLINE: The Setback for Advanced TV

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK

 BODY:
Proponents of a national effort to make American companies competitive in future television manufacturing are scrambling to regain lost momentum after a decision by the Commerce Department to abandon its plans for a focused initiative on HDTV, as the new technology is called.
But lack of support from the Bush Administration and the electronics industry's own uncertainty over the importance of high-definition television make it increasingly unlikely that the nation will mount a Government-industry program in that area, numerous Government and industry officials say.
The best that can be hoped for now, some say, are broader efforts to improve the nation's overall technological competitiveness. Some proponents of HDTV fear that such generic efforts will be too diffuse to allow American companies to re-enter the television business, which they largely abandoned to Japanese and European companies.
 
Shift by Mosbacher
Commerce Secretary Robert A. Mosbacher said recently that he was dropping his plan to develop a Government-industry initiative in high-definition television in favor of a broader policy. 
''What we've learned in the last six months is that there are a lot of other technologies that are equally important,'' said Wayne Berman, counselor to Mr. Mosbacher. A new policy, he said, would not identify a particular industry for Government assistance but would stress efforts like tax credits for research and development and relaxation of antitrust rules to allow companies to form consortiums that could make all American industries more competitive.
Proponents of a high-definition television strategy acknowledge that it is part of a broader issue but fear that a generic approach would be too weak to make much of a difference. They also suspect that some of the calls for a broader effort are really excuses for doing nothing at all. ''They're just saying it is a cop-out so they don't have to confront the HDTV issue specifically,'' said one Congressional staff member.
Even before Mr. Mosbacher's change of heart, the HDTV bandwagon, which once seemed virtually unstoppable, had been losing steam, the victim of attacks from two sides. Economic conservatives inside and outside the Administration had been opposing any Government effort to choose specific industries to assist. Michael J. Boskin, chairman of the Council of Economic Advisers, and Richard G. Darman, head of the Office of Management and Budget, are said to be leaders of this camp.
 
Too Narrow a Target
Others have been favoring some intervention but have said that high-definition television is either the wrong target or too narrow a target. Support for this view is likely to come from the National Advisory Committee on Semiconductors, a group of Government and industry leaders formed by last year's trade law.
The committee is expected to deliver a report to the President in November that will recommend Government action to help American companies enter consumer electronics ''as soon as possible and as broadly as possible,'' said Ian Ross, chairman of the committee and president of AT&T Bell Laboratories.
High-definition television is just one such product, and might not be the most desirable one to focus on because it is still years away, he said.
''I suspect you will have a hard time finding HDTV mentioned in the report as anything except a specific example,'' said Fred Horne, vice president for strategic development at the National Semiconductor Corporation, who is working on the report.
 
Proponents Not Giving Up
Despite the recent setbacks, proponents of an HDTV effort are not giving up. Recently, Mel Levine, Democrat of California, and Don Ritter, Republican of Pennsylvania, leading House proponents of a national effort in high-definition television, urged Mr. Mosbacher in a letter not to abandon his commitment to a policy backing the technology.
Edward J. Markey, the Massachusetts Democrat who is chairman of the House Energy and Commerce Committee's Subcommittee on Telecommunications and Finance, hopes to introduce a bill soon aimed at high-definition television as well as some other technologies like supercomputers, a spokesman for Mr. Markey said. He said it would probably include a mixture of broad steps like tax credits and antitrust relaxations, as well as aid to particular technologies.
Still, Congressional staff members say that a huge program for high-definition television, like the $1.3 billion Government effort recommended earlier this year by the American Electronics Association, an industry trade group, is all but dead, and any effort now will at least have to be couched in broader terms. The best that supporters can hope for in the short run is increased financing from the Defense Department.
 
Movie-Theater Clarity
High-definition television is a system that would display pictures with movie-theater clarity. Japan is already planning to begin high-definition broadcasts next year, and Europe is working on its own system.
Many industry and Government officials - including the American Electronics Association and companies like Zenith and A.T.&T., as well as, until recently, the Commerce Department - have been arguing that the United States needs to be competitive in the new television. Not only would advanced television prove to be a huge market, providing jobs and lowering the trade deficit, they say, but the technology used in such systems would also benefit industries like computers and semiconductors.
Part of the reason for this is that economies of scale in producing mass-market consumer products lower the costs for other products. Japan's consumer electronics industry has been an important market for its semiconductor companies, a market not easily available to American chip producers, they say.
Another factor is that technologies used in one product can be used in others, a phenomenon some industry officials call economies of scope.
High-definition television, industry executives say, would involve development of two key technologies: large, flat screens, and electronic chips for manipulating and displaying images quickly. Both would also have uses in areas like medical imaging and computer graphics.
 
Replacing All Sets
Advanced television represents a sharp change in technology, one that would eventually require all sets to be replaced. That would make it easier for newcomers - meaning the United States in the Japanese- and European-dominated television market -to enter a business. Otherwise, ''it's like saying, 'Let's go into an existing business and try to shove the existing competitors out,' '' said Pat Hill Hubbard, a vice president of the American Electronics Association. ''That is not a prudent business decision.''
Proponents of an HDTV effort said they never viewed the technology as a cure-all, only as a starting point for America's re-entry into consumer electronics.
If that was the intention, though, it got lost along the way. In Government and industry, high-definition television became almost the sole focus of attention, the key to restoring American competitiveness.
Such a focus was misguided, critics say. HDTV has never been the important thing, said Michael Borrus, deputy director of the Berkeley Roundtable on the International Economy at the University of California. ''High-volume high technology is the issue.''
 
High-Volume Products
He said high-volume products that use semiconductors and can drive other technologies include compact disk players, videocassette recorders, cameras, cellular telephones, facsimile machines, copiers and computer printers.
In August, the Congressional Budget Office issued a report that sharply questioned how important HDTV alone would be for American competitiveness. It pointed out that using the American Electronics Association's own rosy projections for development of the technology, sales would total only about $30 billion in 2010. That would be a small part of the total electronics equipment market, which even today is $500 billion.
By the trade association's own projections, the value of semiconductors used in American-made high-definition sets would be less than 1 percent of total world semiconductor sales, far too small for the technology to mean life or death for the United States chip industry.
''That's like saying that Ferrari or Porsche demand drives the tire market,'' said Philip Webre, principal analyst at the Congressional Budget Office.
 
Chip Demand Is Irrelevant
Mr. Webre, the main author of the report, said the often-made argument that high-definition sets use a lot of memory chips, and are therefore important to the semiconductor industry, was almost irrelevant. The main users of memory chips have been computers, an industry led by American companies. But that has not stopped the Japanese from dominating the memory-chip market. The presence of American television manufacturers will not necessarily change that, he said.
The HDTV lobbying effort suffered from other problems as well. Some people argued that the technology itself would soon be outmoded and that the nation should concentrate on what could be yet the next generation of technology - digital television delivered by optical fibers.
Another problem was that high-definition television had the image of being a trivial use of technology, one whose main advantage would be to allow viewers to see the blades of grass more clearly when they watch a football game.
Another factor, some Congressional staff members say, is that the industry never really presented a clear idea of what it wanted to do. The American Electronics Association, many people say, also erred in asking too quickly for more than $1 billion in Government aid. That alienated Mr. Mosbacher, who complained that the industry wanted to rely too much on ''Uncle Sugar.''
 
Hoping for More Money
The electronics group hopes that the Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency will get more money to finance work in high-resolution displays and related technology. The agency, which began a program on HDTV that is spending $10 million a year, sees a need for the technology both for military uses like radar screens and for supporting the American electronics industry, on which it relies.
Backers of high-definition television hope to increase the amount to $100 million a year, but that does not appear likely. So far in the appropriations process for next year, the House has allocated $50 million and the Senate only $10 million.
More support could come from the National Institute for Standards and Technology, formerly the National Bureau of Standards. The 1988 trade law authorized the agency to support advanced technology development, but no money has been appropriated for this purpose. Senator Ernest F. Hollings, Democrat of South Carolina, has introduced a bill to give that program money, but the initial amount would be only $10 million, to be spread among several technologies.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Falls On Selling By Allies

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
After a week of relentless effort to lower the value of the dollar, the United States and its economic allies succeeded yesterday in forcing the currency below key levels against the West German mark and the Japanese yen.
For the first time since early August, the dollar stood below the ceilings believed set for it by the leading industrial nations against both the mark and the yen.
The dollar had fallen below its 1.90 ceiling against the mark earlier in the week but it took five days to get it to close below the 140-yen ceiling. The dollar dropped more than seven pfennigs, or 4.2 percent, against the mark since Sept. 22 and more than seven yen, or 4.8 percent, against the Japanese currency. 
 
Started With Communique
The coordinated dollar selling was started with a communique last Saturday from the Group of Seven - the United States, Japan, West Germany, Britain, France, Italy and Canada -saying the dollar was too high. On Monday, the first day of trading after the communique, the seven central banks began immediately to support their intentions.
The dollar selling has revived respect for coordination, which has aimed at maintaining currency stability for the last two years but was overrun by a rising dollar this summer. And it has knocked much of the bullish feeling out of the market for now.
''Every other day this week the dollar has been lower,'' said Frank Watson, vice president of the Swiss Bank Corporation. ''So nobody is going to rush in and buy dollars. They want to see the decline stop first.''
 
Wave of Intervention
In late trading yesterday, after another wave of intervention by the Fed and other central banks, the dollar was at 1.8710 marks, down from 1.8818 Thursday and 1.9530 a week earlier. Against the yen, the dollar was 139.00, down from 140.50 Thursday and 146.05 a week earlier. The dollar also fell against other major currencies, including the British pound and the French franc.
But much of the decline this week, traders said, is based in part on the assumption that the United Sates, Japan and West Germany will soon back up the dollar selling by changing interest rates. Traders could be disappointed next week when the policy-setting committees of the Federal Reserve and the West German Bundesbank meet to review interest rates. Without the changes, the dollar could move higher again unless the huge intervention continues.
Many analysts expect the Bundesbank to raise rates on Thursday to help support the German currency, But the Japanese are not expected to move their rates higher. And the Federal Reserve is not expected to ease rates here, which would lower the value of the dollar by decreasing the attractiveness of dollar-denominated investments.
John Lipsky, director of international bond trading at Salomon Brothers in London, said the Group of Seven communique ''was an announcement that the G-7 was going to do strategic bombing and the traders naturally ran for cover.''
The bond trader added, ''But it is also inevitable that the all-clear will sound and unless there is a clear shift in attitude, the traders will come back and test again,'' trying to push the dollar higher.
 
Rate Expectations
He said he believes that many traders are expecting interest rate changes, especially from the West Germans on Thursday. So if there is not one, the reaction would be to push the dollar higher and raise some doubts about the commitment of Group of Seven to coordination on interest rates.
Mr. Watson expects the West Germans to raise rates, but does not think the Fed or the Japanese will act. But he said that continued intervention could hold the dollar down because he believes the underlying strength is weaker than some other traders contend.
The Saturday communique said the rise of the dollar this summer was ''inconsistent with longer-run economic fundamentals'' and that a continued rise ''could adversely affect prospects for the world economy.''
On Sunday, bankers who were in Washington for the annual meeting of the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank were divided over how strong the communique was. But on Monday, when the Federal Reserve and the Bank of Canada were reported selling dollars in the Tokyo market the dollar fell quickly and was down more than five pfennings and almost four yen for the day.
 
Up to $5 Billion
Some traders have estimated that the central banks may have sold as much as $5 billion this week to push the dollar down. But just as important was the intervention outside of home markets, a technique that is rarely used because it means keeping staff members on duty all night. When that is done, it sends an extra signal of commitment that wakes up the market.
In trading yesterday, the Fed and the Bank of Canada joined the Bank of Japan in selling dollars in Tokyo and the Bundesbank said it sold dollars in Asia.

HEADLINE: Control Data Searching for A New Chief

BYLINE: By JOHN MARKOFF

 BODY:
The Control Data Corporation's chairman and chief executive, Robert M. Price, said yesterday that the company's board had established a committee to select his successor as chief executive - a position he plans to give up in the next three months.
The Minneapolis-based company, which has struggled financially during the 1980's, withdrew from the supercomputer business earlier this year and declared a $497.3 million loss for the second quarter. Control Data was once a leading maker of mainframe computers, but Mr. Price had recently said that he would shift the company from hardware manufacturing to the information services business. 
Control Data had large losses in 1985 and 1986 and only small profits in 1987 and 1988. The company said yesterday that it expected to lose money in 1989.
 
Will Remain as Chairman
Mr. Price, who is 59 years old, said that he did not intend to give up the chairmanship, but he felt that he had already accomplished much of the turnabout. Taking note of criticism that has been leveled at the company's management, he said that replacing himself as chief executive officer would remove speculation and rumor.
''The company is positioned exactly where I set out four years ago to position it,'' he said. ''I think that at this point the company could benefit from a change of leadership. I don't think it would be bad if I stayed on; it's just that I think it would be better if I didn't.''
In addition to closing its ETA supercomputer subsidiary earlier this year, Mr. Price said yesterday that Control Data was completing the $450 million sale of its Imprimis Technology disk drive subsidiary to Seagate Technology Inc., a Scotts Valley, Calif., disk drive maker.
Much of Control Data's success with Imprimis has been credited to Larry Perlman, the subsidiary's president and chief operating officer, who is widely viewed as Mr. Price's likely successor.
 
Process Questioned
Several analysts said they were surprised that Mr. Perlman had not been immediately appointed chief executive and questioned the company's strategy of setting up a public search.
Company sources said that although Mr. Perlman was the leading candidate for the chief executive job, Mr. Price did not want it to appear as if he were single-handedly choosing a successor. Mr. Price said that the search committee would consider outside candidates, as well as Mr. Perlman.
Control Data also said yesterday that it had elected Charles Marshall to its board. Mr. Marshall was vice chairman of the American Telephone and Telegraph Company from 1985 until his retirement earlier this year.

HEADLINE: Top-Grade Issues Gain on Wall St.

BYLINE: By KURT EICHENWALD

 BODY:
Declining interest rates made Wall Street a fertile environment in the third quarter for American companies seeking to borrow, so long as their bonds were investment grade.
But the quarter was not particularly good for investment banks, as a declining appetite for new issues of common stock and the troubles of the high-yield ''junk bond'' market chopped two of the businesses where Wall Street earns much of its fees.
A total of $71.1 billion in domestic securities was issued in the quarter ended yesterday, according to the Securities Data Company. That was up 10 percent from $64.7 billion in the third quarter of 1988. 
 
Effect of 'Junk Bond' Troubles
In some ways, the increase in corporate debt issuance reflected the difficulties of the ''junk bond'' market, which were precipitated by the financial troubles of the Campeau Corporation, a large junk bond issuer, underwriters said. As the view spread that outside raiders would have more difficulty starting takeover bids because of the trouble in the junk bond market, the appetite among investors for investment-grade debt increased, they said.
''As the takeover environment has changed, the investors out there have perceived less risk in buying corporate bonds,'' said Roger M. Vasey, senior vice president at Merrill Lynch Capital Markets. ''It was an excellent quarter for us in a number of product areas.'' Buyers of high-grade corporate bonds have feared that takeovers could convert companies' debt from investment grade to speculative, causing bond prices to tumble.
Industrial and banking companies led the way in borrowing. Many of the industrial concerns were attracted by the decline of interest rates, and they issued bonds to replace high levels of commercial paper. The banking industry, faced with new international capital requirements, issued debt in an effort to conform to the rules, the underwriters said.
 
An 11.6% Rise in Bond Issues
Reflecting the demand in the debt market, a total of more than $61.6 billion in bonds was issued in the third quarter, up 11.6 percent from $55.2 billion in the third quarter of last year, according to Securities Data.
But despite record performance in the Dow Jones industrial average, corporations continued to shy away from issuing common stock, as they have since the market crash in October 1987. Equity issues dropped 20.1 percent in the quarter: companies issued $6.1 billion in common stock, compared with $7.7 billion in the third quarter last year.
Also, as uncertainty spread through the junk bond market, issues began to drop off. Only $5.3 billion in junk debt was issued in the third quarter, down 24.2 percent from $7 billion in the period in 1988. But for the first nine months, the volume of $20.2 billion in junk underwritings rose 4.5 percent from a year earlier.
 
Total Underwritings Up 1.9%
For the first nine months of the year, underwritings of all domestic securities totaled $214.5 billion, up 1.9 percent, from $210.4 billion in the period of 1988. Debt issues rose, but the volume of new common stock issues fell 37.5 percent to a five-year low of $15 billion, from $24 billion.
For the first nine months, Merrill Lynch Capital Markets ranked as the leading underwriter of domestic issues, being the lead manager of $35.5 billion in securities, or 16.5 percent of the total market. But for the third quarter, Goldman, Sachs & Company, which ranked second for the year to date, finished first, with $11.9 billion in underwritings, or 16.7 percent of the market. Merrill was second, with $10.3 billion.
For the first nine months of 1989, Drexel Burnham Lambert Inc.'s legal problems may have cut into its dominance of the junk bond market. Although it remained the leading underwriter, its market share fell to 42.7 percent, from 49 percent a year earlier. In the third quarter, its market share fell to 33.1 percent, as it underwrote $1.7 billion in new junk issues. Still, that was more than twice the $775 million underwritten by Salomon Brothers Inc., which ranked second.
 
Leader in Common Stock Issues
In common stock issues, Goldman, Sachs was the leading underwriter for both the quarter and the first nine months.
Fees for the underwriters came to $2.2 billion in the first nine months, Securities Data said, down 26.7 percent from $3 billion a year earlier. In the quarter, the total fell 18.6 percent, to $768 million, from $943 million a year earlier.
Merrill ranked first in fees for both the quarter and the nine months, with $132 million in the quarter and $332 million for the year. Drexel was second for the nine months, with fees of $314 million, but fell to seventh in the quarter, with $53 million, a third of the fees it had in the corresponding quarter of 1988. Securities Data said $50 million of Drexel's fees came from junk bonds.

HEADLINE: Your Money;
Gains-Tax Sales: Caution Advised

BYLINE: By Jan M. Rosen

 BODY:
Thanks to the House of Representatives, investors across the country have visions of capital gains windfalls dancing in their heads. But before selling any assets in the expectation that gains will be subject to a more favorable tax treatment than under the Tax Reform Act of 1986, which taxes them as ordinary income, investors are well advised to consider the economics of holding versus selling.
The House plan to cut the tax on long-term capital gains by 30 percent ''has widespread ramifications that require careful study before undertaking any action to take advantage of the break,'' said Sidney Kess, a tax lawyer who recently examined the subject at a symposium for the American Institute of Certified Public Accountants.
The first caveat, obviously, is that there will be no cut until the House has finished action on the budget bill and it has gone to the Senate and through a conference committee, where changes could be made. Nevertheless, most tax experts say there is momentum for a cut. So, it is prudent for those who hold appreciated assets to consider what action they should take if a cut is enacted. Mr. Kess passed along several observations from the practitioners at the institute's conference. 
Mr. Kess concludes a cut would be ''good for those who, for good and sufficient investment reasons, are ready to sell and for those who seek liquidity at a price.'' But before selling for the tax cut alone, he advises investors ''to sit down with a sharp pencil'' and figure out if they will really be better off.
If the tax break causes a wave of selling in the stock market, then it would not make sense to join in to qualify for the tax break, he said. Sellers in the 28 percent bracket would still pay 19.6 percent in Federal taxes, plus state and local taxes and transaction charges. Consequently, they might have only 75 percent of their holdings to reinvest. Longer term, the practitioners saw a capital gains tax cut as benefiting equities and negative for fixed-income securities.
Older people concerned with leaving a solid estate for their children or other beneficiaries may not have any advantage from the cut.
''Anyone along in years before selling assets with high appreciation will want to consider that wonderful provision of the income-tax code that still provides for a step-up at death and wipes out all tax liability for appreciation up to that time,'' Mr. Kess said. Of course, large estates are subject to estate taxes.
Collectibles, works of art, stamps and coins would all be excluded from the benefits of the tax cut, as well as from the House plan's later benefits of adjustment for inflation. And over time that exclusion could hurt these markets, the practitioners said. So it might make sense to sell some holdings ''while the unloading is good,'' Mr. Kess said.
Real estate is more complicated, said Bertil Westlin, co-editor with Mr. Kess of the Commerce Clearing House Financial and Estate Planning Reporter. The market is depressed in some areas, and the assets released by savings and loan failures has exacerbated the situation. So, while real estate would be a beneficiary of the tax cuts, market conditions can well militate in favor of holding.
Another consideration in the case of investment real estate is depreciation. The entire amount of depreciation deductions taken on some properties could be recaptured as ordinary income if they are sold now. A residence and raw land will not be subject to the recapture provisions. But with any real estate, there are commissions and legal fees.
Mr. Westlin pointed out one situation in which the new law could be particularly advantageous: gifts of appreciated property to children over age 13 who are in the 15 percent income tax bracket. The child could sell the assets, pay tax at a 10.5 percent rate and use the balance to pay college costs.
Family-owned businesses could also be affected if the rate is lowered. The House proposal would grant preferential treatment to assets sold between Sept. 14, 1989, and Dec. 31, 1991. Therefore, Mr. Westlin said, ''if an individual is a party to a buy-sell agreement that is triggered, among other events, by retirement, he or she might want to consider moving up the date of retirement to take advantage of the cut in capital gains.''
Timber owners and farmers might also want to consider accelerating transactions.
If the House version passes, investors have until 1991 to get the lower rate, Mr. Kess noted, ''so you don't have to act this year.'' They should consider their overall financial and tax situations and determine which year would be best. Assets must be held at least a year to qualify for the 30 percent exclusion.

HEADLINE: Leading Indicators Post 2d Consecutive Increase

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
A Government index used to forecast the nation's economic course rose three-tenths of 1 percent in August, the second consecutive monthly gain, the Commerce Department said today.
The increase in the index of leading indicators suggests modest growth ahead and no immediate recession.
The rise, which follows trendless ups and downs over the earlier months of this year, was in line with estimates and had little perceptible effect on financial markets.
''It's telling us there is no recession within view, and no great surge of economic activity ahead either,'' said Cynthia Latta, senior financial economist at DRI/McGraw Hill in Lexington, Mass. 
 
'Doing Relatively Well'
''The manufacturing economy is sluggish, but the rest of the economy is doing relatively well,'' said Tony Villamil, the Commerce Department's chief economist, who was senior vice president and chief economist with the Southeast Banking Corporation in Miami until April.
In pointing to further expansion, the report also signaled little likelihood of any Federal Reserve moves to lower interest rates, despite international efforts to push the dollar lower. The relatively high interest rates in the United States, which have been attractive to foreign investors, have been among the factors that raised the dollar in the last few months.
After a meeting of the finance ministers of the seven leading industrial powers here last weekend, however, the central banks have been intervening heavily in the currency markets to check the dollar's rise, which some economists fear could hurt future exports and growth. 5 of 11 Statistics Advance The composite index of leading indicators measures 11 sets of business statistics that generally point to directions of future business activity. Five of these showed positive movements that led to the August rise.
These were gains in manufacturers' new orders for consumer goods, by far the biggest influence; higher stock prices; higher money supply figures; lower initial unemployment claims, and increased business permits.
On the negative side were drops in an index measuring consumer confidence; lower prices for sensitive materials like scrap iron or lumber; declining unfilled orders for manufacturers; reduced plant and equipment spending; faster deliveries from suppliers, suggesting sluggish demand, and a shorter workweek.
The three-tenths of 1 percent rise in August followed an one-tenth of 1 percent gain in July. But the index had fallen one-tenth of 1 percent in June. Over the eight months of this year it has declined four times and risen four times. 2 Straight Gains July and August were the first two months that the indicator has been up consecutively since December and January.
Past performance has shown that the indicator has been far from infallible. A commonly held notion says three straight declines suggest that a recession is on the horizon, but five straight declines were recorded around the stock market crash in October 1987. These never interrupted a business expansion that will be seven years old in November, the longest peacetime growth in history.
''With the rate of inflation falling faster than the growth in demand for goods and services, the real gross national product should rise throughout 1990,'' said Lawrence A. Hunter, deputy chief economist at the United States Chamber of Commerce.
 
Economic Growth Predictions
Last July, the Bush Administration projected economic growth next year at 2.6 percent, against 2.7 percent expected this year. In 1987, the economy expanded by 3.4 percent. Given labor and productivity growth, economists believe the economy has a potential for noninflationary growth of around 3 percent.
The various changes in August left the index at 144.4 percent of its 1982 average of 100. So far this year, the index has risen at an annual rate of 3.8 percent, compared with an increase of 4.5 percent for all of 1988.
Despite the message of the Government's chief economic forecasting gauge, some economists, citing rising consumer installment debt and pressure on profits and cash flow of corporations, say they still foresee a recession next year.
David B. Bostian, president of Bostian Research Associates of New York, said his firm's macroeconomic index, which has 25 components against the 11 in the Commerce index, is pointing to a recession ''by January or February.''

HEADLINE: Prices Paid To Farmers Decline 0.7%

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
Prices farmers received for raw products dropped seven-tenths of a percent from August to September, leaving the monthly average also seventh-tenths of a percent below the level a year earlier, the Agriculture Department said today.
Lower prices for cattle, hogs and potatoes were responsible for most of the decrease from the August average, dropping the ''prices received'' index to an 11-month low, the department's Agricultural Statistics Board said.
Higher prices for oranges, milk, lettuce and applies partly offset the decline for the other commodities in the month. 
''Compared with a year earlier, lower prices for soybeans, corn, tomatoes and oats more than offset higher prices for milk, cotton, potatoes and eggs,'' the report said.
Milk prices gained for the fourth consecutive month, winding up in September at the highest level since February 1985.
''Feed grain prices were mixed, but corn, the major component of this index, increased three cents from August,'' the report said. ''An 18-cent decrease in the price of soybeans moved the oil-bearing crop index lower.''
No change was reported in the prices farmers pay to meet expenses. Those are adjusted every three months, and the next will be reported in October. The July ''prices paid'' index was up six-tenths of 1 percent from April and 3.5 percent from July 1988.
Department economists say net cash income of farmers - gross cash income minus cash expenses - is expected to decline 5 percent to 13 percent from last year's record of $59.9 billion.
The 1988 drought forced commodity prices up, and many producers were able to sell harvests at the higher prices. Many farmers also had grain stored from previous crops.
Economists say the cash income decline this year is expected to stem from farmers' larger cash expenses as they increase plantings, and also because of lower Federal subsidies.
Consumer food prices are forecast by the department to rise an average of 5 percent to 7 percent this year, after rising 4.1 percent in 1988.
Overall crop prices rose eight-tenths of 1 percent from August but averaged 5.9 percent less than in September 1988.

HEADLINE: Stocks Mixed on Decline in Dollar

BYLINE: By PHILLIP H. WIGGINS

 BODY:
Stocks ended mixed yesterday, restrained by a softer dollar.
The Dow Jones industrial average fell 2.09 points, to 2,692.82, after being up more than 15 points at 1 P.M. Advances on the New York Stock Exchange, however, topped declines by 885 to 565. For the week, the Dow, which see-sawed throughout the five days, rose 11.21 points. 
For the third quarter the Dow was up 10.4 percent, the best performance since it rose 21.6 percent in the first quarter of 1987.
In the broader market, the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index edged up 0.55, to 349.15, while the New York Stock Exchange composite index added 0.42, to 193.97.
 
'Window Dressing' Support
''The declining dollar currently is putting modest pressure on interest rates,'' said Rao Chalasani, chief investment strategist at Prescott, Ball & Turben Inc. in Cleveland. ''Once the U.S. currency stabilizes, the stock market might make further gains.'' He added that disappointing earnings would be another potential hurdle for equities in the next few weeks.
''Window dressing,'' traders said, provided a modicum of support for the Dow, as institutions continued to fatten their portfolios at the end of the quarter.
Volume on the Big Board was155.3 million shares yesterday, down from 164.2 million on Thursday.
 
Advances Among Airlines
The Dow transportation average jumped 11.74 points, to 1,449.86, as airlines like the UAL Corporation, the parent of United Airlines, rose 4 1/2, to 282; Delta Air Lines gained 2 3/8, to 73 3/4, and USAir Group tacked on 1 3/8, to 46 1/4. The AMR Corporation, the parent of American Airlines, gained 2 3/4, to 86 1/2, on continuing rumors that Donald J. Trump is accumulating a stake in the company. AMR was the second-most heavily traded stock on the Big Board, on a volume of more than 2.6 million shares.
Harcourt Brace Jovanovich Inc., the most active issue on the Big Board, plunged 4 1/8, to 12 1/8, on a volume of 3.6 million shares. The Anheuser-Busch Companies announced yesterday that it would acquire six theme parks from the publisher for $1.1 billion.
Among active blue chips, the International Business Machines Corporation rose 1/4, to 109 1/4; the McDonald's Corporation slipped 5/8, to 29 3/4; the Bristol-Myers Company added 3/8, to 52 3/4; the American Telephone and Telegraph Company, added a point, to 44 7/8; the General Electric Company slipped 1/2, to 56 1/2, and the Chevron Corporation jumped 2 7/8, to 61.
Entergy rose 1/2, to 22 5/8. The company said it would ask the Securities and Exchange Commission for authority to buy back as much as 10 percent of its stock.
On the American Stock Exchange, the Amex market value index rose 3.01 points, to 388.76, while in over-the-counter trading the Nasdaq composite rose 1.78 points, to 472.92.

HEADLINE: Treasury Issues Recover After Early Drop

BYLINE: By H. J. MAIDENBERG

 BODY:
Many banks borrowed excess reserves, also known as Federal funds, from other banks and institutions yesterday, to show lower liabilities on their third-quarter statements, sending the Federal funds rate well above 9 percent briefly. That, along with a weaker dollar, depressed the prices of Treasury securities early in the day, dealers said.
But later in the session, the shorter maturities recovered as the funds rate declined and the longer issues improved somewhat as the dollar firmed. Some traders who had sold Treasuries short bought to cover their positions. 
Dealers said that the Government's report of a three-tenths of a percent rise in the leading economic indicators for August was slightly above expectations but had no impact on the fixed-income market. Nor did the rise in spot crude oil prices to over $20 a barrel, a new contract high, affect prices.
''Over all, the dollar remains the major influence in the Treasury debt market, but yesterday and today it was the end-of-quarter upward pressure on the Federal funds rate that moved prices,'' said William V. Sullivan Jr., senior vice president at Dean Witter Reynolds. ''Come Monday morning, we expect this pressure to evaporate and the funds rate to start easing.''
 
Temporary Loans
The Federal funds rate is what banks and other financial houses charge each other for temporary loans. The demand for the funds was so great that the rate rose despite the Federal Reserve's second infusion of $1.5 billion of reserves into the banking system in as many days.
As a result, Mr. Sullivan noted, the funds rate, which had traded between 9.20 and 9.5 percent on Thursday, jumped as high as 9.75 percent early yesterday, and then declined to close at 8.5 percent. Mr. Sullivan expects the funds rate to trade at 8.875 percent, where many money market analysts think the Fed wants it, sometime next week. ''There are ample reserves in the market,'' he said. ''It's just that they are not being efficiently allocated.''
Because most Treasury securities dealers finance their inventories at about the funds rate, their higher carrying cost prompted many to mark down inventories of the lower-yielding bills and notes.
Consequently, the discount rate on Treasury 90-day bills rose a basis point, to 7.90 percent; the six-month bills gained three basis points, at 7.87 percent, and the one-year bills added two basis points, at 7.86 percent. A basis point is a hundredth of a percentage point.
 
Prices of Notes
Elsewhere in the secondary market for Treasury issues, the new 8 3/8 percent notes that were auctioned Tuesday at an average yield of 8.39 percent were offered late in the day at an unchanged price of 99 28/32, to yield 8.44 percent. The new 8 1/4's of 1993 rose 1/32, to 99 17/32, for a yield of 8.39 percent. The new four-year notes were auctioned last Wednesday at an average yield of 8.35 percent. Many of the speculators who bought the new two- and four-year notes were prominent sellers of these issues yesterday, dealers said.
The bellwether Treasury long bonds, the 8 1/8's of 2019, climbed 7/32, to 98 26/32, after being as low as 98 5/ 32 early in the session. At 98 26/32, the yield eased to 8.23 percent, from 8.24 percent the previous day, but was above the 8.19 percent of a week earlier.
In Chicago, the spot December Treasury bond futures contract settled at 95.27, up 4/32 on the day, but down 12/32 on the week.

HEADLINE: CONSUMER'S WORLD: Guidepost;
Credit-Card Rates

 BODY:
Interest rates on bank credit cards are up an average one-quarter of a percentage point since the beginning of the year, despite the glut of cards in the marketplace. To retain card holders as rates rise, banks have extended periods for interest-free payments to make their cards more attractive, and many have eliminated annual fees. For people who carry over a balance, a low interest rate is desirable. But if you pay off your charges in full each month, look for a card with no annual fee. Many of these have variable rates, generally tied to short-term Government securities, with quarterly adjustments. Remember that since Sept. 1, the Government has required all issuers of credit cards to include full fee and rate details in applications and solicitations. Here are some cards that are among those with the lowest annual percentage rates, two with fixed rates (F) and two with variable rates (V).
